A

I AM VERY GRATEFUL TO LI CHUN, INSTITUTE OF VERTEBRATE PALEontology and Paleoanthropology, Beijing, who invited me to collaborate in the description of many fine fossils he collected. I am equally thankful to Janice Frisch, acquisition editor at the Indiana University Press, and series editor James O. Farlow for their interest in the project, as well as their help and support to see this book through to publication. Marlene Donnelly (scientific illustrator) and John Weinstein (photographer) from the Field Museum of Natural History, Chicago, offered their help and expertise in the illustration of the book. Christine Giannoni offered equally invaluable help in obtaining often obscure literature.

The Henry Fairfield Osborn Papers, curated in Archives of the American Museum of Natural History, were kindly made available by my son, Lukas Rieppel. I also thank the staff at the German Federal Archives (formerly Berlin Documentation Center) and the University Archives of Göttingen and Greifswald for their help and support in my research.

Last but not least, I thank my wife, Myriam, and our sons, Michael and Lukas, who accompanied me on a journey that allowed me to pursue the research recounted in this book.
**Turtles as Hopeful Monsters**
**Introduction: Turtles as Hopeful Monsters**

I

HERE THEY ARE, STILL WITH US, BOXED UP IN A SHELL, SEEMING survivors of the distant geologic past: prehistoric creatures that both predated and outlived the dinosaurs. As a symbol in Hindu and Chinese mythology, the turtle supports the earth—but what supports the turtle? The philosophically intriguing answer is this: it's turtles all the way down. Sluggish on land, seemingly stubborn in their behavior, turtles came to symbolize longevity, strength, and endurance in ancient China. The longevity of the turtle lineage is indeed remarkable, as is its evolutionary strength if measured by the numbers of species that have populated Earth for at least 220 million years, which is the approximate age of the oldest fossil turtle currently known. (For a discussion of fossils some 260 million years old, controversially interpreted as the oldest stem-turtle, see chap. 3.) In spite of their highly constrained body plan, turtles show a surprising potential for evolutionary diversification, with species that conquered a great variety of habitats: forests and grasslands, deserts and karst mountains, rivers, ponds, lakes, even the open sea, with some 331 living species currently recognized (Fritz and Havas, 2007; van Dijk et al., 2012).

As Adler has remarked, "Turtles are one of nature's most immediately recognizable life forms" (2007:139), bizarre in their own way, which is what seems to attract people's attention as well as scientists' curiosity. Where do turtles come from, and how did their unique anatomy and bodily functions evolve? These are questions that have spurred intense research as well as heated debate for 200 years and more. Yet even in the wake of the development of revolutionizing techniques in modern molecular biology, the answer remains as elusive as ever. Progress has been made, but a lot still remains to be discovered.

The eminent twentieth-century evolutionary biologist Theodosius Dobzhansky once noted, "Nothing makes sense in biology except in the light of evolution" (1973:125). Philosophers of biology Kim Sterelny and Paul E. Griffiths paraphrased this famous line as, "Nothing in biology makes sense except in the context of its place in phylogeny, its context in the great tree of life" (1999:379). To ask where turtles come from is to raise the question of turtle ancestry. It certainly is uncontroversial that turtles are reptiles, but that does not tell us what group of reptiles gave rise to the turtle lineage in the distant part. What was the ancestor of turtles? That question was certainly a legitimate one to ask in the middle of the twentieth century and earlier, when the study of fossils was largely motivated by the search for ancestors of still-living descendants. The study of fossils retrieved from successive layers of rock was at that time compared to leafing through an illustrated book on evolution, supposedly revealing a gallery of ancestors and descendants.

Paleontologists started to research the ancestry of turtles well back in the nineteenth century, but until recently, all the fossil turtles pulled from sedimentary rock spanning vast geologic time were already finished exemplars, complete with carapace and plastron. It's indeed turtles all the way down! In the 1970s and 1980s, however, the study of evolutionary relationships was revolutionized; comparative biology went through a cladistic revolution. The search for ancestors was rejected as dilettante science; instead, the search for sister groups became all the rage. When, through evolutionary time, a species lineage splits into two, the stem species gives rise to two daughter species. These two daughter species are sister species; their respective descendants thus form sister groups. Rather than asking what was the ancestor of turtles, the question—in the aftermath of the cladistic revolution—must be, what kind of a reptile species was it that shared with the turtle lineage a common ancestor that was not also ancestral to any other reptile lineage? In other words, which lineage of reptiles is more closely related to turtles than either is to any other reptile group? To answer this question requires some foundational knowledge about the basic structure of the reptile skull and the various modifications it underwent as reptiles diversified into turtles, crocodiles, lizards, and snakes. This is because the skull, as well as other parts of the skeleton, may hold the key to understanding turtle origins. More recently, molecular data, in particular DNA data, have in large measure been adduced in the search for turtle origins. But in spite of all these efforts, the deep evolutionary history of the turtle lineage remains only dimly lit.

Some paleontologists may still maintain that turtles are living fossils, descendants of ancient stem-reptiles that have long vanished from the surface of the earth. Other authors, working mostly with skeletal features, have argued that turtles are more closely related to the tuatara and the scaly reptiles, such as lizards and snakes, than they are related to any other reptile group, including crocodiles and birds. Researchers working with DNA data, in contrast, have found that turtles are more closely related to crocodiles and birds than they are related to any other reptile group, including the tuatara and the scaly reptiles. Although discussion and debate continue, the current situation looks like one where the experts have agreed to disagree. Beyond hard yet still inconclusive evidence, rhetoric is also at work in science. Science is not just about observation and measurement; it is also about how scientists talk about their observations and measurements. The controversial case of turtle relationships provides prime examples of how the language used by a group of scientists can guide scientific inquiry in a particular direction to the exclusion of alternative perspectives. Science, at least the kind that seeks to decipher evolutionary relationships, does not happen in the germ-free environment of a clean lab, pursued by a cold, detached, and unbiased intellect. It is instead an eminently social enterprise, collaborative and competitive at the same time.

Better knowledge of turtle sister group relationships would certainly help to understand how the extravagant turtle body plan evolved. How did the turtle shell, its associated structures, and the consequent physiological peculiarities of turtles evolve? Everybody would certainly agree that a finished turtle, complete with carapace and plastron, differs radically in its anatomy from all other reptiles, such as crocodiles or lizards. But how are such radical evolutionary transformations possible? Darwin argued that evolution is a slow, gradual, stepwise process of transformation, fueled by random variation and constrained by natural selection. No biologist would question the reality of such Darwinian evolution, a process that plays out at the level of populations of interbreeding organisms that represent a species—perhaps a species that is about to split, slowly and gradually, into two daughter species. What some evolutionary biologists doubted in the past, however, and continue to doubt today, is that such Darwinian evolutionary processes are sufficient to explain radical evolutionary change as is evident in the turtle body plan.

There thus must exist additional mechanisms of evolutionary transformation—mechanisms that cut deeper than Darwinian variation and natural selection. The historical and contemporary research into these mechanisms could collectively be subsumed under an umbrella theory of "hopeful monsters," a term introduced by Richard Goldschmidt. Past—and now obsolete—theories of orthogenetic evolution stipulated a goal directedness and cyclicality of evolutionary processes. Every major evolutionary lineage, each representing a distinct body plan, would lawfully have cycled through a phase of youthfulness, adulthood, senescence, and death (extinction). The birth (origin) of a new major evolutionary lineage would have occurred through a disruption and reorganization of early embryonic development in representatives of the youthful phase of the ancestral evolutionary lineage. The evolutionary development of a major evolutionary lineage would thus have run parallel to the individual development of an organism. Modern theories of evolutionary developmental biology stipulate that it is the rewiring of the developmental program guiding early embryonic development that lays the foundation for the emergence of evolutionary novelties, for which the turtle shell is a prime example. The embryonic development of the turtle shell, together with recently found fossils of the oldest turtle currently known, conclusively refute the theory advanced by Jan Versluys in 1914 of a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor that treats the carapace not as an evolutionary novelty but as the product of a gradual and stepwise transformation of ancestral bony structures. Although the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor was an entirely theoretical construct, it was nevertheless infused with a touch of reality, although not through paleontological discovery but rather through consensus building among scientists—a construction rather than a tangible fossil.

The journey we are about to embark on starts with an entry-level account of early stages of investigations into the reptile tree of life. A number of authorities on reptile evolution are introduced from an insider's perspective of science, as are the protagonists of the cladistic revolution and the goals of the battle they fought. The historical quest for the turtle ancestor will be sketched, followed by an account of modern, i.e., cladistic, approaches to the investigation of turtle relationships. In order to address the question of the origin and evolution of the turtle shell, it is first necessary to contrast the Darwinian account of species origination and transformation with various theories of hopeful monsters, both old and new. Tensions will become apparent between the burgeoning science of genetics and the equally youthful sciences of paleobiology and population biology—tensions that reflected the social politics of the times leading up to World War II. The years after the war saw a progressive hardening of the modern synthesis of evolutionary theory, which reduced all evolutionary change to population-level processes driven by the Darwinian twin mechanisms of variation and natural selection. However, some critics remained unconvinced of this monolithic account of evolution and raised their voices in the late 1970s and 1980s in the first blossoming of a research program that would later be dubbed evolutionary developmental biology, or evo-devo. The old idea of the hopeful monster once again began to guide research into the mechanisms of major evolutionary transitions. This perspective opens the door to a discussion of the origin and evolution of the turtle shell, a prime example of an evolutionary novelty that will be illuminated through an account of its embryonic development. It has long been claimed that embryonic development can teach us something about evolutionary transformations. What's even more exciting is the claim, issued by a selected few, that embryonic development could even allow prediction of the kinds of fossils that still remain to be discovered. But such discoveries are not easy to come by.

For the remains of an organism to become fossilized is a rare chance event. For a fossil to be found is an even chancier event—unless there prevailed in the past regional conditions highly favorable to the fossilization of carcasses, as was the case some 252 to 220 million years ago at the bottom of the sea that covered what today is southwestern China. Add to this an army of fossil hunters leaving no stone unturned or unsplit, and your chances of finding important fossils greatly increase. Since the late 1990s, the rural population of southwestern China has engaged in a veritable gold rush, with their gold being cashed out in the form of Triassic marine reptiles. Unauthorized—and often dangerous—excavation activity, the forgery of fossils, and illegal fossil trade all hint at an unbridled capitalist spirit. In the midst of all this frenzy, science has reaped huge profits as well, albeit of an intellectual nature. The idea that embryonic development might help predict what kind of transitional fossil might be expected was spectacularly born out by the discovery of fossils of the oldest turtle currently known, which come from marine Triassic deposits in southwestern China. (Older fossils have recently, and controversially, been placed on the turtle stem, as discussed in chap. 3.) Truly a turtle in the making—a turtle on the half shell!

**1.1.** Turtle skull illustrations from Edwin S. Goodrich, _Studies on the Structure and Development of Vertebrates_ (1930). _Left_ , Skull of green sea turtle ( _Chelonia mydas_ ). _Right, top_ , Skull of soft-shelled turtle _Aspideretes gangeticus_ in dorsal ( _left_ ) and ventral ( _right_ ) views. _Right, bottom_ , Skull of fossil _Proganochleys quenstedti_ from Late Triassic of Germany.

_Reproduced from E. S. Goodrich_ , Studies on the Structure and Development of Vertebrates, _Vol. 1 (New York: Dover Publications, 1958), figs. 371, 372, 373; with permission from publisher_.

**Misplaced Turtles**

1

Herr Professor

OFF WE WERE ON A PLANE TO LONDON, OUR SPIRITS RISING HIGH in anticipation. It must have been the fall of 1972. I was an undergraduate student in zoology at the University of Basel in Switzerland, my companion a graduate student in zoology at the University of Zurich. We had both dedicated ourselves to the study of amphibians and reptiles, a branch of zoological science called herpetology. Othmar Stemmler, son of local celebrity Carl Stemmler, informally tutored us both (Honegger and Obst, 2014). Othmar's father was a keeper at the Basel Zoological Garden responsible for the great apes, who had acquired some prominence through his popular and engaging books and radio shows about zoo animals and African safaris. Othmar was an elementary school teacher, spending most of his free time working as a volunteer in the herpetological section at the Basel Natural History Museum. Herpetology had had a long and famous history at the museum, which had resulted in a large and historically significant collection of amphibians and reptiles, with numerous rare and important specimens from all over the world. However, herpetological research at the Basel museum, once of international reputation, tapered off and came to a halt toward the mid-twentieth century, the collections left orphaned. Heini Hediger, a renowned pioneer in the field of animal psychology and director of the Basel zoo from 1944 through 1953, was the last scientist of international reputation who, again as a volunteer, had worked in the herpetological collections of the Basel museum before Othmar's time (Honegger, 2015).

Although he was self-trained, Othmar Stemmler was in the early 1970s one of only two or three experts in Switzerland who specialized in the study of species-level taxonomy of amphibians and reptiles. He took me under his tutelage at the Basel Natural History Museum while I was still a high school student in the late 1960s and introduced me to the art and science of species-level taxonomy. Ernst Mayr's (1904–2005) then brand-new textbook, _Principles of Systematic Zoology_ (1969), was never far from reach. Mayr, originally of German extraction, was the undisputed preeminent evolutionary biologist of the day, professor at Harvard's Museum of Comparative Zoology, and author of the then-current standard text on evolutionary theory, his 1963 _Animal Species and Evolution_. Mayr's systematics was heavily based on his own field studies of population biology and patterns of speciation, i.e., modes of species origination in birds, especially those of New Guinea. After graduating from the University of Berlin, Mayr accepted appointment as a curator at the American Museum of Natural History (AMNH) in New York in 1931—an employment that offered a most welcome escape from the troubled economy and politics of the Weimar Republic. He vacated his New York position in 1953 when he moved to the Museum of Comparative Zoology. After the collapse of the Third Reich in his home country, Mayr was instrumental in organizing, with the help of the American Ornithologists' Union, the mailing of CARE packages to needy German ornithologists, drawing on his old contacts to make sure that no Nazi fellow travelers would profit from these efforts (Rieppel, 2013a).

At the Basel museum, with Mayr's book at hand, Othmar and I measured size, recorded colors, and counted scales until we had convinced ourselves that some local population of lizards or snakes in some corner of this world was sufficiently distinct from its neighbors so as to be recognized as a new species, or subspecies. Together with like-minded reptile aficionados, all of them older and more experienced than me, Othmar loved to put together collecting expeditions that ventured out to places around the Mediterranean, though with a preference for the Atlas Mountains in Morocco. Back at the museum, the collected animals had to be properly pickled, identified, tagged, and cataloged, tasks I went about in the customary white lab coat but without latex gloves (the carcinogenic properties of formalin had not yet been recognized). Some gecko lizards of the genus _Tarentola_ brought back from localities ranging along the northern margins of the Sahara desert seemed to not quite fit any of the diagnoses of the species then known and formally recognized in the genus. But the number of specimens at our disposal was too small to build a solid taxonomic case for the description of a new species. So off we went to London, my fellow student from Zurich and I, where we had arranged for an appointment in the section of amphibians and reptiles at what was then called the British Museum of Natural History in South Kensington.

The friendly and helpful staff at the museum put all we needed at our disposal: specimens, work space, calipers, and microscopes. But we did not seize this great opportunity after all. Instead, we enjoyed Carnaby Street and King's Road, bookstores and record stores, curries and fish and chips for dinner, and of course Guinness at the pub. London was still affordable at the time. The new species, if it was one, remained undescribed. Perhaps scale counting and calculating limb proportions was not my thing after all. Indeed, as unlikely as this may sound, while sitting in the plane to London, I was studying a classic textbook on the anatomy and embryonic development of vertebrates—that is, animals with a backbone—published by Goodrich in 1930. Edwin S. Goodrich (1868–1946) was an eminent zoologist at Oxford University, specializing in comparative anatomy, embryology, paleontology (the study of fossils), and phylogeny, i.e., the reconstruction of evolutionary relationships based on the insights generated by the aforementioned disciplines. Pages sporting complex drawings of snake skulls with their many teeth and fangs were followed by no less hard-to-understand illustrations of toothless turtle skulls: the massive skull of a sea turtle, the more delicately built skull of a soft-shelled turtle, and the skull of the oldest fossil turtle then known, from the Late Triassic of Germany, adorned with bumps and encrustations some 220 million years old. The text around those illustrations rambled on about the "temporal roofing" that in snakes had "almost entirely disappeared," quite in contrast to turtles, where, at least in the pictured sea turtle and in the fossil one, the cheek region of the skull located behind the eye socket was entirely closed. But this, Goodrich claimed, was not the primitive, ancestral condition for turtles; it was instead "a secondary covering of the temporal region by dermal scutes" (Goodrich, 1930:354). What was I to make of that? With such verbiage still difficult for me to grasp, Goodrich's account and illustrations of embryonic reptile skulls, of blood vessels and nerves snaking their way through a cartilaginous labyrinth, and the significance of all this for the understanding of reptile evolution completely evaded me. I marveled at the illustrations, underlined text I thought I could make sense of, and asked myself if Goodrich's rich, strange, and fascinating world would ever become fully intelligible to me. The intellectual challenge certainly seemed bigger and more attractive than that of counting scales and measuring limbs.

My search for Goodrich's 1930 textbook, _Studies on the Structure and Development of Vertebrates_ , which I found in a famous secondhand bookstore located at the south head of the Mittlere Brücke in Basel, had been motivated by Georg Haas (1905–1981), the Herr Professor, as everybody called him, at the University of Basel (and later the University of Zurich) (Werner, 1965, 1982; Gans, 1983; Adler, 1989:101). He frequented that store whenever he was in town, as did every foreign visitor to the Zoological Institute, the latter located just a few steps from the store up a steep and narrow alley into the historic center of the town clustered around the Basel Minster. Haas, who was born into a Jewish family in Vienna on January 19, 1905, obtained the foundation for a classical, broad-based education at the Humanistisches Gymnasium (classical grammar school) before enrolling at the University of Vienna to study zoology and paleontology under herpetologists Franz Werner (1867–1939) and Otto von Wettstein (1892–1967), and Dutch functional anatomist Jan Versluys (1873–1939), the latter as of 1925 director of the Second Zoological Institute (Salvini-Plawen and Mizzaro, 1999:30). Versulys—a protégé of the politically compromised paleobiologist Othenio Abel (Ehrenberg, 1975:101; Svojtka, 2011:63n56; Taschwer, 2012)—was the principal advisor for his PhD thesis, which he completed in 1928, and which dealt with the functional anatomy of the head skeleton in primitive and poisonous snakes. Explaining the highly complex jaw mechanics of the burrowing blind snakes in the genus _Typhlops_ in the courses he taught later, Haas would provoke incredulous stares from his students when he exclaimed, "Imagine myself being the skull of _Typhlops_ , my arms its upper jaws," all the while moving about his angled arms in no clearly discernible pattern. Indeed, these snakes use their upper jaws in alternating movements that create a unique raking mechanism when feasting on termite and ant eggs and larvae, although they would not spurn adults as prey either.

The Herr Professor also reminisced about taking courses from eccentric paleontologist Franz Baron Nopcsa (1877–1933), of aristocratic Transylvanian extraction, who in the years immediately before the Great War lobbied for an independent kingdom of Albania. He thought that the Habsburg government of Austria-Hungary should install him as king of Albania, which would then allow him to instigate a guerrilla war against the Turks (Elsie, 1999; Weishampel and Kerscher, 2013:404). Haas pictured Nopcsa sitting at his desk, leafing through an enormous folio-sized sketch book featuring illustrations of all kinds of reptile skulls in an attempt to decipher possible evolutionary, i.e., phylogenetic, relationships among them. He also recounted an episode from a seminar on paleobiology offered by Othenio Abel, the founder of said discipline, which aims to reconstruct the biology of extinct plants and animals (Rieppel, 2013b). Could she deduce the direction from which the wind was blowing on the basis of ripple marks manifest on Paleozoic sedimentary rock from Morocco, Abel had asked a baffled student? Nopcsa, who sat in as well, quickly responded in the student's stead: "Could it be that the wind blew once from the West, once from the East, once from the North, and once from the South, just as it does today?"

**1.2.** Skull of blind snake _Typhlops jamaicensis_ , in left lateral ( _top_ ) and ventral ( _bottom_ ) views. Transversely positioned tooth-bearing upper jaws perform highly unusual alternating raking movements during feeding.

_Image courtesy of University of Texas High-Resolution X-ray CT Facility; imaging sponsored by NSF grant EF-0334961_.

**1.3.** Herr Professor Georg Haas demonstrating fossil of mid-Cretaceous snake _Pachyrhachis_ with well-developed hind limbs from 'Ein Yabrud on West Bank, with former student Ephraim Katzir (at microscope), fourth president of Israel.

_Photo courtesy of Professor Yehuda Werner, Hebrew University, Jerusalem_.

In the years 1931–1932, Haas was a postdoctoral fellow at the Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Biology in Berlin-Dahlem, where he studied the microscopic structure of unicellular organisms called protozoans under its director, zoologist and natural philosopher Max Hartmann (1876–1962). Dark, or rather brown, clouds were rising ever faster on the horizon, however. News reached him from Vienna of the growing antisemitism at the university, which increasingly turned violent (e.g., Pauley, 1992:192). Othenio Abel, president of the University of Vienna during the academic year 1932–1933, has been implicated in pulling strings behind the scene to stir up antisemitic sentiments and was accused of using his influence to prevent Jews from pursuing an academic career at the university (Taschwer, 2012). Later, effective with the beginning of the academic year 1934–1935, Abel would be sent into early retirement because of his political, indeed National Socialist, activities, which he pursued when he was president of the University of Vienna (Deichmann, 1996:71; see also Rieppel, 2013b). In anticipation of such developments, and advised by faculty from the University of Vienna, Haas decided in 1932 to heed the invitation to join the fledging Hebrew University of Jerusalem in the British Mandate of Palestine (Werner, 1982:491). The department of zoology was located in an old, picturesque building on the Russian Compound, with Mount Scopus in sight across the valley.

Haas was an expansive personality—a bachelor gourmet cook, lover of classical music, and avid reader of history. At least in his older age he had trouble enduring the summer heat in the Levant, and he looked for opportunities to teach as a guest lecturer at the University of Basel, where I first met him, and later at the University of Zurich, where I met him again. A course he offered on the origin of mammals set me on the track to study Goodrich's monograph. The platypus: here is a "duckbilled, beaver-tailed, otter-footed" mammal that lays eggs—Wikipedia facts well known to anybody only remotely interested in natural history (<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platypus>, accessed June 21, 2016). What I did not know, and what is also not recounted in Wikipedia, is the fact, pointed out in great detail by Herr Professor, that the pectoral girdle of the platypus more closely resembles that of a reptile than that of a mammal. Herr Professor got so excited in his account of such bony structures and their evolutionary significance that he almost crushed the precious mounted skeleton.

Herr Professor's scientific interests were wide ranging. A major part of his research was spent on the head anatomy of scaly reptiles (squamates), in particular the many forms of burrowing lizards and primitive snakes, hoping to discover an evolutionary link between those. But he was equally at home in systematic and biogeographic studies of the reptile fauna of the Levant, and Israel in particular. Biogeographers study the historical distribution of plant and animal species, as this provides one more key to an understanding of their evolutionary relationships. The Levant is of particular biogeographic interest, as it lies on the intersection between the Arabian Peninsula and Africa. And as if this were not enough, exposures of Triassic rocks at Makhtesh Ramon, a large ancient meteor crater in the Negev Desert, allowed Haas and his students to collect marine reptiles some 240 million years old, such as nothosaurs and placodonts (of which more later).

An unexpected but important door opened in the wake of the Six-Day War of 1967. As the Suk in Eastern Jerusalem became accessible to Israelis, Haas rushed to get highly praised Arab tobacco for his pipe. Surprisingly, he found fossils for sale on the doorsteps of a tobacco store. As he inquired about their provenance, he found them to come from 'Ein Yabrud on the West Bank, approximately 20 kilometers north of Jerusalem, where limestone of mid-Cretaceous age was being quarried for building stone and facing. The delicate, fine-grained, and flat-cleaving limestone had to be quarried by hand, which is how the fossils were found in these marine deposits, roughly 95 million years old. Haas befriended the quarry owners, asking them to show him their fossil finds first. He purchased the scientifically important specimens for the National Natural History Collections kept at Hebrew University, and the remainder could go to the tobacco stores in the Suk. This is how Haas built an impressive collection of mid-Cretaceous (Cenomanian) marine reptiles for the Hebrew University—a collection that included lots of turtles, but most prominently simoliophid snakes with well-developed hind limbs, destined to become the objects of a heated debate about snake origins that erupted twenty years after Haas's death.

**1.4.** Reptile Valley in Makhtesh Ramon, Negev, Israel, home of Triassic marine reptiles.

_Photograph by author_.

Over in London, with Goodrich in hand, I took time out from fooling around and set out to find the University College, and within it the department of zoology, where vertebrate paleontology was taught, a subject that had no representative at the University of Basel. Kenneth Kermack (1919–2000) was an expert in the study of the earliest mammals from the uppermost Triassic, fossil remains some 205 million years old, which he studied in collaboration with his wife, Doris, and his assistant, Frances Mussett. He taught a master's course in vertebrate paleontology and offered support for my application to the British Council for a scholarship that would allow me to study in London for one year. I passed my diploma examinations in the spring of 1974 and got ready to leave for London in the late summer with scholarship in hand. Haas again taught at Basel University that summer semester and was thrilled to send me off: "Go to the British Museum (Natural History)," he urged. "They have a fully disarticulated skull of an ichthyosaur [a dolphinlike marine reptile from the Jurassic, some 190 million years old] there, where all the bones have been separated from one another for closer inspection, a specimen well worth of analysis and description. And don't forget to have a good look at the skeletal collection of primitive snakes they have in the section of amphibians and reptiles!" Would I be allowed back, after the fiasco with the gecko lizards just a couple of years ago?

The year in London proved transformative. Kermack, an utterly eccentric personality, took us on a whirlwind tour through vertebrate paleontology, as well as on a memorable field trip to the Devonian Old Red Sandstone on the Orkney Islands. A search for fossil fish some 390 million years old up and down a stretch of the coast was spectacularly unsuccessful—Kermack had misread the geological map. I asked him about the possibility of writing my master's thesis on the disarticulated ichthyosaur skull that Haas had pointed me to, but he was decidedly opposed to the idea of me wandering off to the Natural History Museum in South Kensington. My topic was to be a certain group of lobe-finned fishes called rhipidistians, the probable ancestors of the earliest land-dwelling tetrapods, the amphibians. What made things worse was that my master's thesis was to be a pure literature review, with no real fossils involved. Since the course was pass or fail, I planned to invest just enough time into my thesis to secure a pass, but I secretly went off to the museum all the same, telling people there not to let Kermack know. The helpful and friendly staff in the section for amphibians and reptiles did not blink an eye when I showed up again, allowing me unrestricted access to their collection of skeletons. Garth Underwood (1919–2002), at the time the world's leading expert on snake evolution and classification, patiently introduced me to the intricacies of the osteology of primitive snakes, especially their skull (Stewart, 2003). Together with Angus d'A. Bellairs, Underwood had published a hugely influential review paper on the origin of snakes, which, although dating back to 1951, still set the standard. They concluded that snakes had evolved from some burrowing, lizardlike ancestor, a hypothesis that had also motivated much of Haas's research.

Angus d'A. Bellairs (1918–1990) was one of the most distinguished herpetologists of his time, expert in reptile anatomy, embryology, ecology, and behavior as well as author of numerous technical and popular books and papers (Bryant, 1991; Gans, 1991). In his office at St. Mary's Hospital Medical School, we argued endlessly about the significance of the primary and secondary lateral head vein for a proper understanding of the reptile braincase wall, and what this could tell us about the evolution of the major lineages of reptiles. I had come a long way from first poking my nose into Goodrich's monograph. When discussions heated up, Angus would pull a fat volume from his bookshelf, the pages of which he had carved out so as to be able to hide a bottle of sherry inside. I was introduced to the annual meetings on Vertebrate Paleontology and Comparative Anatomy that were organized at some British university or alternatively in France, where in the years to come I would meet a host of illustrious representatives of the discipline from all over the world. Beverly Halstead (1933–1991), who off and on changed his name to Tarlo, once climbed on a table to give a daring demonstration of the sex life of dinosaurs—not too different, he thought, from that of agamid lizards he had observed in Africa. He showed up at the meetings with a different female companion each year, whereupon Kermack never failed to ask him in a manner audible to all: "Ah, Bev, you got married again?" Bellairs, an avid collector of antiques, was by his own testimony delighted to find that in most British cities adult stores could be found in the same neighborhood as antique stores; he showed up at the meetings with a broad grin on his face, a telling bag in hand containing his latest acquisitions. The young Michael Benton, a rising star in vertebrate paleontology, in one of his talks on reptile phylogeny, characterized the acclaimed doyen of British dinosaur research, Alan Charig (1927–1997), as pursuing a dilettante approach to science—strong rhetoric, although it was not meant in a hostile sense; rather, it signaled a paradigm shift in evolutionary studies that had been brought about by the cladistic revolution. None of that would have been imaginable in the stiff scientific circles back home in Switzerland.

The Cladistic Revolution: Darwin's Death in South Kensington

Although I would go on to spend a lifetime studying reptile anatomy, embryology, and evolution, with many a specimen from the London Natural History Museum under my microscope and scalpel, another entirely new world opened for me during my master's studies—not in the section of amphibians and reptiles of the museum, however, but in the section of fossil fishes in the department of paleontology. A scientific revolution was going on in biological systematics during those times, and the coffee room in the fossil fishes section was one of its epicenters. As an undergraduate in Basel, I had once already borrowed Willi Hennig's (1913–1976) _Grundzüge der Phylogenetischen Systematik_ (Fundamentals of phylogenetic systematics) of 1950 from the university library (Schmitt, 2013). I had sat down with large-format index cards to take excerpts from the book during what I expected to become long evenings. I soon gave up in despair, or was it ennui? The text was long and winding, the language impenetrable in places, and it was loaded with neologisms I had a hard time understanding, let alone remembering. How much easier to understand Ernst Mayr's _Principles of Systematic Zoology_ of 1969, the textbook that I had used in my work at the Basel Natural History Museum! In contrast to Mayr's more recent text, Hennig's 1950 volume had a remarkable lack of broader impact, in hindsight the result of its contorted and difficult-to-understand prose. It is fair to say that outside the circle of systematists who study insects, the book was largely ignored.

Hennig, himself an entomologist who worked first in Berlin, then in Ludwigsburg near Stuttgart, must have recognized the shortfalls of his first effort. He revised his 1950 _Grundzüge_ in a manuscript that—with much delay—was translated into English at the Field Museum of Natural History in Chicago, then published by the University of Illinois Press in 1966, without the author having approved the final text. This version of the book, entitled _Phylogenetic Systematics_ , at last triggered an intense debate between the classical evolutionary systematists à la Mayr (of which I was one), the mathematically inclined numerical taxonomists who were the first to make computers the standard tool of their trade, and Hennig's new adepts. The latter group was dubbed the cladists by Mayr, and the scientific revolution they fought and won accordingly became known as the cladistic revolution (Mayr, 1965, 1968; Hull, 1988). The term "cladistic" derives from ancient Greek _clados_ , "branch" or "twig," thus recognizing the fact that Hennig's phylogenetic system is to be represented by a strictly bifurcating treelike diagram, a cladogram, which is meant to picture successive species lineage splitting events that ultimately gave rise to the tree of life. Although the systematists who gathered for coffee in the fossil fishes section at the London Museum—not all of them specializing in the study of fossil fishes, however, but one in butterflies and others in botany—took center stage in the cladistic revolution, they became known not just as cladists but as transformed cladists, or pattern cladists (Beatty, 1982). The most charismatic and influential among them was renowned paleoichthyologist Colin Patterson (1933–1998) (Forey, Gardiner, and Humphries, 2000).

Patterson's initial transformation to a pattern cladist was motivated not through his reading of Hennig's book but rather through a monograph on Transantarctic chironomid midges published by Swedish entomologist Lars Brundin (1907–1993) in 1966—indeed an unlikely read for a paleontologist specializing in fossil fishes (Brundin, 1966). Significantly, Brundin prefaced his monograph with a summary of his understanding of Hennig's principles of phylogeny reconstruction. The publication of Brundin's monograph coincided with the arrival of a young graduate student from the University of Hawaii, who had secured a postdoctoral position at the Swedish Museum of Natural History in Stockholm, beginning in 1966. Gareth Nelson researched systematics and the evolutionary history of fishes for his PhD, work that motivated him to spend some time as a guest researcher at the Swedish Natural History Museum in Stockholm, Brundin's home institution (Nelson, 2014). At the time, this museum was one of the world's foremost centers for the study of fossil fishes, especially Paleozoic fishes that comprise bizarre, bottom-dwelling, and filter-feeding forms without jaws (the agnathans), as well as the putative ancestors of land-dwelling tetrapods (the crossopterygians, on which I had to write my master's thesis). While interacting with the paleontologists, Nelson shared a deep interest in biogeography with Brundin, to whom he was introduced by a resident fish paleontologist. Brundin's account of Hennig's principles electrified Nelson, as he recognized in those a method of phylogeny reconstruction far superior to the evolutionary approach championed by ornithologist Ernst Mayr and paleontologist George Gaylord Simpson (1902–1984) (Simpson, 1961). Nelson found that systematics, as represented in Brundin's account of Hennig's work, was no longer a matter of aesthetics, as Simpson had proclaimed, nor a matter of expert authority, as Mayr would have liked it to be, but rather a true science that was ultimately based in logic. In November of the same year, Nelson stopped over in London to inquire about a job opening at the AMNH in New York. He timed his trip to the British Museum of Natural History so he could meet a member of the search committee, Donn Rosen (1929–1986), a specialist in fish research from the AMNH visiting his British colleagues to pursue collaborative projects. Colin Patterson recalled the memorable visit: "Then, one day early in 1967, Gary Nelson, who was spending six months in the BM [British Museum (Natural History)], told me that something had just appeared in the library that I might find interesting.... It was Brundin's monograph on chironomids just arrived. I was bowled over by it—it was like discovering logic for the first time."1

In Nelson's recollection, Brundin's "critique... began the [cladistic] revolution" (2004:133), one he himself helped ignite in the natural history museums in London and New York, where he eventually landed a job. An intense discussion of cladistic principles of phylogeny reconstruction flared up, forging close ties and avid intellectual interchange between the two institutions. Brundin was invited to present, at the Fourth Nobel Symposium on Current Problems of Lower Vertebrate Phylogeny, held at the Swedish Museum of Natural History in June 1967, the insights on systematics and its relationship to paleontology that he had gained through his reading of Hennig, a presentation that both Nelson and Patterson attended (Brundin, 1968; Hull, 1988:144; for a more detailed account, and subsequent Nobel symposia, see Nelson, 2014). Opponents of cladistics judged his contribution a "vigorous defense of Hennig's view... long on rhetoric, but short on methodology" (Wake, 1970:95). Patterson saw things differently: "The heart of Brundin's paper was one message: 'phylogenetics is the search for the sister group.' That message eventually got through" (1989:472). This amounted, in Nelson's recollection, to a revolution of paleontology, as it moved the study of fossils from "the search for ancestors" to the "search for the sister group" (2014:145). Brundin's revolutionary insight gained from reading Hennig was that even in the presence of fossils, Hennig's method cannot recover sequences of ancestors and descendants, but only relative degrees of phylogenetic (evolutionary) relationships in terms of a nested hierarchy of sister groups. Two species are sister species if they uniquely share a common ancestor (stem species) that is not also ancestral to a third, more distantly related species. The same holds for more inclusive groups—that is, groups of species of common descent. Consider a species lineage evolving through time. As it splits, the stem species lineage will give rise to two daughter species lineages. These two daughter lineages will be sister species, and their descendants will form sister groups. Consider, as Hennig did, evolutionary history to consist at its core in species lineages splitting and splitting again. The graphic representation of such a history will be a dichotomously structured diagram specifying sister group relationships as they result from successive species lineage splitting events. In Hennig's account, the tree of life assumes the structure of a nested hierarchy of sister groups, not a flowchart of ancestors and descendants. The concept of sister groups came famously—and controversially—into play at the 26th Symposium of Vertebrate Paleontology and Comparative Anatomy held at Reading University in 1978, organized by Beverly Halstead, a bitter opponent of cladistics. In attendance at a special session where Hennig's principles of phylogenetic systematics were to be debated was Rex Parrington (1905–1981) from the University of Cambridge, Kermack's archrival in the study of early mammals. (Kermack claimed that Parrington had stolen some bags full of material that he had set aside in a quarry where they both excavated.) The result of the debate of Hennig's principles, in Halstead's account, "was summed up for many by Parrington's... exasperated exclamation that according to a cladist a lungfish is more closely related to a cow than to a salmon, to which C. Patterson... answered 'Yes, I cannot see what is wrong in that'" (1978:760).

The reason simply is that the lungfish and the cow share a character that is not also shared by the salmon: the presence of internal nares, or choanae, which among living animals are present in the lungfish and all tetrapods but are absent in all other (living) vertebrates. This indicates that the lungfish and the cow share a common ancestor from which they inherited the internal nares, one that is not also ancestral to the salmon, where the character is absent. In other words, the salmon descends from a species lineage-splitting event that occurred much earlier than the species lineage splitting event to which the lungfish and the cow can be traced back. Considering a salmon, a lungfish, and a cow, and no other organisms, the lungfish and the cow are thus more closely related to one another than either of them is to the salmon, as they share a more recent common ancestor that is not also ancestral to the salmon.

These were the kinds of arguments, emotions, and debates that I first encountered during my master's studies in London, drinking diluted (by Italian standards) coffee in the fossil fishes section of the Natural History Museum. I would return for years thereafter, first to listen and learn, then to participate in the discussions, which carried over from the morning coffee break to the pub at lunchtime. The group would leave the museum around noon or shortly thereafter, cross Cromwell Road, and pass by the South Kensington Tube station to stroll down Onslow Square. The destination was a pub called the Cranley. We called it the Cladists' Arms because it was there that a lot of cladistic theory emerged from discussions over ales and gin and tonics during what essentially amounted to a liquid lunch. The Gang of Four, comprising Gary Nelson's colleague, Donn Rosen, from the American Museum; Colin Patterson and his colleague, Peter L. Forey, from the British Museum (Natural History); and Brian Gardiner from Queen Elizabeth College, in London, had embarked on a highly controversial study, arguing on cladistics principles, and contrary to the received wisdom of the time, that lungfishes, not the rhipidistian lobe-finned fishes I had to write about for my master's thesis, were the sister group of tetrapods (Rosen et al., 1981). The study was published in 1981 at a time when things had further deteriorated since my year spent as a master's student in London.

The Natural History Museum in London had opened two new exhibits, Dinosaurs and Their Living Relatives in 1979 and Man's Place in Evolution in 1980, both organized around cladistic principles and both inviting visitors, through interactive stations, to take their own stab at the reconstruction of evolutionary, i.e., sister group, relationships that result from repeated species lineage splitting events. Such a move from "stuffed animals" to interactives was part of the new exhibition scheme developed and implemented by Roger Miles, an expert in fossil fishes, a cladist of the first generation, the newly appointed head of the department of public services—and of Marxist conviction, as expressed in private settings (Miles and Tout, 1978). While the interactive stations offered an opportunity to museum visitors to explore and apply the essence of cladistic methodology, the exhibit developers went further in making the claim, in the exhibit's guide, that "none of the species we are considering is the ancestor of any of the others." (Properly expressed, the controversial claim should have been, and still is, that no one species can be ancestral to one other species; any species can only be ancestral to two daughter species that result from a species lineage splitting event, which gives rise to sister species.) This statement, in its brief—indeed rudimentary, and hence misleading—wording, immediately provoked harsh criticism from Beverly Halstead, who once again raised his rattling voice against cladistics in a letter of correspondence to the prestigious science journal _Nature_ , associating cladistics with Marxist ideology along the way and proclaiming the death of scholarship at the museum (1980:208). Halstead's polemics triggered a flurry of responses in the correspondence pages of _Nature_ , one of them from Patterson, who stated boldly that "cladistics is not about evolution, but about the pattern of character distribution in organisms," i.e., about classification: "Halstead's complaint is his failure to grasp the distinction between pattern [systematics that studies the hierarchical patterns of sister group relationships] and process [the evolutionary explanation of the pattern of relationships]" (Patterson, 1980:430). Halstead retorted, calling on the director of the Natural History Museum in London to face the charges of deteriorating scholarship and political indoctrination allegedly manifest in its public exhibits: "A continued 'dignified silence' in the present circumstances is simply not good enough" (1981:742). The February 1981 issue of _Nature_ finally carried a one-page editorial titled "Darwin's Death in South Kensington," penned by the editor himself, John Maddox, who was never one to shy away from controversy:

What is the British Museum (Natural History) for? Research? Public enlightenment, even entertainment?... The museum... has not replied to the several issues which have been raised since Dr. Colin Patterson's cool comment on the role of cladistics in taxonomy [i.e., that it is not about evolution, but about classification instead]. Halstead... is right to draw attention to this deafening silence.

The editorial went on to pluck a half sentence from the latest brochure published by the museum, one that read "if the theory of evolution is true," and concluded, "The rot at the museum has gone further than Halstead ever thought. Can it be that the managers of the museum which is the nearest thing to a citadel of Darwinism have lost their nerve, not to mention their good sense?" (Maddox 1981:735; cited with permission from the publisher). How could anybody doubt, at such time as the 1970s and early 1980s of the twentieth century, the truth of evolutionary theory? At that time, it might have been at the 1982 symposium on Vertebrate Paleontology and Comparative Anatomy, held at the University of Cambridge; I remember a day of heavy rain. Attendees faced the problem of what to do with their raincoats until a janitor rolled a clothes rack into the classroom where the lectures were scheduled to be delivered. "Bev [Halstead], there's something for you to hang yourself from," Colin Patterson's sonorous voice rang from the row of back seats. Back in his office at St. Mary's Hospital Medical School, Angus Bellairs raged that he could no longer take his grandson to the museum to show him the whale skeleton that had so fascinated him when he was a boy. All this occurred because the space had been taken up by a new exhibit on Human Biology ("An Exhibition of Ourselves," as it was subtitled), one that even introduced schoolchildren to such delicate issues as gender and sex. Time for a swig of sherry!

The Pupil-turned-Colleague

In 1982, I was working as a senior scientific assistant at the Paleontological Institute and Museum of the University of Zurich.2 My goal was the _Habilitation_ , i.e., to write a thesis that would earn me the qualification for a career as a university docent and eventually, I hoped, professor. Haas had been instrumental in securing me this employment late in 1978, the year during which, in the spring, I had defended my PhD thesis at the University of Basel. My PhD thesis had two parts. One concerned the evolutionary relationships of anguimorph lizards (monitor lizards and their relatives) as deciphered on the basis of the anatomy of the skull and jaw adductor musculature. The other was an analysis of jaw mechanics in a monitor lizard, _Varanus salvator_ , a protected species, four juvenile specimens of which hade been seized from animal traders by Swiss customs officers and turned over to me for my research. At one point I traveled with my four lizards to the University of Michigan in Ann Arbor to perform electromyography on the jaw adductor muscles in the lab of one of the leading North American herpetologists, Carl Gans (1923–2009), a Jew of German extraction himself who was locked into a love–hate relationship with Georg Haas.

Gans was a workaholic, famous for his high expectations, his abrasiveness, and his wide-ranging interests in reptiles. He set himself a monument by initiating, and editing through Academic Press in London, the multivolume series Biology of the Reptilia, the first volume of which was published in 1969. Three months before leaving for Ann Arbor, I submitted a manuscript on the braincase of primitive snakes to the _Journal of Morphology_ , which at that time was edited by Gans. When I arrived in his lab, he handed the manuscript back to me, without having sent it out for peer review. He was unwilling to publish my paper, he explained, and anyway, unless I did what he told me to do, I would never get a job. Later during my stay, he asked me on a Saturday night to review a chapter for his book series by the next morning. The chapter was written by an eminent senior comparative anatomist. My endless earlier discussions of the head anatomy of reptiles with Angus Bellairs came in handy at this point. I don't remember the details, only that I found the chapter flawed. When I told Gans so Sunday morning, he turned white. The coeditor, standing next to him, broke out in laughter and exclaimed, "See, that's what you get from courting these big names of senior scientists all the time for your book series, instead of turning to younger researchers." The collection of manuscripts constituting the volume was meant to go to press more or less immediately. Would I mind rewriting the chapter, Gans asked in despair? If I agreed, he would publish my paper on the snake skulls in the _Journal of Morphology_ after all. Such was my first serious exposure to high politics in academia. I had long ago sent my manuscript to a different journal that was published in London; I lied as I claimed it had already been accepted for publication there. But I agreed to fix the chapter just so there would be no mistakes.

My lizards eventually stopped eating, as they were hurting from the electrodes I had to stick into their jaw muscles. Gans then wanted me to study drinking in pythons instead, but I declined to again hurt animals for a research question that I did not find particularly interesting. Although experimental functional anatomy was fascinating, it turned out that it was not my thing. Better to dissect pickled museum specimens and study fossils instead, neither of which would suffer from my research interests. At my thesis defense back in Basel, the dean of the faculty of natural sciences grabbed my thesis manuscript and offered to publish it, as written, in the highly prestigious _Denkschriften der Schweizerischen Naturforschenden Gesellschaft_. I was surprised because during my undergraduate student years I had never been able to forge a good relationship with him as a professor; my interest in reptiles, and vertebrates in general, prevented me from recognizing any value in his uninspiring courses in invertebrate zoology. I still remember him getting hopelessly confused and lost in his account of cell fate determination in developing sea urchin embryos.

In 1979, my wife and I toured Israel on our honeymoon and took the opportunity to visit Haas at home and at work. For dinner, he scooped a mixture of cooked tomato slices and chicken hearts from an enormous cooking pot that appeared subject to continued replenishment for God only knows how long. When he introduced me to his staff at the Russian Compound, he explained in his resounding voice, "He plays the same trumpet as I do," meaning that our research interests overlapped. We had by that time started to collaborate, specifically on the head anatomy of an extremely rare burrowing lizard from New Guinea in the genus _Dibamus_ , of which a specimen from the Basel Natural History Museum had been made available for thin sectioning to allow microscopic investigation.

Lecturing at the University of Zurich, Herr Professor would take me midafternoon to a café to discuss the perplexing mid-Cretaceous snakes with well-developed hind limbs from 'Ein Yabrud. "Snakes must have had a marine origin after all," he asserted.

"You have spent a lifetime researching the origin of snakes from secretive, burrowing lizards," I retorted.

"Yes, I know, I know, but what should I do?" he exclaimed, throwing up his hands in distress. In his publication, he eventually hinted at the possibility—anathema to a cladist like me—of multiple snake origins, once from the sea and once from underground (Haas, 1979).

After coffee or ice cream, we passed the most luxurious specialty deli in Zurich. "You'll send me a package for Christmas again this year, will you?" he asked the head of staff. "You know what I like!" With that, he turned on his heels and left the store.

As I followed him, my mind wandered down the nearby Bahnhofstrasse to the headquarters of Swiss banks at the Paradeplatz, the home of highly controversial Jewish fortunes, which their owners had tragically lost through murderous persecution during World War II. Haas "died unexpectedly in his sleep, in Jerusalem on 13. Sept. 1981. Next to his bed was a suitcase, packed in readiness for yet another of his near-annual journeys to Switzerland" (Werner, 1982:491). Herr Professor and I had planned to finally put the finishing touches on the manuscript describing the skull and jaw adductor musculature of _Dibamus_ , and discussing its implications for the origin of snakes. I was left to complete the work on my own, which I dedicated to my deceased mentor.

Cladi-boy

My situation in Zurich started to slowly deteriorate—not with students but with faculty. I slowly turned into the much criticized, even ridiculed, "cladi-boy," as a professor in zoology, an ardent admirer of Ernst Mayr's work, called me. Like Patterson, I loved the logic on which the so-called transformed cladists based their systematics—their classifications. Like Patterson and his colleagues, I realized that such stringent logic cannot be compatible with Darwinian historical contingency, the serendipity that necessarily characterizes evolution driven by a process of random variation and natural selection. And like Patterson and his colleagues such as Gareth Nelson, I recognized a hierarchical structure in embryonic development that would seem relevant to the hierarchical classification of organisms based on cladistic principles. Such a tie between embryonic development and hierarchical classification had already been recognized by eminent German embryologist Karl Ernst von Baer (1792–1876) in a groundbreaking monograph that he published in 1828. We all agreed that evolutionary relationships, i.e., the phylogeny of organisms as reconstructed on the principles of cladistics, cannot be read off the fossil record. They can, however, be researched using embryonic development as a clue. Again, cladistics is not about ancestors and descendants but about sister group relationships, i.e., relative degrees of evolutionary relationships. If so, might it not be possible to read the phylogeny of organisms off their embryonic development? At the end of the day it is not, but we gave it the best possible try.

The retired director of the Paleontological Institute and Museum of the University of Zurich, Emil Kuhn-Schnyder (1905–1994), had spent a career on the systematic excavation of Middle Triassic marine fishes and reptiles at Monte San Giorgio in southern Switzerland (canton of Ticino). Today Monte San Giorgio and its fossiliferous sediments is a UNESCO World Heritage Site. When I first arrived in Zurich, Kuhn-Schnyder told me that for him, to dig through successive layers of fossiliferous rock was the same as to leaf through the pages of an illustrated book on evolution. Small wonder he could not stomach the emergence of cladistics. Nor could his successor, my supervisor, Hans Rieber, an invertebrate paleontologist. Nor could any one among the biology professors at the university at the time. In 1983, I published a book titled _Kladismus oder die Legende vom Stammbaum_ (Cladistics, or the legend of the phylogenetic tree). It earned me applause from my students and a sympathetic review in the conservative _Neue Zürcher Zeitung_ that praised the theoretical austerity of cladistic principles, but in the eyes of Kuhn-Schnyder and Rieber (1984), the time had come to expose the creationist implications of cladistics. Academic freedom was enshrined in the system; I kept my job. But I successively lost my privileges, eventually even the free access to the collections of fossil fishes and reptiles from Monte San Giorgio that I had originally been hired to curate. With the help of a friend on the staff of the university library, I turned to secretive studies of eighteenth-century French biology. Naturalist turned philosopher Charles Bonnet (1720–1793) from Geneva became my new hero: to this day, his _Palingénésie Philosophique_ , published in two volumes in 1769, remains one of the most intriguing books I have ever read.

One day in the late 1980s, Peter Crane, then curator of fossil plants (paleobotany) at the Field Museum of Natural History in Chicago, was brought to my office. Crane, one of the few first-generation cladists among botanists, was on an extended research visit to the Institute of Botany at the University of Zurich. My publications on the head anatomy of lizards and primitive snakes and on the fossil fishes and reptiles from Monte San Giorgio had by that time started to earn me some international reputation. But that was not what Crane came to see me about. He wanted to talk cladistics, especially now, after my book on the _Fundamentals of Comparative Biology_ , published in 1988, had earned a favorable review by Gareth Nelson (1989) in the leading journal _Systematic Zoology_. Someone told me that one morning, Gary Nelson showed up for coffee break in his department at the AMNH, slammed my recently published book on the table, and said, "I liked that." In the course of our conversation, Crane must have realized how unhappy I was in my position. Back in Chicago, Crane informed the department of geology at the Field Museum of Natural History, where a curatorial line had become vacant, of my situation, alluding to the fact that I might be "movable," as they say in academic circles. Crossing Grant Park on my way to my job interview at the Field Museum on a sunny morning, I sensed a feeling of immense relief and liberation. A few months later, on my way to my new position in Chicago, the flight attendants on Swissair served up a bottle of Champagne for me that had been ordered as a going-away gift by the students I left behind. I started my job as curator of fossil amphibians and reptiles at the Field Museum in May 1990.

Working in Zurich, I had acquired familiarity with the diversity of Triassic marine reptiles, especially the sauropterygians, which were represented in large numbers. But my work remained more or less restricted to the material from Monte San Giorgio and from an equivalent neighboring locality just across the border in Lombardy, northern Italy. Swiss civil servants such as university employees at the time did not travel much, and if they did, they rarely went on extended trips. In any case, that was the policy of my supervisor, Professor Hans Rieber. Now in Chicago, I felt the possibility to embark on a far-flung, indeed global, review of Triassic sauropterygians. The sauropterygians are a branch on the reptile tree of life, i.e., a clade of reptiles that secondarily invaded the sea early in the Triassic. They first show up in the fossil record in the late Early Triassic, about 250 million years ago. Thereafter, they rapidly diversified. All the various groups were already represented in the sediments of Monte San Giorgio, spanning a period of 245 to 235 million years ago. There were the placodonts, carrying large, flat tooth plates in their upper and lower jaws indicating a diet of hard-shelled invertebrate prey. Some of these placodonts developed a bony armor, seemingly much like turtles do. There were the smaller pachypleurosaurs, lizardlike animals with thickened vertebrae and ribs that inhabited coastal waters. Related to them are the larger nothosaurs, which diversified in shallow and warm-water seas that had developed as a consequence of a general sea-level rise during the Early Triassic, flooding low-lying areas of continents. The skull of advanced nothosaurs was elongate and very low, with the long jaws carrying protruding fangs that well equipped them to catch squid and fish with a sudden sideways snapping bite. Some of the larger nothosaurs occasionally ventured farther into the sea, as indicated by the supernumerary phalanges they developed in their hands and feet, rendering those larger and more efficient for paddling. All of these forms went extinct in the course of the Late Triassic, some 205 million years ago, a time when the climate got hotter and the sea level dropped, causing their habitats to disappear. Only the Triassic pistosaurs, more distantly related to the nothosaurs but outwardly of similar build, survived in a certain sense: they gave rise, at the end of the Triassic, to the more widely known plesiosaurs, pliosaurs, and elasmosaurs of the Jurassic and Cretaceous seas. These large creatures roamed the deep waters of the open ocean, their limbs transformed into flippers. These were the famous sea monsters featured in Victorian accounts of extinct life. Some claim they still see them today in Loch Ness in Scotland.

The marine biota of the Triassic harbored a great variety of sauropterygians, all of them adapted to shallow-water near-coastal environments, and hence more regionally distributed than their Jurassic and Cretaceous descendants that roamed the open sea. They offered a rich potential for systematic revision, phylogeny reconstruction, functional anatomical analysis, and paleobiogeographic studies at a time when modern continents only just started to take shape. How did terrestrial reptiles anatomically and functionally adapt to a life in the sea? How did they diversify, where, and how fast? How were the species of sauropterygians distributed in time and space, and how did their distribution relate to the slowly progressing evolution of modern continents? Questions over questions, which—accustomed to the Swiss system—I carefully outlined in my first grant proposal to the US National Science Foundation (NSF). It was rejected. My new colleagues at the Field Museum sat me down, however, and taught me how to write an NSF grant proposal the American way. I scored the next one. I had more money for research travel and equipment than I could ever have hoped to get in Switzerland. The State Museum of Baden-Württemberg (where Willi Hennig had worked during the second half of his career), now located in Stuttgart instead of Ludwigsburg and holding exquisite collections of Triassic sauropterygians from the German Muschelkalk, was for me from a logistic point of view closer to Chicago than to Zurich. The world was wide open; the journey could begin. In order to properly research the pattern of sauropterygian diversification and distribution throughout the Triassic period, I first needed to locate their proper place on the evolutionary tree of reptiles as a whole, however. Nobody had tried to do so on cladistic principles before.

Misplaced Turtles

I rushed to amass as many characters as I could that would allow me to assess the evolutionary relationships of sauropterygians within the reptiles as a whole. I needed to score these characters across the whole variety of reptiles, representing all major groups from the Paleozoic to the present. That, however, included not just crocodiles, lizards, and snakes, their fossil relatives, and many others, but also turtles. I had little experience working with turtles. I had done some work on the anatomy and embryonic development of the skull and associated jaw adductor muscles in the snapping turtle _Chelydra serpentina_ , and I had used the same species to investigate patterns of ossification throughout the skeleton (Rieppel, 1976, 1977, 1990, 1993a). In the collections of the institute in Zurich, there was also a skull of a Jurassic turtle from the area of the city Solothurn, roughly 155 million years old, that I had described, along with comments on the evolution of the braincase in turtles (Rieppel, 1980). But a turtle specialist I was not. Once I had assembled what I thought was sufficient evidence and encoded it, I ran it through my computer—with most unexpected results. Never before had sauropterygians been included in an analysis of evolutionary relationships across the whole range of reptile diversity. The inclusion of sauropterygians into the analysis of reptile interrelationships pulled the turtles from the root system of the reptile family tree, where they had traditionally been placed, all the way up into the upper crown. Goodrich's memoir came to mind—the way I had struggled to understand his remarks on the turtle skull while sitting in the plane on my way to London. I had an anomaly on my hands, a maverick, if not heretical, hypothesis of turtle relationships, one universally rejected by the accredited vertebrate paleontologists of the time, yet one that Goodrich seemed to have thought of as well. I remembered Haas, who had agonized about his marine mid-Cretaceous snakes with well-developed hind limbs, and their implications for snake origins from sea or land. Now it was my turn to throw up my arms and ask myself, what should I do?

I had always had an interest in philosophy of science. When I was an undergraduate, Karl R. Popper (1902–1994) was my favorite philosopher of science. He enjoyed an enormous reputation, at least among scientists. Like Haas, he was the scion of a Jewish family born in Vienna, but his parents had converted to Lutheranism before he was born. In 1937 he emigrated to New Zealand, and then in 1946 he moved on to England, were he obtained a position at the London School of Economics. He ended his stellar career as Sir Karl. A friend of mine, a professional philosopher, urged me to leave Popper behind, to move on and look at the work of more modern, avant-garde philosophers of science such as Thomas S. Kuhn, who had written about scientific revolutions, but I would have none of it. I cannot pretend to have fully understood at the time what Popper was saying, or rather writing about, in his 1934 volume _Logik der Forschung_ (published in English in 1959 as _The Logic of Scientific_ Discovery)—not as an undergraduate, and not when I was first facing the conundrum of turtle relationships. It would take me another ten years to come to grips with his hard-core hypothetico-deductivism and its significance—or rather lack thereof—for practical, i.e., empirical, science.

My enthusiasm for Popper's philosophy of science was fueled by the fact that fellow cladists tried to cast systematics as a science that would measure up to those criteria of a true science that had been articulated by Popper—quite in contrast to Mayr's _Principles of Systematic Zoology_ (1969), which they dismissed as a pseudoscience. Roger Miles from the British Museum (Natural History) was among the first ones to do so. It also did not help my understanding of Popper that cladists from the AMNH misrepresented Popper's philosophy to some degree as they tried to make it work for cladistics in a series of reviews of Popper's books, which they published in the journal _Systematic Zoology_ (Rieppel, 2009). The way I understood Popper was that science can never obtain secure knowledge; it can never obtain the truth. But it can test a given hypothesis and in the process potentially falsify it, thus opening a door to learn from negatives. Conversely, according to Popper, only those hypotheses and theories can be considered scientific that lend themselves to test and potential falsification. For Popper, science is like a hugely complicated building with theories as its building blocks. That building, Popper argued, was erected on a swamp, and while science could ram the pillars supporting that building ever deeper into the ground, it could never reach firm bedrock. Taking Popper to heart, I decided not to simply ignore my result about turtle relationships but to put this result out there for others (and myself) to test further, and to possibly falsify. The heretical hypothesis of turtle relationships far up in the reptile tree thus made its way into the first major monograph that resulted from my NSF-sponsored sauropterygian research; it also contained a discussion of sauropterygian relationships within reptiles as a whole. One of the reviewers, a colleague and friend, urged me to put the strange turtle business into an appendix, so that it would not detract from the main point of my paper. So I did. "The results remain problematical," I conceded in that appendix, yet at the same time,

the results remain interesting... because if nature is hierarchically ordered... then the relationships of any three taxa should remain stable (if they reflect history) no matter how many taxa are later added to the analysis: the lungfish should always come out closer to the cow than to the trout, no matter how many other "fishes" or tetrapods are subsequently added to the analysis. (Rieppel, 1994:82)

There was a glaring hole in my analysis, however, and that was my relative ignorance of Paleozoic reptiles, stem-reptiles some 250 to 300 million years old, to which turtles had traditionally been thought to be related. I needed help, so I turned to another colleague and friend of mine, Robert Reisz, a top expert on Paleozoic reptiles from the University of Toronto. At first he thought I was joking; then said he was too involved with a host of other projects to take on yet another one. But he had a graduate student, Michael deBraga, who was interested in collaborating and making research on turtle relationships part of his PhD thesis. In an effort to test the earlier result, we scrutinized the data and added to them—Michael of course not only for a host of Paleozoic reptiles but also for synapsids, the earliest fossil relatives of mammals. The signal was confirmed, with the turtles high up in the reptile tree. The results of our collaboration were duly published in 1996 in _Nature_. The following year, Gene Gaffney, then the undisputed leader in the study of fossil turtles and turtle evolution from the AMNH, organized a turtle symposium under the umbrella of the annual meeting of the Society of Vertebrate Paleontology, held in New York that year. When I got up to deliver a presentation of our findings, Gaffney introduced my talk with the words, "And now everybody may hiss as much as you like" (<http://www.dlblanc.com/coloherp/cb-news/archive/taxonomy/TurtleTax.php>, accessed June 20, 2016).

Yet our paper motivated a number of molecular systematists, working with DNA data, to test turtle relationships (on which more later). When the first large-scale molecular study, published in _Science_ in February 1999, robustly confirmed heterodox turtle relationships, the story made the front page of the _New York Times_ (Hedges and Poling, 1999; Yoon, 1999). Later that same year, I received an invitation to contribute a chapter on turtle origins to the highly prestigious book series Annual Review of Ecology and Systematics (Rieppel and Reisz, 1999). I agreed, but only on the condition that Robert Reisz would coauthor the paper. The editor hesitated, saying that this could throw the whole project into jeopardy. My answer was that if Robert's input would show the novel placement of turtles high up on the reptile tree to be wrong, then that hypothesis ought not be perpetuated. Eventually the editor agreed, and with such a prestigious outlet, Robert became interested in collaboration after all. So I applied for a Field Museum scholarship to bring him to Chicago.

Once he was set up in his hotel, Robert strolled into my office one morning and announced in a weighty voice, "I will now start going through your data matrix"—the one that Michael deBraga and I had put together, which in the meantime had been published in detail in the _Zoological Journal of the Linnean Society_ (deBraga and Rieppel, 1997). While Robert critically scanned through our database, surely expecting to see the strange reptile tree it supported to eventually fall apart, I turned to the study of the embryonic development of the turtle shell, with stunning surprises in store. Robert found a few mistakes, as is common in phylogenetic analyses of such broad scope, but the heterodox result was once again confirmed. Our collaboration resulted in the identification of three main problem areas relevant to the question of turtle origins.

The first is the assessment of the precise relationships of turtles within reptiles as a whole, or, in cladistic terms, the identification of the sister group of turtles. A well-supported hypothesis concerning turtle relationships within reptiles as a whole is an indispensable prerequisite for an understanding of which aspects of turtle anatomy, physiology, and behavior are primitive and which ones are evolutionarily advanced, or specialized.
