If turtles were the sister group of some branch of Paleozoic reptiles, then they might indeed represent living fossils—but not so if turtles nested high up in the reptile tree.

The second problem area concerns this most extraordinary characteristic of turtles: the turtle shell. In virtue of its shell, the turtle differs radically from other tetrapods, both in its anatomy and its physiology (Jackson, 2011). Here is a poor creature encased in a rigid shell; how does it move about, ever so clumsily? How does it breathe? Compared to a lizard or a crocodile, a turtle looks truly monstrous. What are the evolutionary mechanisms at work in such a transformation, and how well can they be accommodated by a strictly Darwinian account of evolution?

The third problem area concerns the environment within which such radical reorganization of the tetrapod body plan took place. In Darwin's account, evolutionary transformation promotes adaptation. Is it possible to explain the origin of the turtle body plan, and the evolution of the turtle shell specifically, in adaptive terms? If so, in which way would the turtle shell represent an adaptation? Darwin opened his groundbreaking _On the Origin of Species_ of 1859, with a quote from a letter renowned astronomer Sir John Frederick Herschel (1792–1871) had posted from Cape Colony in South Africa to the leading British geologist of the time, Charles Lyell (1797–1875), on February 20, 1836. In the course of his geological studies, Lyell had recognized that species go extinct, i.e., disappear from the fossil record and hence from the surface of the globe. He had no answer to the question, however, of how those extinct species were replaced through geologic time by new and different ones, as was indeed documented by the fossil record. "Of course I allude to that mystery of mysteries," Herschel wrote, "the replacement of extinct species by others" (Cannon, 1961:305). Darwin in turn hoped to "throw some light on the origin of species—that mystery of mysteries, as it has been called by one of our greatest philosophers" (1859:1). It might indeed similarly be argued that the origin of turtles constitutes such a mystery of mysteries for those trying not only to reconstruct the tree of life but also to understand the evolutionary mechanisms that promote such fundamental anatomical transformations as are represented by the turtle and its shell. The two mysteries are intimately interconnected; it is precisely the fundamental transformation of the tetrapod body plan that renders the placement of turtles on the reptile tree of life so difficult.

Notes

1. Patterson's talk, delivered at the 2nd Annual Willi Hennig Society Meeting on October 3, 1981, in Ann Arbor, Michigan, was transcribed and made available by D. M. Williams, Department of Botany, Natural History Museum, London.

2. The line "The Pupil-turned-Colleague" is from Werner (1982:491).

**2.1.** Constant Duméril's (1805) classification of "reptiles" based on logical subdivision.

**Reptile Classification and Evolution**

2

Early Debates on Turtle Relationships

THE CLASSIFICATION OF PLANTS AND ANIMALS IN WHAT IN LATIN—at the time the universal language of science—was called a _systema naturae_ , a system of nature's contents, was first formalized by Swedish botanist Carl von Linné, better known under the Latinized version of his name, Carolus Linnaeus (1707–1778), with which he signed his publications. It was Linnaeus who introduced the binomial nomenclature for species, where each species name comprises two terms, the name of the genus to which the species belongs and the specific epithet naming the species in its genus. For example, the neighbor's dog is part of the species _Canis familiaris_ : _Canis_ is the genus name, and _familiaris_ is the specific epithet that distinguishes the family dog from other species in the same genus, such as _Canis lupus_ , the gray wolf. Having thus established a formal framework for the naming of species, Linnaeus then sorted genera into families, families into orders, and orders into classes in a hierarchical classificatory scheme that is still in use today. Most importantly, Linnaeus distinguished an artificial from a natural system. The artificial system would be the one that reflects the logic of human thought; the natural system, in contrast, would reflect "affinities"—that is, something like overall similarity among species. The artificial system is built on the logical subdivision of nature's contents in terms of dichotomies: teeth present or absent; claws present or absent. The natural system, in contrast, tries to capture all the subtle similarities or differences between species: the greater and more numerous the similarities, the closer the affinity between species.

At the time of Linnaeus's writing, the term "affinity" was meant to express degrees of structural similarity, not evolutionary relationships, the latter a connotation that the term "affinity" acquired only later, most prominently through the influence of Charles Darwin. Linnaeus distinguished plant genera primarily on the basis of the geometrical arrangement of the reproductive organs in plants, such as the sepals and petals, the carpels and stamens in flowers (Stevens, 1994:12). The genera, once recognized and diagnosed, were then sorted into families, these into orders, and the latter into classes using two different methods. One method enforces a regular dichotomous subdivision in a way that ensures the easy and unequivocal identification of each genus. The result resembles a dichotomously structured identification key and was called an artificial system by Linnaeus, who flattered himself to have been the first to have consistently implemented it in botany (Daudin, 1926:38). The other method would take into account as best as possible all the variable degrees of similarity and consequent affinity between genera that can be discerned by a detailed comparative study of the entire plant anatomy. The resulting natural system would thus capture a "network of affinities," which Linnaeus expressed in the metaphor of a maplike structure with mutual affinities on all sides (Daudin, 1926:47; Müller-Wille, 2007).

What survived the test of time are Linnaeus's hierarchical system of classification and its associated nomenclature, as well as the principle of dichotomization of nature in biological classification. A nice example (among many others) for the application of the principle of dichotomy to animal classification that relates to turtles was provided by Constant Duméril (1774–1860), a disciple of the famous Georges Cuvier (of whom much more later) at the National Natural History Museum in Paris. Duméril called his approach to animal classification _zoologie analytique_ (analytical zoology), thus underscoring its logical structure.

Duméril's 1805 classification of amphibians and reptiles is built on the presence or absence of certain characters: limbs present (lizards + crocs, turtles, amphibians) or limbs absent (snakes); claws present (lizards + crocs, turtles) or claws absent (amphibians); teeth present (lizards + crocs) or teeth absent (turtles). The logic underlying this classification is impeccable, but the biology is obviously flawed. Snakes would certainly seem to be more closely related to lizards and crocs than would frogs and salamanders. But to be entirely fair to Duméril, it should be noted that at the time, obscure and little understood limbless amphibians, the caecilians or gymnophionans, were grouped with snakes, thus obscuring the latter group's otherwise obvious reptile relationships.

The pressure to capture natural affinities, i.e., historical relations in biological classification, increased with the dawn of evolutionary thought. With Charles Darwin, the idea that new species arise from ancestral ones—one that had been around for quite a while—was for the first time validated through an explanation based on entirely natural causes: variation and natural selection. When thinking about the way these mechanisms would promote species origination, Darwin—in his notebook B, which he started in July 1837—drew a famous sketch, a treelike branching diagram that today, in its more fleshed-out version, is known as the tree of life.1 Darwin intended the branching in the tree to represent the splitting of an ancestral species lineage into descendant ones. Since then, a treelike branching diagram has become the paradigmatic way to represent evolutionary (phylogenetic) relationships, although it is well known that some organisms enter into reticulated evolutionary relationships that would have to be graphically represented as a network. One of these mechanisms is the origin of a new species through hybridization between two parental species, a mode of species origination that is relatively frequent in plants and that had already been recognized by Linnaeus. Another mechanism promoting a network of relationships is the transfer of genes, or bits of DNA, between organisms that belong to two or more different species, a process called either horizontal or lateral gene transfer. This is most frequently—but not exclusively—observed among bacteria and unicellular organisms, the protists. Even so, and especially for more complex, multicellular organisms, a hierarchical system that can be graphically represented as a branching tree of life remains the best way to capture evolutionary relationships, i.e., the successive splitting of species lineages. The information such a system is meant to convey is no longer to facilitate the identification and reidentification of species and genera, however, as was the case in Linnaeus's day, before the theory of evolution. After Darwin, such a natural system is meant to represent the evolutionary, i.e., phylogenetic, relationships among species and genera.

But why the need to introduce a new, special term—phylogeny—to refer to descent with modification? The reason is the long, checkered history of the term "evolution" (from French _évolution_ ). It was first introduced into biology to denote the unfolding of the embryo during its development (Bowler, 1975). Many prominent eighteenth-century French biologists and philosophers believed the embryo to preexist in its entirety, with all its parts present, in the egg produced by females. (A rival theory located the preexisting embryo in the head portion of the spermatozoon produced by males.) The elusiveness of such preexisting embryos in chicken eggs opened for inspection was attributed to their exceedingly small size and the transparency of the tissues. On that account, embryonic development is simply a process of the successive unfolding—through nutrition, growth, and enlargement—of preexisting structures, and the French term for "unfolding" is _évolution_. Only later did the term come to refer to the Darwinian process of descent with modification. It may be such ambiguity in meaning that motivated Darwin to use the term only once in his _Origin_ , in the very last sentence: "From so simple a beginning endless forms most beautiful and most wonderful have been, and are being evolved" (Darwin, 1859:490). To disambiguate the language of biology, Ernst Haeckel (1834–1919)—the German Darwin, as many called him—introduced the term "ontogeny" to refer to embryonic development, while the term "phylogeny" he introduced to refer to evolutionary development, i.e., descent with modification.

But the ontogeny–phylogeny distinction was not the only innovation that Haeckel brought to the language of biology. In his immensely influential 1866 treatise, _Generelle Morphologie_ , a two-volume tract on animal comparative anatomy, ontogeny, and phylogeny, Haeckel was the first to introduce the term "monophyletic," and he was also the first to provide graphic renditions of phylogenetic trees. A phylogenetic tree with a single root, instead of multiple separate roots, Haeckel called a monophyletic tree ( _mono_ , "single," and _phyletic_ , "evolutionary"; a monophyletic tree has a single evolutionary origin). Haeckel returned to the concept of monophyly many times in his later writings, leaving behind a legacy that left the term poorly defined—indeed rather ambiguous—in its meaning. But on a charitable reading of his first use of the term in his treatise of 1866, one could argue that Haeckel initially wanted monophyly to apply to a tree that depicts the evolutionary history of a section of the animal kingdom that derives from a single common ancestor—hence the single root. Unicellular organisms, the protists, Haeckel did not believe to form a monophyletic group. Instead, he believed the great diversity of protists to have had multiple independent evolutionary origins, the protists collectively thus constituting a polyphyletic group ( _poly_ , "multiple," and _phyletic_ "evolutionary"; a polyphyletic tree has multiple roots, or evolutionary origins). Conversely, and in modern parlance, a monophyletic group is a group of organisms that comprises their most recent common ancestor and all, and only, its descendants. Each human being has a unique origin, from a specific egg fertilized by a specific spermatozoan. Many philosophers argue that it is this particular, unique origin that individuates a human being, i.e., renders each human being a unique individual. Similarly, these philosophers argue, it is the uniqueness of its evolutionary origin that individuates a species (renders a species an individual), and hence also the group that comprises all, and only, the descendants of a certain (stem) species.

**2.2.** First use of term "monophyly" by Ernst Haeckel, in text accompanying plate I in volume 2 of _Generelle Morphologie_ (1866). At right bottom corner of plate I, he writes: "Monophyletic genealogic tree of organisms, mapped out and drawn by Ernst Haeckel, Jena, 1866."

A modern classification is required to represent the phylogenetic relationships of organisms and thus to correspond to a natural system, not an artificial one. Because new species originate from ancestral species through speciation, and because speciation is generally (but not uncontroversially) held to result from the splitting of a species lineage into two daughter species, the natural system is graphically represented by a dichotomously branching treelike diagram, which cladists call a cladogram. The classification that corresponds to such a cladogram is considered natural if all the groups of species it contains are monophyletic. Placing turtles either close to the root of the reptile tree or far up in its crown will thus imply radically different phylogenetic relationships of turtles, and hence also a radically different classification of turtles within a hierarchical system of monophyletic groups of reptiles. Placing turtles near the root of the reptile tree renders them archaic survivors of ancestral species that went extinct 250 and more million years ago. With turtles nesting in the crown of the reptile tree, these archaic-looking creatures suddenly become modernized. This has important consequences. If turtles are something like living fossils, physiologists, for example, would have reason to believe that body functions in turtles represent the primitive condition for reptiles and their descendants, the birds. This would not be the case if turtles nest high up in the reptile tree.

Well into the twentieth century, reptile classification remained rather fluid, indeed controversial. The basic groups of living reptiles, such as turtles, crocodiles, lizards, and snakes, had long been recognized, but their precise composition, and especially their relationship to one another, remained a matter of debate. An early contribution that triggered an extensive, long-lasting conversation came from a young Germanborn paleontologist, Georg Baur (1859–1898), who in the years 1884–1890 served as assistant to paleontologist Othniel Charles Marsh (1831–1899) at Yale University. Marsh, as is widely known among dinosaur aficionados, was locked in an epic battle against his archrival from Philadelphia, Edward Drinker Cope (1840–1897). The two men fought over dinosaur bones dug up in the American West, engaging in a race for priority in the description of as many of new dinosaur species as possible. They have been immortalized as protagonists of the so-called bone wars or dinosaur wars, which often took ethical standards of science to their limit—and even beyond. Intrigues between the excavation crews they sent to the boneyards out West sabotaged fieldwork, publications were predated in order to secure priority of species descriptions, and accusations of theft of fossils and plagiarism all intertwined in a rivalry that had reached its pinnacle by the end of the 1870s. Baur stepped out of Marsh's shadow much later, and in a much less controversial manner, with a contribution on the reptile skull that he published in the _Journal of Morphology_ in 1889. Baur's paper is short, a compact read that presupposes foundational knowledge of the reptile skull in order to be understood. Such fundamental knowledge of the structure of the reptile skull is likewise required to understand reptile classification and the problem of turtle relationships. The reptile skull is better understood, however, from a bottom-up perspective (from fish to reptile) rather than from a top-down perspective (from mammal to reptile), as the bottom-up perspective also represents the evolutionary trajectory.

When considering a dog, a human, or any other mammal, the skull appears—at least superficially—to be a relatively simple structure: a cranial box housing the brain, two eye sockets, and paired openings for the nose and ears, respectively. On that cranial box hinges the lower jaw. That will do for most conversations at Halloween parties; only students of anatomy will have to delve into the details that characterize the many cranial bones and the multiple openings transmitting cranial nerves. What seems simple at the level of mammals is, however, an evolutionarily highly derived condition, resulting in a structure that, in terms of its evolutionary history, is intricate and highly complex. This is not the place to trace the evolutionary history of the mammalian skull, however. But we do need to understand some of the evolutionary history of the reptile skull to get a handle on reptile classification. So let's start with fish—say, some bony fish, such as a gar or bowfin. Here, the skull is composed of three evolutionarily independent yet tightly interrelated structural components. In the lower vertebrates, the skull can be best comprehended on the model of Russian nesting dolls, with an outer shell (the enclosing doll) comprising an inner shell (the enclosed doll), and space between the two to accommodate third-component elements. Perhaps it is anatomically more correct to say, as the eminent American paleontologist Alfred Sherwood Romer (1894–1973) did, that the skull in a gar or bowfin, but also that of a sea turtle, for example, forms a system composed of two telescoped bony cylinders, the larger, outer one (first component) enclosing the smaller, inner one (second component), with space between the two to accommodate third-component elements. The inner cylinder is the braincase, housing the brain and the sense organs such as the nasal capsules, the eye sockets, and the ear capsules. The braincase is preformed in cartilage in the embryo and may persist in a cartilaginous state in the adult at least in some of its parts, while in other parts the cartilage may be replaced by bone in a process called ossification. Because it encapsulates the brain, the braincase is also known as the neurocranium. And because of its position inside the outer bony cylinder, the braincase is sometimes also called the endocranium. Given its deep position relative to the outer bony cylinder, and given the fact that the whole structure is preformed in cartilage before part of it may ossify, the braincase is a component of the inner skeleton, or endoskeleton, derived from the ancient Greek term _endon_ , "within."

**2.3.** Schematic and idealized representation of primitive structure of vertebrate skull.

_Redrawn after Portmann, 1976, fig. 78; drawing by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

The outer bony cylinder is, by comparison, part of the outer skeleton, or exoskeleton, derived from the Greek exo, "outside." It is a protective covering of the head and of the anterior braincase floor made up of bones that ossify directly in the deeper layer of the skin, the dermis, without any cartilaginous precursors. The skin of vertebrates has two layers: the outer epidermis (the Greek prefix _epi_ \- means "above") and the inner dermis. Flakes of skin that peel off our body as a consequence of a sunburn are superficial layers of the epidermis, as is the "skin" periodically shed by lizards and snakes. A cut that results in bleeding has penetrated the epidermis and reached the underlying dermis. Bone that ossifies directly in the dermis without being preformed in cartilage is accordingly called dermal bone. The outer cylinder of the skull is correspondingly called the dermatocranium. In the space that remains between the neurocranium and the dermatocranium are located the gill arches and their associated musculature. The gill arches are collectively referred to as the splanch-nocranium, and being preformed in cartilage during development, they are again endoskeletal structures. The jaw apparatus of jawed vertebrates (the gnathostomes) evolved from the anteriormost two gill arches. But this also means that the muscles operating the jaws must be located in the space between the outer dermatocranium and the inner neurocranium. In the adult human skull, as in other mammals, the endoskeletal neurocranium (the inner cylinder) and the exoskeletal dermatocranium (the outer cylinder) have combined to form a single unified cranial box, rendering the original components difficult to discern. The jaw muscles are consequently superficially positioned on the skull. Ossified remnants of the ancestral gill arch skeleton are retained in mammals in form of a bone (alisphenoid) located just behind the eye socket (orbit) as well as in the three ear ossicles; other parts of the ancestral gill arch skeleton are retained in the throat skeleton. The seemingly simple cranial box so characteristic of mammals is thus revealed to be a highly complex structure comprising elements from three systems, each of which has its own evolutionary history: the braincase, the dermatocranium, and the ancestral gill arch skeleton.

With that introduction, we should now be ready to follow Baur's expositions on the reptile skull. Baur's 1889 paper had two parts to it. The first dealt with the braincase in ways that are not relevant here. The second treated the various modifications that affect the temporal region in the dermatocranium, also called the cheek region, located behind the eye socket (orbit) in the reptile skull. Baur recognized that by comparison with ancestral (fossil) amphibians, a complete dermal roofing of the temporal region of the skull—as it occurs in the earliest stem-reptiles from the Paleozoic—is primitive for the group. In this primitive condition, the outer dermal covering of the skull behind the eye socket is complete, concealing the muscles that operate the jaws from view. However, this primitive condition of the reptile skull was modified in various ways during the evolution of reptiles, mostly in ways that would reduce the dermal covering of the cheek region. Crocodiles, for example, and their fossil relatives, such as dinosaurs, as well as the tuatara from islands around New Zealand, develop two openings, or fenestrae (Latin for "windows"), in the temporal region of the skull (dermatocranium), one located above the other. Thus there is on either side of a crocodile or tuatara skull, from front to back, an external naris, an eye socket, and an upper and lower temporal fenestra. The bony bar that separates the upper from the lower temporal fenestra is called the upper temporal arch, or arcade; the bony bar that closes the lower temporal fossa along the lower (ventral) margin of the cheek region is called the lower temporal arch, or arcade. The functional reasons for the initial evolution of such temporal fenestration remain poorly understood. Lizards have only an upper temporal fenestra bounded by an upper temporal arch. Below that, the cheek region is wide open, i.e., not covered by dermal bone. The lower temporal arch has been lost. Snakes have a wide-open temporal region of the skull, with no trace of an upper or lower temporal arch. Such further modifications in the temporal region of the dermatocranium had important yet different consequences for the jaw mechanics in lizards and snakes. The wide-open temporal region in the snake skull, for example, allowed for a rearrangement of the jaw adductor musculature, now located in a superficial position that made it possible, for evolutionarily advanced snakes, to feed on prey that has a diameter larger than the diameter of the snake's own head.

In turtles, things are different yet variable again, and hence less easily assessed. Sea turtles, like the leatherback sea turtle ( _Dermochelys coriacea_ ) or the green sea turtle ( _Chelonia mydas_ ), have a complete dermatocranial covering across the temporal region of the skull. The dermatocranium thus forms a complete outer cylinder enclosing the inner cylinder (braincase), with the jaw muscles accommodated between the two. That would seem to correspond to the primitive condition for reptiles, as exemplified by Paleozoic stem-reptiles. The problem already noted by Goodrich in his 1930 textbook is that the number of bones making up the complete dermatocranial covering of the cheek region is smaller in turtles than in primitive reptiles. Some bony elements have been lost in turtles; others have correspondingly expanded, or perhaps some have fused with their neighbors in the course of turtle evolution. In other turtles, the dermal covering of the cheek has been reduced in a way that can, in certain extreme cases, mimic the presence of an upper temporal arch. In yet others, the temporal region of the skull lacks any dermatocranial covering.

**2.4.** Fenestration versus emargination of temporal region in reptile skull (dermatocranium). (A) Stem-reptile _Captorhinus_. (B) Early (Paleozoic) diapsid reptile _Petrolacosaurus_. (C) Marine green turtle _Chelonia mydas_. (D) Ornate box turtle _Terrapene ornata_. (E) Keeled box turtle _Cuora mouhotii_.

_Redrawn after Rieppel, 1993b, figs. 7.6, and 7.7; drawing by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

How to make sense of this variation? The first question to ask, of course, is whether the complete dermatocranial covering of the cheek region in modern sea turtle skulls by a reduced number of bony elements does indeed correspond to the primitive reptile condition, or whether this is a secondary development—a secondary "roofing over," as was argued by Goodrich in his classic text of 1930. Setting this question aside, Baur further recognized that if the dermal covering of the cheek region is reduced—to variable degrees—in turtles, this never occurs through the development of any fenestration such as is the case in crocodiles, dinosaurs, or the tuatara. Instead, the bony covering of the cheek region is reduced by emargination: bone is, metaphorically speaking, eaten away from the back margin of the skull forward and from the lower margin of the skull upward, creating deep embayments in the upper and lower cheek region. As this process continues, the dermal covering of the cheek region is eventually reduced to a slender bar of bone that mimics an upper temporal arch, until even that disappears.

Putting a series of turtle skulls on a table alongside a skull of a crocodile, of the tuatara, of a lizard, and of a snake renders several comparative judgments possible. The crocodile and the tuatara have two temporal openings, or fenestrae, behind the eye socket, each bounded by the upper and lower temporal arch. They thus differ fundamentally from turtles. The lizard has only an upper temporal fenestra bounded by an upper temporal arch, but the cheek below it is wide open. Outwardly, some lizards, especially agamids, look very much like the tuatara, and the condition of the dermatocranium in the temporal region of the lizard skull would indeed seem to be easily explained as a consequence of the loss of the lower temporal arch. Snakes, with a completely open temporal region of the skull, could then be said to have lost both the lower and upper temporal arches. This is precisely the opinion Baur had previously held, but he later changed his mind: "It seems very much probable that the Squamata [i.e., lizards and snakes] never possessed an infratemporal fossa [a lower temporal fenestra], but that the broad arch was reduced [by emargination] from below as in the Testudinata," i.e., as in turtles (1889:473). The implication here is that unlike the tuatara, ancestral lizards would have had an upper temporal fenestra only, while the der-matocranial covering of the cheek region would have been complete below it. In the course of lizard evolution, this lower part of the cheek region would have become emarginated, eventually to a degree that only a slender bar of bone was left to close off the upper temporal fenestra. In snakes, even that bar of bone would then have been eaten away through reduction. The implication (which he did not spell out) of Baur's change of mind, then, is that lizards (and snakes) share an evolutionary pattern of reduction in the cheek region of the dermatocranium with turtles and not with crocodiles, dinosaurs, and the tuatara. On that account, lizards (and snakes) would be more closely related to turtles than to crocodiles, dinosaurs, and the tuatara.

That implied conclusion certainly did not sit well with his supervisor's archrival, Cope. "At the meeting of the American Association for the Advancement of Science, held in Troy, N.Y., in 1870," he had presented his views on the significance of the differentiation of the dermatocranium in the temporal region of the reptile skull (Cope, 1892:11; see Cope, 1871). "It is evident that the correct classification and phylogeny of the Reptilia will not be completed without" a proper understanding of the dermatocranial arches in the skull of reptiles, he continued, and—quoting extensively from Baur's 1889 paper—cited the latter's view that lizards never had a lower temporal fenestra and arch but instead had emarginated the lower cheek region in a turtlelike manner. Cope then launched into an extensive review of reptile skulls, first in Paleozoic and then in Mesozoic forms, to which living forms could be related. On the basis of his interpretations, Cope derived turtles directly from Paleozoic stem-reptiles then called "Cotylosauria," while the lizards he characterized—in contrast to Baur's revised opinion—as having lost the lower temporal arch that forms the ventral closure of the lower temporal fossa in the tuatara. He believed the snakes to have lost the upper temporal arch as well, thus fully opening up the temporal region of the skull. It might perhaps seem difficult to understand why paleontologists would get caught up in such esoteric debates, but they did because the results mattered. Both Baur and Cope agreed that the primitive reptile skull had a completely covered cheek region. They also both agreed that turtles reduced the bony covering of the cheek region through emargination, not through fenestration. And they agreed that the two temporal openings in the tuatara, in crocodiles, and in dinosaurs are the result of fenestration, not of emargination. They both saw the exact same thing when looking at a lizard skull: a reptile skull with an upper temporal opening bounded by an upper temporal arch, below which the cheek region is not covered by bone. Finally, they both asked the same question: how is the lizard skull to be evolutionarily derived from the primitive reptile condition? It is at this juncture where they each read a different evolutionary story into the lizard skull. Baur thought the lower cheek region in lizards that once was covered by bone had become emarginated like in turtles, whereas Cope thought lizards had lost the lower temporal arch, which in ancestral lizards closed off a lower temporal fenestra. In Baur's revised account, lizard skulls shared a feature with turtle skulls, namely the emargination of the lower cheek region from below. In Cope's account, there was nothing that would link turtles to lizards. The two antagonists relied on the same evidence, but they invoked different evolutionary interpretations that implied different hypotheses of turtle relationships.

Baur, who moved to a professorship at the University of Chicago, published a lengthy defense of his views in 1895 in the German journal _Anatomischer Anzeiger_. His defense targeted not only Cope's critique but also that voiced by famous German comparative anatomist Ernst Gaupp (1865–1916), who could "absolutely not follow" Baur's expositions of 1889 (Gaupp, 1895:110n1; on Gaupp, see Fischer, 1917). Gaupp was a student of the enormously influential systematist, comparative anatomist, and phylogeneticist Ernst Haeckel from the University of Jena, and he was also heavily influenced by Haeckel's friend, Carl Gegenbaur (1826–1903), the preeminent comparative anatomist among zoologists of his time (Richards, 2008; Rieppel, 2011). In a lengthy monograph, Gaupp found both lizards and (some) turtles to share the presence of what looks like an upper temporal arch, but unlike Baur, he interpreted the lizard skull the same way as Cope had before, i.e., implying the loss of a lower temporal arch rather than an emargination from below (Gaupp, 1895:115).

Baur, Cope, Gaupp—these were just a few protagonists among many who contributed to these early debates about reptile classification and the relationships of turtles. A contribution, however, that would finally stand the test of time was offered by a former student of Cope's, Henry Fairfield Osborn (1857–1935), with a short note in _Science_ published in 1903 (on Osborn, see Gregory, 1937). At that time, Osborn was a professor at New York's Columbia University and a curator in the department of vertebrate paleontology at the AMNH, where he rose to the post of president in 1908. On the bright side of Osborn's personality shone his preeminence in dinosaur research and dinosaur collection building at the AMNH. The dark side of his personality was committed to eugenics, a pseudoscientific movement that promoted measures to prevent so-called genetically inferior individuals from reproduction, with the goal of improving racial strength, vitality, and superiority. Such a commitment would later culminate in Osborn's enthusiasm for the Hitler–Hindenburg regime; indeed, he expressed hopes for a bright future in Germany that were to be bitterly disappointed.2 In his short 1903 commentary to _Science_ on reptile classification, Osborn referred to a note he had received on February 7, 1903, from the wannabe king of Albania, Viennese paleontologist Franz Baron Nopcsa, informing him of a paper by Broom who, in 1901, had not only divided the reptiles into two major groups on the basis of the differentiation of temporal arches in the dermatocranium, "but also went so far as to assign a phylogenetic value to that distinction" (Osborn, 1903:275).

Robert Broom (1866–1951) was an eccentric Scottish MD with a deep interest in paleontology who was living in South Africa. His medical practice was interrupted for a few years by his appointment, from 1903 through 1909, as professor of geology and zoology at the University of Stellenbosch. His travel abroad took him mainly back to Britain, but he also followed up on an invitation from Osborn to visit the AMNH in 1909 (Watson, 1952; Le Gros Clark, 1951). Although he published on a wide variety of topics in reptile comparative anatomy and phylogeny, Broom specialized on what used to be called mammal-like reptiles from the Karoo region with Permian and Triassic outcrops. The mammal-like reptiles preceded the dinosaurs as the dominant land-dwelling tetrapods and gave rise to mammals before their demise in an Upper Triassic mass extinction event that opened up the opportunity for dinosaurs to rapidly diversify. Broom donated the specimens he collected to the South African Museum of Cape Town, which also appointed him as curator of fossil reptiles. Broom's 1901 monograph—the one Nopcsa alerted Osborn to—dealt with the anatomy and relationships of _Oudenodon_ (spelled _Udenodon_ by Broom), a Late Permian mammal-like reptile from the Karoo, some 260 million years old (Broom, 1901). In discussing the phylogenetic relationships of _Oudenodon_ , Broom again recognized the fully closed temporal region in the dermatocranium as the ancestral condition for reptiles. From that ancestral condition, he derived two main lines of reptile evolution. The first is the mammal-like reptiles that develop a single temporal arch composed of the jugal and squamosal bones, a lineage that would eventually give rise to mammals. The other major line of reptiles is characterized by the development of two temporal arches, Broom argued, neither of which is made up from the same elements of the dermatocranium, as is the single temporal arch characteristic of mammal-like reptiles. In this second line of reptile evolution recognized by Broom, the jugal forms part of the lower temporal arch, and the squamosal forms part of the upper temporal arch. Osborn, who had harbored similar thoughts, followed up on Broom's observations with his 1903 note in _Science_ , adducing additional evidence in support of the division of fossil reptiles into two major evolutionary lineages. The first of Broom's lineage, the one leading to mammals, he called the Synapsida, and the second lineage characterized by two temporal arches he called the Diapsida. Both of these names are still in use today. Significantly, Osborn placed turtles and the marine plesiosaurs into the synapsid lineage, together with mammal-like reptiles, whereas crocodiles, the tuatara, lizards, and snakes he considered part of the diapsid line.

Reptile classification started to take on a modern shape with a landmark publication in 1917. Samuel W. Williston (1851–1918) from the University of Kansas (and later the University of Chicago) was a paleontologist and entomologist who earned his first paleontological accolades on a fossil-hunting expedition for Othniel Charles Marsh. The trip was followed by postgraduate studies at Yale University. Later in life, Williston became a leading vertebrate paleontologist specializing on Mesozoic marine reptiles (Williston, 1914). He was also a member of a committee charged with the task of standardizing the nomenclature for the bones in reptile skulls; he also authored a classic text on the _Osteology of the Reptiles_ , published posthumously by Harvard University Press in 1925. In his 1917 paper, Williston credited Cope for having been the first to have recognized the significance of the temporal arches for the classification of reptiles, but he named Osborn as the one who—with the help of "Dr. J. Howard McGregor"—first followed through on that basis by naming the two major evolutionary lineages of reptiles he recognized, the Synapsida and the Diapsida (Cope, 1871; Osborn, 1903:276). Building on that foundation, Williston recognized two more branches, i.e., evolutionary lineages in the reptile tree, of which only one concerns us here. It is the one that comprises the Paleozoic stem-reptiles in which the temporal region of the dermatocranium is fully closed, a branch Williston called the Anapsida. Together with those ancient stem-reptiles, Williston included turtles within his Anapsida, thus laying down what was to become the classic orthodox view of turtle relationships. In Williston's own words,

It was Baur who first asserted that turtles could not have originated from reptiles with a perforated [i.e., fenestrated] temporal region. Cope derived the order directly from the Cotylosauria.... I approved and emphasized Baur's views in 1904. Case and Hay both hold the same opinion, and only recently Watson has forcibly and convincingly presented the claim of _Eunatosaurus_ [ _sic_ ], from the Permian, as a real connecting link between the two orders. And Broom and others are of the same opinion. This unanimity of opinion renders further discussion superfluous. (1917:420)

A consensus to evolutionarily derive turtles from anapsid stem-reptiles from the Paleozoic may have been hammered out among American paleontologists, with the honorable Davis M. S. Watson (1886–1973) from the University College London on their side, but this consensus definitely did not include Goodrich, who kept the discussion going. Deriving turtles from anapsid stem-reptiles implied that the fully closed temporal region in the skull of those turtles where it occurs is primitive for the group, even if that dermatocranial cover of the cheek region comprises a lesser number of individual elements. Goodrich saw things differently. Reptiles and their fossil relatives, he argued in a paper published in 1916, are not a monophyletic group that can be traced back to a single common ancestor—a single stem species from which they were all descended. Instead, the reptilian assemblage will one day have to be broken up, he said with brilliant foresight, some to be allied with mammals (a lineage he called Theropsida) and others to be allied with birds (a lineage he called Sauropsida). The remaining Paleozoic stem-reptiles with their anapsid skull (i.e., with a completely closed temporal region of the der-matocranium) he sorted into a third group he called the Protosauria. He then went on to criticize the consensus concerning turtle classification that was conjured up by Williston, pointing out that the corresponding classification is based on just a single character complex, namely the differentiation of the dermatocranium in the temporal region of the skull: "Moreover, as is always the case when we endeavor to classify by a single character, we are liable to confuse forms in which foramina [i.e., fenestrae] have begun to appear with others in which they have been or are being secondarily obliterated, and to misinterpret aberrant modifications" (Goodrich, 1916:263). In other words, it was Goodrich's view that the skull alone could not offer a reliable clue to turtle relationships. He thought it was impossible to interpret the closed temporal region of the dermatocranium observed in some turtles—fossil and extant—as either primitive or as a secondary condition (a secondary roofing over) on the basis of a comparison of skull structure alone.

Goodrich, rejecting skull structure as the only relevant character complex for reptile classification, turned his attention to the ankle, which in turtles, the tuatara, crocodiles, and lizards he found to share a certain striking specialization (a hooked fifth metatarsal bone, of which more below), one that he then presented as a character diagnostic of his sauropsidan lineage. The tuatara, crocodiles, and lizards are, of course, classic diapsid reptiles, part of Osborn's Diapsida. Including turtles in his Sauropsida on the basis of the hooked fifth metatarsal, Goodrich implicitly allied the turtles with Osborn's Diapsida. The conclusion thus had to be that in those turtles where the temporal region of the dermatocranium is closed, it was the consequence of a secondary roofing over, not the primitive condition inherited from Paleozoic stem-reptiles.

Between Goodrich's paper of 1916 and Williston's of 1917, most points had been articulated that still dominate the debate on turtle origins and relationships today. Does the complete dermatocranial covering of the temporal region of the skull in those turtles where it occurs represent the primitive condition, or is it the result of a reversal of evolutionary history, i.e., a secondary closure? Are turtles to be derived from Paleozoic anapsid stem-reptiles, perhaps such forms as _Eunotosaurus_ from the late Middle Permian (some 260 million years old) of the Karoo in South Africa, as was argued by H. G. Seeley in 1892, and later—in a much more expanded argument—by D. M. S. Watson in 1914? Or should turtles be allied with diapsid reptiles? (For a critical evaluation of Watson's study, see Parsons and Williams, 1961.) What is the exact embryonic composition of the hooked fifth metatarsal, and viewed from such a developmental perspective, is it indeed the same highly specialized characteristic in the foot of the tuatara, lizards, crocodiles, and turtles? The dilemma concerning turtle relationships was nicely put by Broom in a paper he published in 1924 in memory of Williston. The paper, he announced in a footnote, had already been written in 1919 for a proposed memorial volume honoring Williston—a project that eventually fell through. Then, as now, Broom's statements were valid: "The second group of reptiles which has given rise to trouble is the Chelonia [turtles], and there is no order of reptiles living or extinct concerning whose affinities greater differences of opinion have been expressed" (Broom, 1924:48, cited with permission from the publisher). His lucid diagnosis of the conundrum likewise remains valid today:

The chelonian is manifestly an extremely aberrant type, where the ancestral characters are so obscured by their being intermingled with remarkable specializations that it is very difficult to tell which is which.... Those who regard the structure of the temporal region of the skull as the safest guide to affinity will naturally place the Chelonia with the primitive [anapsid Paleozoic stem-reptiles]; those who hold that more reliance can be placed on the structure of the girdles and limbs will be more impressed with the affinities to primitive diapsids such as _Sphenodon_ [the tuatara]. (Broom, 1924:50, cited with permission from the publisher)

The Cladistic Revolution Captures Turtles

Many of Goodrich's original insights that he published in 1916 have in their essence been vindicated by modern cladistic analysis of reptile relationships. On cladistic principles, the amniotes, i.e., all organisms whose reproduction involves an amniote egg characterized by extraembryonic membranes (i.e., all reptiles, birds and mammals), are subdivided into two major evolutionary lineages, or clades: the Synapsida and the Reptilia, respectively. The Synapsida comprise the so-called mammal-like reptiles of earlier authors and all of their descendants, i.e., the mammals. The Reptilia include the primitive, anapsid Paleozoic stem-reptiles and all of their descendants, including the birds. When fossils are excluded from consideration, the modern concept of Reptilia corresponds to Goodrich's concept of Sauropsida. In other words, if only living organisms are considered, the names Reptilia and Sauropsida are synonymous. Within the Reptilia, the Anapsida is no longer considered a natural (monophyletic) group of common descent (i.e., a group that includes its most recent common ancestor and all, and only, its descendants). Anapsid is today only an anatomic term and no longer a taxonomic one. It no longer denotes an evolutionary lineage but merely refers to the closed temporal region in the dermatocranium that is characteristic of Paleozoic stem-reptiles and some turtles. Instead, the Paleozoic stem-reptiles sort into two major evolutionary lineages, the Parareptilia and the Captorhinomorpha, both thought to be characterized by an anapsid skull (with exceptions among parareptiles).

The parareptiles are a diverse assemblage, including the first herbivores that evolved among tetrapods, the pareiasaurs, mostly large and bulky creatures from the Late Permian of South Africa and Russia. Another parareptilian branch closely related to the pareiasaurs are the procolophonids, relatively small forms with a specialized dentition comprising peglike teeth. The clade first appeared in the Late Permian and diversified in the Early Triassic in the Karoo of South Africa. The Paleozoic (Permian) mesosaurs are aquatic parareptiles, up to about 60 cm (2 feet) long and once thought to be found in continental rather than open marine deposits on both sides of the Atlantic in South Africa and South America. They were historically used as evidence in early discussions of continental drift, as it was thought that their peculiar geographic distribution indicated that the southern continents must at one time have formed a supercontinent known as Gondwana.

The Captorhinomorpha are the second Paleozoic reptile lineage characterized by an anapsid skull. They comprise the classic stem-reptiles, relatively small, land-dwelling, insect-eating animals that diversified during the Permian, some 260 million years ago. They are not themselves the sister group of the parareptiles, however. Instead, the Parareptilia are the sister group of the Eureptilia, the latter representing the major branch of reptile evolution. The Captorhinomorpha are a basal branch in the eureptilian lineage, and as such they constitute the sister group to all remaining reptiles, all of them included in the Diapsida. As a consequence, the Parareptilia and the Captorhinomorpha cannot be grouped together in a monophyletic clade that includes their most recent common ancestor and all, and only, its descendants. The term "Anapsida" thus cannot be used as a taxonomic term but can only stand as an anatomical term, referring to a skull in which the cheek region in the dermatocranium is completely closed. It is perhaps ironic that the anapsid skull has recently been claimed not to represent the primitive condition for the Parareptilia. The most recent analysis showed the most basal parareptiles to have a skull with a lower fenestra in the temporal region of the skull. Within the Parareptilia, this primitive condition was modified in two ways: either the lower temporal arch was lost, which resulted in a ventral embayment of the cheek region, or the lower temporal opening was secondarily closed, which resulted in an anapsid skull (MacDougall and Reisz, 2014). In contrast to the term "Anapsida," the term "Diapsida" can be retained not just as an anatomical term denoting a skull characterized by the presence of two temporal openings but also as a taxonomic term denoting the by far most diversified group of reptiles, one that also includes the birds.

**2.5.** Current understanding of amniote relationships.

_Drawing by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

A couple species of early diapsids from the Late Paleozoic (Carboniferous, Permian) form a separate basal branch among the Diapsida, one that constitutes the sister group of the Neodiapsida, or Sauria of some authors, i.e., of those diapsids that have living descendants. The Neodiapsida, or Sauria, divide into two major branches of reptile evolution, the Lepidosauromorpha and the Archosauromorpha. The lepidosauromorphs include the tuatara ( _Sphenodon_ ) and the scaly reptiles (the Squamata), which comprise lizards, a group of burrowing lizards called amphisbaenians, and the snakes—and of course their fossil relatives. Although the squamates are a highly diverse group, with nearly 9,000 living species, the diversity of the lepidosauromorph clade still pales in comparison to that of the archosauromorphs. The Archosauromorpha include the crocodiles and their fossil relatives, among which are counted such diverse groups as the dinosaurs and the pterosaurs. Because birds are descended from theropod dinosaurs, they need to be included in the archosauromorphs in order to render the Archosauromorpha a natural group, i.e., a monophyletic clade that includes its most recent common ancestor and all, and only, its descendants. In addition to all the reptile groups just named, there is a host more, such as the marine dolphinlike ichthyosaurs; the sauropterygians; an enigmatic and poorly understood group of marine reptiles, the saurosphargids; and a host of others, none of which matters for the turtle story. In a nutshell, and as of today, turtles have been related—on the basis of cladistic analysis—alternatively to the pareiasaurs or procolophonids among the Parareptilia, but also to the captorhinomorphs among the Paleozoic eureptiles. Turtles have been related along with the Sauropterygia to the lepidosauromorph branch of diapsids, implying a Mesozoic rather than a Paleozoic origin, whereas molecular (DNA) data place them on the archosauromorph branch with the crocodiles and birds. Although sophisticated computer algorithms are being used in the search for turtle sister group relationships, the situation still resembles the one aptly characterized by Broom in the early twentieth century: there is hardly any major reptile group with which turtles have not been at least tentatively allied, even on the basis of cladistic analysis. This is remarkable, as cladistics has been touted to be a nonauthoritarian, objective method with which to reconstruct evolutionary relationships.

The modern understanding—or rather lack thereof—of the evolutionary relationships of turtles with other major branches in the reptile tree started with the work of Eugene S. Gaffney (2013) from the AMNH. Influenced again by Gary Nelson and others, Gaffney was among the first-generation cladists at that institution. In his self-assessment, Gaffney stated, "Although I was an active participant and minor contributor to the Clade Wars of the 1970s, I've never been much of a systematics theoretician" (2013:11). Still, together with Norman I. Platnick from the same institution, he coauthored the influential reviews—mentioned in the previous chapter—of Popper's books on the philosophy of science in _Systematic Zoology_ , thereby adapting through some distortion Popper's characterization of what a true science is, or should be, to cladistic methodology. This was one of the strategies to bolster the claim for superiority of cladistics compared to other schools of biological systematics. But Gaffney is much better known, indeed famous, for his lifelong work on the comparative anatomy and phylogeny of fossil and living turtles: "The first effect of cladism or phylogenetic systematics on turtle taxonomy," Gaffney said, "can be seen in my revision of the Baenidae in 1972" (1984:292). The Baenidae is an extinct family of turtles from the Late Cretaceous of North America, which Gaffney chose to study as part of his PhD project. Whereas most of Gaffney's work focuses on the interrelationships among turtles, he also wrote two papers (Gaffney and McKenna, 1979; Gaffney, 1980) that addressed the broader issue of turtle origins, i.e., the sister group relationships of turtles to other reptiles.

In one of his rare excursions into reptile diversity outside turtles, Gaffney coauthored a description of a Late Permian captorhinid stem-reptile from Rhodesia, some 250 million years old. In their comments on captorhinid systematics, Gaffney and his coauthor obliquely mentioned the possibility that turtles might be closely related to a subgroup of these early anapsid stem-reptiles (Gaffney and McKenna, 1979). The reason for their hesitant comment on turtle relationships was the lack of a well-corroborated analysis of amniote relationships that should have provided the framework for a more rigorous test of potential relations tying turtles to captorhinids. Those higher-level relationships of amniotes Gaffney tackled in a paper that was published the following year, in 1980. Going back to Osborn's distinction of Synapsida and Diapsida, Gaffney framed the problem of turtle relationships in the form of the most basic statement amenable to cladistic analysis, a so-called three-taxon statement (as in the salmon–lungfish–cow debate sketched in the previous chapter). Given three taxa, i.e., the Synapsida (mammals and their fossil relatives), the Diapsida (crocodiles, the tuatara, lizards, and snakes, their fossil relatives, and their descendants), and the turtles, the question becomes, which two taxa among the three are more closely related to each other than either of them is to the third? Given three taxa, there obtain three, and only three, possible answers: the Synapsida is closer to the Diapsida than either is to turtles; the Synapsida is closer to turtles than either is to the Diapsida; or the Diapsida is closer to turtles than either is to the Synapsida. Primarily on the basis of characteristics that relate to the structure of the nose, Gaffney opted for the first of these three alternatives. Much later, Gaffney reiterated more explicitly his hypothesis of a sister group relationship between captorhinid stem-reptiles and turtles, summarizing new evidence in support of a "refined Anapsida that is not very different from its initial formulation by Williston (1925)" (Gaffney and Meylan, 1988:160). At the time of his writing, the Parareptilia had not yet been recognized as a natural, i.e., monophyletic, group, with the consequence that Gaffney could continue to use the term "Anapsida" as a taxonomic name, just like Williston had done.

The first signal for the existence of a parareptilian clade was picked up and published in the same edited volume that also contained Gaff-ney's paper on turtle phylogeny (Gauthier, Kluge, and Rowe, 1988). The authors of the relevant chapter were not convinced, however, that the Parareptilia their analysis had recovered was a well-supported monophyletic clade, one that includes its most recent ancestor and all, and only, its descendants. A group that includes the ancestor and only some of its descendants is called paraphyletic in cladistic jargon—hence the name Parareptilia. But the possibility of the existence of a parareptilian clade was still put out there for others to confirm or reject through additional research. The monophyly of Parareptilia was indeed tested, and confirmed, seven years later, in a paper published in 1995 by Michel Laurin and his doctoral supervisor, Robert Reisz, from the University of Toronto. Already in January 1991 these two authors had made headlines with a paper published in _Nature_ in which they described the oldest known procolophonid, _Owenetta_ from the Late Permian of the Karoo, Broom's old hunting grounds in South Africa. That in itself was perhaps not overly exciting, but the authors used the insights they had gained through the study of _Owenetta_ to argue that turtles were in fact related to, and hence ultimately descended from, procolophonids, a subgroup of the Parareptilia (Reisz and Laurin, 1991). _Owenetta_ was to be recognized as close to the ancestry of turtles. At the time, this idea was both new and revolutionary. "The tale the mock turtle tells in _Alice in Wonderland_ is obscure and incomplete, so we are unsure whether certain fossils are mock ancestors or true ancestors," noted one commentator (Fraser, 1991:278). But in their more comprehensive 1995 study, Laurin and Reisz reaffirmed their position on the basis of a large-scale cladistic analysis of amniote interrelationships that robustly supported a procolophonid–turtle relationship within the monophyletic Parareptilia. Among the many features—mostly subtle characteristics of skull structure—that the authors found turtles to share with _Owenetta_ , as also with other procolophonids, was the funnel-shaped configuration of the bone located at the back end of the skull (the quadrate) that supports the tympanic membrane, and from which the lower jaw is suspended. Laurin and Reisz grouped the procolophonids and turtles together under the name "testudinomorphs," finding the pareiasaurs to be the sister group of the latter clade within the parareptiles, again robustly supported by cladistic analysis.

The hypothesis of a procolophonid–turtle sister group relationship would not stand unchallenged for long, however. Michael S. Y. Lee, then a graduate student at the University of Cambridge, in 1993 published a paper in _Science_ titled "The Origin of the Turtle Body Plan: Bridging a Famous Morphological Gap." The gap invoked here refers to the radically different body plan of a turtle, most distinctly characterized by the shell, which separates the turtles from other reptiles with a more generalized tetrapod appearance. Lee argued that turtles are the sister group to the pareiasaurs (Pareiasauridae), not the procolophonids, both nested within the parareptiles. With his hypothesis, Lee revived, on the basis of a comprehensive cladistic analysis, a hypothesis that had first been issued by Gregory in a classic 1946 paper. William K. Gregory (1876–1970) was a graduate student of Osborn's at the AMNH and Columbia University in New York, two institutions at which Gregory would himself pursue a highly successful career as curator and professor, respectively (Colbert, 1975). It was Gregory who arranged the posthumous publication of Williston's landmark _Osteology of the Reptiles_ by Harvard University Press in 1925. He would later go on to publish his own books, one of his most famous being _Evolution Emerging—A Survey of Changing Patterns from Primeval Life to Man_ that appeared in 1951. His work on the evolution of humans led him to publish on the infamous fossil hoax, the Piltdown Man, allegedly collected in the Weald of Sussex, England. The "fossil" remains were described in 1913 and named _Eoanthropus dawsoni_ by Arthur Smith Woodward (1864–1944), in honor of its supposed discoverer, Charles Dawson (Smith Woodward, 1913:137). Woodward, an expert on fossil fishes at the British Museum (Natural History), did not recognize the fossil as a fake. In contrast, Gregory in 1916 confirmed other critics' opinion that at least the mandible is that of a modern chimpanzee, not that of an ancestral fossil human. But in 1946, his interest focused not on the origin of humans but of turtles.

**2.6.** Reconstruction of armored placodont from Reptile Valley in Negev Desert, Middle Triassic, Israel.

_Artwork by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

Before arguing his case that pareiasaurs should be considered the ancestors of turtles, Gregory commented on alternative theories that had been proposed earlier. He opened his review of turtle origins with reference to two authors who had derived turtles from sauropterygians—more precisely, from placodonts. The latter are a group of marine reptiles of diapsid affinities, which first appeared in the fossil record some 245 million years ago in marine Triassic deposits near Winterswijk in the Netherlands (Neenan, Klein, and Scheyer, 2013). The group, known from central European, circum-Mediterranean, and Chinese localities, persisted into the latest Triassic in the Southern Alps. As indicated by their name, the placodonts' trademark is the platelike crushing teeth in the upper and lower jaws, which they used in pursuit of hard-shelled invertebrate prey such as bivalves, cephalopods, and crustaceans. The greatest diversity and evolutionary longevity within the group was achieved by the armored placodonts, which developed a complex protective armor composed of dermal ossifications, known as osteoderms (bony plates that ossify directly in the deep layer of the skin, the dermis). All armored placodonts developed a bony shield covering the back of the animal known as a carapace; some added a tail shield that covered the pelvic region and part of the tail; others still added belly armor, known as a plastron.

Superficially, the body armor of some of these placodonts strikingly resembles that of turtles. But German paleontologist Otto Jaekel (1863–1929) thought he had found, in addition, a great many resemblances in the skull of turtles and that of an armored placodont he was describing, and he consequently concluded that turtles are descended from armored placodonts. Jaekel was a graduate of the University of Breslau; after stints at the universities of Munich, Strasbourg, and Berlin, in 1906, he was named professor of geology and paleontology at the University of Greifswald. His major claim to fame was his excavation of the Late Triassic dinosaur _Plateosaurus_ from the Halberstadt locality near Berlin. It was during these excavations, which lasted from 1909 through 1911, that Jaekel also found new specimens of the extremely rare _Proganochelys_ , then the oldest known fossil turtle. In the aftermath of these activities, Jaekel was in 1927 accused of all sort of misdeeds in the _Völkischer Beobachter_ , the main publicist outlet of Hitler's National Socialist German Worker's Party (NSDAP). He had allegedly flown the Communist red flag from his window while pursuing fieldwork in Halberstadt, and he sold dinosaur bones for personal profit. Jaekel thereupon wrote a stern letter to "Sehr geehrter Herr Hitler!" as well as to the editor of the journal, threatening legal action if these allegations were not withdrawn: "Unless you publish in your journal a full rectification, I shall find myself forced to hand over the entire affair to the state attorney." It was still possible in 1927 to address Hitler in such a tone. Jaekel eventually prevailed and had the informer as well as the managing editor of the piece incriminating him convicted of libel.3

**2.7.** Skull of cyamodontoid placodont _Placochelys placodonta_ from Late Triassic of Bakony Mountains, Hungary, identified by Otto Jaekel in 1902a as turtle ancestor.

_Drawing by author_.

Long before he got embroiled in such disputes, Jaekel published in 1907 a beautiful monograph of a newly discovered armored placodont, one he named _Placochelys placodonta_ (Jaekel, 1902a, 1907). The specimen came from the Late Triassic (Carnian, some 230 million years old) of the Bakony Mountains overlooking Lake Balaton, collected at a locality near the city of Veszprém in what was then Habsburg Austria-Hungary. In a preliminary note (1902a), he had already announced the find, named the species, and claimed it to be an ancestor of turtles. The hypothesis that this placodont is close to the ancestry of turtles is what gave the species its name, _Placochelys placodonta_ , which translates to "turtlelike placodont with crushing teeth." When he later described the fossil turtles he had himself collected in the Late Triassic deposits of Halberstadt, Jaekel radically changed his views about turtle relationships; the fossils, he stated, "clearly prove that turtles have nothing to do with reptiles, but instead are close to the Paratheria, and hence close to the origin of mammals" (1918:213ff.).

In his memoir _On the Classification of the Reptiles_ commemorating Williston, written in 1919 but published in 1924, Broom had voiced a similar opinion (independent of Jaekel, as Gregory concluded), namely that turtles are to be derived from placodonts. According to Broom, "At first sight, the skull [of placodonts] looks very unlike that of a chelonian, but, on a more careful examination, there are seen to be some striking similarities" (Broom, 1924:53). However, in reviewing these earlier propositions, Gregory found such striking similarities to lie elsewhere: "When I turned my attention to the pareiasaurs... they seemed on the whole to afford an excellent starting point for the chelonian line, both in their general construction and in many features of the skull, vertebrae, ribs, girdles, limb bones, hands and feet" (1946:282).

In 1996, Michael S. Y. Lee—now at the University of Sydney—further elaborated on the idea of a pareiasaur–turtle relationship, this time in _Nature_. But in this paper, Lee went much further in his expositions on turtle origins than Gregory had done, or than he himself had done in his 1993 paper. No longer content to merely state relative degrees of phylogenetic relationships as they are recovered through cladistic analysis and represented by a bifurcating cladogram, he now developed what is known as an evolutionary scenario. Using the cladogram as a scaffold, he hung on it a scenario as to how an early, primitive (anapsid) stem-reptile could have evolved and transformed in such a way as to become a pareiasaur first, then a turtle through further evolutionary transformation. "Turtles exhibit some of the most extreme postcranial modifications in vertebrates," Lee wrote. "Our understanding of chelonian origins has been restricted by a paucity of information on intermediate forms" (1996:812). What this says is that a wide anatomical gap separates turtles from ordinary tetrapods such as lizards, and that in the absence of intermediate forms, it is difficult to imagine how evolutionary transformation resulted in such an aberrant body plan as the turtle's. In his reappraisal of turtle origins, however, Lee set out to "show that certain pareiasaurs—dwarf, heavily armored forms such a [ _sic_ ] _Nanoparia_ —approach the chelonian morphology [i.e., anatomy] even more closely than previously thought" (1996:812; see also Lee, 1997). However, to go from a stem-reptile to a large pareiasaur, and from there to a dwarf, heavily armored pareiasaur, and from there to turtles in terms of a continuous series of evolutionary transformations, landed Lee in a conundrum: are the pareiasaurs (i.e., members of the family Pareiasauridae) as a whole the sister group to turtles, or is a dwarf, armored pareiasaur species the sister group to turtles? In the latter case, turtles would not be the sister group of pareiasaurs but instead would be nested within the pareiasaur tree. With respect to the latter hypothesis, Lee recognized "conflicting evidence suggesting that pareiasaurs are the monophyletic sister group of turtles" (1996:812). Lee thus had two conflicting hypotheses. Cladistic analysis showed turtles to be the sister group of pareiasaurs as a whole. His evolutionary scenario placed turtles inside the pareiasaurs as a descendant of a specialized, dwarfed, heavily armored pareiasaur species. This is a typical case of conflict between cladistic thinking and classic evolutionary thinking. Cladistic thinking moves in terms of sister group relationships, whereas evolutionary thinking moves in terms of ancestors and descendants. The "bridging [of] a famous morphological gap" that separates turtles from other tetrapods through a series of intermediate forms that stand in an ancestor–descendant relationship to one another stands in direct conflict with cladistic methodology that sorts organisms into a nested hierarchy of sister groups (Lee, 1993:1716).

In bridging the gap between cladistic thinking and evolutionary thinking, Lee adopted the classical, i.e., Darwinian, model of evolutionary change as a process of gradual, steady evolutionary transformation in small, successive steps, as is implied by the twin mechanisms identified by Darwin. Variation, in ever so slight degrees, and natural selection must result in an evolutionary transformation of organisms that occurs in small steps, one after the other, such that all intermediate conditions of form must be realized through time. There cannot be, under this transformationist paradigm, any abrupt, saltational evolutionary change that leaves a morphological gap between the ancestral and decendant conditions of form. A second, theoretical conception of evolutionary change adopted by Lee was that of a correlated progression in evolutionary transformation. This is "the idea that evolutionary changes in one feature of the organism allow changes to take place in some other traits, changes which in turn influence the further evolution of the original feature. These feedbacks and interactions mean that the different organs appear to coevolve with each other" (Lee, 1996:814).

The concept of correlated progression threatens to place an enormous metaphysical burden on evolutionary scenarios, as will be discussed in more detail later, in the context of a historical review of theories of saltational evolution. Suffice it to say at this juncture that the concept of correlated progression could be interpreted to imply progression toward something—toward some sort of goal, such as, for example, the turtle body plan. Could there be a goal directedness in the evolution of the turtle shell? If there is, what would be the mechanism of such directed evolutionary transformation? What would be a natural mechanism that would guide a whole series of successive steps of evolutionary transformation unerringly toward a life in a shell?4 For Darwin himself, the evolutionary process was progressive. He wrote: "And as natural selection works solely by and for the good of each being, all corporal and mental endowments will tend to progress toward perfection" (1859:489). But modern Darwinian theory, with its mathematical models explaining the working of random variation and natural election, forcefully denies any possibility of a goal directedness of evolutionary processes. Second, the concept of correlated progression requires the coevolution of organs or organ complexes, the latter evolving in tandem in a process of evolutionary transformation that is guided by some hypothetical system of feedback loops. But what exactly are the developmental (ontogenetic) or evolutionary (phylogenetic) mechanisms that constitute these feedback loops? How would the development and evolution of the turtle shell guide the development and evolution of the limbs and their girdles, and vice versa? How would the development and evolution of a rigid turtle shell trigger correlated changes in the development and evolution of new mechanisms of respiration, and vice versa? Those are some of the questions that will have to be addressed in a discussion of the evolution of the turtle shell. This will also be the context in which to return to a discussion of Lee's scenario of correlated progression in the evolution of turtles.

Let us return to the original project in this chapter, which is to achieve an understanding of reptile classification that would hint at the turtles' place in the reptile tree of life. In the mid-1990s, two competing hypotheses derived turtles from two different groups of Paleozoic parareptiles: procolophonids versus pareiasaurs. None of the analyses that had generated these results included any reptiles, fossil or living, from times beyond the Paleozoic, other than turtles, of course. If diapsids were included at all, as in the Laurin and Reisz analysis, they were very basal forms from the Late Paleozoic, some 310 to 250 million years old. The decision to restrict the analysis of turtle relationships to a comparison with reptiles from the Paleozoic only was rooted in the firm conviction that turtles are living fossils, an ancient branch of reptile evolution whose origin reaches all the way back to the earliest stem-reptiles. The same conviction was also implicit in Gaffney's proposal, which linked turtles with Paleozoic captorhinomorphs. Goodrich's call for caution and circumspection had faded over time.

This is where the study that I conducted with Michael deBraga fundamentally differed: we included in our analysis of turtle relationships a host of Paleozoic synapsids as well as parareptiles, plus eureptiles ranging from the Paleozoic captorhinomorphs all the way up to living forms. Those newly added groups of reptiles from the Mesozoic to modern times created a pull that shifted turtle origins from the Paleozoic into the Triassic, which made a lot of sense. The robust bony remains of turtles, especially the shell, should stand a good chance of fossilization, at least in terms of bits and pieces. Given that the first undisputed fossil turtles come from the Late Triassic, the origin of turtles from Paleozoic procolophonids or pareiasaurs would leave the turtle lineage undocumented in the fossil record for maximally 80 million years and minimally 40 million years—a ghost lineage of remarkable duration. In our analysis, turtles came out as the sister group of sauropterygians, which themselves make their first appearance in the fossil record in the late Early Triassic. This significantly reduces the ghost lineage for turtles. The ghost lineage for turtles might be even more reduced if a controversial fragmentary fossil some 240 million years old, from the Middle Triassic Muschelkalk of Germany, was indeed a turtle.

This fragment of a carapace was collected in 1969 in an abandoned quarry near Hegnabrunn in Bavaria. It was preliminarily identified as a turtle by the curator of fossil reptiles at the State Museum of Natural History in Stuttgart, Rupert Wild, and formally described by its collector, Hans-Volker Karl, under the name _Priscochelys hegnabrunnensis_ in 2005. Because the German Muschelkalk also harbors placodonts, including armored placodonts of the genus _Cyamodus_ , a controversy soon emerged whether the carapace fragment called _Priscochelys_ indeed represents a turtle (it would have been the oldest one then known) or whether it instead represents the remains of an armored placodont. One expert opinion, based on anatomical considerations, confirmed the chelonian nature of the fossil; another expert opinion, based on microstructural analysis of the bone, identified the carapace fragment as one of a cyamodontoid placodont (Joyce and Karl, 2006; Scheyer, 2008). The (surprisingly) rare carapace fragments from the Muschelkalk of Germany that have been unequivocally assigned to cyamodontoid placodonts look different from _Priscochelys_ , however, rendering the latter's chelonian nature more plausible. At the end of the day, the specimen is too incomplete to significantly contribute to the discussions of turtle origins; its importance lies more in fixing the time of the first appearance of turtles in the fossil record, if indeed it does.

Be that as it may, the debate surrounding this carapace fragment shows how similar the body armor in turtles and cyamodontoid plac-odonts can be perceived—an issue that will have to be revisited in the discussion of the evolution of the turtle shell. In our analysis, however, turtles did not come out as the sister group to placodonts, or cyamodontoid placodonts, as had been argued by Jaekel and Broom, but as the sister group of Sauropterygia as a whole. This result implies an independent, i.e., convergent, evolution of body armor in turtles and placodonts. Turtles and sauropterygians thus together form an evolutionary lineage, or clade, that in our analysis is linked to the lepidosauromorph branch of reptiles, one that also includes the tuatara, the squamates (lizards, amphisbaenians, and snakes), and their more immediate fossil relatives. Such a position of turtles on the reptile tree implies, however, that turtles are diapsid reptiles—much more modern in their origins than if they had been descended from Paleozoic parareptiles. That result also implies that the complete dermatocranial roofing of the temporal region of the skull is secondary in those turtles where it occurs, mimicking the ancestral condition of reptiles rather than representing it, just as Goodrich had argued.

The publication of our results triggered a flurry of investigations into turtle relationships, based both on anatomical comparison and molecular, mostly DNA, data. DNA can, of course, not be retrieved from Mesozoic fossils—at least not outside Hollywood; molecular studies had to restrict the analysis of turtle relationships to living forms. Here again we get a classic three-taxon statement concerning the turtle, the lizard, and the crocodile, although most studies threw in a bird as well.
