If the turtle is not a diapsid reptile but has to be derived from Paleozoic stem-reptiles, then the DNA data should show the crocodiles (and birds, if included) to be more closely related to lizards than either of those is to turtles. If, on the other hand, the DNA data show the turtles to be more closely related to lizards than to crocodiles, our result based on anatomical characteristics would have been confirmed by molecular data. What the great majority of the molecular studies showed instead is that turtles are more closely related to crocodiles (and birds, if included) than to lizards. In contrast to our own studies, most molecular analyses thus link turtles to the archosauromorph branch of reptile evolution, although still within diapsids.

Since the late 1990s, the controversial relationships of turtles have spawned a large body of literature that needs not be reviewed here. Suffice it to say that investigations based on a comparative anatomical approach strongly, but not exclusively, favor a lepidosauromorph relationship of turtles within diapsids, rendering the tuatara, lizards, and snakes the closest relatives of turtles among living reptiles. Molecular studies overwhelmingly, but not exclusively, support an archosauromorph relationship of turtles within diapsids reptiles, rendering the crocodiles and birds the closest relatives of turtles among living reptiles. Most recently, molecular analyses of turtle relationships have been expanded to a genomic scale, with masses of data overwhelmingly supporting archosauromorph relations of turtles (Crawford et al., 2012; Wang et al., 2013). This is not to say that such a conclusion has been generally accepted today. There is an obvious discrepancy in the signals concerning turtle relationships generated by comparative anatomical versus molecular studies—a discrepancy that still has no satisfactory explication today. There is a general tendency among contemporary systematists to proclaim the supremacy of the molecular data over anatomical ones, and thus to anchor the truth about the phylogenetic relationships of turtles in their genetic code. Rather than seeking a truth that may not be at arm's length of science, however, it would seem more interesting to investigate why it is that comparative anatomical, as opposed to genomic, approaches yield such disparate results concerning turtle relationships with lepidosaurs or archosaurs, respectively. Unfortunately, that conversation is largely eschewed in favor of arguments about the superiority of one method over another. In addition, there are still some paleontologists who argue that turtles are in fact not diapsids but rather must be derived from some Paleozoic stem-reptile, procolophonian, pareiasaurian, or otherwise.

Language Games

Many people think that science is all about observation and measurement. Indeed, figuring out the evolutionary relationships between organisms starts with observation in some form or other—direct observation in the case of comparative anatomical studies, indirect observation in the case of the alignment of strands of DNA in search of a phylogenetic signal. What is perhaps less well understood is the fact that science is not just about observation but is also about the way scientists talk about their observations. There is a reality of facts based on tangible objects, but there is also a reality that emerges from dialogue, as every lawyer or diplomat well knows. Popper famously argued that there is no such thing as raw, theory-free observation. Another philosopher, Norwood R. Hanson (1958), put that insight to work in an enlightening example. Imagine a patient sitting across the doctor's desk. The day before, an X-ray tube had expired, which the doctor had put on his desk so he would not forget to order a replacement. Utterly ignorant of X-ray technology, the patient looks at this strange object on the doctor's desk, wondering what it is. Hanson argued that at this moment, the doctor and the patient both see the same thing, yet they see the same thing differently. Given his background knowledge, the doctor sees the expired X-ray tube for what it is, such that he might worry about the cost of replacement. The patient, in contrast, sees the object as something in need of an explanation. It is only after having heard and understood the doctor's explanation that both doctor and patient see the same thing: see the expired X-ray tube for what it is. To merely see an object is thus not the same as to see an object while understanding what it actually is.

Popper was certainly right when he claimed that every one of us, scientists included, approaches the world around us in light of previous experiences, insights, hopes, and fears—in short, in light of our background knowledge. Every observation reported by scientists is thus prone to be tainted by background knowledge in their field, i.e., by beliefs and expectations that derive from theories that scientists hope to confirm or refute. As a consequence of this insight, one that today is generally accepted among philosophers of science, Popper compared the scientific community to a jury in court. It is this jury, i.e., the community of scientists that shares common research interests, that decides whether any given report on observations should be accepted or rejected. But that puts scientists in a position of having to advocate for their observational reports in as convincing a manner as possible, just as lawyers try to argue as convincingly as possible in defense of their clients. If that is the case, then the reality as pictured by science is constituted not just and only by the observable furniture of the world, but also, in some part at least, by language and the way it is used by scientists in the description of the furniture of the world. The concept of language games was developed by Austrian philosopher Ludwig Wittgenstein (1889–1951), likely the most influential philosopher of the twentieth century. The concept is concerned with the way in which the words that comprise language acquire meaning through their use in relation to actions rather than through precise definitions. Another philosopher, Nelson Goodman (1906–1998), spoke of the entrenchment that secures the meaning of predicates through language use. Again, predicates such as "blue" or "green" acquire their meaning through repeated use with reference to objects of the respective color, not through precise definition. Such philosophical perspectives would seem to interfere with the objective of scientists who seek to provide as precise a definition as possible for the terms used to report on scientific findings.

It is a fact rooted in observation that most land-dwelling animals with a backbone share four limbs that come in two pairs. (Of course, there are exceptions, such as limbless salamanders and snakes.) It is not an observation but a conjecture, albeit one that can be substantiated with reference to fossils, that land-dwelling vertebrates inherited four limbs that come in two pairs from a common ancestor. By virtue of them generally having four limbs, the land-dwelling vertebrates are called tetrapods (Tetrapoda). Conversely, the tetrapod limbs have been recognized as a key character of Tetrapoda, hinting at a common evolutionary origin for all tetrapods. The conclusion is that the tetrapod limbs originated only once, namely in the most recent common ancestor of all tetrapods. The limbs of tetrapods can serve vastly different functions, and hence they can differ profoundly in shape, such as the wings of birds and the forelimbs of horses, bats, and moles, but the bird wing can easily be recognized as an evolutionary transformation and specialization of the primitive tetrapod limb, and the same is true for the forelimb of a horse, bat, or mole. The bird wing is the same but different as the forelimb of a mole: birds modified the tetrapod limb in an adaptation to flight, whereas moles modified the tetrapod limb in an adaptation to burrowing. This example illuminates the fundamental scheme that underlies all efforts to decipher evolutionary relationships on the basis of comparative anatomy. First there is the recognition that all tetrapods—some exceptions not withstanding—share four limbs that come in two pairs, anterior and posterior. This correspondence reveals the limbs to be homologous throughout all tetrapods. Homology here means a structural correspondence that is revealed by anatomical comparison, which is explained by a common evolutionary origin. Of limbless tetrapods such as snakes, we consequently do not say that they have no limbs; instead, we say that they have lost limbs (Platnick, 1978). Second, calling limbs homologous throughout Tetrapoda carries an important evolutionary connotation: homologues are corresponding parts of two or more organisms that enter into a relation called homology, and homology is defined as a structural correspondence due to common ancestry (Brigandt, 2002). If the limbs are said to be homologous throughout tetrapods, the evolutionary implication is that tetrapods share a common evolutionary origin, i.e., they share a common ancestor from which they inherited the developmental pathway that, if it plays out to completion, results in four limbs that come in two pairs.

Homologues are namesakes. Homologous parts in two or more organisms are called by the same name. The heart is homologous throughout vertebrates; it is the same but different in different species of vertebrates. The heart of a fish has two chambers and the heart of a mammal has four chambers, but is still called by the same name in both. Names, however, are difficult to deal with—so much so that careful unpacking of that issue is necessary. The man who introduced the term "homologue" was Sir Richard Owen (1804–1892), at the time Hunterian Professor at the Royal College of Surgeons in London. In that capacity, he published in 1843 his _Lectures on the Comparative Anatomy and Physiology of the Invertebrate Animals_ , a book to which he appended a glossary in which he introduced the term "homologue," from "(Gr[eek] _homos; logos_ , speech)," defining it as "the same organ in different animals under every variety of form and function" (1843:379). Writing in 1843, he did not link homology to common ancestry but only to structural correspondence. The link between homology and evolution was left for Darwin to discover. Lecturing on the anatomy of vertebrate animals later in life, a time when he served as superintendent of the natural history section of the British Museum, Owen modified his definition: "A 'homologue' is a part or organ in one organism so answering to that in another as to require the same name" (1866:xii). The "skull" is such a name, as are the "tetrapod limb" or "heart." We freely use the name "skull" to pick out the anterior part of the vertebrate axial skeleton, the bony structure that sits on the anterior end of the vertebral column, that encapsulates the brain and major sense organs, and that carries the jaws in both salmon and mammal, even though the skull in salmon and mammals differs widely in shape and function. The skull is the same in salmon and mammals because of its common evolutionary origin, but it is different in shape and function because it had a different evolutionary history in salmon and mammals.

The skull has played an important part in reptile classification: emargination (in turtles) versus fenestration (in diapsids) of the cheek region of the dermatocranium, the formation or loss of an upper and lower temporal arch, and so on. "Dermatocranium," "upper temporal arch"—these are names picking out homologues, the relevance of which lies in the fact that today, in a world after Darwin, we define homology as structural correspondence due to common ancestry. Homologues are namesakes, i.e., called by the same name. The temporal region of the dermatocranium is the same in all reptiles but different because it was affected by different modifications in the course of the evolutionary history of different lineages of reptile phylogeny. Fenestration, the formation of temporal fenestrae, characterizes diapsid reptiles, whereas the cheek region in turtles is subject to emargination. The upper temporal arch, forming as a consequence of fenestration, is homologous in crocodiles, dinosaurs, the tuatara, and those lizards that retain it. What looks like an upper temporal arch in some turtles with a deeply emarginated cheek region is not a true temporal arch because it results from a different developmental and evolutionary process. The term "upper temporal arch" thus cannot be used as a label in the description of (some) turtle skulls. Conversely, if the name were used for (some) turtles, it would also carry certain evolutionary, i.e., phylogenetic, implications, namely that turtles and diapsid reptiles derive from a common ancestor from which they inherited the upper temporal arch. However, Goodrich did not ascribe an upper temporal arch to turtles when proposing them to be related to diapsids; instead, one of the characters he based his argument on concerned a bone in the foot, the fifth metatarsal, the bone that in humans supports the pinky toe.

The crux of all classification, to the extent that it should represent the evolutionary (phylogenetic) relationships among the organisms classified, is to identify the right homologies—that is, to identify those structural correspondences between two or more organisms that provide a key to their evolutionary past, which are indicative of their common ancestry. When Goodrich asserted that the peculiarly bent, indeed hooked, structure of the enlarged fifth metatarsal, as observed in turtles, crocodiles, the tuatara, and lizards, is suggestive of a homology of the hooked fifth metatarsal in diapsid reptiles and turtles, he was in effect saying that the hooked fifth metatarsal is of common evolutionary origin in diapsid reptiles and turtles. In other words, diapsids and turtles share a common ancestor from which they inherited the hooked fifth metatarsal. If diapsid reptiles are to be a monophyletic, i.e., a natural group that includes its most recent common ancestor and all, and only, its descendants, then on the basis of that argument, turtles must be classified with diapsid reptiles, not with anapsid stem-reptiles. It is the same but different: the same name (hooked fifth metatarsal) applies to the homologous structures in the foot of turtles and diapsid reptiles, even though the details of articulation in the foot may be different in crocodiles as opposed to lizards or turtles.

A name functions like a tag; it picks out things of the outer world and signifies those things in language, including scientific discourse. However, it is one thing to pick out and tag an organism through baptism, thus bestowing a proper name on that organism, such as Bill Clinton. No ambiguities obtain here. Bill Clinton did in fact become the 42nd president of the United States, but it could have been possible (at least, and however unlikely, it would not have been in principle impossible) for Bill Clinton to become a saxophone player with the Rolling Stones. No matter; the name "Bill Clinton" always picks out the same individual, whatever that individual is doing (again, the notion of the same but different). This is how proper names function, although it is a function that requires the persistence of the self-identity of the individual named from the time the egg cell is fertilized until the time of death. It is quite clearly another thing, however, to claim that the name "hooked fifth metatarsal" picks out the same thing in the foot of a crocodile, a lizard, and a turtle, especially if these same things may differ in detail as a consequence of their evolutionary history. For many authors, the "same but different" that characterizes homologues implies identity of these homologues—in our example, the identity of the hooked fifth metatarsal in turtles, lizards, and crocs, an identity that is rooted in the unique evolutionary origin of the hooked fifth metatarsal in the most recent ancestor of turtles, crocodiles, and lizards.

A prerequisite for all classifications that are meant to represent evolutionary (phylogenetic) relationships is certainly the correct identification of homologies. That depends on the one hand on a comparative method that allows us to pinpoint corresponding parts, or organs, in two or more organisms that could be homologous. Beyond that, however, homology implies common ancestry, and in the absence of a time machine, the latter implication cannot be confirmed through observation. The relation of homology therefore does not obtain from raw observation alone. Instead, while ultimately rooted in a comparative analysis of anatomy, it nevertheless remains to a large degree a hypothetical relation. That opens up space—philosopher Willard Quine (1908–2000) called it "empirical slack" (1951:179)—for different applications of names as we talk about homologues and the implied relation of common ancestry. Philosopher of biology John Griffiths used the concept of entrenchment (in Nelson Goodman's sense) in relation to the terms that pick out homologues (Griffiths, 2007:653). The meaning of the terms "skull," heart," and "tetrapod limbs," as homologues of vertebrates and tetrapods, respectively, is fixed by the repeated, successful deployment of these terms in the search for evolutionary relationships. It would thus seem to be possible for an evolutionary biologists to break out of such entrenchment by dropping an entrenched term and introducing a new, different one.

If we say, "Turtles, crocodiles, and lizards share a hooked fifth metatarsal," homology is implied, and with it the hypothesis that they all share a common ancestor from which they inherited the hooked fifth metatarsal. However, if we say, "Turtles have a turtle-type hooked fifth metatarsal, crocodiles have a crocodile-type hooked fifth metatarsal, and lizards have a lizard-type hooked fifth metatarsal," no homology, and hence no common ancestry of turtles, crocodiles, and lizards, is implied. But we can go even further by dropping a name and replacing it with a different one. That way, identities of homologues are first withdrawn when a name is dropped; then new identities of alternative homologues are established when a new name is introduced.

The cladistic analyses of turtle relationships sketched above made use of an ever-increasing number of characters in search for homologies. A quantum leap in the number of characters used occurred when cladistic analysis shifted from the comparative anatomical approach to the use of DNA data. Another quantum leap occurred when the analyses shifted from genetic to genomic data, where it is not just select strands of DNA but rather the complete DNA within a single cell of an organism that is used in phylogeny reconstruction. Other than in extremely exceptional cases, a paleontologist has nothing but fossilized bones to compare, often incompletely preserved ones. In terms of numbers of characters used, fossils can certainly provide no match to counter molecular, especially genomic, analyses of turtle relationships. What to do, then, if you are a paleontologist whose background intuition it is that Paleozoic stem-reptiles may be a far better match for turtle ancestry than any of the diapsid reptile lineages, and who hence tends to disagree with the results of molecular analyses that place turtles with crocodiles on the archosauromorph branch of the reptile tree? How could paleontologists set about to test such placement of turtles on the basis of DNA data? The "hooked fifth metatarsal" is an entrenched name for a structure considered by Goodrich as homologous in turtles and diapsid reptiles, and as such, it carries an important evolutionary connotation. But that connotation, i.e., a common evolutionary origin of turtles and diapsid reptiles, is exactly the one that is to be subjected to a test. One strategy would seem to be to break out of such entrenchment by changing the language, and with it the names of homologues embedded in it. In an attempt to avoid preconceived (entrenched) theoretical commitments, the removal of the name tag from the structure under discussion removes its putative identity, whereas the introduction of new names that imply different evolutionary histories, and hence different phylogenetic relationships, establishes new identities of different homologues. This is what happened in the discussion of the hooked fifth metatarsal of turtles and diapsids. Critics of the diapsid affinities of turtles removed the name and with it the identity of the structure under consideration, disassembled the structure into constituent parts, and put those back together again differently in turtles and diapsids.

When Goodrich (1916) pondered the possible homology of the hooked fifth metatarsal in turtles and diapsid reptiles, he referred back to earlier authors who, like him, had attempted to disentangle the evolutionary history of that curious bone. In particular, the hooked fifth metatarsal (call it mt5) appears distinctly enlarged, encroaching into space that in other reptiles is occupied by a neighboring element, the fifth distal tarsal (call it x), a small bone that sits at the base of the fifth digit (toe). Goodrich then proceeded to list all possible alternative interpretations of the hooked fifth metatarsal: (1) mt5 is just mt5 but has expanded into the territory primitively occupied by x, and x has been lost in lizards, crocs, and turtles; (2) mt5 has been lost in lizards, crocs, and turtles, and x has expanded to mimic mt5; and (3) mt5 has fused with x in lizards, crocs, and turtles. Goodrich settled on the first alternative on the basis of "Sewertzoff's excellent account" of tetrapod limb development (1916:264). There is an important distinction here to be drawn, however, as was indeed highlighted in Sewertzoff's excellent account. Historically there have been two fundamentally different ways to look at and understand animal development in general: atomistic and epigenetic. The atomistic point of view sees animal development as a coming together of originally separate parts. Just as atoms come together to form molecules, so would parts come together to form an organism during embryonic development. On an atomistic account, the third alternative above would imply that mt5 has fused with x in the course of evolution of the lizard, croc, and turtle foot: two originally separate elements would have fused to form one. The epigenetic perspective, in contrast, sees animal development as the development of one part from and out of the other. In other words, one part is giving rise to one or more other parts through a process of growth and division. On that account, blood vessels do not join the heart during development but instead grow out from the heart. In his account of the embryonic development of the foot in a gecko lizard, Sewertzoff had found that, in contrast to the fourth metatarsal at the base of the fourth digit, the metatarsal supporting the fifth digit never gives rise to a separate element x (Sewertzoff, 1908:130). He thus interpreted the development of the gecko foot from an epigenetic perspective. Indeed, modern analyses of limb development have shown that most elements in the hands and feet of tetrapods—with the notable exception of the elements in the fifth digit—develop from one another in a process that involves growth, bifurcation, and subdivision of cartilaginous rudiments (Shubin and Alberch, 1986).

This contrasts with a recent account of the embryonic development of mt5 in turtles, which concludes that mt5 fuses with x during embryonic development. I will not discuss this study here in terms of its empirical merits; this is not the place to critically evaluate complex conjectures of homology. Rather, I will attempt to transcend the purely empirical base of comparative anatomy and embryology, instead aiming at the metalevel of a more philosophical discussion. In the discussion to follow, I am speaking not as a scientist looking through a microscope; instead, I am an observer talking about scientists who look through the microscope studying the development of mt5. The study under discussion here was coauthored by three collaborators, two of whom had already published earlier papers that argued against the diapsid relationships of turtles, instead deriving turtles from _Eunotosaurus_ , at the time still considered a parareptile (Lyson et al., 2010). The study of the embryonic development of mt5 in turtles thus proceeded on the background supposition that turtles are nested within Paleozoic anapsid stem-reptiles rather than within diapsids. The first step taken by the investigators was to remove the identity of the hooked fifth metatarsal in the various organisms where it occurs by withdrawing its name: "We herein use the term 'hooked element' when wishing to remain homology neutral" (Joyce, Werneburg, and Lyson, 2013:422). This is the step of breaking out of the entrenchment that ties turtles to diapsid reptiles on the basis of the hooked fifth metatarsal. The goal is to provide a theory-neutral perspective from which to proceed with the investigation. Philosophically speaking, such theory neutrality is not really achievable but rather is itself a theoretical claim: by withdrawing its entrenched name, the hooked fifth metatarsal loses its putative identity in turtles and diapsids. The authors then proceeded to investigate the embryology of this hooked element in turtles; they found it to be the result of a fusion of mt5 with element x. This observation clashed with earlier reports such as Goodrich's and Sewertzoff's, who found that element x is absent rather than fused with mt5 in diapsids. This is the basis on which a new name could be introduced, thus bestowing a different identity on the hooked element of turtles: "[We] suggest the new term 'ansulate bone'... when wishing to express that this bone is the result of fusion" (Joyce, Werneburg, and Lyson, 2013:422). Now there are two names in play: the "ansulate bone" for turtles and the "hooked element" for diapsids. Because the hooked element in diapsids is concluded to be of a different nature than the ansulate bone in turtles, it can retain the traditional name "hooked fifth metatarsal." The use of two different names for mt5 in turtles and diapsid reptiles implies nonhomology and removes the hypothesis of common ancestry. In that way, the use of names—in this case of the names of homologues—and their consequent entrenchment can have profound implications for scientific theory construction.

The above review of the history of investigations into turtle ancestry reveals, I hope, some of the difficulties that plague all attempts to decipher evolutionary, i.e., phylogenetic, relationships. The difficulties start with a disunity of comparative anatomy and molecular biology in phylogenetic analysis, where talks between the two camps break down. The comparative anatomists insist on the centuries-old success of their approach; the DNA people, in contrast, insist on the putative objectivity, and hence superiority, of their modern methods. Further, there are deep conceptual problems in how to comprehend animal development. Is it the case that parts come together to form the whole, or is it the case that the whole is formed by parts that develop from, and out of, one another? A different theoretical understanding of embryonic development can fundamentally influence the ensuing interpretation of observations. Finally, there is the use of language and the names of homologues embedded in it, which can shift the emphasis from one to another hypothesis of phylogenetic relationships. Then add to those difficulties the more fundamental debates about the virtues or flaws of various methods of analysis, which I have not addressed in this chapter. The reconstruction of evolutionary, i.e., phylogenetic, relationships is not an easy feat, which explains some of the ongoing debates that in one way or another affect most major parts of the tree of life. But amid all these discussions, the issue of turtle relationships stands out as particularly difficult and problematic. The reason is that turtles are indeed separated from all other reptiles, and in fact from all other tetrapods, by a wide anatomical (morphological) gap. Their anatomy is, in many ways, highly unique. This renders their comparison with other reptiles difficult and the assessment of homologies problematic, sometimes controversial. The discussion of how to bridge that gap—how to link turtles to other branches of the reptile tree—takes us to the questions concerning the evolutionary mechanisms that might have resulted in such a distinct anatomical gap. Should the turtle be seen as a hopeful monster? Or alternatively, if evolutionary mechanisms that would result in hopeful monsters do not exist, would the hope instead have to be to bridge the gap that separates turtles from other reptiles with as yet undiscovered fossils?

Turtles Climbing Up the Tree of Life

Opponents of the placement of turtles within diapsid reptiles have drawn attention to a number of features that contradict such a hypothesis. One of these has been the already discussed hooked fifth metatarsal. Another has been the cleithrum, a dermal bone in the shoulder girdle that was lost quite early in reptile evolution and consequently is absent in all diapsids. In a first approach, a process that rises up from the anterior lateral corner of the epiplastral plate in the stem-turtle _Kayentachelys aprix_ from the Early Jurassic of northern Arizona was homologized with the cleithrum: "The presence of cleithra in primitive turtles is a character that supports a basal placement of turtles within Reptilia, rather than a placement within crown Sauria, a clade that demonstrably lacks cleithra" (Joyce, Jenkins, and Rowe, 2006:101). The homology of the epiplastral process with the cleithrum had first been proposed by Otto Jaekel in his 1918 description of _Proganochelys_ from the Late Triassic of Halberstadt, Germany. In his monographic treatment of _Proganochelys_ , Gaffney scrutinized the issue and rejected a putative homology of the epiplastral process with the cleithrum. Jaekel had been misled, Gaffney concluded, because of the incompleteness of the material at his disposal (1990:131).

Indeed, the most recent claim that the epiplastral process of basal turtles is homologous with the cleithrum was dropped in favor of a new hypothesis, which stipulates a homology of the cleithrum with the nuchal bone of the carapace. "The homology of the nuchal with the cleithra bears upon the contentious issue of turtle relationships," as it places turtles once again outside the diapsid reptile radiation (Lyson et al., 2013:323). This interpretation of the nuchal plate as being derived from the left and right cleithrum was supported with reference to "transitional fossils," i.e., "the putative stem-turtle _Eunotosaurus africanus_ " (Lyson et al., 2013:321). But wait a moment: _Eunotosaurus_ —not a parareptile after all, but a stem-turtle instead?

When discussing Lee's (1993) large-scale cladistic analysis of turtle relationships, reference was made to the transformationist paradigm. Adherence to a transformationist paradigm is prevalent in paleontology, and it is beautifully illustrated by recent work on turtle origins and evolution. In the following chapters, the transformationist paradigm will be contrasted with the emergentist paradigm that is more prevalent in evolutionary developmental biology. The transformationist paradigm is firmly rooted in the neo-Darwinian theory of evolution, where organismal change is driven by random variation and natural selection. The result is a gradual and continuous process of transformation, in the course of which all intermediate stages of change must be realized for natural selection to work on. From a transformationist perspective, all the highly derived and specialized features of the turtle body plan must have gradually assembled along the turtle lineage during its evolution. It is at this point where a further commitment of the transformationist paradigm becomes apparent, which is to attribute an ontologically privileged status to fossils in the reconstruction of evolutionary relationships. That is to say, fossils are valued as the most important evidence in the search for evolutionary (phylogenetic) relationships. For any major evolutionary lineage, such as turtles, transformationists tend to distinguish a crown section of living taxa and a stem section accommodating the fossil taxa. The expectation is that the gradual, successive, and stepwise assembly of all the highly derived, specialized features of the turtle body plan can be demonstrated and documented by transitional fossils that line up along the stem section of the turtle tree—provided, of course, that the fossil record is complete enough to allow such a reconstruction of evolutionary history.

Recall the honorable D. M. S. Watson, who in 1914 had claimed that _Eunotosaurus_ is not itself a turtle but rather the ancestor of turtles. After an ancestral turtle from the early Late Triassic of southwestern China, _Odontochelys semitestacea_ , was first described _Eunotosaurus_ from the Permian of South Africa was coded into the data matrix that was used to research the phylogenetic relationships of _Odontochelys_ (Li et al., 2008). _Eunotosaurus_ , represented by newly discovered and better-preserved specimens than had previously been available, was thereby found to be the sister taxon to _Odontochelys_ and all other turtles (Lyson et al., 2010). But the result thus obtained provided no motivation to interpret _Eunotosaurus_ as a stem-turtle. Instead, given the then-accepted classification of _Eunotosaurus_ within the anapsid Paleozoic parareptiles, the result was taken to indicate that "turtles [nest] within 'parareptiles' as the extant sister to Diapsida" (Lyson et al., 2010:830). Significantly, _Eunotosaurus_ was described as preserving "a small, splint-like cleithrum," an element generally absent in diapsid reptiles (Lyson et al., 2013:321). Turtles accordingly are derived from Paleozoic stem-reptiles, not from diapsids. Such a conclusion continued to clash with the molecular findings, which consistently place turtles on the archosaur branch of the reptile tree (Lyson et al., 2012; Field et al., 2014). The conundrum is resolved if _Eunotosaurus_ is removed from the parareptiles and declared a stem-turtle instead. As a transitional fossil that nests at the very base of the turtle stem, illustrating the primitive morphology of the turtle lineage, _Eunotosaurus_ was used to shed new light on the evolution of the turtle shell (carapace) and the correlated changes in the respiratory system of turtles (Lyson et al., 2013, 2014).

The early evolution of the turtle lineage is believed to be further illuminated by another transitional fossil, " _Pappochelys_ , that is structurally and chronologically intermediate between _Eunotosaurus_ and _Odontochelys_ ," the latter the ancestral turtle from the early Upper Triassic of southwestern China (Schoch and Sues, 2015:584). _Pappochelys_ stems from the late Middle Triassic of Baden-Württemberg, Germany, and was described as sporting an upper as well as a ventrally open lower temporal fenestra. If correctly identified as a turtle, largely on the basis of broadened ribs with a T-shaped cross section that the fossil shares with _Eunotosaurus_ and _Odontochelys, Pappochelys_ seals a diapsid origin of turtles. These insights motivated a renewed investigation of the skull of _Eunotosaurus_ , which had previously been described as anapsid, i.e., without temporal fenestration (Bever et al., 2015). The skull of _Eunotosaurus_ had long been known to be characterized by a ventral embayment of the cheek region. But the application of modern imaging techniques to new fossils led to the discovery of an upper temporal fenestra in a juvenile skull of _Eunotosaurus_ , a finding that allowed the interpretation of the cheek region as displaying a lower temporal fenestra, with the lower temporal arch having been lost. In adult specimens of _Eunotosaurus_ , the upper temporal fossa is obliterated by an expansion of a neighboring element, the supratemporal. The upshot is that " _Eunotosaurus_ is a diapsid reptile in the process of becoming secondarily anapsid" (Bever et al., 2015:239). Such early obliteration of temporal fenestration in the turtle lineage would thus explain why early fossil turtles such as _Proganochelys_ possess an anapsid skull despite a diapsid origin of turtles. " _Eunotosaurus_ represents a crucially important link in a chain" of turtle evolution, with _Eunotosaurus_ thus being placed at the bottom of the stem section of the turtle tree, followed by _Pappochelys_ and _Odontochelys_ "along the turtle stem," and on to "more crown-ward turtles" (Bever et al., 2015:239, 242, 241).

This is a genuine transformationist perspective, and one that the cladistic revolution had sought to overcome. The cladistic revolution had replaced the search for ancestors and descendants by a search for sister group relationships. Within such a conceptual framework, fossils cannot claim ontological privilege over extant organisms in phylogeny reconstruction. This was one of the profound conceptual innovations that the cladistic revolution brought about. A famous episode relates to a presentation of his phylogenetic systematics by Willi Hennig at the Berlin Natural History Museum on October 30, 1950. Present at that seminar was professor of paleontology Walter Gross (1903–1974), who in the discussion section maintained that only fossils afford the proof of phylogenetic relationships. Hennig reportedly answered, "Your fossils are of no interest to me," whereupon Gross angrily exclaimed, "Then your theories are of no interest to me either," slamming the door as he left the room (Schmitt, 2001:327). What are we to make of such fossils as _Pappochelys_ and _Eunotosaurus_ presented in that transformationist perspective? Unquestionably, the new material of _Eunotosaurus_ recently described has taken us a long way from eminent American paleontologist Alfred Sherwood Romer's earlier remark that "the animal is... so far removed in so many characters from the true chelonians that herpetologists shudder at the thought of" chelonian relationships (1968:108). More recently, developmental biologist Ann C. Burke found the recognition of _Eunotosaurus_ as a stem-turtle to have "interesting implications for the diapsid root suggested by the genomic data mentioned above, and is likely to be a cause for lively debate" (2015:83).

Notes

2. Osborn to Hanfstaengl, October 18, 1934, Folder 5, Box 1, Henry Fairfield Osborn Papers, Archives of the American Museum of Natural History.

3. University Archives Greifswald, Acta des Kratoriums der Universität zu Greifswald betreffend Dr. Jaekel, Heft I. Anfang 1906, Ende 1936. Signatur PA 65. All translations throughout the volume unless otherwise indicated are my own.

4. _Life in a Shell_ is the title of Donald C. Jackson's 2011 book on turtle physiology.

**Levels of Evolution**

3

Running to Stand Still

CHARLES DARWIN WAS NOT THE FIRST TO OFFER A THEORY OF EVOlution. His grandfather, Erasmus Darwin (1731–1802), for example, had Stand Still famously proposed a theory of species transformation in a two-volume treatise published in 1794–1796. Another illustrious precursor of Darwin was French biologist and paleontologist Jean-Baptiste Lamarck (1744–1829), who studied living and fossil mollusks at the National Natural History Museum in Paris. His book on _Philosophie Zoologique_ , published in 1809, proposed an evolutionary theory that was hotly debated well into the first half of the twentieth century, with some discussion lingering on to the present day (Weiss, 2015). What Darwin had achieved was not just to promote the idea that new species originate from ancestral ones, but more importantly to have proposed evolutionary mechanisms that explained such descent with modification without taking recourse to any occult forces active in nature. His is indeed the evolutionary theory that stood the test of time and ultimately prevailed over Lamarck's, even though Darwin's own views were closer to Lamarck's than those of present-day Darwinists, who categorically—and correctly—reject the Lamarckist claim of the inheritance of acquired characteristics. There is, however, a recently growing and rather technical discussion of "environmentally induced gene imprinting" (Wagner, 2014:159; see also the discussion in West-Eberhard, 2003:chap. 1.5), genetic accommodation (West-Eberhard, 2005), or genetic assimilation (e.g., Pigliucci, Murren, and Schlichting, 2006). Both Lamarck and Darwin admitted the possibility that characters, which an organism acquires in the course of its lifetime though use or disuse of organs, could eventually become heritable and thus could be transmitted to offspring. The idea is easily refuted: generations of upper-class Chinese women had their feet bound during childhood and youth to keep them small, a custom pursued for centuries that never rendered small feet a heritable trait.

The remarkable thing about Darwin's achievement was that he had absolutely no theory of inheritance to back up the first sketch of his evolutionary theory, published in _On the Origin of Species_ in 1859. Only much later, in 1868—the _Origin_ was in its fourth edition by that time—did Darwin proffer a theory of inheritance when writing on _The Variation of Plants and Animals under Domestication_. Laws governing regular patterns of inheritance in pea plants discovered by Silesian friar and naturalist Gregor Mendel (1822–1884) had been published two years before, in 1866, in a backwater journal, for which reason they remained ignored and obscure for almost 40 years. Likewise, strange structures had been observed inside the cells of plants and animals, but as of 1866, there was not yet any talk of chromosomes, let alone their role as carriers of heritable traits. Darwin thus did what scientists generally do in such circumstances: he invoked theoretical, i.e., unobserved, particles that would fulfill the role of carriers of hereditary traits in his explanation of the observed phenomena of heritable variation. Darwin assessed the ubiquity of variation, like people having dark hair or blond hair, or blue eyes or brown eyes, and he observed that there was a certain degree of regularity to the patterns of variation, which he thought must be grounded in mechanisms of inheritance. But he and his contemporaries knew of no material basis that would explain how heritable traits are transmitted from parent to offspring. He thus invented it. This is not just old science gone wrong. The stipulation of theoretical, i.e., unobserved, entities is a common tactic during early stages of scientific theory construction. For a modern example, look at the monstrous mutant fruit fly, which grows a leg instead of an antenna out of its head. This is obviously a major change—a fundamental mutation, not just a minor, slight, Darwiniantype variation. How can such freaks of nature happen? How can they be explained? Today, such changes are explained through the recognition of a special class of genes, the so-called _Hox_ genes, an abbreviation for _Homeobox_ genes. The first steps in research that led to the discovery of _Hox_ genes date back to 1983, but the comprehension of their true nature and the way they work took time and effort. In the absence of better knowledge, a purely hypothetical entity was invoked, a so-called switch gene that would flip early developmental pathways from one trajectory into another, as from an antenna to a leg (Raff and Kaufman, 1983:272).

Darwin called the theoretical carriers of heritable traits that he invoked _gemmulae_ , picturing them as minute particles that come together in the formation of the developing embryo. Darwin understood plant and animal development as a coming together of parts rather than as a development of parts from one another. The _gemmulae_ provided the (hypothetical) material basis for Darwin's pangenesis theory. The latter was in turn built on the cell theory developed by botanist Matthias Jacob Schleiden (1804–1881) and zoologist Theodor Schwann (1810–1882). The cell theory states that all organisms are composed of fundamental, divisible building blocks, the cells. The cell theory was firmly established around the mid-nineteenth century and was greatly enhanced by eminent pioneer of cancer research Rudolf Virchow (1821–1902), who in 1858 issued a famous statement asserting that "all living cells originate from living cells." The cell was thus recognized as the basic building block of life, meaning that all organisms are composed of one or multiple cells. It is because viruses lack some fundamental components of the cellular machinery that they cannot be considered cellular structures, and hence for many do not represent true organisms. But even with the cell theory in place, Darwin thought that some fundamental aspects of life still lacked a unifying explanation: what is the source of variation so ubiquitously observed in all organisms, both in the wild and under domestication? How are maternal and paternal traits transmitted to offspring? How is it that variation can affect different organs or organ systems in different ways without affecting the harmonious function of the whole organism? And how does all of this tie in with the mechanisms of embryonic development?

The classical ideal of a great scientific explanation has been achieved once as many seemingly disparate observable phenomena as possible have successfully been linked to a common cause, which is exactly the program that Darwin pursued throughout his career. Darwin's _Origin_ of 1859 is a masterpiece in this respect, as it links observable facts of anatomy, embryology, physiology, ecology, biogeography, and animal behavior to a common cause: evolution. Given the particular questions he raised in his 1868 treatise on variation of animals and plants under domestication, he first stated the obvious: all of the observable phenomena in need of an explanation relate back to the fact that organisms, from the fertilized egg through to the fully grown adult, are composed of cells. So Darwin stipulated that the cells of all organisms produce a special kind of offspring, not just other cells through cell division but also _gemmulae_ , minute particles that embody the nature of the cells from which they derive. These _gemmulae_ , circulating through the entire body, would eventually accumulate in the reproductive organs of both parental organisms, male and female. The formation of offspring is thus explained as the coming together of the accumulated stock of maternal and paternal _gemmulae_ , which, under the right circumstances, i.e., in the female uterus, could themselves multiply though division and develop into cells of the same kind as the ones they were themselves derived from. This is an utterly atomistic conception of a developing organism, as it is indeed required for Darwin's theory of fundamental variation. Such an atomistic conception of life has deep roots: it can be traced back to ancient Greek philosophers such as Democritus. A more modern version predating Darwin's had been articulated in the mid-eighteenth century by Georges Buffon (1707–1788), director of the Royal Gardens and Natural History Collections in Paris (the precursor to the Paris National Natural History Museum). In about May 1865, perhaps three years before the publication of _Variation of Plants and Animals under Domestication_ , Darwin wrote to his friend and ally, Thomas Huxley (1825–1895), asking him for a "very great favour." Darwin asked Huxley to review what was to become chapter 27 of his book on _Variation of Plants and Animals_ —the very chapter that explicated his pangenesis theory: "It is a very rash and crude hypothesis, yet it has been a considerable relief to my mind, and I can hang on it a good many groups of facts." On July 12, Darwin wrote Huxley again, confiding to his friend, who had previously urged him to do so: "I have read Buffon: whole pages are laughably like mine. It is surprising how candid it makes one to see one's views in another man's words. I am rather ashamed of this whole affair" (Holterhoff, 2014:670; Darwin, 1887:43–45).

Apart from the fact that Darwin's pangenesis theory was eventually superseded by the chromosome theory of inheritance and all the subsequent breathtaking developments of modern molecular genetics, it had one consequence that did not bother Darwin himself but was most unwelcome to his successors. If _gemmulae_ embody the nature of the cells from which they originate, then if a cell's nature changes during the lifetime of an organism, the nature of the _gemmulae_ thrown off by that cell would change accordingly. That means that characteristics an organism acquires during its lifetime, through the intensified use or lack of use of one or the other organ, could be transmitted to its offspring. The cells in the skin covering a farmer's hand change when calluses develop in the course of hard manual labor in the field. According to Darwin's theory, there would be an option for such calluses to become heritable. The inheritance of acquired characteristics was a hallmark of Lamarck's evolutionary theory, one adopted by Darwin but largely rejected by modern genetics.

But what does it mean to say that Darwin's is a theory of fundamental variation? Darwin's pangenesis theory piggybacked on the cell theory. The ultimate building blocks of organisms are the cells. They come together, in his view, to form organs, organ systems, and eventually the whole organism. As a result of its cellular composition, variation can affect the organism in every one of its parts, in ever so slight, perhaps even hardly noticeable, degrees, all the way down to the level of cells. And thanks to the functional independence of the parts, which Darwin believed in, variation could affect one organ system and not the other without interrupting the harmonious functioning of the whole. Such fundamental variation has also been called the basis of evolutionary tinkering: variation tinkers with the organism in one way or another, which then has to prove itself in the organism's interaction with its environment (Monod, 1971). This is the point at which the second component of Darwin's twin mechanisms of evolution, the principle of natural selection, gains purchase. It could be said that variation takes an organism in this or that direction; natural selection approves or rejects. Darwin's reasoning started from a premise he adopted from British scholar Robert Malthus (1766–1834), according to whom natural populations tend to overreproduction, generating offspring in numbers too large to be sustainable given the limited resources the environment has on offer. This constellation must result in a struggle for existence in which favorable variants prevail over less well-adapted ones. The evolutionary success of the better-adapted variants, Darwin argued, is measured in their greater reproductive success compared to the less well-adapted variants. That is to say, favorable variations are transmitted to offspring more frequently than less favorable traits. The net result of this process, driven by random variation and natural selection, is evolutionary change through time. In Darwin's theory, based as it is on the concept of fundamental variation, this process of evolutionary change is a slow, continuous one, proceeding in small steps and playing out at the level of reproductive communities, i.e., in populations of interbreeding organisms. Variation is random, Darwin asserted, but the evolutionary process of change is an adaptive one, as natural selection keeps populations on track with the ever-changing environmental conditions. To maintain adaptation to an ever-changing environment, populations, indeed species, must continuously change as well in a slow and gradual process that is kept going through random variation and natural selection. This is the classical neo-Darwinian theory of evolution that could most easily accommodate quantitative, statistical population genetics as it was developed in the first half of the twentieth century. Ecologist Leigh van Valen (1973) called this process the Red Queen hypothesis. In Darwin's theory, there is no gap possible in the transformation of organisms and the species to which they belong. All intermediate conditions of form must be realized. There cannot be any abrupt, saltational change.

Evolution proceeds along the chain of generations that make up an evolutionary lineage—perhaps at different paces, here faster, there slower, but never in leaps. Take a reproductive community, a population that belongs to a particular species. Interbreeding across the population keeps the species together; the population as a whole forms a gene pool, a shared genetic background. Assume some environmental change splits the population in two in such a way that interbreeding between the two parts of the formerly contiguous population is no longer possible. Imagine, for example, one of the regular sea-level rises as they occurred in the distant past, which resulted in the flooding of low-lying continental areas. The development of such epicontinental seaways could easily split a population of land-roaming animals in two. Given enough time, the two now separated populations would drift apart, genetically speaking. Eventually, when reproductive isolation is achieved through the accumulation of enough genetic change, two new species would have evolved from one ancestral one. When the sea recedes from the continents as a result of a sea-level drop, the two species would no longer be able to interbreed. Darwinian evolution is about the origin of new species from an ancestral one through a gradual, stepwise process of change due to variation and natural selection. Consider what Darwin had to say about his law of natural selection:

It may be said that natural selection is daily and hourly scrutinizing, throughout the world, every variation, even the slightest; rejecting that which is bad and adding up all that is good; silently and insensibly working, whenever and wherever opportunity offers, at the improvement of each organic being in relation to its organic and inorganic conditions of life. We see nothing but slow changes in progress until the hand of time has marked the long lapse of ages, and then so imperfect is our view into the long past geological ages that we only see that the forms of life are now different from what they formerly were. (1859:84)

For Darwin, all evolution is contained in the gradual change of species, as well as in the gradual evolution of new species from an ancestral one. If paleontology (the study of fossils) or comparative anatomy suggested patterns of more profound and abrupt evolutionary change, then Darwin's principles required the explanation of such more profound and seemingly saltational change as one that results from the summation of population-level changes over immense stretches of time. Such a conclusion was hard to stomach for many of his readers and critics. Take the turtle, for example. Here is an animal that develops a shell, a rigid structure encasing the entire trunk, and with it the heart, the kidneys, the intestine, and all other organs, including the lungs. The chest, or more technically speaking the space that contains the lungs, i.e., the pleuroperitoneal cavity, needs to be able to expand as the lungs are filled with air and to recoil as air is expelled from the lungs. How does that go together with the evolution of the turtle shell? It is impossible to evolve a complete turtle shell without also, and concomitantly, changing the mechanism or mechanisms of respiration (for a discussion, see Lyson et al., 2014). There may, in certain cases, obtain a functional independence of organs or organ systems, but in other cases, as in the one just sketched, such independence does not obtain.

Starting with the first edition of the _Origin_ , published in 1859, there were a number of criticisms leveled against Darwin's theory, most of which later came to be ascribed to the camp of neo-Lamarckians, i.e., early twentieth-century biologists and paleontologists who revived, in one form or another, ideas about the inheritance of acquired characteristics that in a loose sense could be traced back to Darwin's predecessor, Jean-Baptiste Lamarck. Some readers of Darwin's 1859 sketch of evolutionary theory first and foremost objected to his materialistic, indeed mechanistic, approach to the mysteries of life. How could it be that random variation and natural selection could provide a sufficient explanation for the coming into being of those "endless forms most beautiful and most wonderful," as Darwin (1859:490) had himself called them, and for their persistence on the surface of the globe? How could it be that all those "forms most beautiful and most wonderful" had been shaped in the past by only two mechanisms, random variation and natural selection? How could it be that the harmonious function of the complex whole that is an organism would not be disrupted by variation? Variation is a property that manifests itself in organisms, but it cannot—according to Darwin—shape the organism, as it is random in its nature. The force that shapes organisms in Darwin's theory is natural selection, but that is a force external to the organism. The actions of natural selection have once been compared to those of a sculptor, who chips away at a chunk of rock so as to form from it an object of art. With natural selection, it is the environment taxing the effects of random variation. Even more, that selective tax is levied by the environment on adult organisms—those that are ready to compete for reproductive success. How does such an external mechanism targeting the reproductive stage of organisms work on changes that affect early embryonic development, asked many a critic of Darwin? For these critics, a plethora of questions remained to be answered. Others agreed that natural selection is a necessary component driving evolutionary change but that it is not also a sufficient component to explain all evolutionary change, especially the patterns of change manifest in the fossil record.

François Jules Pictet de La Rive (1809–1872) was only one of many reviewers of Darwin's _Origin_ of 1859. Pictet was born into a family that formed part of the gentility of Geneva. After studies in Geneva and Paris, he became professor of geology, paleontology, and zoology at the Academy, later renamed University, of Geneva. In addition to his duties as an academician, Pictet upheld the family tradition of engaging in the political leadership of his hometown and state, canton of Geneva (<http://www.hls-dhs-dss.ch/textes/f/F3884.php>, accessed June 20, 2016). Darwin himself considered Pictet's review " _perfectly_ fair and just, and I agree to every word he says.... Of all the opposed reviews, I think this the only quite fair one, and I never expected to see one" (Darwin to Asa Gray, April 3, 1860, quoted in Hull, 1973:142). Pictet published his review in French in the _Archives des Sciences de la Bibliothèque Universelle_ in March 1860. There is "in Mr. Darwin's book," Pictet complained, "a sudden leap by which the reader is asked to pass from the prudent study of facts to the most extreme theoretical consequences" (cited in Hull, 1973:144). In Darwin's account, all those characters essential for survival that mark out species, genera, families, orders, and classes of organisms would have evolved from a small number of ancestral types over an "immense series of centuries"; "because these bold deductions do not appear to be justified by the facts, it is necessary to have a more powerful argument before accepting them" (cited in Hull, 1973:145). Pictet ultimately embraced the existence of two forces active in nature, "one normal generation and the other the creative force" (cited in Hull, 1973:151). Normal generation results in the type of variation seen within and between species, the type of variation so arduously studied by Darwin. But to arrive at a new body plan, such as that of an ichthyosaur, a pterosaur, or a turtle, to him seemed to require a special creative force. Reading Pictet's review, Darwin concluded, "Our only difference being that he attaches less weight to arguments in my favour, and more to arguments opposed, than I do" (Darwin to Asa Gray, April 3, 1860, cited in Hull, 1973:142).

The creative force that the Lamarckian opponents of Darwin such as Pictet appealed to was not a force exerted by some almighty Creator, however, but instead a creative force that would drive evolutionary change from within the organisms. Variation and a force such as natural selection that works exclusively from the outside they viewed as insufficient to explain major evolutionary changes, such as those involved in the origin of fundamentally different body plans: mollusks, echinoderms, vertebrates, tetrapods, turtles. The reason was that Darwinian natural selection was understood to tax organisms at their adult, reproductive stage in terms of relative reproductive success. How could a mechanism like that explain the fact that major body plans are laid down during early stages of embryonic development? A gastropod and a sea urchin differ from one another in most fundamental ways during embryonic development, starting from the very first cleavage stages of the fertilized egg cell. Starfish exhibit radial symmetry, vertebrates (like humans) exhibit bilateral symmetry, and many shelled gastropods (snails) exhibit torsion in their body plans. How do such deep differences between phyla originate? How can one body plan change into another? It seemed to many biologists of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries that an internal force was required that would redirect inherited developmental processes toward new, indeed innovative, pathways. Few would deny natural selection the role Darwin ascribed to it, but they denied natural selection the power to cause such profound evolutionary changes during early development as are required in the evolution of new body plans. Critics of Darwin also rejected the notion of evolutionary tinkering. They did not believe in a fundamental autonomy and functional independence of organs and organ systems. Instead, they viewed the organism as a developmentally and functionally integrated complex whole, whose parts are interdependent in many ways. Such integration, indeed a fundamental unity of the organism, they found to be most evident in its embryonic development. To them, it seemed unacceptable that chance variation could interfere here and there while still allowing the embryo to continue normal development. An inner creative principle seemed to be required that would maintain harmonious functional integration among the parts of the organisms through evolutionary changes that would manifest themselves along new developmental pathways that deviate from the ancestral ones.

Finally, there was the problem of the succession of intermediate forms. If it was true that even the most profound changes in body plan, as are evident in a comparison of a lizard with a turtle, could exclusively be explained through a slow, gradual, species-level process of change, as Darwin's theory required, all intermediate conditions of form would have to have been realized in the deep past. We would expect to find at least some of them in the form of fossilized remains. Echinoderms have a rich fossil record, but intermediate forms illuminating their evolutionary origin have so far remained elusive. Turtle origins are controversial precisely because intermediate forms that would settle the question remain controversial. The "number of intermediate varieties," Darwin mused, must have been "truly enormous": "Why then is not every geological formation and every stratum full of such intermediate links? Geology assuredly does not reveal any such finely graduated organic chain; and this, perhaps, is the most obvious and gravest objection which can be urged against my theory. The explanation lies, I believe, in the extreme imperfection of the geological record" (1859:280).

The Sudden Appearance of Turtles

One of the pioneering experts working on fossil turtles was Ludwig Rütimeyer (1825–1895), born in the hamlet of Biglen in Emmental, canton of Bern, in Switzerland (Schmidt, 1896). After graduating from grammar school, Rütimeyer enrolled at the University of Bern, where—following family tradition—he took up the study of theology. He eventually switched to medical school, however, passing his state examination in 1850. A student still, he participated in the 33rd annual meeting of the Swiss Academy of Sciences in Solothurn, Switzerland, in 1848, where he presented the results of his investigations into the regional geology. Not much attracted to medical practice, Rütimeyer left in November 1850 for Paris to study zoology at the National Natural History Museum. After a period of teaching at the University of Bern, punctuated by travels all across Europe, the newly wed Rütimeyer was named professor of zoology and comparative anatomy at the University of Basel in 1855, where he embarked on a stellar academic career. He was elected president of the University of Basel in 1864 and became an honorary citizen of Basel in 1867. His research focused on paleontology of vertebrates, especially mammals, including mammalian remains from archaeological sites, and geology, including hydrogeology. Rütimeyer died in Basel on November 25, 1895.

Rütimeyer delivered his inaugural speech to mark his appointment as professor at the University of Basel on January 18, 1856. The title of his lecture was "Ueber Form und Geschichte des Wirbelthier-Skelettes" (The form and history of the vertebrate skeleton) (Rütimeyer, 1898a). Writing three years before the publication of Darwin's _Origin_ , Rüti-meyer's understanding of nature was firmly rooted in divine creation, yet he sketched the modifications of the vertebrate skeleton in an ascending series from lamprey to shark to human in a way that would readily lend itself to a transformationist, i.e., evolutionary, interpretation. Indeed, Rütimeyer introduced a number of laws of form generation he had heard about during his studies in Paris, emphasizing that in all major groups of vertebrates, there still existed the potential for a multiplication and diversification of variant forms beyond the extinct and still existing ones. On that account, the Creator becomes the entity to trigger the process of transformation and diversification of organisms, a process that, once set in motion, is governed by laws of nature, the latter enacted by the Creator again.

Rütimeyer's laudatory speech, delivered in November 1865, after the first year of his tenure as president of the University of Basel, marks another milestone in his career and at the same time reveals that in the meantime, he had come to endorse the Darwinian theory of evolution. Paleontology, the study of fossils, adds the dimension of time to natural history, he argued, thus turning the study of natural history into the study of the history of nature. The historical dimension is also what distinguishes natural history from experimental sciences such as physics and chemistry. The latter allow for experimentation, whereas the study of the history of nature does not. History is, in Rütimeyer's account, a unique, irreversible process unfolding through time. The great embryologist Karl Ernst von Baer, who taught at the universities of Königsberg (now Kaliningrad), St. Petersburg, and Dorpat, had traced the transformation of animal form throughout embryonic development, Rütimeyer recalled. The great comparative and functional anatomist as well as paleontologist Georges Cuvier (1769–1832), from the Paris National Natural History Museum, had traced the alterations of animal form throughout earth's history. Interestingly, some parallels appeared to be present in the alterations of animal form through embryonic development and through geological time. The question remained, argued Rütimeyer, whether these alterations of form that are evident in the fossil record have separately been created, as Cuvier had argued, or whether they are connected through an organic tie of descent with modification, as Darwin had argued (Rütimeyer, 1898b:119). To investigate this latter question, Rütimeyer insisted, is the most noble current task of natural history. Darwin, he concluded, had made the most recent, most illuminating effort in that direction. Rütimeyer had personal contact with Darwin. When in 1859 he started to investigate subfossil animal, especially mammal, remains recovered from pile dwellings discovered along lakesides in Switzerland, "Darwin took a most animated interest in that research and asked me many questions," Rütimeyer reminisced (1898a:21). On Darwin, he wrote in 1882, "It cannot be denied that the shockwave that was triggered by Darwin's publications was powerful and far-reaching to such an extent as is generally known to be set off only by sparks emanating from theological or philosophical dogmatics" (1898c:373). Rütimeyer "welcomed Darwin's ideas and made the theory of descent the foundation of his future research program" (Reif, 1986:84). Although he all the while recognized the epoch-making scientific achievements of Darwin, as well as the moderation with which the great man laid out his thoughts in his books, Rütimeyer nonetheless challenged the twin mechanisms of variation and natural selection as a sufficient explanation of all organic change, especially as observed in the fossil record. He rejected the brute physicalism of Darwin's theory of pangenesis in favor of a progressive force inherent in organisms, pushing evolutionary transformation of organic beings to ever higher levels of perfection (Rütimeyer, 1898c:381).

Although Rütimeyer established himself as a leading pioneer expert in turtle anatomy and classification, he considered his work on fossil turtles a sideline of his research, which otherwise mostly centered on fossil mammals. "Casual work" he called his research on turtles (Rütimeyer, 1898d:28), which he undertook during the years 1859 through 1874 to accommodate the request of his friend, Professor Franz Lang (1821–1899), successor of Franz Josef Hugi (1791–1855) in the position of the director of the Natural History Museum in Solothurn, Switzerland. Fossil turtle remains had started to show up in limestone quarries north of the city of Solothurn in the early 1820s. Their frequency and exquisite state of preservation secured the quarry workers welcome additional income through the sale of fossils to collectors: a well-preserved carapace fetched up to 1,500 Swiss francs—a handsome sum at the time. One of the first collectors was F. J. Hugi, professor of physics and natural history at the collegium (grammar school) in Solothurn. Hugi eventually lost his professorship after his conversion from the Catholic to the Evangelical church, a development that forced him to strike a deal with the city: Solothurn would build a natural history museum and install him as its director with a pension of 900 Swiss francs annually, while Hugi would donate his extensive collection to the city, with it becoming the core of the museum's holdings. The museum collections continued to grow, for at Hugi's request, the workers employed in the quarries operated by the city of Solothurn had to relinquish their findings—for adequate compensation—to the museum. (For more information on F. J. Hugi, see <http://wiki.stadtgeschichte-grenchen.ch/mediawiki/index.php?title=Franz_Josef_Hugi>, accessed June 20, 2016.) The quarries, the commercial operation of which came to an end in the early 1920s as a result of economic conditions, yielded not just turtles but also sharks' teeth, the odd rare crocodile jaw, and a variety of invertebrates. However, turtles were the most spectacular faunal elements and gave the limestone its name: _calcaire à tortues de Soleure_ (Solothurn Turtle Limestone). Swiss geologist and paleontologist Amanz Gressly (1814–1865) introduced the name in 1841. The limestone is a marine deposit of Kimmeridgian age, some 155 million years old, and its fossils are thus inhabitants of the Jurassic sea. Hugi sent a fossil turtle skull from the Solothurn Turtle Limestone to the grand master of vertebrate paleontology, Georges Cuvier in Paris, who published an illustration of the specimen in the second edition of his multivolume encyclopedia on fossil vertebrates. The copper engraving represents "the oldest identifiable figure of a fossil turtle skull," dating from 1824 (Gaffney, 1975a:2). (For a history of the discovery and a review of the Jurassic turtles of Solothurn, see Bräm 1965.)

Rütimeyer published his first monograph on the Jurassic sea turtles from Solothurn in 1866, with his friend Franz Lang as first author; a second monograph with Rütimeyer as the sole author followed in 1873. In that same year, he summarized his findings in the serial of the Natural History Society of Basel. The turtles, he wrote, was the first project that got him involved with the study of the osteology of reptiles, and he expressed his opinion—one that is still valid today—that fossils can really only be properly understood in the light of a comparison with living relatives (Rütimeyer, 1873:5, 7). In his autobiography, he recounted how he obtained comparative material of extant turtles from zoological collections in Munich, Leiden, Frankfurt am Main, and Stuttgart, but also from acquaintances and friends traveling in distant countries such as Guatemala, Japan, India, Sri Lanka, and the wilderness of West Africa (Rütimeyer, 1898d:28ff.). The material he amassed became an important addition to the skeletal collections kept at the Basel Natural History Museum. As many would do after him, Rütimeyer already fretted about what the primitive condition of the turtle body plan would be. On account of their rudimentary carapace, he found soft-shelled turtles to be the closest to lizards, but he noted the even stranger condition of the carapace in living sea turtles, one that combines with a complete bony roofing of the cheek region in the skull. There prevailed among turtles a mosaic distribution of characters he found difficult to understand. In contrast to, for example, the transformation series of fossil horses, where every step of progressive digit reduction in fore- and hind limbs is documented in the fossil record, he was unable to identify a progressive evolutionary trend in fossil and living turtles, which all display the classic turtle body plan, including a completely developed shell. This rendered identification of the primitive condition of shell and skull in turtles all the more difficult, quite in contrast to the horses. In horses, the earliest fossils were small animals with four digits in the hand (manus) and three digits in the foot (pes), clearly the primitive condition compared to the large modern horse, which has retained only a single digit (the third) in its hands and feet. All of this rendered any conclusion as to the origin of turtles highly conjectural:

The fossil record as much as living forms do not provide any information on the relations of the bizarre and isolated type of turtles to other groups of reptiles. As far as we know them today, the turtles appear fully armored on the scene. We have so far not discovered any possible root form.... Perhaps the discovery of placodont skeletons might help in that regard.... One last point deserves attention: it is the finished appearance of the type of turtles with highly perfected representatives in relatively old geological formations. (Rütimeyer, 1873:135ff.)

Turtle origins remained obscured by their sudden appearance in a "finished" form in the fossil record. No intermediate forms were known that would link them to other groups of reptiles—a link that might have provided a clue as to what the primitive condition of the turtle body plan would have been. Perhaps Darwin's account of slow, gradual evolutionary transformation was incomplete after all. Perhaps the question of sudden saltational evolutionary change still had to be considered. These were questions that Rütimeyer left for others to ponder, however.

Echinoderm Begets Mollusk, Turtle Emerges from an Amphibian Egg

The recognition of the sudden appearance of finished body plans in the fossil record, without intermediate forms disclosing their ancestry (or affinity, as it was talked about in the decades before Darwin), is as old as the science of paleontology itself. Similarly, the question of how a transformation could be possible that would bridge the sometimes profound anatomical gaps that separate the major body plans exemplified by the major groups of organisms in existence today is as old as the sciences of comparative anatomy and embryology. Both of these questions were certainly the focus of interest of mid-nineteenth-century comparative anatomists, comparative embryologists, and paleontologists. The era was rife with unbridled speculation and bold theories, but none was as incredulous as the one proposed by perhaps the preeminent comparative anatomist, physiologist, and marine biologist of his time, Johannes Müller (1801–1858), from the University of Berlin. The publication of his _Handbuch der Physiologie des Menschen_ in 1833–1840 (which appeared in English as _Elements of Physiology_ in 1837–1843) was an international success, as it marked the beginning of a modern integrative approach to human physiology. Working in the marine zoological station in Trieste, Italy, in the summer of 1851, Müller focused his attention on the reproductive anatomy and physiology of the echinoderm holothurian called _Synapta digitata_ , which was found in abundance in the Bay of Muggia in the Adriatic Sea near Trieste. What his observations revealed, however, failed to ignite in him that

joyful excitement that comes with a fruitful insight into nature, or with the discovery of an understandable fact that furthers comprehension; instead, the first and lasting impression was disquieting, confusing, and humiliating. I felt that I would for a long time, perhaps forever, remain incapable to understand, what the testimony of the senses demonstrated to me on a daily basis.... When observation had progressed to the establishment of several most extraordinary facts, I felt it necessary that these be confirmed through the testimony of more than merely one pair of eyes. (1852a:iii)

He went to great lengths to have a famous colleague, German physiologist Rudolph Wagner (1805–1864) from the University of Göttingen, whom he coincidentally met in Trieste, confirm his observations, and he asked his own son, who accompanied him on this trip, to do the same. He then proceeded to present his observations at meetings of the Berlin Academy of Sciences in October and November 1851, and at the same time he wrote a letter summarizing his findings to the National Academy of Sciences in Paris. He published a monograph detailing his investigations (1852a), as well as a summary in English in _Annals and Magazine of Natural History_ (1852b).
