Müller's incredible claim was that with _Synapta_ , he had found a sea cucumber that begets snails: an echinoderm gives birth to a mollusk. Initially confirming previous descriptions by other authors of the reproductive organs in _Synapta_ , he reported: "Around the middle of August I met for the first time with an individual with a totally different generative organ," one that contained "young mollusks with spiral shells" (1852b:230ff.). This organ he called the "molluskigerous organ," noting, "It is impossible to determine with any certainty, what is the proportion of individuals with molluskigerous organs to those with normal ovaria" (1852b:24). A commentator writing about his English abstract attributed to its author "the well-known care and accuracy" for which the latter was famous, in light of which there was no reason to cast doubt on the observed facts. "It is another affair, however, with regard to the _deductions_ from those facts... we must confess to being anything but convinced, that the Professor's conclusions are either necessary or even well-founded" ( _Annals and Magazine of Natural History_ [1852] 9:37ff). Indeed, Müller had failed to recognize the parasitic nature of the minute snails he had found inside the holothurian. However, given Müller's stature in German biology, it is hardly surprising that Ludwig Büchner (1824–1899) commented in his hugely influential best-selling book, _Kraft und Stoff_ , on Müller's observations concerning the "generation of snails in holothurians": "Holothurians and snails belong to two fundamentally different sections of the animal kingdom... [Müller's] observations, should they be confirmed in all aspects, would prove the possibility, still denied today, that in historical times, one kind of animal can originate from another kind in an instantaneous transition or act of emergence" (Büchner, 1856:80).

Bizarre as this episode is, it testifies to the readiness to entertain even the most implausible conjectures to explain the transition from one body plan to another on the basis of purely natural, mechanistic forces. A certain consensus was building that major transformations of animal organization could only occur in a saltational way, just as organisms with a complex life cycle, such as insects, change from larva to pupa to imago without intermediate stages between those three. No lesser a person than eminent German philosopher Arthur Schopenhauer (1788–1860), who never pursued any biological research himself, had, in a text published in 1851, emphatically denied the possibility of the spontaneous generation of higher animals from decaying organic matter. But how, then, did the diversity of higher animals come into being? His conclusion was through "birth from a parent belonging to a different species from that of the offspring":

The genesis of these higher forms can be conceived only as a _generatio in utero heterogeneo_ , such that from the womb, or rather from the egg, of some especially favored pair of animals, when the life force of their species was in them raised to an abnormal potency, there arose... [under the right circumstances]... exceptionally, no longer a being of the same kind as its parents.... In such a case the parent would for once have produced not merely an individual but a species. (Schopenhauer, 1851:121; translation from Lovejoy, 1959:425ff.)

Some examples for such origins adduced by Schopenhauer included a snake emerging from the egg of a fish, the emergence of a turtle from the egg of an amphibian, and the emergence of the platypus from the egg of a duck (1851:122). A turtle hatching from a batrachian egg might sound a bit extreme, yet Rütimeyer himself toyed with the idea that chelonians could have had a separate origin from long-extinct amphibians called labyrinthodonts (their name accounting for the specialized labyrinthine internal structure of their teeth). Even as late as 1962, Kuhn-Schnyder proclaimed at the annual meeting of the Paläontologische Gesellschaft (Paleontological Society) in Tübingen, Germany, that turtles might very well have had a separate origin from among fossil amphibians (1962:80).1 Nielsson (1945) had previously already postulated an independent origin of turtles from Triassic plagiosaur labyrinthodont amphibians. Kuhn-Schnyder thought the same of placodonts and ichthyosaurs, thus rendering reptiles a thoroughly unnatural, polyphyletic grouping of multiple separate origins. A group is called polyphyletic if it includes the descendants of more than one ancestor. It was this enthusiasm for multiple and diverse origins, popular at the time in vertebrate paleontology, that would become the target of the cladistic revolution, which insisted on the principle of monophyly to circumscribe natural groups.

Insight into turtle origins remained largely elusive during the second half of the nineteenth century. The years 1887 to 1890 saw the publication of the massive third volume of the _Handbuch der Palaeontologie_ (Textbook of paleontology), edited by Munich paleontologist Karl A. Zittel (1839–1904), who summarized the various propositions about turtle origins entertained at that time: hypothetical turtle ancestors included fossil amphibians (labyrinthodonts), various subgroups of mammal-like reptiles (synapsids), sauropterygians such as placodonts, and rhynchocephalians, the fossil relatives of the tuatara (Zittel, 1887–1890:552). The famous turtle from the Late Triassic of Germany, _Proganochelys quenstedti_ , had already been described by Baur in 1887, who noted, "The darkness that surrounds the origin of turtles yet intensifies when one realizes that the oldest turtle (at this time)... has a completely closed carapace and plastron" (cited in Gaffney, 1990:12). _Proganochelys_ , which was some 215 million years old, was to remain the oldest known fossil turtle for more than a century. A slightly younger fossil (approximately 205 million years old) from the Late Triassic of the Bavarian Alps named _Psephoderma_ was at that time considered to represent a turtle as well. _Psephoderma_ consisted of the fossil remains of a carapace-like structure that Frankfurt-based paleontologist Hermann von Meyer (1801–1869) had described in 1858. Of that fossil, Cope noted, "The oldest Tortoises have generally the most incomplete carapace and plastron; among them the _Psephoderma_ allied to _Sphargis_ " (1871:232)—a relationship Meyer (1858:250) had explicitly rejected. _Sphargis_ is an invalid old name for _Dermochelys_ , the leatherback sea turtle. _Psephoderma_ is today recognized as an armored placodont unrelated to turtles, but these early uncertainties about its placement show how similar the carapace can appear between a placodont and some turtles, especially the leatherback.

Evolution by Leaps and Bounds

The first to build a substantial case for turtle origins from armored placodonts was, as already mentioned, Otto Jaekel, dinosaur hunter of Halberstadt fame. How did he explain the evolutionary origin of such distinct body plans as the turtle's? Jaekel sketched his ideas on the evolutionary transformation of body plans in a highly influential talk he presented at the Fifth International Zoological Congress held in Berlin in 1901, and again in the keynote presentation on the "Tasks and Goals of Paleontology," which he delivered at the founding meeting of the Paläontologische Gesellschaft in 1912, held in Budapest (Jaekel, 1902b; Jaekel, 1914). Jaekel returned to the topic one more time at the 1921 meeting of the Paläontologische Gesellschaft, held in Frankfurt am Main (Jaekel, 1922). Jaekel was a neo-Lamarckian, rejecting natural selection as the major force driving evolution. Like other neo-Lamarckians at the time, he stipulated an internal force that would sustain evolutionary change. In addition, he also defended the doctrine of the inheritance of acquired characteristics. Function, in his view, shaped the form of organs and organ systems in organisms, not the other way around, as was claimed by Darwinians, according to whom the shape of an organ determines its function. Jaekel famously opposed the Darwinian selectionist doctrine with his slogan: "Form changes as a consequence of its activity" (1916:38). The reception of the Darwinian evolutionary theory in Germany was largely linked to August Weismann's (1834–1914) hugely influential doctrine that an impenetrable barrier prevents the body of the organism from having any influence on its genes. Genes control the development and function of an organism for sure, and to the degree that genes mutate and are recombined through sexual reproduction, the organism's offspring will be subject to variation, the raw material for natural selection to work on. What is impossible, according to Weismann and the Darwinians who endorsed his views, is that changes that an organism undergoes during its lifetime as the consequence of the use or disuse of organs can be inscribed in its genes. This remains the widely accepted view today, but it means that the form of organs and organ systems is primarily inherited, then in its function tested by natural selection. The Lamarckian principle requires, in contrast, that the functionality of an organ or organ system is first tested through its use, after which—if it passes the test—it is inscribed in the genes of the organism. Such neo-Lamarckism was widespread in the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries on both sides of the Atlantic. Edward Drinker Cope and Henry Fairfield Osborn, whose influence on reptile classification was sketched in the previous chapter, were both prominent neo-Lamarckians in the United States.

However, the internal force active within organisms that Jaekel appealed to did not serve only as a conduit allowing external factors to shape the genetic constitution of organisms by shaping their organs and organ systems first. It was more forceful than that—indeed, it put the evolutionary transformation of organisms "on rails," i.e., it endowed the evolutionary process with a predetermined direction (the expression "evolution on rails" is borrowed from Levit and Olsson, 2006). According to Jaekel, and many of his fellow neo-Lamarckians, each major evolutionary lineage—the dinosaurs, the ichthyosaurs, the pterosaurs, the turtles—represents a distinct body plan. Driven by an internal force active within each individual organism, every one of these major evolutionary lineages would evolve along a trajectory that runs parallel to an organism's individual life cycle. Just as an organism is born, passes through a juvenile stage to adulthood, then following adult life enters senescence and eventually dies, so is every major evolutionary lineage first born (through its origin), then passes in geologic time through stages of youthfulness, adulthood, senescence, and death (i.e., extinction). The most vigorous stage along that evolutionary trajectory is that of youth. It is during that youthful phase of its evolution that a rapid evolutionary diversification within an evolutionary lineage can occur through the multifarious adaptation of the basic body plan that characterizes each respective lineage to various conditions of life. It is during the youthful stage of their evolutionary lineage that dinosaurs diversified to become herbivores or carnivores, small and large. It is during the youthful stage of its evolution that the turtle lineage gave rise to species that invaded the sea, the lakes, or the desert. All turtles retained the same basic body plan while evolving, within that frame, specializations that made life in freshwater ponds or in the karst mountains possible.

Such goal-directed and cyclical evolution that takes each major evolutionary lineage through periods of origination, youth and adulthood, and senescence and death is called orthogenetic evolution, or orthogenesis. The word is derived from the Greek terms _orthos_ , "straight," and _genesis_ , "origin" and/or "mode of formation of something." Orthogenesis—a goal-directed and cyclical pattern of evolution—and the internal force supposedly driving it are no longer maintained by modern evolutionary theory, although the fossil record does in certain cases display patterns of change that invite such far-fetched explanations. However, an even more difficult question that confronted Jaekel concerned the birth, i.e., origin, of such major evolutionary lineages, because this implies the origin of a new, distinct body plan from an ancestral one. How does the highly distinctive body plan, such as that of a turtle, come into being in the first place? More importantly, barring special creation, evolutionary lineages must have an ancestor from which they originate; turtles must have descended from some other reptiles. This in turn means that the evolutionary origin of a new body plan implies the transformation of an ancestral body plan into a descendant one. How can that happen?

Orthogenetic evolution mirrors the individual life cycle, from origin/birth to extinction/death. For evolutionary lineages, Jaekel envisaged the youthful stage to be the most pliable; it is at this stage that adaptive diversification occurs within an evolutionary lineage—always within the constraints of the underlying body plan, of course. Birds of paradise and ostriches live vastly different lives, but both are still immediately recognizable as birds. Because orthogenetic evolution mirrors the individual life history of an organism, it should then, by analogy, be the early stages during embryonic development of an organism that should be the most pliable—the stages at which developmental patterns that establish the ancestral body plan can be disrupted and new developmental patterns put into place that result in a new, descendant body plan. This indeed was Jaekel's conclusion. The process involved in the evolution of a new body plan Jaekel called metakinesis: "Metakinesis means jolting... an almost violent, in any case profound transformation of form... one that is unthinkable in the adult condition of form, but rather one that can occur during early embryonic stages only, at which the cellular structure of various organs is not yet fully differentiated and for that reason retain developmental plasticity" (1902b:51).

The further an organism progresses in its development, the more its cellular structure becomes determined, and the less plasticity remains for profound transformation. Jaekel was quick to point out that his concept of metakinesis is not the same as the origin of a new species from an ancestral one. The latter, he claimed, is just the summation of variation and natural selection over time the way Darwin had described it. Metakinesis, in contrast, results in a radical rejuvenation of major evolutionary lineages. This is important, because Jaekel here draws a distinction between two different levels and two different modes of evolution. Darwin and his successors had reduced all evolutionary change to the summation of small changes over vast stretches of time, playing out at the level of interbreeding populations that represent species. Evolutionary change is thereby tied to either the gradual transformation of a species through time or the origin of two daughter species from an ancestral one through a process called speciation. To evolve a new body plan from an ancestral one would require, in Darwin's account, a prolonged process of species transformation or an extended string of speciation processes, with the resulting expectation that the fossil record should be packed with intermediates. Jaekel did not deny that evolution occurs at the species level, as new species originate from ancestral ones, but he rejected the idea that the origin of new body plans could be reduced to this level of evolution. Instead, he invoked a second, higher level of evolution: the level of major evolutionary lineages. It is at this higher level that new body plans evolve from ancestral ones, he argued, and they originate through the perturbation and reorganization of early developmental processes at a stage at which the embryo still retains a high degree of plasticity and potential for transformation. The shaking up of early developmental stages, the falling into place of new developmental pathways—this is how Jaekel thought new body plans come into being: via a rapid, indeed saltational, process of change that, if successful and viable, results in the evolutionary establishment of a new body plan. A new major evolutionary lineage is then ready to take off—to enter its youthful phase of proliferation and diversification. Jaekel's model of orthogenetic evolution is thoroughly cyclical, with the senescence of major evolutionary lineages being punctuated by evolutionary rejuvenation through metakinesis.

Jaekel was an extraordinarily prolific writer, publishing papers and books in geology, paleontology, human prehistory, arts, and politics. Not all of his contributions were judged successful by his peers. One obituary, penned by a fellow paleontologist, noted, "Who would argue that with so much light, shadows could be missing?" (Drevermann, 1929:183). His views on evolution gained widespread acceptance, however. Among his most influential admirers was the neo-Lamarckian Othenio Abel (1875–1946) from the University of Vienna (and later from Göttingen), one of the most influential paleontologists of the early twentieth century. Abel is generally considered the father of paleobiology, the science that pursues the analysis of fossil remains from a distinctly biological perspective in an attempt to reconstruct the mode of life of long-extinct organisms. In the course of his career, Abel compromised himself through his entanglement with Nazi biopolitics (Taschwer, 2012; Rieppel, 2013b). As a consequence, he lost his position at the University of Vienna in 1934, but he was reinstated as full professor at the University of Göttingen in 1935. In 1938, Abel joined Hitler's National Socialist German Workers' Party (NSDAP). After his retirement in 1940, he tried to establish an institute for paleobiology in Salzburg with the financial support of an ss organization, the Ancient Heritage Foundation (Ahnenerbe). The Ancient Heritage Foundation was a branch of the ss that funded research, particularly research illuminating the origins of the Aryan race both physically and culturally. Salzburg, a town in Austria that had become part of the German Reich in 1938, had been selected by Heinrich Himmler to become a stronghold of Aryan biology.2 The turmoil of war meant that Abel's plan never came to fruition. Another admirer of Jaekel was neo-Lamarckian invertebrate paleontologist Karl Beurlen (1901–1985), another Nazi sympathizer who joined the NSDAP in 1933, with the consequent prospect of a stellar career at German universities in Kiel and Munich, not just in paleontology, but in the fiscal management of earth sciences in the Third Reich in general until his fall in 1945 (Rieppel, 2012). After World War II, Beurlen had to support his family as a construction worker until his emigration to Brazil in 1950, where he succeeded to launch a second academic career. After his retirement in 1969, he returned to Tübingen and became an activist campaigning against the construction of nuclear power plants (Tollmann, 1986). Whereas his first publication on the foundations, methods, and problems of phylogenic reconstruction, dating back to 1930 and based mainly on his PhD thesis, remains sober and factual in tone, his "big book" of 1937 presents an evolutionary synthesis heavily, indeed grotesquely, tainted with _völkisch_ nature mysticism, the latter aligned with Nazi ideology (Beurlen, 1930, 1937).

The publication of Beurlen's PhD thesis in 1930 exerted a major influence on another young invertebrate paleontologist, Otto Schindewolf (1896–1971). Born in Hanover, Schindewolf found early fascination in geology and paleontology when, as a high school student, he became member of the Niedersächsischer Geologischer Verein (Geological Society of Lower Saxony). In 1914, he enrolled at Georg August University in Göttingen to study paleontology under Rudolf Wedekind (1883–1961), a biostratigrapher. In 1917, Schindewolf followed Wedekind to the University of Marburg, where he earned his PhD in 1919. His prizewinning doctoral thesis on the systematics, biostratigraphy, and paleobiogeography of Paleozoic (Late Devonian through Early Carboniferous) cephalopods (ammonites) from Bavaria established Schindewolf's reputation as a gifted biostratigrapher. His _Habilitation_ followed in 1921, and in 1927 he was named honorary professor (somewhat equivalent to adjunct professor). In the same year, after his promotion, Schindewolf relocated to Berlin to join the Prussian Geological Survey. With the dawn of the National Socialist regime, Schindewolf kept up a loyal public face to the degree necessary, but he distanced himself from the Nazis in private and avoided colleagues who openly collaborated with the regime, such as Beurlen, as best he could.3 Schindewolf emerged from the war uncompromised and became an associate, and soon thereafter full, professor at Humboldt University in Berlin, the latter located in the eastern sector of the city. In 1948, he accepted the offer of a position as full professor of geology and paleontology at the Eberhard Karls University in Tübingen, where he spent the rest of his career. It is particularly during his Tübingen years that Schindewolf exerted tremendous influence in German paleontology, not only through his larger-than-life personality but also through training scores of students, many of whom secured academic careers. He died in Tübingen on June 10, 1971 (for more details and references to obituaries for Schindewolf, see Rieppel, 2008).

Jaekel, Abel, Beurlen, Schindewolf—they all differed to some degree in their views on phylogeny and the evolutionary mechanisms driving it, but only with respect to details and terminology. Unlike the others, Schindewolf—a latecomer in that respect—was not a neo-Lamarckian; indeed, he rejected neo-Lamarckism outright. But they all shared some important ground that is common to all orthogenetic renditions of evolutionary theory: (1) the cyclicity of evolution, where major evolutionary lineages pass through stages of birth, youthfulness, adulthood (maturity), senescence, and death; (2) the recognition of two levels of evolutionary change, the lower being that of the species and the higher being that of major evolutionary lineages representing distinct body plans; (3) the origin of new body plans from ancestral ones through the repatterning of early developmental (embryonic) stages; and (4) the fact that such early ontogenetic repatterning results in saltational evolutionary change, leaving no intermediates behind. To characterize such saltational patterns of evolutionary change, Schindewolf introduced the term "typostrophism." "Typo-" refers to "type," a distinct body plan, meaning a type of animal that undergoes catastrophic (-strophism) changes in development as a new type is being born. It was one thing for a group of paleontologists to claim that the patterns of change revealed by the fossil record called for an orthogenetic evolutionary explanation. It was an entirely different thing to marry such an orthogenetic interpretation of evolutionary change with the results of the rapidly evolving science of genetics. Of the four orthogeneticists here mentioned—Jaekel, Abel, Beurlen, and Schindewolf—the latter was the only one to attempt such a synthesis, which he did in a small book he published in 1936.

Of Fossils and Genes

The late 1920s and early 1930s were difficult times in Germany economically, politically, and socially. Reflecting the general mood of the times, Schindewolf's starting point was his impression that the theory of descent with modification was in a crisis:

Wherever the foundations of secure knowledge seem to be shaky or missing, metaphysical arguments start to spread.... Irrational circumlocutions are replacing well-defined concepts and research questions, without the promise of any valuable scientific insights.... One of the reasons for this unsatisfactory state of affairs is the longstanding antithesis between the theory of descent and genetics. While one should have expected that the two disciplines would come together under a unified theoretical umbrella, they have instead continued to diverge. (1936:iii)

There is perhaps no better illustration of such a schism between the sciences of paleontology and genetics than the famous standoff between paleontologists and geneticists on September 8, 1929, in the university town of Tübingen, near Stuttgart, Germany. In that year, the Paläontologische Gesellschaft and the German Genetics Society held their annual meetings concurrently, with one afternoon being devoted to a joint meeting and discussion. On that afternoon on September 8, the prevailing paleontological perspective was articulated by Franz Weidenreich (1873–1948), a comparative anatomist and physical anthropologist who, after having worked in several German universities, later in his life went to Beijing, China, and subsequently to New York, where he died. Weidenreich had made his first appearance in the circle of the Paläontologische Gesellschaft at its annual meeting—again in Tübingen—in 1922, where his exposition of his understanding of evolutionary transformations of the foot in the evolutionary lineage leading from quadruped primates to bipedal humans was warmly, indeed enthusiastically, received by the audience, especially by Viennese professor of paleobiology Othenio Abel. The point made by Weidenreich that garnered the greatest applause was his tearing down the impermeable wall that August Weismann had erected between the bodily organs and functions (soma) and the genome (germ line). Tearing down this barrier opened up an avenue for acquired characters to become inscribed in the genes. Every trait that today is encoded in the genotype must once have been a trait of the phenotype, was his slogan—one quickly adopted by Abel—again cementing the view that function shapes form, and not the other way around. His other commitment that earned him high praise was his holistic conception of the organisms: cells, organ, and organ systems were, in his view, developmentally as well as functionally tightly integrated and interdependent—a view that diametrically opposed the atomistic conception of an organism so characteristic of Darwinism. There was no evolutionary tinkering possible, in his view. Instead, a concerted transformation of the whole organism had to happen, in response to changing behavior and within the norm of reaction coded for by the genome. Genes, in his view, did not—one by one—determine specific characteristics of organisms. Instead, he thought that genes determined a norm of reaction, i.e., a degree of developmental plasticity, and hence a concomitant potential for change of an organism in response to environmental stimulations. In the evolutionary lineage leading to humans, for example, one could think of changes in behavior that gradually moved away from a quadrupedal stance toward a bipedal stance. Such stepwise building of a preference for bipedal locomotion would have caused changes in the loading patterns in the foot, to which both the skeleton and the muscles would adapt in concert. The consequences of such complex adaptive transformations, triggered by changes in behavioral preferences, would eventually get inscribed in the genes. That, of course, is neo-Lamarckism, pure and simple.

In his presentation at the 1929 joint meeting of German paleontologists and geneticists, Weidenreich again attacked the Weismannian dogma that genes are hermetically sealed off from environmental influence. Defending the "frowned upon 'Lamarckian tendencies'" in evolutionary theory against the geneticists, he pointed to the insufficient duration of mutation experiments pursued by geneticists in their labs with plants and fruit flies, so that long-term consequences remained unknown. He further highlighted the fact that experimentally induced mutations (e.g., by exposing fruit flies to elevated temperatures) only resulted in the variation of minor characters, never in any more fundamental transformation of the body plan. Only if geneticists were ready to soften their dogmatic position with respect to the Weismann doctrine would there be any chance to "terminate this exasperating state of affairs, where two disciplines keep talking past each other, when their coming together would be of greatest importance for a fruitful collaboration concerning the very foundations of biology" (Weidenreich, 1929:286). Weidenreich's presentation was answered by Finnish geneticist (and eugenicist) Harry Federley (1879–1951), who at that time worked in the Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Biology in Berlin-Dahlem. Eugenicists of the time, developing a program of racial hygiene, had ideological reasons to reject neo-Lamarckism. If there were a way in which environmental stimulation could modify the genotype, then there would be at least a potential to remedy alleged genetic defects. But if the Weismannian barrier completely shielded the genotype from environmental influences, any individual would be hostage to its genes, and through them inexorably tied to alleged genetic defects, as much as to whatever were thought to be genetically determined racial traits. From a geneticist's point of view, Federley declared the question of the inheritance of acquired characters to be merely of historical significance. It is the fertilized egg that forms the organism, not the other way around. In that developmental process, however, any particular trait of the organism can be conditioned by several genes, with these possibly even located on different chromosomes (a phenomenon referred to as polygenic inheritance), whereas any given gene can contribute to the development of several organs or organ systems (a phenomenon referred to as pleiotropy). Even if it were possible, there is no simple way to imprint changes of organic form due to environmental influences onto the genome. Federley located neo-Lamarckists primarily among paleontologists, with Othenio Abel and Karl Beurlen as prime examples, whose science—the study of the fossil record—he called an historical one. This he contrasted with genetics, which he classified as a precise and experimental science whose conclusions can be formulated in the language of mathematical statistics. This precise science delivers no proof for the inheritance of acquired characters but instead provides a host of facts speaking against such possibility. Although Federley expressed his understanding for paleontologists who took recourse to Lamarckian explanations in their attempt to forge evolutionary links between the different body plans that were in existence at different times of the earth's geologic past, he urged, "We geneticists... would only wish that those paleontologists who tackle evolutionary questions would endorse the secure insights of genetics, even if these appear inconvenient" (1929:297).

The chairman of the joint session, geologist and paleontologist Josef F. Pompeckj (1867–1930), concluded the ensuing discussion of the two presentations with an expression of hope that the event would result in a rapprochement of the two sciences, paleontology and genetics. Given that both disciplines investigate evolution, it should be possible to find common ground after all, and to jointly aspire to a unified explanation for the evolutionary transformation of body plans. Such a unification, or synthesis, is exactly what Otto Schindewolf aspired to deliver with his book of 1936 on _Paleontology, the Theory of Descent, and Genetics_. In his attempt to bridge the perceived gap between the theory of descent and genetics, Schindewolf attributed foundational importance to paleontology over genetics. Paleontology alone can access, in the fossil record, the only direct evidence that is available for descent with modification through deep time.

Schindewolf followed in that respect the widely read and highly influential book on the theory of descent that was published by Tschulok in 1922. Sinai (Samuel) Tschulok (1875–1945), a native of the Ukraine, arrived with his parents in Zurich in March 1894 as part of the first immigration wave of Eastern Jews into Switzerland. After his graduation from the Swiss Federal Institute of Technology, Tschulok founded a private grammar school, but he also taught a course on evolution at the University of Zurich. The lecture notes for that course served as the basis for his 1922 book. With regard to evolution, Tschulok argued, there are three questions to be asked. Of those, the first and principal question is, did evolution occur? If that question is answered in the affirmative, i.e., if it is agreed that evolution did indeed occur, then two subordinated questions obtain. The first one of those is, how did evolution occur, i.e., what is the shape of the tree of life? The second subordinated question is, why did evolution occur, i.e., what are the mechanisms that drive evolutionary change? Paleontology, Schindewolf argued, falls under the first and genetics under the second of those subordinate questions. The study of fossils, he claimed, provides evidence for patterns of evolutionary change through time. The task of genetics is to help explain those patterns of change as recovered by paleontological research. The patterns Schindewolf believed to have discovered in the fossil record were largely those already described by Jaekel: orthogenesis, where major evolutionary lineages that represent a distinct body plan cycle through stages of origination, youthful diversification, adolescent fine-tuning, adulthood, senescence, and death. The second salient point documented by the fossil record was, in his opinion, the discontinuous, indeed saltational, origin of new body plans. A new body plan is established through the reshaping and repatterning, indeed through the entire reprogramming of early embryonic development, a process Schindewolf called proterogenesis. It is because of the saltational—or as Schindewolf often put it, explosive—origin of new body plans that intermediate forms linking the ancestral and descendant body plan with each other are absent in the fossil record: "As has repeatedly and aptly been said: 'the first bird hatched from a reptile egg'" (Schindewolf, 1936:59). Among the authors who had endorsed such typostrophism, Schindewolf of course listed fellow paleontologists, as well as embryologists, but he listed only one geneticist: Richard Goldschmidt (1878–1958), the father of the idea of hopeful monsters (of whom much more later). Schindewolf found the major as yet unsolved problem of genetics to be the explanation of such saltational evolutionary change, and in that respect, he found Darwinism (the theory of gradual change through random variation and natural selection) insufficient and neo-Lamarckism (function determining form through the inscription of acquired characters into genes) downright wrong. It is against this background that Schindewolf found Goldschmidt's investigations into the genetic basis of embryonic development so important. Starting from a fertilized egg, i.e., a single cell, any given body plan is progressively more and more determined and structured the further embryonic development proceeds, until it reaches its full differentiation. It is thus unlikely that a new body plan can evolve from an ancestral one during late developmental stages, at a time when the ancestral body plan is already nearly, or fully, realized. Conversely, and as Goldschmidt had argued, mutations that affect early embryonic stages can have a major effect through redirecting subsequent developmental pathways. Schindewolf concluded, "The origin of a new body plan is thus due to the influence of mutations on early ontogenetic stages" (1936:87). The job of fine-tuning a newly originated body plan with respect to the environmental intricacies can then be left to Darwinian variation and natural selection. The origin of new species of the same body plan can thus be explained exactly along the lines Darwin had argued. The origin of a new body plan cannot be so explained, however, as it requires different mechanisms that have more profound effects on evolutionary transformation. Schindewolf, like Jaekel before him, thus recognized two levels of evolutionary transformation, and with them two different mechanisms of evolution.

The way Schindewolf married the results of paleontological research with genetics merits more discussion, however. Against neo-Lamarckians, Schindewolf insisted that "paleontology and the theory of descent will have to recognize and incorporate the laws of genetics that have been discovered through meticulous experimental research as being correct and binding, unless they are called into question by decisive, truly evidential findings" (1936:85). But what did Schindewolf recognize as those secure laws of contemporary genetics? One was the claim that there are no other known mechanisms driving evolutionary transformation than mutations, their combination, their recombination through sexual reproduction, and subsequent selection among viable combinations of mutations. Second, mutations are random and not directional. Third, and most importantly, "The transformation and evolution of organisms occurs in leaps, through sudden, more or less large mutational steps, which are caused by changes in the genes and the genome" (1936:85). There is the rub: more or less large mutations. Weidenreich, in his 1929 address, had argued that experimental genetics had so far revealed mutations that affected organisms in minor traits only, such as the color pattern on the wings of butterflies or the color of the eye in the fruit fly _Drosophila_. Experimental genetics as it stood at that time, he claimed, could therefore not explain the origin of major, discrete body plans. Schindewolf, for his part, thought that the fossil record unequivocally documented the saltational, indeed explosive, origin of new body plans. Given that he thought this to be a well-established fact, he borrowed the notion of mutation from experimental genetics, but, going beyond experimental results available at the time, he added the qualifier that these mutations could be minor or major ones. This is where he called on Goldschmidt for support of his conclusion—but Goldschmidt was an outspoken critic of contemporary experimental genetics.

Notes

1. To translate Paläontologische Gesellschaft as "German Paleontological Society" would be misleading because the society was renamed Deutsche Paläontologische Gesellschaft during the Nazi regime to signal its falling in line with Nazi ideology. The society returned to its old name, Paläontologische Gesellschaft, when it reconstituted itself after World War II.

2. "Besprechung mit Gauleiter und Notes Reichsstatthalter Dr. Scheel über verschiedene Angelegenheiten des 'Ahnenerbes' in Salzburg," Bundesarchiv, DS (formerly Berlin Documentation Center), microfilm signature DS G 119 (file Greite).

3. Schindewolf signed the published annual report of the Deutsche Paläontologische Gesellschaft for the year 1942 with "Heil Hitler" (1942:264).

**4.1.** Richard Goldschmidt in Berkeley, California, in 1947.

_Photograph courtesy of Bancroft Library, University of California, Berkeley, call number UARC PIC 13:4013_.

**Hopeful Monsters**

4

A Monk in His Garden

GREGOR WAS NOT HIS ORIGINAL NAME. HE WAS GIVEN THAT NAME when, in 1843, he decided to become a priest and Augustinian monk at the Abbey of St. Thomas in Brünn (today Brno) in Moravia, then part of the Habsburg Austrian-Hungarian empire, now located in the Czech Republic. He was born on July 22, 1822, to Anton and Rosine Mendel, who baptized him Johann. They were a German couple running a farm in Silesia, at that time also part of Austria-Hungary. Johann (later Gregor) Mendel's student years, which ultimately took him to the University of Vienna, saw him excel in mathematics and physics. However, he also took a course from Franz Joseph Andreas Nicolaus Unger (1800–1870), a medical doctor, botanist, and cofounder of the science of paleobotany (the study of fossil plants), who taught first at the University of Graz and later at the University of Vienna. Having been raised in a rural setting, Gregor Mendel was intimately familiar with the significance of crop selection and livestock breeding (for a short biography, see <http://www.biography.com/people/gregor-mendel-39282?page=1>, accessed June 21, 2016). His later experiments with pea plants in the gardens of the Abbey of St. Thomas may very well have been influenced by Unger, who had developed an evolutionary theory he published in 1852, seven years before Darwin's epoch-making _Origin_. In the "Historical Sketch" included in the third edition of the _Origin_ , published in 1861, Darwin praised Unger as the "celebrated botanist and paleontologist" who had "published... his belief that species undergo development and modification" (1861:xix; see also Temkin, 1959:339). Unger's evolutionary theory was, however, much more romantic than Darwin's materialistic and mechanistic outlook.

Looking back on the period of German Romanticism—personified by such luminaries as philosopher Friedrich Schelling (1775–1854) and polymath Johann Wolfgang von Goethe (1749–1832)—Unger introduced the notion of a creative force active within organisms, driving plants toward diversification and perfection, in a course parallel to the animal kingdom, which reached its pinnacle of perfection with the emergence of humans. The motivation for such evolutionary thought was grounded in the philosophical background of German Romanticism, which understood nature—indeed the whole world—as an immense organism subject to continuous change, a dynamic complex whole that in its entirety is more than the mere sum of its parts. In Schelling's terms, the whole of nature was not only begotten but was continuously begetting, thus sustaining a creative process that cast matter into transient forms, such as organisms or species. Thus, in Unger's view, if the world of plants were not tied together through its development in time, driven as it were by a creative force he stipulated to work within the plants, then the multiplicity of plant species and genera would have to be considered a mere aggregate rather than an integrated complex whole composed of interdependent parts, i.e., a thriving superorganism: "In one word, each newly originating plant species cannot be the result of the interaction of external forces, but must be rooted in the synergistic effect of those already organized forces that we recognize in the world of plants—one species of plant must originate from another" (1852:344). Thus, the entire plant kingdom can be understood as a whole, "a truly integrated organism" (1852:345; see also Temkin, 1959:340; Gliboff, 1998:181).

According to eminent evolutionary biologist Ernst Mayr, Mendel set out to test Unger's theory. He approached the question of plant species originating from one another through hybridization experiments using pea plants (Mayr, 1982:711). The great botanist Linnaeus, the founder of biological classification and nomenclature whom we met in chapter 2, had already suggested that hybridization might give rise to new species within the same genus of plants. So Mendel could claim to be in good company when tackling such a heretical question. In crossbreeding pea plants, Mendel discovered that the offspring did not display a blending of parental characters in a random pattern. Instead, he found that discrete traits of either parent plant were transmitted to the offspring in a highly regular, seemingly lawlike pattern that could be described in mathematical terms. Through this work, Mendel established himself as the father of genetics. In 1865, he presented his results in two talks delivered at the February and March meetings of the Natural History Society of Brünn, after which his findings were published in the proceedings volume issued by that society in 1866. But if anybody read it, its revolutionary significance was not recognized. The proceedings of the Natural History Society of Brünn were sent to "115 or more institutions," and "Mendel had forty reprints made of his paper," one of which he sent to eminent Swiss botanist Carl Wilhelm von Nägeli (1817–1891), who at that time was teaching at the University of Munich (Mayr, 1982:723). Already by 1856 Nägeli had endorsed the idea of "the origin of a new species from a vanishing one," but since his own views on inheritance differed profoundly from Mendel's theory, he chose to ignore Mendel's paper: "To accept Mendel's theory would have meant, for Nägeli, a complete refutation of his own" (Nägeli 1856:203; Mayr, 1982:723). It would take until the year 1900 for Mendel's laws of inheritance to be rediscovered independently by three people: Dutch botanist Hugo de Vries (1848–1935), from the University of Amsterdam; German botanist Carl Correns (1864–1933), a student of Carl Nägeli's and the first director of the Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Biology in Berlin-Dahlem; and Austrian agronomist Erich Tschermak (1871–1962), from the University of Vienna. Their publications from the spring of 1900 ushered in a new era of biology that saw the rapid development of research in genetics.

Vigorous genetic research programs sprung up on both sides of the Atlantic, involving both plant and animal model organisms. Some of the central questions asked and answered included, what is the material basis of heritable traits? Where in the cell is this material located? How is it transmitted to the offspring? It was perhaps Thomas Hunt Morgan (1866–1945) and his students and collaborators who most aggressively pursued a Mendelian program in their genetic research at Columbia University in New York, famously using the fruit fly _Drosophila_ as a model organism. In 1933, Morgan was awarded the Nobel Prize in medicine for his discoveries concerning the role that the chromosomes play in the transmission of hereditary traits. Morgan's philosophy was that of a sober scientist, strictly focusing on questions that—given the instrumental means available at the time—could be answered through experimental research: what is the material basis of hereditary traits, and what are the mechanisms that transmit those to offspring? A milestone had been reached by 1915, the year Morgan and his students published their landmark volume _The Mechanism of Mendelian Heredity_. With this publication, the chromosome theory became firmly married to the Mendelian laws. This theory identified chromosomes as the material basis of heritable traits or factors. These factors, which came to be known as genes, were proven to be stable as they were transmitted to the offspring, except for those that had undergone a mutation. Finally, these Mendelian genes were found to be arranged in a linear pattern on the chromosomes, the latter located in the cell's nucleus. The chromosome thus came to be viewed as a string of genes, and the gene was understood as a bit of a chromosome—a material particle, a discrete unit. The genes would be aligned on a chromosome like beads in a necklace. It is this conception of the genes, in conjunction with the behavior of the chromosomes first in the formation of egg and sperm cells, then in the process of fertilization during which egg and sperm cells fuse, that provided a materialistic and mechanistic explanation of the Mendelian laws of heredity (Allen, 1974a:62).

This, of course, was not the end of the story, as we certainly know from modern science. There was not yet any talk of DNA, whose structure was identified only in 1953, opening the door to the rapid ascent of molecular genetics as the queen of the biological sciences. Earlier in the twentieth century, however, two issues were prominent in genetics, one scientific and the other philosophical. The scientific debate turned on the dominance of the cell nucleus and its chromosomes, as opposed to the cell plasma and its organelles, in heredity. Was it the nucleus and its chromosomes that dominated the transmission of hereditary traits to the offspring, or was the hereditary constitution of the offspring more shaped by the cell plasma and its organelles? It was that debate, one vigorously pursued among German geneticists in particular, that famously pitched the _Genom_ against the _Plasmon_. The philosophical debate sprung up against the background of modern physics, which by the early twentieth century, under Ernst Mach's and Albert Einstein's leadership, had boldly replaced matter by energy in its explanatory strategy. In modern physics, matter dissolved into energy, substance into processes. The electron was no longer conceived of as a tiny lump of matter but rather as a pulse of energy taking part in events. Could not a similar explanatory strategy prove fruitful in genetics—one that replaced a material, corpuscular conception with a dynamic, functional conception of genes?

Mutation Theory: Micro- and Macroevolution

Researchers investigating fossils, comparative anatomy, and comparative embryology faced a whole catalog of questions in the early twentieth century. Species and their populations are subject to all-pervading variation, an observation that contrasts with the persistence of major body plans through geological time. The variations that affect species and their populations generally affect minor characters that appear late during embryonic development, an observation that contrasts with the recognition that major body plans are generally laid down during early phases of embryonic development. The newly discovered Mendelian genes could seamlessly be wedded to the insights gained through the study of the geographic variation of species and their populations. But that said nothing about how those genes that obey Mendel's laws relate to the evolution of major body plans, let alone the origin of a new body plan from an ancestral one. At least most paleontologists were happy to entertain the idea that there are two levels of evolutionary change, and correspondingly two different mechanisms of evolutionary transformation. The lower level is that of the species and its populations, and the higher level is that of major evolutionary lineages that represent discrete body plans. It was Russian zoologist and geneticist Jurij Philiptschenko (1882–1930) who—in a small book published in 1927—introduced the terms "microevolution" and "macroevolution" to characterize these two levels of evolutionary change. In so doing, Philiptschenko—a sympathizer of the orthogenetic models of evolution discussed in the previous chapter—started out by contrasting genetics, the study of patterns of inheritance, with phylogenetics, the study of evolutionary relationships (Junker, 2004:354). Like others before him, he declared these two sciences to be unequal. Genetics he declared to be an exact, experimental science, as opposed to phylogenetics, which he considered a highly speculative, historical science. To back up this claim, he cited the perhaps most prominent experimental embryologist and biophilosopher of the time, Hans Driesch (1867–1941), who had said that "the theory of descent is the hypothetical statement that the organisms are really allied by blood among each other, in spite of their diversities" (Driesch, 1908:251; cited in Philiptschenko, 1927:83).

In Philiptschenko's understanding, the contrast between experimentation in genetics and speculation in phylogenetics opened an asymmetry between the two sciences. He insisted that the geneticist could—indeed should—study his pea or maize plants, fruit flies, or butterflies completely independently of evolutionary theory. In contrast, the phylogeneticist, he argued, could not afford to ignore the insights of genetics as he or she goes about deciphering evolutionary relationships. Microevolution, the study of evolutionary processes at the level of species and their populations, can easily be tied to the genetic "factors that we know about today. These are primarily mutations, their combination, and natural selection" (Philiptschenko, 1927:90). What at the time was branded as the neo-Darwinian doctrine—or dogma—was the claim that all evolutionary processes, including macroevolution (i.e., the origin of new body plans), can be explained as a summation of such microevolutionary processes. Philiptschenko himself rejected that claim. The characters that mark out major body plans that characterize higher systematic categories, such as classes or phyla (i.e., echinoderms, mollusks, or turtles), differ from the characters that demarcate species and their populations in several ways, he claimed: first by their invariance and stability. Here Philiptschenko again cited famed botanist Carl Nägeli, who had claimed that "the essence of a taxonomic unit lies not in its characters, but in the latter's degree of constancy" (Philiptschenko, 1927:92; Nägeli, 1884:281). That is to say, whereas species fluctuate through time due to continuous variation and natural selection, traits that mark out major evolutionary lineages, such as the coiled shell of snails or the carapace of turtles, are conserved through million of years, as documented by the fossil record. Next, Philiptschenko invoked the great ninteenth-century comparative embryologist Karl Ernst von Baer, who had shown that characters that mark out major taxonomic groups, or corresponding body plans, develop early during embryogenesis, while the characters that mark out species and their populations develop last (Philiptschenko, 1927:92; Baer, 1828:225). Finally, he made the point that it is quite well established that species-specific characters are determined by genes that obey Mendel's laws, whereas it has not been possible to demonstrate a link between Mendelian genes and the characters of either major taxonomic groups or early embryonic stages. This, to him, indicated that macroevolution could not (yet) be explained by the findings of modern genetics, concerned as it was with the chromosomes, the genes aligned on them, and Mendelian patterns of inheritance. His conclusion was that it is perhaps the cell plasma (the _Plasmon_ as opposed to the _Genom_ ) that is instead responsible for the organization of early developmental stages after all (Philiptschenko, 1927:93ff.).

In light of these discussions, it became obvious that the reconstruction of evolutionary relationships in essence boils down to the study of the evolutionary transformation of characters, i.e., of those characters that mark out taxonomic groups. Species and their populations (subspecies) are the lowest taxonomic units, and classes and phyla are the highest taxonomic units, the latter marked out by fundamentally different body plans. The distinction of micro- versus macroevolution minimally implies the possibility that characters that mark out taxonomic units of lower or higher rank may be subject to different evolutionary mechanisms. Given such a research agenda, it becomes clear that whereas genetic research might indeed proceed independently from paleontology, genetics could not declare independence from taxonomy. It was the taxonomist who told the geneticist what a species is, and whether any two or more of its populations deserved to be recognized as separate subspecies. It was such input from the expert taxonomist that informed the geneticist whether the patterns of inheritance he or she was studying held at the species level or below, i.e., at the level of subspecies.

Therein lay precisely the conundrum: if Darwin's theory was right, then there could be no real species out there in nature. The title of his famous 1859 book was _On the Origin of Species_ , yet in it, Darwin nonchalantly declared: "I look at the term species, as one arbitrarily given for the sake of convenience to a set of individuals closely resembling each other" (1859:52). The reasons for such a surprising conclusion were the twin mechanisms of evolution he invoked: random variation and natural selection. Evolutionary transformation occurs as a continuous gradual process that plays out along the chain of successive generations, necessarily robbing species of sharp boundaries. Darwin's theory rendered the transition from an ancestral to a descendant species a fuzzy one. Where would one species end and another begin? In the wake of the rediscovery of Mendelian laws and the subsequent blossoming of genetics, there sprung up a vivid interest in the question of species delimitation on both sides of the Atlantic. One of the important voices in North America that was also heard in Europe belonged to entomologist Alfred C. Kinsey (1894–1965), who studied the taxonomy and distribution of gall wasps. Later in life, Kinsey would through the Kinsey Reports make a name for himself as America's first sexologist. On species and their demarcation, Kinsey the entomologist had the following to say: "[The species is] the unit beneath which there are in nature no subdivisions which maintain themselves for any length of time or over any large area.... It is the unit directly involved with the origin of species" (1937:207).

Bernhard Rensch (1900–1990) was a prominent German zoologist whose investigations into species-level evolution earned him the accolade of having been the most important European contributor to the modern synthesis that merged Darwinian evolutionary theory with population genetics, which otherwise developed mostly in the United States in the mid-twentieth century. Rensch found that what Kinsey called species most European biologists would call geographic races, or subspecies. Indeed, early twentieth-century investigations of species origins focused on the fact that most species, certainly those with a wide geographic distribution, were composed of a number of at least partially isolated populations that could be recognized as being at least somewhat different from one another. It was soon recognized that such geographically isolated populations represent the cradle for the origin of new species. Such a conclusion certainly corresponded well to Darwin's earlier statement: "Hence I believe a well-marked variety may be justly called an incipient species" (1859:52). The work on the dynamics of evolutionary processes at the level species and their populations (potential subspecies) that was most important with respect to the theory of hopeful monsters was probably pursued in Germany before World War II, with Rensch and his academic mentor, Erwin Stresemann (1889–1972), representing only two of many outstanding scholars active in this field. Another was Adolf Remane (1898–1976). In their work on speciation—the origin of a new species from an ancestral one—these three authors all at one point or another referred back to influential self-taught ornithologist Otto Kleinschmidt (1870–1954). Kleinschmidt has been characterized as a "leading ornithologist, protestant pastor, creationist and sometime Nazi sympathizer" (Williams, 2006:4). The reason that evolutionary biologists such as those just mentioned would refer back to a creationist was Kleinschmidt's _Formenkreis_ theory.

Kleinschmidt did not subscribe to evolutionary theory; for him, every species had been separately created. On that account, species have sharp boundaries, not the fuzzy boundaries of Darwinian evolution. There is no gradual transition between an ancestral and a descendant species possible. However, as an avid birder, Kleinschmidt realized that species are structured in terms of geographically vicariant populations, which to a greater or lesser degree may differ from one another, often justifying the recognition of geographic races, today called subspecies. Kleinschmidt recognized these geographic races as different forms of one and the same species. The species as a whole is thus composed of a number of such forms, i.e., it becomes a _Formenkreis_ (group of forms). Erwin Stresemann from the Berlin Museum of Natural History, possibly Germany's most eminent and influential ornithologist of the twentieth century, took up Kleinschmidt's concept of a _Formenkreis_ , but he embedded it in an evolutionary context. Pictured from that perspective, the geographic race, or subspecies, became the point of departure for the origin of a new species. A subspecies was thus understood as a potential incipient new species. It was, however, Bernhard Rensch from the University of Münster who made these ideas the foundation of a research program that attracted much attention—and applause—on both sides of the Atlantic. What Kleinschmidt had called a _Formenkreis_ , Rensch at the International Ornithological Congress, held in Copenhagen in 1926, dubbed a _Rassenkreis_. A _Rassenkreis_ is a species composed of a number of geographic races (subspecies), each adapted to the environment of its specific geographic location. Just as a single population is composed of variable individual organisms, so is a species composed of diversely adapted geographic races (subspecies). And it is from such geographic races that the process of speciation that leads to the origin of a new species takes its beginning. The precondition for such speciation to take place, however, is the effective genetic isolation of a geographic race from all other populations (subspecies) of the same species. Because the gene pool of such an isolated geographic race is reproductively isolated from other populations of its species, it acquires the potential to embark on a new evolutionary trajectory, driven by mutation and recombination of Mendelian genes on the one hand and natural selection on the other. Such population-level processes of species origination could be brought into complete accordance with the insights provided by Mendelian genetics of the time (Rensch, 1933).

Early in his career, however, Rensch adapted a neo-Lamarckian argumentation scheme. The geographic races of wide-ranging species he often found to exhibit a gradient in their local adaptations, mirroring a gradient prevalent in environmental conditions. An example would be a gradient apparent in the local adaptations of a string of local populations that corresponds to a gradual change of climatic conditions encountered by a species with a distribution that ranges from the far North to the Deep South. Such a graded pattern of geographic variation is, for example, captured by Bergmann's rule—one much discussed by Rensch—which states that body size in populations of the same species increases with colder climate or higher latitude. How could such a gradient of local adaptations of populations within a species be explained if mutations are purely random and the genetic code is completely shielded from the environment? Initially, Rensch accepted the first but not the second of these premises, arguing that the environment can influence genes. While still a scientific assistant at the Berlin Museum of Natural History, Rensch in 1934 laid out these ideas about species formation in a public exhibit at the museum, which got him into serious trouble with Nazi authorities (Rensch, 1979:770ff.; see also Junker, 2001). Some Nazi ideologues, especially those engaged in racial hygiene, waged a merciless campaign against neo-Lamarckism because the latter doctrine would allow—indeed promote—the adaptation of non-Aryan races not only to the Aryan landscape but also to Aryan culture. That threatened to jeopardize the argument for a superiority of the Aryan race and to invalidate the Darwinian (in this case, social Darwinian) foundation that was called on in support of the pseudoscience of racial hygiene. Later in life, however, Rensch turned to neo-Darwinism pure and simple, endorsing the idea that all macroevolutionary phenomena could seamlessly be reduced to microevolutionary, i.e., population-level, processes fueled by the mutation and recombination of Mendelian genes (Rensch, 1980).

Adolf Remane wanted to exercise greater caution in regard to such sweeping conclusions. Unlike Rensch, he was not part of the Stresemann school in Berlin; he worked at the University of Kiel under the tutelage of the famous freshwater ecologist August Thienemann (1882–1960). In 1927, Remane published an influential paper on _Species and Race_. For a zoologist, this is an uncompromising title; among anthropologists studying human races and societies, such a title might raise eyebrows, especially after World War II. The compromising thing was that Remane published his article not in a zoological or biological journal but in a journal of physical anthropology, which shows how at the time zoological studies on race formation informed corresponding research in physical anthropology. In his 1927 paper, Remane did indeed discuss the nature of plant, animal, and human races in a reciprocally illuminating context, but without ideological connotations. Still, according to his son, Jürgen Remane (1934–2004), this paper landed his father in British detainment in 1945 for almost a year and earned him the classification as fellow traveler during the denazification campaign (Remane, 2003). The truth is that Adolf Remane—as did his mentor, Thienemann—paid lip service to Nazi biopolitics in several other, later publications (Rieppel, 2013a). Honoring an invitation to contribute, Remane published one particularly incriminating piece on race formation in 1941 in the _Archiv für Rassenund Gesellschaftsbiologie_ (Archive for racial and social biology), a journal dedicated to eugenics and racial hygiene edited by the former Weismann student and leading German eugenicist Fritz Lenz (1887–1976), and published by racist and Nazi sympathizer J. F. Lehmann, a publisher in Munich. Having been involved in the drafting of Nazi legislation concerning racial hygiene, Lenz claimed after the war that although he stayed on as a member of the editorial board of the _Archiv_ , he no longer exerted any influence in shaping the content of the journal after 1933.1 As unlikely as this sounds, it was nevertheless probably another member of the editorial board who invited Remane's contribution, Gerhard Heberer (1901–1973), a Nazi sympathizer and the leading synthesizer of the work pursued by German evolutionary biologists (Hoßfeld, 1997).

Remane never endorsed neo-Lamarckism, the idea that acquired characters could become heritable. He again argued that in the Darwinian account of evolution, a species does not have sharp boundaries. In his 1927 paper, his first on that topic, he therefore declared a species definition making reference to physical characters that mark out a species, such as those indicated in an illustrated field guide, for example, to be impossible; only a physiological definition of species is possible, namely in terms of a reproductive community. The consequence of such a definition of a species as a reproductive community anchors the nature of any given species in its gene pool, more so than in the physical appearance of individual organisms, Remane contended. Other relevant factors that condition the nature of a species, and that of its geographic races, are the geographical and ecological settings in which they occur. In this early paper, Remane did not address issues of micro- versus macroevolution, nor how these would relate to the insights of contemporary experimental genetics. In 1937, the year he joined the NSDAP, Remane published another paper on animal race formation, once again emphasizing the difficulties that frequently obtain in distinguishing a species from geographic races or subspecies, adding that these distinctions, as drawn by taxonomists, often entail an element of arbitrariness. (On Remane's affiliation with the NSDAP, see Zachos and Hoßfeld, 2006:318.) While this again emphasized the idea that geographic races, or subspecies, are the starting point for the origin of a new species, it was not until 1939 that Remane finally addressed the issue as to how these microevolutionary processes compared to macroevolutionary transformations, and how either of those related to contemporary genetics. Remane was almost uniquely qualified to do so, as his research covered an exceptional range of organisms, including a wide variety of invertebrates through vertebrates all the way to fossil hominids. He had also written, and would continue to do so, entries on many invertebrate phyla for a German encyclopedia of biology, the _Handbuch der Biologie_ , and was thus intimately acquainted with the difficulties involved in explaining the origin and evolutionary transformation of major body plans, such as sponges, worms, or jellyfish. In addition to his encyclopedic taxonomic expertise, he also pursued deep interests in comparative anatomy and embryonic development. He wrote on the origin of segmentation in such invertebrates as annelid worms, as well as about the evolution of the vertebral column and its associated organs.

In his 1939 paper on the significance of the modern mutation theory, Remane, like Schindewolf before him, again referred back to the three distinctions drawn by the Zurich biotheoretician Sinai Tschulok (discussed in the previous chapter): did evolution occur, and if so, how, and why, did it occur? Mutation theory, Remane concluded, falls under the last of these three questions, i.e., the question about the causal mechanisms that drive evolutionary transformation. Going from there, Remane emphasized the experimental nature of modern genetics, from which he derived an important distinction. In his paper, he argued, he would only discuss what he called real mutations, i.e., mutations of the kind that had been revealed through experimentation. He would not countenance so-called desktop mutations (I would have called them armchair mutations), i.e., hypothetical mutations that geneticists invoked as hypothetical explanatory devices while sitting at their desk, instead of running experiments. The experimental nature of modern genetics Remane then contrasted with the historical nature of evolutionary studies. Nevertheless, he thought that there was a possibility to have the two research programs reciprocally illuminate each other. The comparative anatomist and paleontologist, he contended, often find evolutionary transformations in different groups of organisms to independently follow similar trends. This means that patterns, or types, of phylogenetic transformations could be identified. Geneticists, he claimed, similarly find that mutations follow a certain set of patterns, such that it should be possible to compare types of evolutionary patterns with types of mutational patterns in an effort to find some overlap, or congruence, between them. Should such congruence obtain, then and only then would he be willing to accept the insights gleaned from genetics as an explanation for the corresponding evolutionary change. The two phylogenetic patterns that he then proceeded to test against the current theories of genetics were the understanding of a species as a composite of geographic races, as opposed to the evolutionary transformation of complex, functionally integrated organic systems such as major body plans. His conclusions are hardly surprising. The real mutations known from experimental genetics do indeed explain how a species comes to be structured as a complex of geographic races, or subspecies, as it expands its geographic distribution. But in his view, experimental genetics had not yet succeeded in documenting the kind of mutation that would explain the transformation of functionally integrated complex organic systems such as major body plans. Refraining from the invocation of desktop mutations, he declared ignorance with respect to the explanation of such macroevolutionary events while at the same time banning neo-Lamarckian tendencies from any consideration.

The father of the doctrine of hopeful monsters would not exert the same degree of caution in scientific theory construction.

A German Jew in America

Richard Benedict Goldschmidt came, as he himself put it, "from an old German-Jewish family" (Stern, 1967:172; for a review of Goldschmidt's life and work, see Dietrich, 2003). He was born in Frankfurt am Main on April 12, 1878, and died in Berkeley, California, on April 24, 1958. His parents were of high social standing, receiving "well-known scientists, bankers, and philanthropists" at their house for dinner and entertainment (Stern, 1967:141ff.). In Goldschmidt's own words, his was "a typical German bourgeois family, comfortable but strict and even parsimonious in spite of cooks, nursemaids and French governesses" (Stern, 1967:142). Later in life, he reminisced about the antisemitic mobbing he experienced in the streets of his hometown and at school. As was typical for members of his social class in Germany, Richard enjoyed a broad, classical education in the ancient languages of Latin and Greek as well as in the humanities, arts, and sciences. "Music is still to me what religion is to the believer," Goldschmidt once said later in his life (Stern, 1967:172). He played the violin and viola, all the while excelling in sports, and grew up to become an aficionado of Far Eastern arts. Acquaintance with the works of Goethe was de rigueur in the social circles known as the _Bildungsbürgertum_ , as was the study of philosophical luminaries "from Spinoza to Nietzsche" (Stern, 1967:142).

At his parent's request, he enrolled in medical school at the University of Heidelberg. What is important for a proper understanding of his later theorizing in genetics is the fact that as a medical student, Goldschmidt took classes from the most illustrious comparative anatomist of his time, Carl Gegenbaur (1826–1903), who had transferred from Jena University to Heidelberg in 1873. Gegenbaur imbued the young medical student with a deep sense of appreciation for the organization of the major body plans that mark out higher taxonomic units, such as mollusks, arthropods, and vertebrates. In the Gegenbaur school, such body plans were referred to as types of organization: turtles and birds are clearly different types of organization within vertebrates. At the outset of his academic career, while still a professor in Jena, Gegenbaur held these types as having been designed by the Creator. Gegenbaur was instrumental in bringing the young Ernst Haeckel to the University of Jena, where a deep friendship blossomed between the two men (Richards, 2008:80ff.). Ernst Haeckel immediately caught the Darwinian bug when he read the German translation of Darwin's _Origin_ , published by the paleontologist Heinrich Bronn (1800–1862) in 1860 (Gliboff, 2008). Soon Haeckel had managed to infect his friend Gegenbaur with that same bug. The two embarked on a joint project, which was to render comparative anatomy into what they called a true science "through its infusion with evolutionary meaning" (Nyhart, 2003:163; see also Rieppel, 2011). Whereas before comparative anatomy strove to sketch the ideal, i.e., purely conceptual, derivation of one body plan from another through a series of imagined intermediate stages, it now attempted to understand the origin and transformation of body plans in evolutionary terms. Once again, both Haeckel and Gegenbaur sought answers to questions that arose from the comparison of radically different body plans—and their evolutionary derivation from one another—in early stages of embryonic development. Given their state of lesser differentiation and determination, it was again early stages of embryonic development that seemed to hold the key to an understanding of the origin of a new body plan from an ancestral one.

After some semesters in Heidelberg, Goldschmidt decided to transfer to the University of Munich to study zoology under former Haeckel student Richard Hertwig (1850–1937), "with minors in botany, physiology and paleontology" (Stern, 1967:142). His early research focused on cytology (the study of cellular structures) and comparative anatomy. In 1909, the year he turned to genetics, Goldschmidt became an associate professor at the University of Munich. In 1911, the Kaiser Wilhelm Society was founded (renamed the Max Planck Society after World War II), with the mission to promote science through the establishment of research institutes specializing in various scientific disciplines. The institute that would specialize in biology was built in Berlin-Dahlem; Goldschmidt was appointed as a staff member upon its opening in 1914. The award of a traveling stipend in the same year allowed him to embark on a field trip to Japan. His return to Germany was thwarted by the British blockade of the German coastline at the outset of the Great War, forcing Goldschmidt to pursue breeding experiments as a guest researcher in the United States. He returned to his post in Berlin-Dahlem when the war was over in 1919 to resume his position at the Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Biology, a post he retained even after the Law for the Restoration of the Professional Civil Service was passed two months after Hitler took power on April 7, 1933. While this law banned non-Aryans (as well as opponents of the regime) from tenured positions in the civil service, it was only the Nuremberg laws passed in 1935 that provided supposedly clear and precise definitions of degrees of Jewishness on the basis of parenthood. Once these laws had been enacted, Goldschmidt and his family were harassed and accused of "racial defilement ( _Rassenschande_ )," which drove them out of Germany (Satzinger, 2009:169). Goldschmidt emigrated to the United States, where in 1936 he became a professor in the department of zoology at the University of California at Berkeley. Goldschmidt earned his place in the history of biology as the most outspoken critic of the atomistic and mechanistic approach to genetics exemplified at the highest level of perfection by the Morgan school at Columbia University. Upon his arrival in the United States, Goldschmidt—in his typical confrontational style—announced "in a funereal voice: 'the theory of the gene is—dead'!" (Stern, 1967:165).

Goldschmidt most eloquently fleshed out his theory of hopeful monsters in his 1940 book _The Material Basis of Evolution_. The book resulted from a lecture series he delivered at Yale University, the Mrs. Hepsa Ely Silliman Memorial Lectures, inaugurated in 1833 with the mission to "illustrate the presence and providence, the wisdom and goodness of God, as manifest in the natural and moral world" (Silliman Foundation, introduction to Goldschmidt, 1940). Goldschmidt took this mission statement for a natural scientist to mean "the demonstration of law and order in his chosen field"—in his case, genetics (1940:3). He had already sketched out the basic ideas of his 1940 book in an article published in _Science_ in 1933. This is also the paper in which he first introduced the term "hopeful monster" (1933:547). Goldschmidt was intimately aware of the close ties that link experimental genetics to taxonomy: "I was an ardent coleopterologist," interested in the species-level taxonomy of beetles, he reminisced (1940:27). Kleinschmidt, Stresemann, Rensch, and Remane are all authors copiously quoted in his 1940 treatise, a fact that clearly reveals the German roots of his education and style of thinking. Of these authors, only Rensch made it into the most important book on population genetics published around the same time in North America, Theodosius Dobzhansky's _Genetics and the Origin of Species_ (1937).
