Goldschmidt himself had a profound knowledge of population genetics. Indeed, he is "recognized as the first person to have investigated the genetics of variation among wild populations" (Harwood, 1993:111). Goldschmidt engaged in an exhaustive analysis of the geographic variation of the gypsy moth, _Lymantria dispar_ : "I was able to make what I believe to be the most complete genetic analysis of a _Rassenkreis_ ," borrowing the latter term from Rensch to designate a group of subspecies that together constitute a species (1933:541). Much to his own horror, Goldschmidt witnessed the co-option of his own genetic research in ideologically charged discussions of racial hygiene by German eugenicists, a development he tried to oppose as best as he could (Satzinger, 2009:167ff.). Goldschmidt defined a _Rassenkreis_ as "a series of typically different forms or subspecies found at different points within the geographic range of a species and often showing a typical order of their characters if arranged geographically" (1933:540). Using his own taxonomic expertise as a foundation, but enriching it with a broad review of the pertinent literature, Goldschmidt proclaimed—against the neo-Darwinian doctrine—that good species do have sharp boundaries after all:

Kleinschmidt... the father of the _Formenkreis_ concept, held that every species was separated from every other by a gap. His intensive studies as an ornithologist of considerable authority, as well as his study of other groups, demonstrated to him that this was the rule. (He was even led to deny an evolution from one species into another, but here the preacher might have influenced the ornithologist.) (1940:143)

If Kleinschmidt's conclusions were tainted by his religious commitment, the same could not be said of Stresemann, whom Goldschmidt considered "one of the leading and most progressive taxonomists" of the time (1940:154). In a paper on Kleinschmidt's _Formenkreis_ theory published in 1936, Stresemann had concluded "that there is really something like natural species, surrounded by sharp structural boundaries" out there in nature (Stresemann, cited in Goldschmidt, 1940:154). All the same, however, Stresemann, like Rensch, had accepted the view that geographic races are incipient species, i.e., that subspecies hold the potential to evolve into new species. For Goldschmidt, who considered species to be separated from one another by a "bridgeless gap," this amounted to idle speculation: "Rensch, who is predominantly an ornithologist, forgets the statements by such leading ornithologists as Kleinschmidt and Stresemann, who recognized the bridgeless gaps [that separate species] and tried to extricate themselves from their dilemma, one by recourse to theology, the other to phylogenetic speculation" (1940:170).

Within his own expertise, as well as in the work of others, Goldschmidt found species to have sharp boundaries—to be separated by a bridgeless gap. He accepted the fact, however, that species are constituted by a variable number of geographic races, or subspecies, each adapted to the specific local circumstances in which it lived. As a geneticist, he of course rejected the neo-Lamarckism countenanced by the young Rensch, which would have allowed local environmental circumstances to influence the gene pool of geographic races in a process of adaptation. Goldschmidt accepted the fact that Mendelian mutations and their recombination through sexual reproduction, not environmental factors, drive the formation of geographic races, but he thought that they do so in a particular way. Mendelian mutations and their recombination would, according to him, preadapt organisms to a new environment. The concept of preadaptation was much discussed at the time and played a central role in Goldschmidt's thinking. With the rejection of neo-Lamarckism, the environment lost its power to modify genes. Genes are modified through mutation and reassorted through sexual reproduction; the consequence is a change of form. Changes in the genotype drive changes in the phenotype, not the other way around. In classical Darwinian terms, the variable phenotypes that result from such genetic modifications then come under the pressure of natural selection. Goldschmidt considered this doctrine an insufficient explanation of how species come to form locally adapted subspecies. He thought that Mendelian mutations and their recombination change the phenotypes in a reproductive community in a way that preadapts these organisms to a new environment. Such preadaptation allows organisms to migrate and spread into a new environment, to create a new ecological niche, and in the end to establish a new subspecies. This is certainly a position that can no longer be defended in modern evolutionary theory. Still, the importance that the concept of preadaptation played in Goldschmidt's theorizing cannot be overstated: Mendelian mutations and their recombination result in variation that preadapts the affected organisms in such a way as to render them capable of invading new environments, thereby expanding the range of a species through the formation of new geographic races (subspecies). This is what, according to Goldschmidt, contemporary experimental genetics contributed to an understanding of evolution—no more, no less.

Microevolutionary processes that play out within a species, i.e., at the level of subspecies, could be illuminated through experimental genetics. It is in microtaxonomy, i.e., taxonomy at and below the level of species, that the taxonomist meets the experimental geneticist. In Goldschmidt's assessment, contemporary genetics as studied in Thomas Hunt Morgan's lab had not yet contributed anything to macrotaxonomy, i.e., to an understanding of the origin of new discrete species, let alone to an understanding of the origin of new body plans (Goldschmidt, 1940:5). To bridge the gap between species, as between body plans, to him seemed to require a different type of mutation than those revealed by Morgan's fruit flies. This is the juncture at which it becomes clear that the scientific debate between Goldschmidt and Morgan was rooted in the different cultural and educational background of the two protagonists.

It is interesting to note that both geneticists, Goldschmidt and Morgan, started their careers in biology as comparative anatomists in the broad sense. Goldschmidt's affiliation with Carl Gegenbaur at the University of Heidelberg has been sketched above. As a graduate student, Morgan worked on the morphology of sea spiders under William K. Brooks (1848–1908) at Johns Hopkins University. After obtaining his PhD in 1890, Morgan was appointed as an associate professor at Bryn Mawr College, where he worked on the description of the anatomy of various marine invertebrates. The more he immersed himself in these studies, the less enchanted he became with what he perceived as wild evolutionary speculations put forward by other authors in the same field of research. In a paper he published in 1894 on the acorn worm _Balanoglossus_ , an organism that at the time was under discussion as being close to the ancestry of vertebrates, Morgan explained that he wanted to "avoid the introduction of extreme phylogenetic speculation and to stick to observed facts" (1894:74; see also Bowler, 1996:188). After joining the faculty at Columbia University in 1894, a stipend allowed Morgan to visit and work at the marine biological station in Naples, where he met Hans Driesch, who convinced Morgan of the virtues of an experimental approach to biology (Allen, 1974b:517). Driesch was at the time one of the leaders in experimental embryology, who early in his career had turned his back on phylogeny reconstruction, praising the virtues of experimentation, which yields neat, exact, and most importantly reproducible and consequently testable results—quite in contrast to comparative anatomy, where similarity often seemed to lie in the eye of the beholder. According to Driesch, comparative anatomists arrange animal forms in a series of putative ancestors and descendants, a mere "portrait gallery of ancestors" without any apparent underlying cause that could be critically tested through experimentation (Driesch, 1899:36; see also Nyhart, 2002:9). Morgan brought the same sober approach to genetics: a quest for experimentally secured, reproducible results, unadulterated by phylogenetic speculations (Harwood, 1993:102).

Morgan's approach to genetics was thoroughly atomistic and mechanistic. Genes were conceived of as material particles, arranged in a linear fashion on a chromosome. The chromosome was conceived of as a string of beads, with the beads being the genes. Chromosomes can break, lose parts, invert segments, and have parts cross over to other chromosomes. This might change the sequence in which the genes are arranged, but the genes were still beads aligned on a string. Through their breeding experiments, the Morgan school tried to identify which genes correlate with which characters of the fruit fly and to identify the location of those genes on the chromosome. In contrast to Morgan, Goldschmidt held a much more comprehensive view of nature in general, as also of chromosomes and of genes in particular. Given his German background and the time and cultural surroundings in which he grew up, it is hardly surprising that Goldschmidt endorsed a much more holistic, organicist conception of nature. Be they embryos, species, ecosystems, or higher taxonomic units, all were seen by many German scholars during the early decades of the twentieth century not as mere aggregates, as are beads on a string, but as tightly integrated, complex wholes. For Goldschmidt, the chromosome was not a linear aggregate of individual genes but instead a tightly integrated complex whole, composed of reciprocally interdependent parts. The inversion of a segment in a chromosome, or the crossing over of a segment from one chromosome to another, amounted in his eyes not just to a realignment and/or rearrangement of genes as beads on a string, but to a fundamental reorganization of that whole system—the complex and highly integrated machinery that a chromosome is as a whole. The chromosome as a whole was in his eyes more than the mere sum of the genes aligned along it (Harwood, 1993:252). Similarly, Goldschmidt was not satisfied with research that sought a mere correlation between genes and fruit fly characters. He wanted to ask bigger questions, even if these could not yet be tackled experimentally. He saw the necessity to understand how genes control the physiology of an organism, and most importantly how genes guide the development of an embryo.

In the tradition of the Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Biology at which he had been employed, Goldschmidt sought to use genetics as a means through which to unify biology—or at least the experimental branches of the biological sciences, which are genetics, physiology, and embryology (Harwood, 1993:44). He was not satisfied with mere "bean-bag" genetics (Mayr, 1982:67); he aspired to a physiological genetics, and even more importantly to a developmental genetics. These were complex questions that could not be answered by breeding experiments alone. They required much more far-reaching, if also more difficult, research. Indeed, given the instruments available at the time, and the then-current knowledge in physiology and molecular biology, Goldschmidt asked questions that were hardly answerable at the time except in a highly speculative way. This is why Morgan largely eschewed those issues. It is also the juncture at which Goldschmidt lost himself in speculation—which he had claimed Stresemann and Rensch to be guilty of with their theories on speciation.

To explain the origin of a new species, or of a new body plan, Goldschmidt invoked what he called systemic mutations. A systemic mutation is one that affects the whole system, i.e., the entire complex whole that is the chromosome as well as the organism within which a chromosome is active. The origin of a new discrete species, as also the origin of a new body plan, Goldschmidt defined as a process that transforms a stable, highly integrated system (or complex whole) into a different, yet again in itself stable and highly integrated system, where the system could be a species, or a body plan (Goldschmidt, 1940:250). Systemic mutations, as they might result from the restructuring of chromosomes through the loss, inversion, or crossing over of parts, would be the type of mutations explaining such systemic evolution: "The repatterning of the chromosome produces at once a new genetic system with all its consequences.... This 'at once' is an important point" (1940:245). The embryo he again conceived of as a developmental system; he argued that the origin of a new species, or of a new body plan, requires a considerable number of developmental processes to be changed so that a different organization would be brought about.

Goldschmidt drew heavily on the work of German paleontologists discussed in the previous chapter, such as Jaekel, Beurlen, and Schindewolf, who had in his view convincingly argued that higher-level, i.e., macroevolutionary, change is saltational and that evolution is orthogenetic, i.e., cyclical and goal directed. His systemic mutations, Goldschmidt claimed, would explain exactly this pattern of change, one documented by the fossil record according to the authorities he quoted. Systemic mutations that affected early stages of embryonic development would have exactly the effect that Jaekel, Beurlen, and Schindewolf had envisaged as well: they would set the embryo on a different developmental trajectory such that a new body plan would develop. In an early review paper, Goldschmidt (1924) had already sketched embryonic development in terms of a system of bifurcating developmental pathways, where systemic mutations would flip the switch from one side to the other of the bifurcations.

However, given that the embryo is a complex, highly integrated system, the reprogramming of early developmental stages through systemic mutations surely carried a high risk for a monstrous outcome that would in all likelihood result in the death of the organism at some later stage of development. What Goldschmidt needed was a theory of hopeful monsters, where the repatterning of embryonic development would result in the successful establishment of a major evolutionary lineage that is marked out by a new body plan. This is where Goldschmidt coupled the theory of orthogenesis with the concept of preadaptation. Orthogenesis would constrain systemic mutations in such a way that their effect would be preadaptive, allowing the embryo to survive: "If there are only a few avenues free for the action of mutational changes without knocking out of the order the whole properly balanced system of reactions, the probability is exceedingly high that repeated mutations will go in the same direction, will be orthogenetic." Goldschmidt went on to claim that paleontology offered "most beautiful examples" of orthogenetic evolutionary change, thus proving that orthogenesis is neither a consequence of Darwinian natural selection, nor the result of some mystical causes, but the necessary outcome of way genes interact during embryonic development (1933:544).

The hopeful monster, then, is one born from Goldschmidt's synthesis of comparative anatomy, embryology, paleontology (of the orthogenetic brand), and genetics (Goldschmidt, 1940:184). The hopeful monster emerges from a process where systemic mutations affecting early stages of embryonic development are constrained through orthogenetic evolution in such a way that the resulting monster is "preadapted to certain [new] modes of living" (1933:545). Any further questions? Not for Goldschmidt, the Herr Professor: "The facts and conclusions regarding the hopeful monster are so obvious that there is no need for a long discussion" (1940:392).

Hopeful Monsters Are Here to Stay

"Hopeful monsters are here to stay" was the title of a paper published in the journal _Theory in Biosciences_ in 2009 (Theiβen, 2009; see also Theiβen, 2006). This is not to say that modern evolutionary theory incorporates Goldschmidt's ideas lock, stock, and barrel. Goldschmidt thought systemic mutations to be preadaptive, due to the constraining nature of the orthogenetic evolutionary progress. The very concept of progressive evolution is in jeopardy in modern evolutionary theory: is a crab or a lobster more or less progressive than an eel or a salamander in their ascent along the ladder of life? Is a turtle with its shell and horny beak more or less progressive than a swiftly moving lizard whose jaws are furnished with daggerlike teeth? Progressionism in evolution is in trouble today, and so is orthogenesis, the theory that claims that major evolutionary lineages go through an evolutionary cycle that mirrors the life cycle of individual organisms. Modern philosophers of biology have developed arguments that are as sophisticated as they are complicated, on the basis of which a species, as well as a natural group of species of common ancestry, a so-called monophyletic taxon, can be considered to constitute an individual, i.e., to form a complex whole as some sort of superorganism. However, no modern evolutionary biologist would contend that such evolutionary lineages of monophyletic origin—such superorganisms—would undergo a cycle of vigorous youth, adulthood, senescence, and death as individual organisms do.

Even the concept of preadaptation has come under heavy fire for its teleological connotations, meaning that the concept of preadaptation implies some sort of goal directedness of evolutionary change, as Goldschmidt indeed believed to be the case. In order to avoid such untenable connotations, eminent Harvard evolutionist Stephen Jay Gould (1941–2002) and Yale paleobiologist Elisabeth Vrba (born 1942) replaced the concept of preadaptation with their own concept of exaptation (Gould and Vrba, 1982). Consider the evolution of feathers, and active flight, in birds. Feathers are highly complex structures, especially those in the wings of actively flying birds. It is inconceivable that such structures evolved in a single step, the consequence of some sort of preadaptive macromutation. Instead, feathers evolved through a series of intermediate steps, as is today particularly well demonstrated by the feathered dinosaurs from the Lower Cretaceous of Liaoning province in China, some 130 million years old. It is from such crude structures as are observed in the theropod dinosaur _Sinosauropteryx_ , for example, that feathers are thought to have evolved (Chen, Dong, and Zhen, 1998; Currie and Chen, 2001). It is tempting to say that these fossil integumentary filaments were preadaptations—that feathers evolved for flight. But that would mean that starting out from such simple integumentary structures as seen in _Sinosauropteryx_ , a series of transformational steps would have taken place with the goal to bring forth feathers of the kind that are required for active flight. There is, however, no evolutionary mechanism known that would impose such goal directedness on any such process of transformation. Rudimentary feathers must first have evolved in response to other adaptational pressures, most likely body insulation against heat loss in an endothermic organism, perhaps also with a display function in social behavior. Only later, at an advanced stage of their transformation, would feathers have been co-opted for flight. In retrospect, and avoiding any connotation of goal directedness, Gould and Vrba proposed to talk of early stages of feather evolution not as being preadapted for flight but instead as being an exaptation for flight. This renders ancestral feathers an adaptation for body insulation, which was later in descendants co-opted for flight.

I hope to have made it clear that much of the theoretical background on which Goldschmidt based his concept of hopeful monsters is no longer accepted by modern evolutionary biology: evolutionary progress, orthogenesis, and to some degree the concept of preadaptation are all out the window. Yet there awakened in the 1980s a renewed interest in Goldschmidt's theorizing, driven initially by Gould, who rejected the neo-Darwinian doctrine that—in his own words—"macroevolution (major structural transition) is nothing more than microevolution (flies in bottles) extended" (Gould, 1977a:22). Gould's provocative essay, "The Return of Hopeful Monsters" (1977a), sought to revitalize the basic ideas about macroevolution proposed by Goldschmidt, the "Jewish refugee from Hitler's decimation of German science" (1977a:22). Gould's interest in Goldschmidt was spurred by his dissatisfaction with the modern synthesis of evolutionary theory. The received wisdom was that all major evolutionary transitions can ultimately be reduced to microevolutionary processes playing out at the level of interbreeding populations. The major focus of evolutionary research consequently was on population genetics and the mechanisms of speciation, i.e., the origin of new species from local populations or subspecies. Given the importance of genetic isolation for local populations to be able to evolve into a new species, geographic as well as ecological, behavioral, physiological, and anatomical aspects of speciation were intensively studied as well, especially with respect to their role in establishing genetic barriers. What Gould found most prominently missing in the modern synthesis of evolutionary theory was an incorporation of developmental, i.e., embryological, investigations of major structural transitions—precisely the issue Goldschmidt had placed center stage in the second part of his classic 1940 book. Indeed, Gould pushed for a reprint of Goldschmidt's _The Material Basis of Evolution_ , which appeared in 1982 through Yale University Press. Gould contributed a foreword to this edition in which he stated, "Goldschmidt's vision was sound—and it supplied (or resupplied) an essential ingredient that strict Darwinism had expunged from evolutionary theory: the idea that evolution works through a hierarchy of distinct levels with important independent properties" (Gould in Goldschmidt, 1982:xl).

Population genetics is one level—but not the only level—at which evolution plays out. The 1980s-era revival of interest in Goldschmidt's macroevolutionary theories was motivated by several factors, at the core of which was the concept of a hierarchical structure of life. The gene became recognized as a dynamic complex whole that forms part of the genome, a dynamic complex whole of a higher order, i.e., of greater inclusiveness, than the gene. From there on come the cell, tissue, organ, organ system, organism—each complex wholes encased one within the other in an order of ascending, more inclusive hierarchical levels. When Gould speaks of "independent properties," he means emergent properties that characterize each level of this organismic hierarchy. This means, as it did for Goldschmidt, that the complex whole at any level of the organismic hierarchy is more than the mere sum of its parts, with properties of its own that are not simply the aggregate properties of its parts.

Gould published a masterpiece titled _Ontogeny and Phylogeny_ (1977b) through Harvard University Press that not only presented a superb historical sketch related to that topic but also, perhaps even more importantly, charted a new direction in which to investigate how embryonic development relates to major structural changes in anatomy. It emphasized the role of genetic control over developmental processes and the importance of changing the relative speed of development in organs or organ systems, as is evident in major evolutionary transitions—issues no doubt close to Goldschmidt's heart. The issue of how studies of embryonic development, at both the genetic and morphological level, can be brought to bear on an understanding of major evolutionary transformations of anatomical structures evolved into a highly innovative branch of research called evolutionary developmental biology. This type of interest and research experienced an initial flourishing during the 1980s, with many books following Gould's pioneering classic. Coupled with these developments was an intensified interest in the molecular basis of homeotic mutations, as when a leg instead of an antenna grows out of a fruit fly's head. A homeotic mutation was defined as a "mutation that causes one structure to develop in a place where another structure would normally develop" (Dietrich, 2003:71). Indeed, Goldschmidt had himself studied homeotic mutations in fruit flies such as the _podoptera_ and _tetraltera_ mutants (Dietrich, 2000). In _podoptera_ , legs develop in the place of wings, whereas in _tetraltera_ , halteres develop in the place of wings (halteres are "small wing-like appendages that are located on the third thoracic segments of _Drosophila_ and act as balancing organs") (Dietrich, 2003:71).

**4.2.** Most frequently cited example of hopeful monster in vertebrates. _Top_ , Skull of Indian python _Python molurus_ , with single (undivided) upper jawbone (maxilla). _Bottom_ , Skull of Round Island boa _Casarea dussumieri_ , with bipartite upper jawbone (maxilla).

_Image courtesy of University of Texas High-Resolution X-ray CT Facility; imaging sponsored by NSF grants IIS-0208675 (_ top _) and EF-0334961 (_ bottom _)_.

Besides the burgeoning field of evo-devo and the related molecular research into homeotic mutations, Gould exploited other aspects of comparative embryology and anatomy in support of saltational evolutionary changes, as are implied by Goldschmidt's concept of hopeful monsters. What Gould used in this context in his seminal 1977 monograph were examples that leave no logical space to invoke one of those famous Darwinian intermediates that would link an ancestral with a descendant condition of form yet that remained prominently missing in the fossil record. One such example had at the time just been popularized by comparative and functional anatomist Thomas H. Frazzetta (1934–2015) from the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, in his book on _Complex Adaptations in Evolving Populations_ (1975:147–150). Frazzetta had already commented on the strange booid snakes broadly related to boas and pythons in an earlier article titled "From Hopeful Monsters to Bolyerine Snakes" (1970). Bolyerine snakes comprise four species in three genera. Two of these species—one of which is now believed to be extinct—are from Round Island just north of Mauritius, and the other two species are from Borneo and the Malay Peninsula, respectively.

Representing "the most often cited vertebrate examples of Goldschmidt's (1940) concept of the evolutionary 'hopeful monster,'" these snakes show a feature that is unique among tetrapods, which is a bipartition of the upper jaw, or maxillary bone (Cundall and Irish, 1989:570). In the Round Island boa, this bipartition of the maxillary resulted in complex, correlated changes in the way the upper jaw has to be suspended from the braincase. The functional significance of such a bipartition of the maxillary bone seems to be related to "selection pressures arising from limited prey diversity," requiring these snakes to feed on prey that is difficult to handle, such as scincoid lizards with a smooth yet hard and slippery skin (Cundall and Irish, 1989:569). From a developmental and evolutionary perspective, it is nevertheless clear that the maxillary bone can be a single undivided element, or it can be split into two halves. No third intermediate seems to be possible. In Gould's words: "How can a jawbone be half broken?" (1977a:28). But then, how did the maxillary bone get subdivided in a single evolutionary step in these boylerine snakes? The answer lies in embryonic development. The snakes in question are so rare that embryos have never become available for investigation. But it turns out that in closely related species, the maxillary bone ossifies from two ossification centers, an anterior and a posterior one. An ossification center is simply a spot where calcification of bone is initiated and from which the formation of bone spreads through a membrane (as in the dermis layer of the skin, as is the case for the maxillary bone) or through degenerating cartilage (as in the endoskeleton). Whereas in related boas and pythons the ossifications that spread from two separate ossification centers eventually fuse to form a single unified maxillary bone, such fusion apparently never happens in the Round Island boa as a result of a simple truncation of development. A simple change of the relative timing of developmental events, in this case a termination of development before the fusion of an anterior and posterior ossification, results in a major structural change: a bipartite upper jaw (Irish and Alberch, 1989).

Goldschmidt's hopeful monsters cut much deeper than that, however. They go to the very heart of how to understand, indeed to think about, evolutionary change and innovation, with the jaws of vertebrates again providing a recently much discussed paradigmatic example (another is the turtle shell, which will be discussed in detail in the next chapter). Consider Darwin's twin mechanisms of evolution: variation and natural selection. Evolutionary success relative to natural selection is measured through relative reproductive success: as it generates more offspring, the better-adapted organism passes on its genes more frequently than the less well-adapted organism. Evolutionary success is thus cashed out at the adult, sexually mature stage. Variation, in turn, always affects only structures already in existence. Variation and natural selection can never create something entirely new or entirely different—something that has no counterpart in the ancestral condition of form. Variation and natural selection can only affect, and transform, something that is already in existence in the ancestor. The descendant may differ from the ancestor in its anatomy, but the difference can only be one of some greater or lesser transformation of a part (organ) that was present in its primitive form in the ancestor as well—the same but different. These parts we have earlier identified as homologues: an organ or organ system that is present in the ancestor is also present in the descendant, although it may have been transformed through descent with modification, the latter driven by variation and natural selection as explained by Darwin. As Darwin's friend and bulldog, Thomas H. Huxley (1825–1895), said in his famous Croonian lecture on the vertebrate skull, delivered to the Royal Society of London on June 17, 1858, "That there is nothing really aberrant in nature; that the most widely different organisms are connected by a hidden bond; that an apparently new and isolated structure will prove, when its characters are thoroughly sifted, to be only a modification of something which existed before" (1858:382).

There is thus never anything genuinely new in nature; everything we see is but a modification or transformation of something that already existed before. This Darwinian conception of evolutionary change can be called the transformationist paradigm, and it is this paradigm that calls for a continuous series of intermediates between two conditions of form—a requirement poorly fulfilled by the fossil record. Make no mistake; there are in the fossil record beautiful transitional lineages documented both for invertebrates and vertebrates. Among the latter, the synapsid (mammal-like reptile) to mammal transition, or the theropod dinosaur to bird transition most prominently come to mind. Still, the question may be asked whether the transformationist paradigm is indeed sufficient to explain the evolution of a tree of life that encompasses everything from the amoeba to humans.

Since Huxley's lecture was about the vertebrate skull, we might as well ask, what about the jaws? What was that ancestral structure that transformed to become the jaws of the so-called jawed vertebrates, the gnathostomes? As already mentioned, the earliest and most primitive fishes had no jaws. Many of these jawless agnathans ( _Agnatha_ ) were bottom-dwelling filter feeders, whose modern descendants are the lampreys and the hagfish. The presence of jaws thus marks out the jawed vertebrates as a natural, i.e., monophyletic, group, the gnathostomes ( _Gnathostomata_ ). But whereas the agnathans had no jaws, they had gill arches supporting the gills. The gills in turn develop along gill slits that perforate the wall of the esophagus. Gills serve the function of oxygen exchange—breathing—but as the water passed across the gill filaments, the gills would also filter planktonic food particles that in these extinct agnathans would be retained in the esophagus and passed through the intestinal tract. A celebrated theory on the origin of the vertebrate jaws goes back to Carl Bogislaus Reichert (1811–1883), who in 1837 suggested that the upper and lower jaw of the jawed vertebrates (gnathostomes) correspond to the upper and lower halves of the first gill arch (called the first visceral arch by Reichert) of agnathans. Seen from a transformationist perspective, this would have to mean that the already present first gill arch of ancestral agnathans transformed to become the jaws of the descendants, the gnathostomes, while the second gill arch (called the second visceral arch by Reichert) transformed to provide support elements for the suspension of the jaws from the braincase. This is how something like a shark would have evolved from something like an extinct (Paleozoic) agnathan. There is only one problem with this account of Reichert's theory: Reichert did not reach his conclusions by comparing fossil or living agnathans with fossil or modern gnathostomes. Instead, he reached his conclusions based on comparative developmental (embryological) investigation of a series of gnathostomes, in particular toad, chicken, sheep, and pig embryos. The gill arch skeleton in both the living hagfish and lampreys is highly specialized, forming a basketlike structure that is hardly directly comparable to the jaws and gill arch skeleton in something like a shark, i.e., a primitive gnathostome. But the gill arch skeleton in fossil agnathans was specialized in its own ways too. There are no simple, unspecialized structures in adult ancestors from which, through stepwise transformation, the upper and lower jaws of a shark could be derived. Every organism, whether extinct or extant, has to be adapted in some way to the special circumstances of its environment. Eminent German comparative anatomist Dietrich Starck (1908–2001) from the University of Frankfurt am Main, who spent a lifetime researching problems concerning the vertebrate head and skull, summed up his frustration as follows: there is no species known, fossil or living, in which the first two gill arches appear in a simple, unspecialized form from which the upper and lower jaws of gnathostomes and their support structures could easily be derived; convincing intermediate conditions of form that would document the evolution of jaws have likewise not been identified in either fossil or extant vertebrates (Starck, 1979:152ff.).

The problem of the transformationist paradigm results—to use the terminology of Adolf Remane—from the nonexistence of a zero-value ancestor, one that would show no special adaptations of any kind to any particular environment, and for this reason would not have been viable ( _Nullwertahne_ ) (Remane, 1948). To derive a transformed, newly adapted structure from an ancestral one requires the ancestral structure to be primitive, or generalized, in all aspects relative to the derived structure. Such a totally generalized ancestral structure would, however, not be adapted to any specific mode of life. But every living organism must somehow be adapted to some sort of mode of life. This renders such a generalized, zero-value ancestor that is not adapted to any specific mode of life a biological impossibility. Conversely, if an ancestral form must to some degree be specialized, or adapted, to pursue a certain mode of life, then it must in some way get rid of its specializations, i.e., adaptations, before it can serve as the starting point for a transformation that takes it in a different direction, i.e., toward a different specialization and adaptation to be realized in the descendant form. However, such a roundabout way of evolutionary transformation does not need to happen if evolutionary transformation starts from early embryonic stages, which have not yet reached a notable degree of specialization.

In recent research, Shigeru Kuratani and his collaborators at the RIKEN Center for Developmental Biology in Japan used sophisticated molecular techniques in an attempt to shed new light on the origin of vertebrate jaws. The starting point for these studies was the insight that "the fossil record has not revealed any ancestral animals with an undifferentiated series of gill arches in their pharynx," i.e., the fossil record has not yielded a zero-value ancestral condition of the gill arch skeleton. Consequently, "an ancestral animal with simple gill arches... is purely hypothetical" (Kuratani, 2004:336; see also Janvier, 1996). The studies conducted at the RIKEN Center targeted the cell populations that in the embryo would give rise to what the authors called an oral apparatus, of which the upper and lower jaws of gnathostomes are parts. During very early stages of development, the head region in a lamprey and a gnathostome show a similar regional patterning at the cellular level, conditioned by shared genes, an indication of homology of these discrete cell populations and hence of a common ancestry of vertebrates. However, the same cell population that in gnathostomes gives rise to the upper and lower jaws in the lamprey ammocete (larva) forms mucocartilaginous structures in the lower lip and in the velum, the latter a functional part of the complex filter-feeding apparatus that characterizes the ammocete (Kuratani, 2005; Kuratani et al., 2012). During their very early development, the lamprey and gnathostomes thus share a closely similar condition of form. During later stages of development, however, the lamprey and gnathostomes deviate from one another as the cell populations contributing to the oral apparatus follow radically different developmental pathways: the cell population that gives rise to the jaws of gnathostomes gives rise to structures in the lower lip and velum in the ammocete instead. This is exactly the classical pattern of development that embryologist Baer had already identified in his immortal monograph of 1828. What this says, however, is not that an ancestral structure, adapted to a certain mode of life and tested by natural selection in terms of the relative reproductive success of the carrier of that structure, is being transformed into a descendant, differently adapted structure—from gill arch to jaw, for example. Instead, what this says is that, starting from an early developmental stage, an only rudimentarily differentiated precursor of some ancestral structure—such as a topologically circumscribed population of cells—can, as a consequence of changes (mutations) in the cascade of regulatory genes, be reprogrammed so as to continue differentiation along a different developmental trajectory in the descendant, with the consequence that a true evolutionary novelty arises.

Technically speaking, an evolutionary novelty was defined in those recent studies on the evolution of vertebrate jaws as "a new structure that arises by overriding ancestral developmental constraints, so that morphological homology is lost between the novel and ancestral structures" (Kuratani, 2004:339, following Wagner and Müller, 2002). That may sound difficult, but it is not. Take an ancestral cell population in the lamprey's head, destined to develop to form structures in the lower lip and velum in the larval oral apparatus. Such destiny of those cells is written into the lamprey's genome; in other words, genetic instructions guide and constrain these cells to form structures in the lower lip and velum of the ammocete. Now let evolutionary transformation override this ancestral developmental program and reprogram that same cell population in the descendant to develop into the upper and lower jaws of gnathostomes. The jaws then represent an evolutionary novelty. It makes little sense to say that structures in the lower lip and velum of the lamprey ammocete are equivalent, i.e., homologous, to the upper and lower jaws of gnathostomes. It makes more sense to leave such tenuous homology assessments aside and to say that gnathostome jaws represent an evolutionary novelty that evolved through the reprogramming of an ancestral cell population so as to develop (differentiate) along a novel developmental pathway (see Havstad, Assis, and Rieppel, 2015, for more discussion).

To understand structural transformation from such a perspective, i.e., a reprogramming of developmental pathways in a way that creates evolutionary novelties, might be called an emergentist paradigm, one that contrasts with the transformationist paradigm sketched above. The transformationist approach seeks to explain morphological evolution as a stepwise transformation of some fully differentiated (adult) ancestral structure into a fully differentiated (adult) descendant condition of form through variation and natural selection. This is how ancestors and descendants get strung together through a series of intermediates. The emergentist approach seeks to explain the emergence of evolutionary novelties through the reprogramming of the development of an ancestral embryonic rudiment in the descendant lineage. Intermediate conditions of form at the adult, i.e., reproductive, stage are not to be expected on the basis of the emergentist paradigm. Ancestor and descendant may remain separated by a morphologic gap. This is the core idea of Goldschmidt's concept of hopeful monsters, and one that would entail saltational evolutionary change.

What is left in modern molecular evolutionary developmental studies of Goldschmidt's core idea of a hopeful monster? The best current answer, I think, comes from Günter Wagner (born 1954) from Yale University, who in 2014 published a book about _Homology, Genes, and Evolutionary Innovation_. Wagner is almost uniquely positioned to answer that question. He is as much at home in mathematical modeling of natural selection as he is in studies on the genetic, or more broadly molecular, mechanisms that guide and constrain embryonic development. Given such an unusually broad perspective, Wagner again confirms that major evolutionary innovations are not the result of variation and natural selection that play out at the population and species levels. Of course, evolution does occur at the population and species levels as a consequence of variation and natural selection, but Wagner calls this level of evolution adaptive. The Darwinian twin mechanisms of evolution promote the adaption of species, and their populations, to their specific conditions of life—to their local environment. To understand major evolutionary innovations, those that bring about novel body plans, one must, according to Wagner, turn to embryonic development and the genetic molecular mechanisms that underlie it. New body plans originate through the evolution of new gene regulatory networks that bring about, and maintain through successive generations, evolutionary innovations. One of the most striking evolutionary innovations, also chosen by Wagner to bring his point across, is the turtle shell.

In the next chapter I will recount how the protracted debate about the evolution of the turtle shell continuously shifted back and forth between the transformationist and the emergentist paradigms. In sketching the history of the alternative theories invoked to explain the evolution of the turtle shell, it is important to understand that this (still ongoing) debate is not just fueled by whatever observations have been made or are being made by the use of ever more sophisticated techniques. The debate is also, and just as much, fueled by the way the observer looks at nature in search for evidence bearing on the problem of the origin of the turtle shell—whether from a transformationist or an emergentist perspective. As embryologist Baer put it, research is an interplay between observation and critical reflection. Research is concept driven to start with. The results of research in turn enrich the concepts that triggered it in the first place—a point again forcefully made by Günter Wagner. Some researchers think evolutionary transformations are best studied by the comparison of adult organisms represented as fossils at successive time intervals in the fossil record. The conceptual justification for that approach is the claim that fossils are the only direct evidence we have of evolutionary transformation. The result will be to bring a transformationist perspective to the study of fossils. Other researchers think that evolutionary transformations are best studied by the comparison of embryonic development and its underlying genetic causes. The conceptual justification for that approach is the claim that what is inherited from parents and ancestors are not adult traits but rather developmental programs that regulate the development of adult traits. The result will be to bring an emergentist perspective to the study of evolutionary developmental biology.

Note

1. Appendices to the questionnaire issued by the Military Government, signed by Lenz on July 22, 1947, University Archive Göttingen (GWDG), Kuratoriumakte Lenz.

**5.1.** Relation of shoulder blade (scapula) to rib cage in ( _top_ ) green iguana _Iguana iguana_ and ( _bottom_ ) northern map turtle _Graptemys geographica_.

_Photograph by John Weinstein, The Field Museum_.

**The Turtle Shell**

5

Prizewinning Research on the Turtle Shell

BORN IN NEW YORK CITY, HERBERT RUCKES (1895–1965) PURSUED A double-tracked research program is his capacity as professor at the Grove City College in New York and research associate at the AMNH. The bulk of his publications turns on insect systematics, but his early work on the turtle skeleton won him the A. Cressy Morrison Prize of the New York Academy of Sciences in 1928 (Anonymous, 1966). It was in the _Annals of the New York Academy of Sciences_ that Ruckes published his startling observations concerning a highly puzzling feature of the turtle body plan. Reach backward with your right arm across your left shoulder, while rolling the latter up and down. This will allow you to feel your shoulder blade moving. As you inspire air into your lungs, you will feel your chest expand; it contracts again as you exhale. These breathing movements are supported by muscles associated with the rib cage and assisted by the diaphragm. The ribs that form the rib cage articulate on the backbone, the vertebral column. From there, they curve around the body to meet the sternum in a cartilaginous connection. This means, however, that as you feel your shoulder blade moving up and down, it must be located superficially, i.e., outside the rib cage. Indeed, the shoulder blade, also known as scapula, is located outside the rib cage in all tetrapods: in amphibians, reptiles, birds, and mammals—but not turtles. They are the single exception in which the scapula is located inside the rib cage. How could that relocation of the scapula evolve? As with the bipartite upper jawbone in bolyerine snakes discussed in the previous chapter, there seem to be no intermediate conditions of form possible. The maxillary bone is either a single element, as in all (other) tetrapods, or bipartite, as in bolyerine snakes; the scapula lies either outside the rib cage, as in all (other) tetrapods, or inside the rib cage as in turtles. There seems to be no third alternative available. Could the turtle shell, the structure of which is responsible for the location of the scapula inside the rib cage, be a case of saltational macroevolution, having brought forth a hopeful monster in the distant geologic past?

As is customary in science, Ruckes noted in his classic paper of 1929 that he was by no means the first to tackle this difficult problem. He duly cited, among others to be introduced later, the honorable D. M. S. Watson from the University College London, who in his 1914 paper on the putative turtle ancestor _Eunotosaurus_ from the Permian of the Karoo of South Africa had claimed—in the tradition of the transformationist paradigm—that during the transition to turtles, the shoulder girdle had migrated backward along the vertebral column, thus dislocating the scapula to a posterior position inside the rib cage. However, "I believe this interpretation is wholly incorrect and that the girdles do not migrate at all," was Ruckes's objection (1929:85). Instead, seeking a solution to this riddle in the study of the embryonic development of modern turtles, he discovered developmental changes that affect the relative rate of growth and differentiation of various organ systems in a way that results in a topological relocation not only of the ribs relative to the scapula but also affecting other parts of the turtle body plan. Needless to say, Ruckes's pioneering investigations were the foundation on which present-day developmental studies that use ever more sophisticated techniques have been built (Burke, 2015:83).

To understand those studies, and hence to get a grip on the intricacies of the evolution of the turtle body plan, it is first necessary to understand the nature of the turtle shell, which is a complex structure indeed. The fact that the scapula comes to lie inside the rib cage in turtles is intimately connected with the development and evolution of the carapace. What is the anatomy of the turtle shell? To fully understand its complexity from an evolutionary perspective, it will be necessary to return to the endoskeleton versus exoskeleton distinction introduced earlier. The fully developed turtle shell comprises two principal components. The armor that covers the back, the dorsal side of the trunk, is called the carapace. The armor that covers the belly, the ventral side of the trunk, is called the plastron. Carapace and plastron are linked on either side of the body by a bony bridge. It is from in front and behind of that bony bridge that the fore- and hind limbs project out from the shell. The gait of turtles looks so clumsy as they move across land because this lateral bony bridge restricts the range of movement of the humerus (upper arm) and femur (upper leg) (Walker, 1979). All reptiles have a keratinized epidermis, which renders the outer layer of the skin an efficient barrier to evaporative water loss. The bony carapace, as well as the bony plastron (and of course the bones in the lateral bridge as well), are consequently covered by keratinized scutes. Confusion often arises from the fact that the sutures between these horny scutes do not also match the sutures formed by the underlying bony elements that compose the carapace and plastron. As one inspects the carapace of a skeletonized turtle, it is thus important to accurately distinguish the interdigitating sutures between the bony plates that together compose the carapace and the grooves that are marked out on the surface of those bony plates, which indicate the contours of the overlying keratinized scutes now removed. The story of the relative position of the scapula and the ribs, and indeed the intricacies that relate to the development and evolution of the turtle body plan, relate to the carapace more than to the plastron.

The plastron protects a swimming turtle from predators attacking from below. It is composed of a series of bony plates, all but one of which come in pairs. At the anterior end of the plastron, the paired epiplastra embrace the anterior tip of the unpaired entoplastron. These three anterior elements in the plastron are believed to derive from ancestral elements in the shoulder girdle. The primitive tetrapod shoulder girdle comprises exoskeletal (dermal) and endoskeletal (chondral) elements. The dermal elements are the ventral clavicle and the dorsal cleithrum. The clavicles of the left and right pectoral girdle are connected with one another through the interclavicle. The chondral elements, lying deep to the dermal ones, are the ventral coracoid and the dorsal scapula. It is believed that in the turtle plastron, the epiplastra represent the clavicles and the entoplastron represents the interclavicle. Behind those three anterior elements there follow a series of paired bony plates, which, proceeding from front to back, are called the hyoplastron, mesoplastron, hypoplastron, and xiphiplastron. Of these, the mesoplastron can either be missing, or one or two pairs of mesoplastra may be present. Other variations may exist as well. Although it is believed that the epi- and entoplastron represent elements of the ancestral pectoral girdle, the remainder of the plastron is generally derived from gastral ribs (Zangerl, 1969). The latter have nothing to do with the true ribs that grow from and articulate with the axial skeleton (vertebral column). The gastral ribs are not preformed in cartilage, as are the true ribs, but are of dermal origin. They are barshaped elements of variable composition that ossify in the deep dermis layer of the skin, forming a gastral basket in the ventral body wall. The bony elements in the lateral bridge are likewise simple dermal ossifications that form directly in the deep layer of the skin (dermis) without being preformed in cartilage.

**5.2.** Turtle shell. _Left_ , Carapace of snapping turtle _Chelydra serpentina. Right_ , Plastron of yellow-footed tortoise _Geochelone denticulata_. Note tips of ribs protruding from costal plates in carapace.

_Drawing by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

The anatomy of the carapace is, by comparison, much more complicated, creating headaches and seemingly endless debate among comparative anatomists, developmental biologists, and paleontologists. It too is composed of a series of bony plates, some of which have a complex origin, as will be discussed below. But let us sort out the geometry of the carapace first before talking about its embryonic development. The anteriormost element in the carapace is the broad nuchal pate, characterized by an anterior embayment. It shields the posterior part of the neck of turtles, hence its name. It is the first element to ossify in the embryonic turtle carapace, and it does so, oddly enough, in two distinct phases of ossification, without having been preformed in cartilage (Gilbert et al., 2007). Behind the nuchal plate follows a series of eight oblong rectangular elements running down the midline of the carapace. These are called the neural plates because they are underlain by the neural arches of the trunk vertebrae. A reptile—indeed a tetrapod—vertebra generally comprises two parts, the vertebral body, or centrum, and the neural arch that sits on top of the centrum, with the two often fused in the adult stage. The vertebral centrum underlies the spinal cord, the latter being accommodated in a furrow that is hollowed out on the upper surface of the centrum. The neural arch roofs over the spinal cord; it forms two pedicels that on either side of the spinal cord reach down to come to rest on the surface of the centrum, to the left and the right of the furrow containing the spinal cord. That way, the spinal cord is safely encased in a bony tube formed jointly by the centrum and the neural arch of successive vertebrae. Dorsally, the neural arch expands into a neural spine, to which attach muscles that help bend the vertebral column. If you run your finger down the midline of your back, you will feel a series of bumps, each representing the tip of the neural spines of the trunk vertebrae. In the trunk region of turtles, the neural spine of each dorsal vertebra remains very low and appears to be capped by the neural plate of the carapace. At the back end on the midline of the carapace sits the pygal plate, which covers the pelvic region and the base of the tail.

On either side of the neural plates, and alternating with them, expand the costal plates. They form the major part of the carapace, shielding the dorsal aspect of a turtle's trunk. The name of the costal plates derives from the fact that they are intimately related to the ribs of the trunk region, i.e., the dorsal ribs. Given that what was said above, this leaves only the periphery of the carapace to be dealt with. That is composed of a series of so-called marginal plates, aligned alongside the costal plates. Many fossil turtles, but also some living turtles—the snapping turtle and the soft-shelled turtles are good examples—are unable to hide their head and neck under the carapace. Those that can do so developed specialized articulations between some of the neck vertebrae, and they come in two kinds. The side-necked or pleurodire turtles simply bend the head and neck sideways to hide them under the nuchal and adjacent marginal plates. The hidden-necked or cryptodire turtles, in contrast, are able to pull the head back under the nuchal plate by folding the neck, and with it the series of cervical vertebrae, into a complicated, vertically positioned S-shaped curve. Those turtles, both living and fossil, which cannot bring head and neck under the carapace can be assigned to the pleurodire or cryptodire lineage on the basis of fundamental differences that characterize the jaw adductor muscle system and correlated skull features in those two lineages of turtle evolution. Such classification of turtles leaves only a handful of early fossil turtles from the Triassic to be classified outside the pleurodire–cryptodire dichotomy (Gaffney, 1975b; but see Joyce, 2007, for a dissenting view).

To understand why the carapace is such a headache-inducing structure, it is further necessary to understand the ubiquitous potential for the dermis layer of the vertebrate skin to form bone. Some of the earliest vertebrates, Silurian and Devonian jawless fishes (agnathans), some 400 million years old, were encased in a heavy dermal bony armor. However, a trout or a salmon are also entirely covered by bony scales. Much thinner and of a simpler structure than those of their ancestors, these delicate and translucent scales of trout and salmon are nonetheless genuine dermal ossifications, i.e., bony plates that ossify in the deep (dermis) layer of the skin without having first been preformed in cartilage. Such dermal ossifications also occur in tetrapods—just think of the armadillo—yet here they are no longer called scales but osteoderms instead, from _osteo_ , "bone," and _derm_ , which signifies their formation in the dermis, the deep layer of the skin. The cyamodontoid placodonts already touched on come to mind again, with a carapace entirely composed of osteoderms. Many other reptile lineages, most notably crocodiles, sport osteoderms as well. In one of the earliest known turtles, _Proganochelys_ from the Late Triassic of Germany, osteoderms formed in the skin covering the limbs, neck, and tail. How about the carapace? Did it evolve from an osteoderm cover shielding the back of the turtle ancestor? This is exactly were the controversy begins—or rather, where it began more than 200 years ago.

Before delving into that history and debate, let us add just one more layer of complexity to do justice to the intriguing structure of the turtle carapace, and return to the distinction of the exoskeleton from the endoskeleton, previously introduced in the discussion of the reptile skull and its significance for reptile classification. The heavy dermal armor covering the jawless fishes from eons past, the scales of the salmon and the trout, and the osteoderms of crocodiles and armadillos are all exoskeletal in nature: they ossify directly in the dermis layer of the skin, with no cartilaginous precursors. In contrast, the elements of the endoskeleton—the vertebrae, the ribs, and the limb bones—are first preformed in cartilage that may, during later development, be replaced either partially or wholly by bone in a process called ossification. But the distinction of exo- versus endoskeleton cuts much deeper. Teeth are exoskeletal in nature, with a crown composed of tissues that are different from bone. The bulk of the tooth crown is composed of dentin that encloses the pulp cavity. The microscopic structure of dentin is different from that of bone and also from that of enamel. Superficially, the dentin is covered by a hypermineralized and hence shiny layer of enamel. In mammals, cementum forms the root of the tooth, with the latter anchored in an alveolus located on a bony tooth-bearing element such as the dentary of the lower jaw. Although things are a bit more complicated in the world out there, it suffices for the present purpose to say that bone, dentin, and enamel are the three fundamental tissues that together make up the primitive exoskeleton. The heavy dermal armor that covered the body of many Paleozoic jawless fishes (agnathans), for example, is three layered, comprising a basal, inner layer of bone, an intermediate layer of dentin, and a superficial layer of enamel. The same three-layered structure is characteristic of the heavy body scales of Paleozoic and early Mesozoic ancestors of today's salmon and trout. What renders the scales of salmon and trout so thin and translucent is simply the evolutionary loss of the enamel and dentin layers, and the thinning of the bony layer. However, bringing dentin and enamel into play in the discussion of the exoskeleton introduces some important implications.

To recapitulate, the vertebrate skin is built up from the superficial (ectodermal) epidermis and the deeper (mesodermal) dermis. Dermal bone forms in the dermis—no problem there. But enamel is formed by cells (ameloblasts) that derive from the innermost layer of cells of the epidermis. Dentin, however, is formed by cells (odontoblasts) that derive from a distinct part of the embryonic central nervous system, the neural crest. Those neural crest cells destined to become odontoblasts migrate through the embryonic body to assemble just below boundary membrane that separates the epidermis from the dermis. Once arrived, they transform to become odontoblasts that start to produce a precursor substance of dentin; the latter in turn triggers the transformation of the innermost epidermis cells into ameloblasts producing enamel. Thus, the development of an armor plate in Paleozoic agnathans, or of a three-layered scale in a distant salmon ancestor, is necessarily tied to the boundary membrane between epidermis (ectoderm) and dermis (mesoderm), as they are composed of layers of bone, dentin, and enamel. This is because it is on either side of this boundary membrane that the enamel- and dentin-producing cells are aligned. In more general terms, then, the evolutionary origin of the vertebrate exoskeleton was primitively tied to the ectoderm–mesoderm interface—to the boundary membrane between epidermis and dermis in the skin. Such, of course, is not the case for the endoskeleton. Considered from that perspective, the exoskeleton and endoskeleton in vertebrates constitute two fundamentally different, indeed independent, evolutionary systems. The distinction of exoskeleton versus endoskeleton is one drawn from an evolutionary perspective, with important consequences to be discussed below. Developmental biologists who focus on the molecular aspects of bone formation, rather than on the evolutionary origin of bony structures, often do not endorse the endo-versus exoskeleton distinction because it falls outside the conceptual space of their research. They are happy to just talk about chondral bone, which is the bone replacing cartilage during ossification, and dermal (or membrane) bone, which is bone formed in the dermis layer of the skin (see, e.g., Burke, 2015).

In earlier research on the evolutionary origin of hard tissues such as cartilage, bone, dentin, and enamel, it was thought that the endoskeleton was the first to evolve in the history of vertebrates, whereas the exoskeleton had a later evolutionary origin. That would render the endoskeleton an evolutionarily more primitive structure than the exoskeleton. However, extensive comparative analysis throughout vertebrates, but especially in the skull of bony fishes (osteichthyans), has indicated that the exo- and endoskeleton evolved as separate systems throughout vertebrate history, never getting mixed up or intermingled (Patterson, 1977). It is the turtle shell that, once again, seems to provide the only vexing exception—or does it? The problem is that as one turns the carapace of a skeletonized turtle upside down to look at its inside, it becomes clear that the costal plates are intimately connected with the ribs of the trunk; indeed, they seem to incorporate them. The same is true for the neural spines of the trunk (dorsal) vertebrae and the neural plates of the carapace, although this is less easily appreciated without cutting through the bony structures.

The Turtle Carapace Takes Shape

The first treatise on the anatomy of turtles appeared in the year 1687, authored by Giovanni Caldesi, physician to the Grand Duke of Tuscany (Adler, 2007:139). However, the debate over the nature of that most conspicuous structure on which Linnaeus, in the first edition of his _Systema Naturae_ of 1735, based his diagnosis of turtles, the _testa munitum_ —the protective shell—started with the doyen of comparative vertebrate osteology, the celebrated Georges Cuvier from the Paris National Natural History Museum. He was among the first to take a stab at an interpretation of the turtle carapace. His treatise on the animal kingdom, _Le Règne Animal Distribué d'Après son Organisation_ , was designed not only to serve as a natural history of animal diversity but also to provide a foundation for comparative anatomy in general. In the second volume, published in Paris in 1817, he defined the turtles as reptiles with a heart that has two auricles, and whose body, supported by four legs, is completely enclosed by two plates, or shields, formed by the ribs and the breastbone (sternum), respectively (Cuvier, 1817:5, 6ff.). Further into the text, he elaborated on the carapace, noting that it is formed by the trunk (dorsal) ribs, eight in number on either side, that are broadened and hence meet each other along their length in interdigitating sutures. He recognized the participation of the neural arches of the trunk vertebrae in the carapace, as he thought that they form the neural plates that mediate between the costal plates of either side. The marginal plates of the carapace, as well as the plates in the lateral bridge, Cuvier somewhat vaguely referred to as being formed by that part of the ribs, which in other tetrapods would attach to the sternum. The plastron, correspondingly, he considered to correspond to the sternum of other tetrapods. In Cuvier's time, the distinction of exoskeleton and endoskeleton was not yet consistently drawn, but taking our present-day discussion presented above into his account, Cuvier's interpretation renders the turtle shell an entirely endoskeletal formation because the vertebral neural arches, the ribs, and the sternum are all preformed in cartilage in the embryo. The endoskeletal carapace and plastron were, in Cuvier's assessment, covered by skin, the latter producing the keratinized scutes that superficially cover the carapace and plastron. Today we know that the keratinized scutes derive from the epidermis layer of the skin only, which might suggest an exoskeletal derivation of the carapace instead. Endoskeleton versus exoskeleton: this distinction lies at the heart of the debate over the evolution of the turtle carapace, from Cuvier to the present day.

The interpretation of the turtle shell offered by Cuvier in 1817 removed some ambiguity that had persisted in his first description of the turtle carapace, published in 1799. In the latter we read, "There are eight dorsal vertebrae: they are all fused, with the ribs and the carapace, into a single immobile structure" (1799:173). This does not exactly say that the vertebrae and ribs actually make up the carapace, but rather that the vertebrae and ribs are fused with the carapace. But further in the same text, Cuvier clarified that the neural plates of the carapace are formed by the underlying vertebrae and the costal plates by a broadening of the ribs that come to meet in sutures, while the marginal plates and those of the lateral bridge he derived from the tips of the ribs (1799:211ff.). Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire (1772–1884) was professor of comparative anatomy at the Paris National Natural History Museum and had been instrumental in bringing Cuvier to this institution. Unlike Cuvier, Geoffroy decided to follow Napoleon Bonaparte on his ultimately unsuccessful military campaign in Egypt, which lasted from 1798 to 1801. When Napoleon withdrew from the victorious English troops, Geoffroy was left behind in Egypt, from where he wrote desperate letters to Cuvier, asking him to help organize his return to France. Cuvier, who left Geoffroy's letters unanswered, used the time of Geoffroy's absence to consolidate his position of power at the Paris museum. Ironically, Cuvier would later use mummified animals from Egyptian tombs that Geoffroy had brought back—for example, ibis mummies—as evidence against theories of evolutionary species transformation, such as those entertained by Lamarck, and, to a more limited degree, by Geoffroy himself. Comparing the skeleton of ibis mummies with that of living ibises, Cuvier could not detect any modifications. If 3,000 years had not brought any evolutionary change, he argued, another 3,000 years would not do so either, for one could multiply 3,000 by zero as many times as one wanted; the result would always be zero! By 1830, Cuvier and Geoffroy were locked in an epic battle that played out at meetings of the French Academy of Sciences in Paris, exchanging arguments about the possibility and significance of anatomic comparisons between vertebrate and invertebrate animals that piqued interest far beyond the French borders. None other than the illustrious German polymath Johann Wolfgang von Goethe felt compelled to provide a detailed philosophical analysis of the debate. The dispute remained unresolved, for it came to an end with Cuvier's death in 1832 (for a detailed account of the events leading up to the Cuvier–Geoffroy debate of 1830, see Appel, 1987; for a summary, see Rieppel, 2001). In 1818, however, Geoffroy was still prepared to accept Cuvier's interpretation of the turtle shell (Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire, 1818:105n1). Earlier, in 1809, Geoffroy had published a monograph on the soft-shelled turtles, including those he had collected in Egypt, and which he described as a new species under the new genus name _Trionyx_ in the _Annales_ of the Paris National Natural History Museum (Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire, 1809). Soft-shelled turtles show a reduced carapace in that they have lost the marginal plates as well as the lateral bridge elements. This leaves the slender tips of the ribs to project from the outer margin of the costal plates—a condition that seemed to perfectly confirm Cuvier's interpretation of the turtle carapace.

**5.3.** Soft-shelled turtle _Trionyx aegyptiacus_ , known today as _Trionyx triunguis_ , collected by Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire in Egypt.

_Reproduced from Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire 1809, plate I_.

But Cuvier's interpretation of the turtle shell had already met resistance and critique, in particular from German authors, as well as from the eminent Sir Richard Owen from London. In 1802, Christian Rudolph Wilhelm Wiedemann (1770–1840), a gynecologist at the University of Kiel who was also known for his studies in anatomy and entomology, published a monograph on the anatomy of turtles, concentrating on the yellow-footed tortoise ( _Chelonoidis denticulata_ ) from the Amazon basin in South America. Comparing turtles to shelled mollusks, crustaceans and insects, Wiedemann noted that whereas these invertebrates only sport an outer skeleton that envelops the soft parts, turtles sport an outer as well as an inner skeleton. The outer skeleton is, of course, the turtle shell composed of carapace and plastron; the latter terms were not used by Wiedemann, however. The inner skeleton is the vertebral column, the girdle and limb bones, and the skull. Of the carapace he wrote, "The hard outer shell is composed of several bony plates that are united by true sutures, covered on the outside by a keratinous substance, and fused on the inside with part of the vertebral column and with the ribs" (Wiedemann, 1802:179). This does not say that the carapace is formed by the dorsal vertebral neural arches and ribs, but rather by bony plates—we would call them osteoderms today—that fuse with the underlying vertebrae and ribs. That interpretation renders the carapace an exoskeletal formation, not an endoskeletal formation, as it would be in Cuvier's account. A similar account to Wiedemann's was offered by Carl Gustav Carus (1789–1869), in his textbook on comparative anatomy, published in a second edition in 1834. Carus—a physician, psychologist, naturalist, philosopher, and artist—distinguished between the _Hautskelet_ , the skeletal parts that ossify in the skin ( _Haut_ is German for skin), and the _Rumpfskelet_ , the (endoskeletal) vertebrae, ribs and sternum ( _Rumpf_ is German for trunk). Both carapace and plastron he considered to be composed of elements of the _Hautskelet_ , i.e., exoskeletal elements that have fused to the underlying (endoskeletal) vertebrae and ribs (carapace), and sternum (plastron), respectively (Carus, 1834:164). Carus's interpretation of the turtle carapace was confirmed by Wilhelm Peters (1815–1883), who later became director of the zoological museum of Humboldt University in Berlin. In his PhD thesis, published in 1838, Peters confirmed the origin of carapacial plates as ossifications that form in the dermis layer of the skin, with the neural and costal plates later fusing to the underlying vertebral neural arches and ribs, respectively.

Neither Cuvier nor Geoffroy, Wiedemann, Carus, or Peters had based their interpretation of the turtle shell on embryological investigations. This is why Martin Heinrich Rathke (1793–1860) judged their work incomplete and hence their interpretations dubious. In 1835, Rathke left Dorpat to take up the position of university professor of anatomy, director of the institute of anatomy, and director of the zoological museum in Königsberg. Rathke succeeded in that position embryologist Karl Ernst von Baer, who had left the University of Königsberg the year before, in 1834. Rathke, who was thankful for having been called back to his fatherland, thus had large shoes to fill, but in the course of his career, he established himself as another eminent embryologist of the nineteenth century (Menz, 2000:69). His plan to investigate the embryonic development of turtles was motivated by the fact that "virtually nothing about this is known so far." Yet it was only through embryological investigations that one could hope to decipher "how this wondrous and puzzling anatomy could be formed" (Rathke, cited in Menz, 2000:196). Baer had pursued similar ambitions before, as he sought to illuminate what he found so peculiar about the anatomy of turtles, namely the fact that "a series of bones, which in many ways identify themselves as ribs, are not only grown together among themselves, but also cover the entire vertebral column from the neck backwards, and even enclose the root elements of the limbs" (1834:546).

In his report on turtle development, published in the year of his retirement, 1834, Baer bemoaned years of futile attempts to obtain turtle embryos suitable for investigation. In the summer of 1833, however, a turtle that had been offered to him for purchase "was so kind to drop an egg into my hand as I picked it up. 'This is an honorable pledge and valuable earnest money,'" Baer thought, and placed his new acquisition into a box filled with humid soil into which the turtle deposited additional eggs (Baer, 1834:544). It proved difficult, however, to provide the eggs with the right amount of humidity and heat to keep the embryos alive. The results Baer was able to obtain were thus rather meager, limited to the description of an embryo six days old.

In the days of Baer and Rathke, Königsberg was the capital city of East Prussia. Baer had already announced that no turtles were to be found in the surroundings of Königsberg but that a species of pond turtle he called _Emys europaea_ was found in Lithuania (Baer, 1834:544). Rathke in turn was made aware of the fact that the many lakes that adorn the landscape of southern East Prussia harbored in copious numbers European pond turtles, which (in an English abstract of his turtle work he published in 1846) Rathke referred to by the same name Baer (1846:316) had previously used, _Emys europaea_. (Today the species is known by the name under which it was first described, _Emys orbicularis_.) Rathke immediately enlisted the help of locals and traveled the region himself two times during summer to collect turtles that, he hoped, would include females carrying eggs. He brought back 26 specimens of the pond turtle, which he released into the marshy garden pond of the anatomical institute. The eggs some of these specimens would produce rotted as a result of exposure to excessive humidity, and to Rathke's chagrin, no further eggs would be forthcoming for the next eight years (Menz, 2000:198). Of nearly 100 turtle eggs sent to him, none produced any embryos, "presumably because the eggs had lost their lives due to the bumpy ride they endured on the wagons that had been used to transport them" (Rathke, cited in Menz, 2000:199). After experiencing these setbacks, Rathke turned to fellow naturalists, scientists, and even pet dealers in the hope that they would have turtle eggs and embryos in their collections. "I was lucky enough to obtain two late embryos and a number of very young turtles which collectively formed a series that allowed me in particular to investigate the development of the carapace and plastron" (Rathke, 1848:vi). In the end, Rathke succeeded in assembling a rich variety of late embryos or juveniles of turtles, including representatives of the soft-shelled turtles and sea turtles, most significantly an embryo of the green sea turtle ( _Chelonia mydas_ ), as well as a juvenile of the leatherback sea turtle ( _Dermochelys coriacea_ ). Curiously, he did not seize on the most peculiar composition of the carapace in the leatherback. The series was complemented by three eggs of _Emys europaea_ , which a friend of the medical faculty of the University of Königsberg gave him in June 1847, when his monograph was already in print. He carefully transferred the eggs to a box filled with sand, which he then exposed to the sun. The two embryos thus obtained became the subject of a special appendix to his monograph (Menz, 2000:201; see also Rathke, 1848:xiv).
