Relating his detailed and careful observations in the verbose, repetitive, and often contorted style typical of his scientific writing in a volume he published in 1848, Rathke forcefully refuted Carus's and Peters's accounts of the turtle shell. A commemorative speech delivered after his passing characterized Rathke's turtle work as truly masterful:

Rathke proved... that the ribs through their highly peculiar growth come to envelope the limbs and together with the vertebrae form the carapace, which then is enlarged by bony plates that develop in the skin; the plastron is in its entirety a formation of dermal ossifications, as the sternum is missing in turtles. That way Rathke's investigations brought complete and final closure to a long-lasting debate in comparative anatomy. (Zaddach, 1860:288)

In Carus's and Peters's accounts, bony plates that ossify in the dermis layer of the skin would fuse with the underlying vertebrae and ribs in the formation of the carapace (Rathke, 1848:113). No such osteoderms, nor their fusion with underlying vertebrae and ribs, was evident in any of the material Rathke had investigated. Interestingly, osteoderms do form in the carapace of the leatherback, but Rathke's specimen seems to have been at an early posthatching developmental stage, at which time the osteoderms had not yet made their appearance. Rathke also rejected Cuvier's claim that the marginal plates take their origin from the ribs. Not only did the marginal plates form quite independently, but they also differed in number from the ribs (1848:112). He did, however, recognize a fundamental difference between the neural and costal plates on the one hand and the nuchal, marginal, and pygal plates on the other, calling the latter collectively the "supplementary plates" of the carapace. The neural spines of the trunk (dorsal) vertebrae, as well as the trunk (dorsal) ribs, he found to be preformed in cartilage, yet embedded in a "dense layer of connective tissue" that directly underlies the skin (1848:57). It is within this dense layer of connective tissue that he found the neural spines of the dorsal vertebrae and the dorsal ribs to ossify. Consequent to their ossification, he found additional bone to spread from the tip of the neural spines in such a way as to form a squarish, horizontally oriented plate—the neural plates that would eventually meet their neighbors in well-defined sutures. Similarly, he found the ribs to first ossify, then broaden in a horizontal plane by the continuous apposition of bone to their anterior and posterior margins. That way, the ribs would broaden to such an extent as to form the costal plates that meet their neighbors in an interdigitating suture. Clearly, then, the neural and costal plates derive from the endoskeletal vertebrae and ribs instead of representing dermal, i.e., exoskeletal, ossifications that fuse with underlying endoskeletal elements.

The same derivation from endoskeletal elements could not be claimed for what Rathke called the "supplementary plates" of the carapace. The nuchal plate—which, as he noted, is the first ossification to appear in the carapace—as well as the marginal and the pygal plates he found to form independently within a structure in the turtle embryo that he called the marginal fold. While definitively not related to ribs or vertebrae, Rathke was unable to unambiguously assess the origin of these supplementary plates—with the exception of the nuchal plate—either from a tissue resembling cartilage or from a more fibrous tissue underlying the skin (1848:111). However, in his description of the two embryos of _Emys europaea_ he had obtained in 1847, he confirmed the nuchal plate to be the only one of the supplementary plates to take its origin from a cartilaginous precursor (1848:238). He was probably driven to this erroneous conclusion by the curious two-phased ossification of the nuchal. Rathke's terminology is cumbersome, and his findings may be more easily understood when translated into modern terminology. What happens in the turtle embryo is that the carapace is first mapped out through a thickening of the dermis layer of the skin covering the back of the animal. Such thickening of the dermis layer of the skin results in the dense layer of connective tissue noted by Rathke. A recent study by Hirasawa, Nagashima, and Kuratani (2013) found this "dense layer of connective tissue" identified by Rathke not to be part of the dermis layer of the skin but rather to represent a deeper layer of tissue in which, in other tetrapods, the muscles develop that lie between the ribs. The result is the appearance of a carapacial disk, and it is within this carapacial disk that the elements of the carapace will ossify. Just as the ossified carapace, the carapacial disk has a well-defined subcircular or circular margin, which in the embryonic condition is called the carapacial ridge. This carapacial ridge is what corresponds to Rathke's marginal fold, and it is within this marginal fold that he observed the nuchal, the pygal, and the marginal plates of the carapace to ossify.

Another illustrious author who set out to investigate the nature of the turtle shell was Sir Richard Owen, who published his results in the esteemed _Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society of London_ , the oldest scientific periodical still in existence, with the first issue appearing in 1665. The carapace, "this seemingly special and anomalous superaddition to the ordinary vertebrate structure," Owen noted, "is due, in a great degree, to the modification of form and size, and, in a less degree, to a change of relative position, of ordinary elements of the vertebrate skeleton" (1849:151). These changes Owen recognized as being precisely of the kind that would obscure relations of homology, thus rendering a detailed anatomical comparison of turtles with other reptiles difficult. The first relevant attempt to decipher the homologies in the turtle shell Owen traced back to Cuvier's _Leçons d'Anatomie Comparée_ of 1799. In contrast, Owen found the interpretation of the turtle shell offered by Carus to have been "neither explained in detail nor supported by any fact of development" (1849:155). On Owen's reading, Rathke had considered the carapace an endoskeletal structure, except for the marginal plates, which he had ascribed to the exoskeleton. These marginal plates Rathke had considered not to be essential parts of the carapace, however, as they are absent in the leatherback sea turtle as also in soft-shelled turtles: "The special deductions by RATHKE will be compared... with my own observations on the development of the carapace in the _Chelonia_ ," Owen proclaimed (Owen, 1849:156). In the discussions of the nature of the turtle shell, Owen was the first to consistently distinguish between endo- and exoskeletal components. As already explained, homology is a correspondence relation between homologues, i.e., between parts (organs or organ systems) considered to be homologous in two or more different organisms (Brigandt, 2002). To ask which are the homologies in the turtle carapace therefore also implies the question of which other organisms turtles should be compared to in the search for homologies. Here Owen opted for crocodiles, with which he thought turtles to share the closest affinities among reptiles (much as the DNA data suggest today). And again, as already noted, crocodiles are characterized by the development of thick osteoderms, which results in the richly structured skin that covers their back. There is, then, in crocodiles a well-developed layer of exoskeletal ossifications superimposed on, but not fused with, the underlying endoskeletal trunk vertebrae and ribs. Naturally, such a comparison with crocodiles would color Owen's interpretation of the turtle carapace: "The neural plates, the costal plates, and the marginal plates, whether attached to the vertebral elements or detached, are lodged in the substance of the derm[is], and form a stratum of bones superficial to the ossified parts of the endo-skeleton" (1849:161). To cite more fully Owen's conclusions, just with respect to the neural plates while bearing in mind that the very same conclusion would apply to the ribs and costal plates, gives a stronger sense of what he was driving at: "The [vertebral] centrum and the neural arch supporting the neural plate are parts the homologies of which admit of no question, and have given rise to none; but the neural plate itself is a dermal bone homologous with the medial dermal scutes of the Crocodile's back-shield, but connate in some of the dorsal segments with the true neural spine in the _Chelonia_ " (1849:165).

**5.4.** Embryo of European pond turtle _Emys europaea_ , known today as _Emys orbicularis_ , showing development of carapacial disk.

_Reproduced from Rathke 1848, plate X_.

In summary, then, there had been articulated two alternative interpretations of the carapace since these early phases of discussion of the turtle shell. Disregarding the plastron as of less importance in the current context, one side in the debate took the elements of the carapace to be of exoskeletal origin, homologous to the osteoderms of other reptiles, some of them (i.e., the neural and costal plates) secondarily fused with underlying elements of the endoskeleton (i.e., with the dorsal vertebrae [neural arches] and ribs). The other side emphasized the endoskeletal nature of the carapace, especially of its central components, the neural and costal plates. Recognizing the erroneous nature of Rathke's tentative derivation of the nuchal and marginal plates from cartilaginous precursors, and siding with Owen in that particular respect, the carapace can also be seen as a composite structure: the neural and costal plates are endoskeletal and the nuchal, pygal, and marginal plates are exoskeletal in their origin. It is fair to say that the discussion of the nature of the carapace continued to move between these alternative interpretations all the way into the twenty-first century. The interesting thing here is that the discussion could only continue to move between these alternative hypotheses for so long because important additional observations and arguments were being ignored. The latter emerged from an attempt, initiated by Cope in 1871 and subsequently pursued mostly by paleontologists, to tie the interpretation of the carapace more closely to the evolutionary history of turtles, and in that context also to identify what could count as the most primitive condition of the carapace among fossil and extant turtles known at the time. The most crucial role to play in that respect fell to the leatherback sea turtle because of the peculiar nature of the body armor that covers its back.

A carapace is clearly delineated in the leatherback, making it immediately identifiable as a turtle. The embryo of _Dermochelys_ consequently also develops a distinct carapacial disk. What is peculiar, though, is the way ossification proceeds in the carapacial disk of the leatherback. Its scientific name, _Dermochelys_ , combines the term _dermis_ , for the thickened dermis layer of the skin marking out the carapace, with the term _chelys_ , identifying the animal as a chelonian, a turtle. At the front end of the carapace sits the ossified nuchal plate, the element that also is the first to ossify in the carapace of turtles more generally. Neural plates are absent in the leatherback, however. The ribs fan out into the dermal carapace and are horizontally somewhat broadened, but only slightly so, leaving ample space between them. They do not meet their neighbors along their length in a well-defined suture, as is the case in true costal plates. In that respect, the ossified ribs of the adult leatherback resemble the cartilaginous ribs of embryos of other turtles. Isolated ossifications along the posterior margin of the carapace of the leatherback have tentatively been compared to marginal plates of other turtles. These are all the ossifications in the carapace of the leatherback that compare to those in the carapace of other turtles. That is not to say, however, that the leatherback is left without a robust bony covering of its back. The carapace in fact includes a layer of osteoderms, small polygonal ossifications that form in the thickened dermis above the dorsal vertebrae and ribs, without fusing with these underlying endoskeletal elements. The osteoderms in the carapace of the leatherback form a closed shield, as they meet in interdigitating sutures, thus forming a structure that closely resembles the carapace of armored placodonts. It is on the basis of a comparison with the leatherback that the early authors misidentified the cyamodontoid placodont _Psephoderma_ from the Middle Triassic of Bavaria as an extinct sea turtle.

It is also the peculiar nature of its carapace that motivated Cope to classify the leatherback and its fossil relatives as representatives of the Athecae, a name that translates as "turtles without a theca," i.e., without a typical carapace. This evolutionary lineage he contrasted with the Cryptodira, a group that in his view would comprise all other turtles, all of them with a properly developed carapace, or theca (Cope, 1871:235). Cope's classification of turtles therefore does not recognize the cryptodire–pleurodire distinction that we draw today, based on the mode of head retraction and jaw adductor system, and for that reason is deficient by modern standards. Nevertheless, in his revision of Cope's terminology published in 1886, paleontologist Louis Dollo (1857–1931) from the Royal Natural History Museum of Belgium retained Cope's term Athecae for the leatherback and its kin while placing all other turtles in a group Dollo called Thecophora. These early discussions surrounding the anatomy of the leatherback and its consequent classification generated a number of important questions and insights.

First, it was realized that the carapace in the leatherback is of a fundamentally different osseous structure than that of other turtles. The nuchal plate and possibly posterior marginal plates were recognized as the only elements that compare to the carapace of other turtles, while the slight broadening of the dorsal ribs in the leatherback recalls an embryonic condition observed in other turtles. Other turtles lack the extensive osteoderm covering that is present in the leatherback. The question thus arises, what is the primitive condition for turtles? Is it the presence of a typical bony carapace—mostly lost in the leatherback and replaced by an osteoderm covering—or is the condition observed in _Dermochelys_ to be taken as the primitive condition for turtles in general? In modern analyses, the leatherback does not come out as a basal, primitive turtle, while early fossil turtles already had a fully developed osseous carapace. The latter must thus have been lost in the leatherback, with the loss compensated for by an extensive osteoderm covering—a conclusion that had been reached by Baur in 1888, in opposition to Cope and Dollo. This conclusion is in accordance with the fact that the shell is reduced in its degree of ossification in other sea turtles as well, although never to the extent observed in the leatherback. It is indeed a quite general observation that those reptiles, which secondarily invade the sea to make it their permanent habitat, show a reduction in the degree of ossification in various parts of their skeleton compared to their terrestrial relatives. Such a reduction in ossification may affect different parts of the skeleton to different degrees and is probably an expression of a reduced gravitational pull in the aquatic environment. However, and significantly, whereas Baur recognized _Dermochelys_ "as the most specialized of the sea-turtles," he nevertheless took the structure of its dermal armor as a model of the primitive, "protochelonian" condition (Baur, 1888:144). The leather-back, according to his view, did not represent the primitive condition of carapace formation but instead exemplified it.

The distinction of Athecae from Thecophora drawn by Dollo carried even more profound implications. It was Baur again who drew attention to an observation previously published in 1872 by Paul Gervais (1816–1879), a comparative anatomist working at the Paris National Natural History Museum. Gervais had noted that the anterior osteoderms in the carapace of the leatherback overlap the (anteriorly positioned) nuchal plate (Baur, 1886:688). Because the nuchal plate was recognized as an element that occurs in the carapace of all turtles, this had to mean that the osteoderms in the carapace of _Dermochelys_ ossified in a more superficial layer of the skin than the nuchal plate. The more general conclusion drawn by Oliver Perry Hay (1846–1930) in a paper from 1898 was that the bony plates that contribute to the dorsal armor in turtles derive from two distinct layers of ossification within the thickened tissue of what today is called the carapacial disk. According to Hay, the outer layer of ossification produced true dermal bone in the form of osteoderms, as are present in the leatherback. The inner layer of ossification produced what Hay called fascial bone that makes up the elements in the true carapace, such as the nuchal, neural, pygal, costal, and marginal plates. The next chapter in the turtle shell saga was the introduction of a new terminology in 1913, where carapacial elements of the inner layer of ossification were called "thecal," with these elements collectively forming the theca (Völker, 1913). Elements of the outer layer, i.e., the osteoderm layer, were called "epithecal," with them correspondingly forming the epitheca. In the classic case of the leatherback, the nuchal plate is an element of the theca, whereas the osteoderm covering forms the epitheca—consistent with the fact that anterior osteoderms overlap the nuchal plate. Following that terminological distinction, there emerged a general consensus that the epithecal ossifications are truly of dermal origin, ossifying in the dermis layer of the skin and hence exoskeletal in nature. The dispute now centered on the nature of the thecal ossifications, not just the nuchal plate, but more importantly, the neural and costal plates in the carapace. Consider the case of certain fossil sea turtles, with a perfectly developed carapace comprising neural and costal plates, but on top of which isolated and scattered osteoderms are superimposed, as may also be the case in the pleurodire genus _Chelus_. With the undisputed acknowledgment that such osteoderms represent epithecal ossifications of dermal, i.e., exoskeletal, origin, it became difficult to claim that the thecal elements such as the neural and costal plates were derived from dermal ossifications as well, representing a first generation of osteoderms, so to speak, that would fuse with underlying endoskeletal elements, i.e., the dorsal vertebrae and ribs. Such an interpretation would require the formation of two successive generations of osteoderms, the first one positioned deep to the second one, and only osteoderms of the first generation fusing with underlying endoskeletal elements. With turtle shells that comprise neural, costal, and marginal plates, as well as osteoderms superimposed on the neurals and costals, the further question arose as to whether the marginal plates are first- or second-generation osteoderms. The "duplicity theory" of the turtle carapace that was built on the distinction of thecal and epithecal ossifications in the carapace, and in particular the nature of the neural and costal plates as either of exo- or endoskeletal derivation, remained a matter of dispute well into the twenty-first century (Kälin, 1945:145). However, as long as the leatherback could still be considered either as a surviving offshoot from the primitive stock leading to turtles or as a specialized turtle that nonetheless provides a model for an ancestral turtle, it seemed appropriate to hypothesize that the evolution of the carapace would have started with ancestral turtles that developed a loose osteoderm covering over an incompletely developed theca. Such was indeed proposed by Jan Versluys, who was also the first to illustrate such a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor adorned with osteoderms scattered across its back (Versluys, 1914). In the Athecae, he argued, i.e., in the leatherback and its kin, the osteoderm covering was perfected to form a closed shield at the expense of the theca, which remained undeveloped except for the nuchal plate and a modest broadening of the ribs. In contrast, in the Thecophora, the osteoderm covering was lost after the evolution of a complete theca comprising neural and costal plates.

Removal of the leatherback from its basal position in the turtle tree—a position that had still been accepted by Versluys—did not diminish the intuitive appeal of those early evolutionary scenarios. Given the broad distribution of osteoderms across reptiles in general, it continued to seem appropriate to hypothesize that the evolution of the carapace would have started with ancestral turtles developing an osteoderm covering. These osteoderms would then have coalesced to form the larger plates characteristic of the carapace (theca), with the neural and costal plates then fusing with the underlying dorsal vertebrae and ribs. However, this intuitively appealing vision could not, and still cannot, be reconciled with two observations that were first reported by Rathke as early as 1849, and that in numerous succeeding studies of the embryonic development of the carapace have stood the test of time: it is a fact that turtle embryos never show any sign of a secondary fusion of bony plates ossifying in the dermis layer of the skin with underlying endoskeletal elements such as the dorsal vertebral neural arches and ribs, and it is also a fact that the ossification of the neural and costal plates continuously spreads from perichondral bone that forms around the dorsal vertebral neural arches and ribs, which are first preformed in cartilage. These observations identify the neural and costal plates as endoskeletal elements, in contrast to the exoskeletal nature of the other plates that complete the turtle carapace. On that interpretation, the turtle carapace is indeed a dual structure, combining endo- and exoskeletal elements in a way that is unique among vertebrates (for an extensive technical review of these issues, see Rieppel, 2013c).

The debate surrounding the evolution of the turtle shell will be revisited below in an account of the social construction of the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor, dotted, as it were, by scattered osteoderms believed to represent the first step in the evolution of a carapace. Social construction means that the arguments about the origin and early evolution of turtles and their shell are resolved not through new discoveries or investigations but through consensus building among a group of collaborating scientists. The early arguments concerning the origin of the turtle shell as sketched above have laid the foundation for such a social construction of the turtle ancestor, but they have not illuminated a more pressing issue in the present discussion, and that is how the shoulder blade of turtles came to be positioned inside the rib cage. Recall Herbert Ruckes, who dismissed D. M. S. Watson's idea that the scapula came to lie inside the rib cage in turtles as a result of a posterior dislocation of the pectoral girdle. What Ruckes was able to show in his prizewinning studies was that the turtle shoulder girdle has never been displaced posteriorly. Indeed, it is not the scapula that changes its position at all. Instead, he found the ribs to have changed their position relative to the shoulder blade. The ribs are endoskeletal structures that grow out from cartilaginous rudiments that are associated with the vertebrae on which the ribs will eventually articulate. In tetrapods generally, the ribs of the trunk grow out curving downward into the body wall in a way that places them internal to the scapula. In turtles, the dorsal ribs grow out from the vertebrae in a novel direction, one that takes them to a position outside the scapula. Rucke's prizewinning discoveries can be summarized as follows: the association of the primordial rudiments of the ribs with a thickened carapacial dermis, and the ribs growing in a more or less horizontal direction, are associated with the lateral growth and radial expansion of the carapacial dermis as the formation of the carapacial disk continues. That way, the scapula (and with it the shoulder girdle) comes to lie inside the turtle shell, positioned below the first costal plate that takes its origin from the first dorsal rib. The turtle shell thus represents a true evolutionary novelty, unique in its nature among vertebrate animals.

Old Genes Perform New Tricks

The first modern study that tackled Ruckes's conclusions experimentally was performed in the 1980s by a graduate student at Harvard's Museum of Comparative Zoology, Ann C. Burke, now a professor at Wesleyan University. In her own words, modern developmental models of the carapace "begin with a 1929 descriptive study by Ruckes that in part inspired my thesis work" (Burke, 2015:83). The 1980s were the heydays of the first blossoming of evolutionary developmental biology, with Stephen Jay Gould—also from Harvard—deploring the incompleteness of the modern synthesis of evolutionary theory. The modern synthesis, hammered out in the mid-twentieth century, had indeed brought previously disparate areas of research together, most prominently population biology, population genetics, and paleontology. But, Gould complained, it had failed to properly incorporate developmental biology and its genetic underpinning. As we have seen in the last chapter, Gould raised issues that had previously been addressed by Goldschmidt. With Gould and a number of other authors pushing the agenda for evolutionary developmental research in the 1980s, talk of macroevolution, and possibly of saltational evolutionary change, was no longer immediately dismissed as heresy, as it had been in the 1950s and 1960s, the heyday of the modern synthesis. While Burke sliced up turtle embryos into ultrathin sections to study their cellular structure under the microscope, a fellow student in the same lab investigated the origin of the novel joint that splits the upper jaw of the Round Island boa in two halves—the very structure which in the previous chapter was identified as the most cited example of a hopeful monster. Both were graduate students under the same supervisor, Pere Alberch (1954–1998) (Wake, 1998). Alberch was a pioneer in evo-devo research, who among other things took up an interest that had also been prominently pursued by Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire's son, Isodore: the classification of monstrosities. Why is it that malformations are not of a purely random nature but instead can be classified? (Alberch, 1989). You can get a turtle—or a salamander, or a lizard—with two heads sitting on the shoulders, but you'll never get a turtle with an extra leg sprouting from the middle of its back. The fact that monstrosities can be classified implies that not all malformations are possible—that there exist so-called forbidden morphologies. This in turn calls into question the Darwinian assumption of fundamental variation (discussed in chapter 3) and invokes the theory that developmental constraints render some developmental pathways impossible.

When Burke investigated the cellular structure of the carapacial disk and its outer margin, the carapacial ridge, in turtle embryos, she was struck by some cellular differentiation she had seen elsewhere. Those cells of the epidermis, i.e., of the outer layer of the skin, which line the margin of the carapacial disk, i.e., which form the carapacial ridge, show a distinctly specialized structure. They are conspicuously tall and slender, looking strikingly similar to those epidermal cells that form the outer margin, or outer edge, of a tetrapod limb bud. The limb bud forms when the limbs first start to sprout from the body wall of the embryo. The epidermal cells aligned along the limb bud's outer margin (technically called the apical ectodermal ridge) show a similar tall and slender structure to the epidermal cells aligned along the carapacial ridge. The tetrapod limb, and its early developmental stage, the limb bud, has been at the center of intensive experimental developmental research for decades. Tetrapod limb development was also, again in the 1980s, the subject of another graduate student research project in Pere Alberch's lab at the Museum of Comparative Zoology in Cambridge, Massachusetts (Shubin and Alberch, 1986). Experiments performed on the tetrapod limb bud had revealed a most important organizing role performed by these specialized epidermis cells in the apical ectodermal ridge during limb development. Boas and pythons, with their much-reduced hind limbs, provide a natural experiment. Boa and python embryos show typical posterior limb buds sprouting during early developmental stages, but then, early on in development, the epidermal cells in the apical ectodermal ridge undergo necrosis and die. Limb development consequently comes to a halt, leaving the hind limbs in a most rudimentary stage of differentiation that persists into adulthood. Recognizing a similar cellular differentiation of the epidermis in the carapacial ridge, Burke set out to test whether these might likewise play some organizing role in the development of the carapace. Indeed, she found that it is under some sort of instruction from the carapacial ridge, emanating from an epithelial mesenchymal interaction between the specialized epidermis cells and the underlying dermis, that the growth of the ribs is redirected (Burke, 1989).

More recent work on the development of the turtle carapace has targeted both its genetic underpinning and its morphological differentiation. This work was pursued predominantly in labs in the United States and in Japan, respectively. Given their different locations, researchers have used different turtle species in their studies, with the North American ones using hard-shelled species and the Japanese ones using soft-shelled species. On the basis of that body of work, two different models of carapace development have been proposed (for a review and references, see Rice at al., 2015).

As the early turtle embryo starts to take shape, the fore- and hind limbs start to sprout from the lateral body wall: the limb buds make their appearance. Then the carapacial ridge makes its first appearance, at first forming a ridge of thickened epidermis that is located just above the limb buds, and bridging the distance between them. During subsequent development, the ridges expand both in an anterior and posterior direction, crossing over the back until the ridges of either side of the body come to meet in a closed circle. That way, the circumference of the carapacial disk is completed. In tetrapods generally, the ribs grow downward into the lateral body wall, thus forming a rib cage that encases the lungs. Such is not the case in turtles, however, for reasons that remain debated. In hard-shelled turtles, the reorientation of rib growth was found to result from an attraction of the rib precursor cells to the carapacial ridge. As a consequence, the ribs become ensnared in the dermis of the carapacial ridge. Researchers using soft-shelled turtles considered the fact that the ribs of turtles do not just grow straight laterally; the anterior ribs grow in an anterolateral direction and the posterior ribs grow in a posterolateral direction. This fan-shaped or radiating pattern of ribs they attribute to the influence of the carapacial ridge. Below the carapacial disk, the body wall was found to fold up in a peculiar way, shifting the appendicular skeleton to a position below the ribs (Nagashima et al., 2009). The differences in development are also reflected in different gene expression patterns observed in the carapacial ridge of hard- and soft-shelled turtles. The question remains whether those two different models of carapace formation reflect taxonomic diversity or result from different techniques of investigation. The most recent review of the debate concluded, "There is more than one way to make a turtle shell" (Rice et al., 2015:6). This is remarkable, given the monophyly of the turtle lineage, as well as the carapace as one of its most telling diagnostic features. But either way, restricting the growth of the ribs to a largely horizontal plane (technically the primaxial domain) has important consequences for all turtles (Shearman and Burke, 2009; Burke, 2009). First, the cartilaginous ribs come to lie in the deep dermis layer of the skin that forms the carapacial disk, or else just below it. Second, because the carapacial ridge is a nearly circular structure, the outgrowing ribs will be arranged in a fanlike manner. Anterior ribs will not point straight laterally but will instead trend anterolaterally; posterior ribs will similarly trend posterolaterally. The anteriormost rib therefore does not grow straight laterally but rather somewhat anterolaterally in the direction of the forelimb, and thus comes to lie in a position above, i.e., dorsal to, the scapula. The shoulder blade is thus technically located inside the rib cage, i.e., inside the carapace. (For a more detailed and technical account, see Gilbert, Cebra-Thomas, and Burke, 2008; Hirasawa, Nagashima, and Kuratani, 2013; and references therein. There is also a nice recent summary of that research in Wagner, 2014:179ff.)

Once fully formed, the cartilaginous ribs begin to ossify, a process by which cartilage is replaced by bone, as is typical of any endoskeletal element. During that process, the rib cartilage releases a molecular signal that incites the formation of bone by competent cells in neighboring tissue. The first step in rib ossification is the deposition of bone on the surface of the cartilaginous rib. This initial bone (perichondral bone, destined to form the periosteum of the ossified rib) eventually forms a solid mineralized sheath for the rib cartilage. While the rib cartilage itself is being eroded away and replaced by bone (endochondral bone), bone continues to be deposited within the dermis of the carapacial disk along the anterior and posterior margin of the ossifying rib. That way, bone formation spreads out from the rib in a horizontal plane along both its anterior and posterior edge. A debate surrounds the question as to whether the expansion of what will become the costal plate is strictly mediated by the periosteum of the ossified rib or whether there is an accretion of bone formed in neighboring tissue to the ossified rib (see discussion in Rice et al., 2015:9). Either way, the result is an increasing width of the "rib" until the costal plate is complete, meeting the neighboring plates in well-defined sutures. The costal plate is not preformed in cartilage in its entirety, only its central part that is constituted by the cartilaginous rib. From a developmental biologist's perspective, the costal plate (as also the neural plate) is consequently a composite structure, composed of chondral bone that encases and eventually replaces the original cartilaginous rib, to which is added intramembranous, or dermal, bone, i.e., bone forming in the dermis layer of the carapacial disk (Gilbert, Cebra-Thomas, and Burke, 2008:6). This developmental perspective takes into account the fact that two different types of bone, which form on the basis of different molecular stimulation, contribute to the costal (and neural) plates. Indeed, debate is still ongoing about the source of the cells that contribute bone to the costal plates, highlighting the possibility that different species of turtles might recruit different cells to complete the costal plates (see discussion in Lubick, 2013; Rice et al., 2015).

**5.5.** Carapace of loggerhead sea turtle _Caretta caretta_ seen from below. Note intimate relationships of ribs and corresponding costal plates.

_Photograph by John Weinstein, The Field Museum_.

From a phylogenetic perspective, which emphasizes the historical, i.e., evolutionary, distinctiveness of endo- and exoskeleton discussed above, such intramembranous bone that spreads from the ossification of an element preformed in cartilage and thus enlarging it is called _Zuwachsknochen_ , a term that translates into "incremental bone" (Starck, 1979:13). An element that includes incremental bone is considered to be endoskeletal in nature. The reason for this is that bony elements, which are formed through the ossification of an endoskeletal element initially preformed in cartilage, to which is then added incremental bone, are not evolved from elements that in the ancestral condition developed at the interface of the dermis and epidermis layers of the skin.

Remember the scales of trout and salmon. These bony scales are thin and translucent, and they ossify directly in the dermis layer of the skin. Compared to the ancestral condition, these scales have in the course of evolution lost the dentin and enamel layers that are primitively part of the exoskeleton. The scales in the distant ancestors of trout and salmon were three layered, composed of bone, dentin, and enamel, and must therefore have developed at the interface of the dermis and epidermis in the skin. The scales of salmon and trout are therefore homologous to scales that, in the ancestral condition, developed at the dermis–epidermis interface, although they themselves no longer do so. For an ossification to be identified as exoskeletal, that bony element must either develop as a three-layered structure at the dermis–epidermis interface in the skin, or it must be homologous to an element that did, in the ancestral condition, develop at this ectoderm–mesoderm interface.

Conversely, an endoskeletal element cannot be one that is homologous to an element that in the ancestral condition developed at the interface of dermis and epidermis in the skin. It is true that the neural and costal plates in the turtle carapace both develop through the addition of intramembranous bone, but that incremental bone enlarges an element that is originally preformed in cartilage before it undergoes ossification. The neural and costal plates in the turtle carapace therefore cannot be derived, evolutionarily, from ancestral structures that once in the distant past were composed of bone, dentin, and enamel, and hence originally developed at the interface of dermis and epidermis in the skin. Such incremental bone enhances structures which in the ancestral condition were—or still are, at least in part—preformed in cartilage; hence the endoskeletal nature of the _Zuwachsknochen_. In conclusion, then, it can be restated that the turtle carapace combines endoskeletal (neural and costal plates) and exoskeletal (nuchal, pygal, and marginal plates) elements in a novel way that is unique among tetrapods, and indeed among vertebrates.

Remember Ann C. Burke, whose interest in the development of the turtle shell was piqued when she discovered that the epidermal cells along the carapacial ridge look similar to the epidermal cells lining the outer (apical) margin of the limb bud. What she found is that these cells along the carapacial ridge play an important organizing role in the formation of the carapace. Perhaps the most interesting part of this story is the recently discovered fact that several genes that are active at the outer margin of the limb bud (in the apical ectodermal ridge) are also expressed in the carapacial ridge. It thus seems that genes that regulate limb development in tetrapods have been co-opted to regulate carapace (rib) development in the carapacial ridge of turtles. There is some controversy around this issue, as there is around the precise function of the carapacial ridge, a discussion that might be due to different turtle species being used in different studies conducted by different authors, but as was eloquently stated in a review, "Underlying the unique presence of the carapacial ridge and its influence on the formation of the carapace are a host of genes and specific tissues that have been co-opted from those associated with the adjacent limb buds" (Carroll, 2013:30).

The expression domain and the function of these co-opted genes have been altered in a unique way, however, so as to influence the growth of the ribs in turtles. Developmental biologist Scott Gilbert, professor at Swarthmore College, Pennsylvania, has invoked the small tool kit metaphor, where the tools are few but versatile in their function, to explain such and similar situations. The tools in this case are the genes, but as the case of turtles illuminates, the same genes can do different things in different contexts or locations. Not every evolutionary innovation requires a totally new set of genes. It is obviously entirely possible to recruit old genes, which, in a different context and location, can perform new tricks. "Turtles seem to accomplish their remarkable anatomical and physiological feats using the same basic set of genes as their amniote relatives.... Evolution generates its novelties by tinkering with existing genes" (Gilbert and Corfe, 2013:328).

The Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor

Old genes performing new tricks in a different context and location is a classic mechanism of evolutionary innovation. The origin of a carapacial ridge between and slightly above the anterior and posterior limb buds, its completion to form a near-circular structure, the folding of the body wall, the redirection of rib growth into the carapacial ridge, and their subsequent incorporation into the osseous carapace—these are all aspects of a classic case of evolutionary innovation. Innovation here does not mean the mere transformation of an ancestral structure into a modified, descendant one, but rather means the emergence of an evolutionary novelty through the reprogramming of embryonic development, resulting in the formation of the carapace, the hallmark of a novel body plan. In the previous chapter, this emergentist paradigm of macroevolutionary change was contrasted with the transformationist paradigm, one that seeks the gradual transformation of adult structures from one generation to the next through variation and natural selection, and thus explains macroevolutionary change on the basis of extended microevolutionary processes. One of the major differences between the emergentist and transformationist paradigm is that the latter, but not necessarily the former, allows, or even seeks, to arrange adult organisms in a transformation series of ancestral and descendant conditions of form putatively documenting continuous morphological change. That way the ancestral and descendant conditions of form are bridged by an unbroken series of intermediate steps, as is required according to the Darwinian theory of evolution through variation and natural selection. Such intermediate steps of evolutionary change may sometimes be gleaned from the fossil record and sometimes from embryonic development. It may be the case that an intermediate step of evolutionary transformation is documented by an actual fossil or in a certain developmental stage of living organisms. It has indeed often been the case that an intermediate stage of evolutionary transformation was first recognized in the embryonic development of modern organisms and then was later found to be represented in the fossil record as well. One such surprising case is the turtle carapace, as will be discussed in the next chapter. In cases where both of these potential sources of information—embryos and fossils—remain mute with respect to transitional steps of evolutionary change, comparative anatomists wedded to the transformationist paradigm may theoretically reconstruct such intermediate stages of change.

To theoretically reconstruct, i.e., to invent or imagine, scientifically relevant evidence may sound highly problematic, but is indeed characteristic of early stages of theory construction in many examples. Remember the theoretical concept of the switch gene that preceded the discovery of _Hox_ genes. There has indeed been a deep philosophical discussion of this issue, especially in German philosophy and comparative anatomy of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. The task of natural science was at that time universally believed to be the search for laws of nature. Such natural laws were thought to be revealed through the observation of regularity in certain phenomena, i.e., a regularity of events, or of processes that tie together cause and effect. Same cause, same effect; it is the observed regularity of phenomena that was believed to allow the inference of underlying lawfulness. The laws of nature that were at the center of scientific inquiry are thus revealed to be causal laws, i.e., laws that link cause to effect. A rock picked up will always fall back to the ground. To pick up a stone is the cause; its falling back on earth is the effect of it having been picked up. The law of gravity is the causal explanation for such regularity of the phenomena.

If, in comparative anatomy, forms can be arranged in a series in a way that seems to disclose gradual transformation, then the very fact that forms can be arranged in this way discloses a regularity that calls for a causal explanation. Fossil horses, for example, can be arranged in a series that documents the successive, stepwise reduction of digits in hands and feet, until only one digit—the third—remains, bearing a hoof. Such a series does not necessarily represent horse evolution, which is much more complex than a mere string of ancestors and descendants, but it does illustrate the gradual evolution of the limbs in horses. More generally speaking, if such a graded series of forms is one of successive embryonic stages, the regularity displayed by the series is explained by the causes that guide embryonic development. If a graded series of forms is composed of a number of species, then the inferred cause that is believed to reign over the entire series of form conditions is gradual evolutionary transformation driven by variation and natural selection.

Now, assume a case where there is a distinct gap in such a continuous series of forms. Assume that a series of embryonic stages of a turtle species has been assembled, both early and late, although a couple of intermediate stages are missing. If the stages available all represent the same turtle species, it seems uncontroversial to reconstruct the missing intermediate stages on the basis of what is known from the available antecedent and subsequent stages. The same is true of a series of species where a couple of intermediate forms are missing. Among lizards, for example, especially among scincid lizards, there are examples of species that can be arranged in a series documenting the gradual reduction of limbs, correlated with a gradual body elongation (Sanger and Gibson-Brown, 2004). There might be a number of species that form the beginning of a series revealing gradual transformation, and additional species that continue the same series, but the latter are separated from the first by a distinct anatomical gap. If anatomical comparison reveals all species under consideration to be part of the same transformation series, albeit one interrupted by a gap, then the assumption is still that the same cause reigns over the entire series—evolution by variation and natural selection. It is the assumption of such continuous underlying causality reigning over the entire series that licenses the invention or imagination of intermediate forms to fill in the gap. The problem here is not so much the invention or imagination, i.e., theoretical reconstruction, of intermediate forms.

Natural sciences are said to be, or are required to be, objective. They are supposed to paint an objective picture of the world—to capture the world as it really is. Natural sciences are also said to be self-correcting. To err, to make (honest) mistakes, is human nature. Should a scientist publish results that are erroneous, for whichever reason, the expectation is that colleagues will sooner or later find out and publish a correction. The belief that science must strive to be objective and self-correcting is anchored in the requirement that scientific results must be reproducible. This is most easily understood in experimental sciences, where the same cause is expected to link to the same effect, always and everywhere. When chemists or physicists report on their discoveries, they are required to carefully describe the experimental setup and the procedural protocol in a way that must allow any fellow scientist to repeat that experiment, and confirm, or alternatively disconfirm, the previously obtained results. One of the most spectacular mistaken scientific claims made during the last decades was the case of cold fusion. The claim here was the possibility to have a nuclear reaction play out at room temperature, as opposed to hot fusion, as occurs in nuclear reactors and nuclear weapons. If the possibility of cold fusion were pointing to reality, it could be one of the most important discoveries of modern physics—important enough such that the mere prospect of its actually being real keeps modest research efforts lingering.

However, as explained in Wikipedia, cold fusion has been rejected by the mainstream scientific community because the original experimental results could not be replicated consistently and reliably, and because there is no accepted theoretical model of cold fusion (<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_fusion>, accessed June 20, 2016). It has further been suggested that the original report on cold fusion, dating back to 1989, represented a case of "pathological science," where pathological science is "the science of things that aren't so" (Langmuir, 1989:36). Analyzing experiments that had gone bitterly wrong in spite of the fact that the scientists had honestly given their fair best, Nobel laureate Irving Langmuir (1881–1957), who coined the term "pathological science," explained, "These are cases where there is no dishonesty involved but where people are tricked into false results by a lack of understanding about what human beings can do to themselves in the way of being led astray by subjective effects, wishful thinking, or threshold interactions" (1989:43). Wishful thinking and consequent self-deception can happen to anybody, anytime. I remember a case where, early in my career, I drew a lizard skull as accurately as I could, thereby identifying a bone that simply was not there. What is more important is that published results from scientific exploration can be tested—and found to be false, should such be the case. Because experimental sciences, such as chemistry and physics, deal with processes that—in general and within certain limits—are reproducible, such testing of published results is routine, especially if they are deemed suspicious for one reason or another. In sciences that are not experimental, the confirmation or falsification of any purported insight or theory is much harder. Think of economics: although economists work with highly sophisticated mathematical models, their theories are much harder to test, and to verify or falsify, compared to experimental procedures in chemistry and physics. If this were not so, we would all stand a good chance of making millions playing the stock market.

Testing theories of comparative anatomy or comparative embryology is likewise hugely messy. Comparative anatomy weighs the degrees of similarity or dissimilarity of organs or organ systems in the plants or animals under comparison, but many say that similarity ultimately lies in the eye of the beholder. What looks similar to one investigator may look dissimilar to another. People who go to the zoo expect to see crocodiles in the reptile house, along with turtles, lizards, and snakes. The birds, in contrast, have their own birdhouse. But among zoologists and paleontologists, there has been vigorous and protracted debate as to whether crocodiles should be classified in a group that also includes lizards but does not include birds, or whether crocodiles should be classified in a group that also includes birds but does not include lizards. At the root of this debate lie different measures of similarity. What is being measured, i.e., the lizard, the crocodile, or the bird on the dissecting table, is the same to all participants in the debate. But how similarity should be measured—this is where the disagreement arises. It is the same old salmon–lungfish–cow problem all over again, one that was touched on in chapters 1 and . The earlier discussion of that problem moved in the context of what was called the cladistic revolution, i.e., a reorientation of biological systematics along new methodological rules that differed from those that had been adopted in the past. The claim that the lungfish is more closely related to the cow than to the salmon was defended with reference to a newly introduced method that was to be used to measure evolutionary relationships, i.e., to reconstruct phylogeny. But methods, and the rules and rulers they prescribe, are not things observed in nature. They are instead things that scientists, or groups of scientists, collectively agree on, then collectively apply to nature. Remember science philosopher Karl R. Popper, who compared the scientific community, or competing parts thereof, to a jury. In the cladistic revolution, competing groups of scientists—the conservative and the progressive—would form adverse juries: they would get on the telephone, lobby to form interest groups, and establish new professional societies that would publish new journals, in defense and support of their competing views. Put that way, the picture that science paints of the world is one that is no longer only and entirely determined by objective observation. This is because those observations will be evaluated, and will gain significance, only in light of methods that are subject to political maneuvering among scientists and their struggle for financial support from foundations and granting agencies. At the end of the day, what counts is a collective agreement among groups of scientists to use one but not the other method in the evaluation of observations. What this all boils down to is the idea, forcefully defended by a number of philosophers of science, that the picture that some sciences paint of the world is certainly not entirely, but sometimes to a remarkable degree, socially constructed.

Comparative anatomy is notorious for carrying on endless debates about potential homology relations and their significance for the reconstruction of the tree of life. The endless debates about the evolutionary relationships of turtles are just one notorious example among many. Historians of science have recorded a general disenchantment among biologists of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries with comparative anatomy and its application in a research program that seeks to decipher evolutionary relationships (Nyhart, 2002). This was because there seemed to be no way to call for a stringent test of alternative theories about evolutionary relationships, or of a stringent test of proposed homology relations on which these theories are based. Many biologists in the early twentieth century turned their backs on the innumerable, often highly speculative phylogenetic trees that sprouted in the front yards of evolutionary morphologists; they turned instead to more experimental branches within the broad realm of biology, such as experimental developmental biology, physiology, and particularly genetics. Our heroes from the previous chapter, Thomas H. Morgan and his nemesis, Richard Goldschmidt, both provide prime examples of that trend. Both started their career, broadly speaking, as comparative anatomists, but both ended up focusing their interests and research efforts on genetics. As historian of biology Peter J. Bowler stated, "Evolutionary morphology will provide much evidence for those historians seeking to argue that scientific knowledge is socially constructed" (1996:73). What lies at the heart of the social construction of the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor is a firm commitment to the Darwinian theory of evolution—that is, the firm commitment to the transformationist, as opposed to emergentist, paradigm that guides the reconstruction of turtle origins.

Such contrasting paradigms, as are the transformationist versus the emergentist perspectives, can also be called contrasting scientific thought styles, i.e., styles of thinking that scientists get used to in the course of their training, or get committed to in the course of their work and interactions with colleagues. Styles of scientific thought were first identified and discussed by Polish Jewish physician and biologist Ludwik Fleck (1896–1961) from Lemberg (now L'viv), later an inmate of the concentration camps of Auschwitz and Buchenwald, in a book manuscript that he submitted for publication in 1933. The book was published in 1935, bearing the title _Entstehung und Entwicklung einer wissenschaftlichen Tatsache: Einführung in die Lehre vom Denkstil und Denkkollektiv_ (Genesis and Development of a Scientific Fact: Introduction to the Theory of Thought Style and Thought Collective) (for further details, see Wojciech, 2012). The idea here is that a scientific fact does not exist as such, in and of itself. Rather, a scientific fact is to some significant degree embedded in the way a scientist, or a group of scientists, see the world, think about the world, and talk about the world. The style of thought that scientists adopt will influence the way in which they develop theories about the world as they see it. A group of scientists all sharing the same style of scientific thought is prone to reach a consensus with respect to the theories about the world that they develop and accept. Of course, this runs against the common understanding that science is about the "discovery of facts that exist 'in the world' out there." Fleck's position was that the world does not confront us readily "structured into facts," but that the factual structure of the world is, to a significant degree, conditioned by the style of thought adopted by scientists (Hacking, 1999:60). The Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor is an example of such a scientific fact, one that emerged from a certain style of scientific thought as captured by the transformationist paradigm.

The concept of a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor is based on the graphic rendition of a hypothetical (i.e., unobserved) turtle ancestor, where the body contours, or at least the back of the sketched animal, is dotted with black spots. The scattered black dots represent osteoderms, plates of bone ossifying in the dermis layer of the skin and thought to have been present in the turtle ancestor. Through subsequent stages of evolutionary transformation, these ancestral osteoderms would in one way or another coalesce to form the larger plates that would eventually make up the turtle carapace, some of which (the neural and costal plates) would subsequently fuse with the underlying endoskeleton (dorsal vertebral neural arches and ribs). The Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor thus illustrates the theory that the turtle carapace is derived from ancestral osteoderms in a series of graded transformational steps. In the absence of clues gleaned from the fossil record or from the embryonic development of turtles, such a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor must of course be a purely hypothetical construct filling in the gaps that remain in a transformational series of observed fossils. However, and despite early claims to the contrary, Rathke's (1848) observations have stood the test of time. No modern study of turtle development—and there have been a few—has shown osteoderms to first form independently, then to coalesce and eventually to fuse with the underlying dorsal vertebral neural arches and ribs in the formation of a carapace. Given the fact, however, that so many reptiles—both living and fossil—had and have osteoderms embedded in their skin, it seemed reasonable to postulate exactly such a process of fusion of osteoderms first with each other, then with the underlying vertebrae and ribs in order to explain the origin of the turtle carapace in terms of an uninterrupted series of steps of evolutionary transformation. This seems all the more justified in that the Late Triassic turtle _Proganochelys_ , thought to be the oldest known turtle for more than 100 years, developed osteoderms on its limbs, neck, and tail (Gaffney, 1990).

The first to illustrate a putative turtle ancestor with scattered, as yet unfused osteoderms covering the back was Versluys (1914). However, taking into account evidence provided by the investigation of the embryonic development of the carapace, he did not end up postulating a fusion of such osteoderms with underlying vertebrae and ribs. He rather envisioned two diverging lines of turtle evolution. The hypothetical turtle ancestor, he thought, was characterized by an as yet incompletely developed carapace (theca), with a scattering of osteoderms on top of it. The first lineage he stipulated was the one leading to the leatherback sea turtle and its fossil relatives, where the osteoderm covering was completed at the expense of the underlying carapace (theca). The other linage of turtle evolution he thought to have fully developed the carapace (theca) at the expense of an osteoderm covering. Of course, such a reconstruction of the development of the turtle shell collapsed with the recognition that the leatherback and its relatives do not constitute a separate evolutionary lineage leading all the way back to the very origin of turtles but are instead deeply nested within the tree of cryptodire turtles.

**5.6.** First graphic rendition of Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor, showing osteoderms (in black) lying atop incompletely developed carapace, or theca.

_Redrawn after Versluys, 1914, fig. 10; drawing by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

The Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor made its second appearance in the scientific literature much later, in a paper published in _Nature_ in 1996. This paper was previously mentioned in chapter 2, as its author, Michael S. Y. Lee, invoked a principle of correlated progression to explain the evolutionary origin of the turtle body plan. The principle is firmly rooted in the transformationist paradigm, as is evident from the accompanying illustration, which was meant to depict the series of intermediate forms that link turtles to Paleozoic stem-reptiles (1996:fig. 1). It was during the course of his graduate student research at Cambridge University that Lee found evidence he thought would link turtles to pareiasaurs, as previously discussed. The reincarnation of the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor was based on two pillars. The first is the result of a cladistic analysis that related turtles to pareiasaurs, the latter known to develop an osteoderm covering in the more advanced members of the group. In fact, pareiasaurs have been said to demonstrate an evolutionary trend, also known as a morphocline, that progresses from large forms covered by few osteoderms that are arranged along the dorsal midline of the body to smaller forms covered by a heavy dermal armor composed of osteoderms (Scheyer and Sander, 2009:fig. 1). The second pillar is the presence of osteoderms on the limbs, neck, and tail in the then oldest fossil turtle known, _Proganochelys_ from the Late Triassic of Germany. In that turtle, osteoderms occur together with a fully formed carapace, and given the fact that in 1996 _Proganochelys_ was still the oldest and most primitive fossil turtle known, it was reasonable to assume that osteoderm formation is a primitive feature of turtles.

The idea, then, to bridge the "famous morphological gap" that separates turtles from Paleozoic (parareptilian) stem-reptiles was to place turtles at the extreme end of the morphocline exhibited by pareiasaurs (Lee, 1993:1716). A morphocline is a theoretical construct, a transformation series, as discussed above, whereby organisms are serially arranged in such a way that a gradual, stepwise, and directed change in anatomy becomes perceptible. Such serial arrangement of inferred or postulated steps of morphological transformation was the hallmark of preevolutionary comparative anatomy pursued in the century predating the publication of Darwin's _Origin_ in 1859. It is easily understood that after the advent of evolutionary thought, such transformation series were reconstructed with increasing fervor but were now interpreted as evidence of successive steps of evolutionary change. There was only one difference in the way such transformation series were interpreted in the times before and after Darwin. Before Darwin, such transformation series were considered to be in principle readable in both directions. After Darwin, such transformation series, now infused with evolutionary meaning, could be read in one direction only, leading from the primitive to the advanced condition of form (Rieppel, 2011). Lee's morphocline, or transformation series, thus sets out from the primitive condition, i.e., some unarmored parareptile. The next link in the chain is a lightly armored pareiasaur, leading on to a more heavily armored pareiasaur, and further on to a dwarf pareiasaur where the osteoderms meet each other in sutures, thus providing a closed dermal armor. The next steps that would lead to turtles would imply the coalescence of the originally small osteoderms to form the larger plates, ultimately those that we know make up the turtle carapace, and then the fusion of some of those plates—the neural and costal plates—with the underlying endoskeletal vertebral neural arches and ribs. The scapula would, according to Lee, come to be positioned inside the rib cage as a consequence of a posterior dislocation of the pectoral girdle, as had been argued by D. M. S. Watson in his _Eunotosaurus_ paper of 1914—a hypothesis that had been rejected by Ruckes in 1929. In this chain of argumentation, the transformationist paradigm focused attention on the gradual, stepwise transformation of adult structures, exemplified in this case by fossils that could be aligned in a progressive transformation series. However, subsequent investigations of the cellular structure of pareiasaur osteoderms revealed "fundamental changes" in the way they ossify compared to elements in the turtle carapace or to osteoderms of other eureptiles (Scheyer and Sander, 2009:1160).

**5.7.** Hypothetical progressive evolution of turtle carapace from ancestral osteoderms.

_Redrawn after Lee, 1996, fig. 1A; drawing by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

The next appearance of the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor was in 2009 in the pages of the _Proceedings of the Royal Society of London_. It followed the discovery of fragmentary remains of a thin-shelled turtle from the Late Triassic (Norian) of the Chinle Group of New Mexico. The age of _Chinlechelys_ is thus similar to that of _Proganochelys_ , some 215 million years old, but the elements of the carapace are much thinner. _Chinlechelys_ was identified by its discoverers as "one of the most primitive unambiguous members of the turtle stem lineage" (Joyce et al., 2009:507). One fragment in particular unquestionably identifies these fossil remains as parts of a turtle carapace: a couple of incomplete neural plates associated with two fragmentary vertebrae and a rib that articulates on the boundary between these two vertebrae. _Chinlechelys_ was further characterized by neck spines composed of multiple osteoderms, evidence the authors took to indicate that "the earliest known turtles were covered with rows of dermal armor," i.e., rows of osteoderms (Joyce et al., 2009:507). The ribs of _Chinlechelys_ —broadened in the vertical plane, not in the horizontal plane, which is the way they broaden in turtle embryos—were further found to be only "poorly associated with the overlying costal bones, indicating that these two structures [i.e., the ribs and the costal plates] are independent ossifications in basal turtles," quite unlike the way ribs develop into costal plates in turtle embryos (Joyce et al., 2009:507). All of this, of course, implies that the elements of the carapace form by the coalescence of initially independent osteoderms to form larger plates, some of which subsequently fuse with the underlying endoskeleton (vertebrae and ribs), thus rendering the turtle shell a composite structure. "The composite ('gradual') origin of the turtle shell" was accordingly illustrated in terms of a transformation series that starts out from a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor, a "hypothetical" ancestor, according to the authors (Joyce et al., 2009:511, fig. 4 caption).

The Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor was a theoretical construct, but it gained acceptance in the context of a shared style of scientific thought, one closely aligned with the transformationist paradigm. This hypothetical ancestor was eventually dropped, however, after the discovery of an ancestral turtle in the Late Triassic of China that aligns almost perfectly with what we know about the embryonic development of turtles. Those fossils will be introduced in the next chapter; at this juncture, it suffices to say that the Chinese fossils rendered the derivation of thecal elements in the carapace, such as the neural and costal plates, from ancestral osteoderms obsolete. However, the historical persistence of the composite theory of carapace evolution, which derived neural and costal plates from ancestral osteoderms that first coalesced, then fused to the underlying vertebrae and ribs, remains remarkable, especially in the light of developmental studies that lent no support to a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor. All of the above is not meant to say that the proponents of such a derivation of the turtle shell did not base their interpretation on the careful study of fossils, their bones, and the bone's cellular structure. But it does show the force with which certain paradigmatic perspectives, i.e., certain ways of seeing or ways of thinking, can influence the construction of scientific theory. The scientists who most recently developed the composite theory of carapace evolution were all firmly wedded to a Darwinian worldview, and consequently to the transformationist perspective. The consequence is a gradual, stepwise modification of ancestral structures into descendant ones. Such a process of gradual, stepwise transition from one anatomical structure to a different one would issue the prediction that the ancestral and descendant condition of form should and could be bridged by a series of intermediates. In a perfect world, one with a complete fossil record, it should be possible to reconstruct such transformation series in terms of a sequence of ancestors and descendants. In our less than perfect world, where the fossil record is woefully incomplete, missing transitional forms become hypothetical ancestors, theoretical constructs that satisfy the requirements to complete the whole transformation series that links the ancestral with its ultimate descendant condition of form. In the case of turtles and their derivation from unarmored ancestors, this requirement resulted in the repeated reconstruction of the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor, a theoretical construct that came to represent a hypothetical reality buried in deep time through a process of consensus building among scientists who were collectively researching the evolutionary origin of turtles from a transformationist perspective.

There is no question that Darwinian evolution through variation and natural selection at the level of interbreeding populations does occur, but the question remains whether this is the only possible way of evolutionary transformation. This was one of the major questions asked, and answered in the negative, by early representatives of evolutionary developmental biology such as Stephen Jay Gould and Pere Alberch. There is, they argued, also the possibility of emergent evolution, the creation of new structures not by the transformation of adult ancestral ones but by the reprogramming of embryonic development through a rewiring of its underlying genetic base (Wagner, 2014). Acceptance of the emergentist paradigm and reference to embryonic development might have rendered the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor problematic a long time ago, but possibly at a cost judged to be simply too high by classical Darwinian transformationists—namely, the need to invoke the possibility of a hopeful monster. Sometimes, however, the monstrosity of such a hopeful monster can be significantly reduced by a lucky fossil find, as was the case with an ancestral turtle that emerged from the Late Triassic of southwestern China. And so it is to Chinese fossils that we now turn.

**6.1.** Paleogeographic reconstruction of Early Triassic continents.

_Reproduced with permission; copyright by Christopher R. Scotese, director, Paleomap Project_.

**Fossil Hunting in China**

6

Why Search for Triassic Marine Reptiles in Southern China?

WHAT TODAY IS SOUTHERN CHINA WAS, AT THE CLOSE OF THE PAleozoic some 250 million years ago, a separate, relatively small continent called the Yangtze Plate, or simply the South China Block. It was during the Triassic, the geologic era that succeeded the Paleozoic, that the South China Block started to slowly dock onto the Asian plate, thus becoming part of the supercontinent Pangaea. Two major ocean systems enveloped this supercontinent, which itself, again during the Triassic, started to slowly break up in other parts of the world into what would eventually become the modern continents we know today. The larger of the two ocean systems, forming the eastern coast of Pangaea and in some loose sense the geologic precursor of the Pacific Ocean, is called Panthalassa or the Panthalassic Ocean. The smaller ocean system to the southwest of Pangaea is called the Tethys, which, in its youngest and westernmost part, and again in a loose sense, was the geologic precursor of the Mediterranean Sea.

The majority of Triassic marine reptiles are found in three distinct faunal provinces. One area where these creatures can be found are in outcrops of central and southern Europe, as well as at localities around the Mediterranean Sea in Turkey, the Levant, and Tunisia. This is correspondingly called the Western Tethyan Faunal Province. A second area of occurrence of Triassic marine reptiles is in western North America, with outcrops ranging from British Columbia through Nevada all the way down into northern California, with a smattering of bone fragments coming from the interior of Alaska. This western North American faunal province, bordered by the Panthalassic Ocean, is called the Eastern Pacific Faunal Province. The third distinct geographic region yielding an abundance of Triassic marine reptiles is southwestern China, i.e., the area that once formed part of the South China Block. The marine Triassic deposits of the South China Block attract attention because at that time, the South China Block sat right on the boundary between the eastern Tethyan and the western Pacific Faunal provinces. This, of course, provokes the curiosity of any paleontologist who wants to relate the distribution and evolutionary relationships of Triassic marine organisms to the evolution of the earth's continents. The science that seeks to understand the evolution of the earth's continents from the perspective of the geographic distribution and evolutionary relationships of extinct organisms is called paleobiogeography.

Triassic marine reptiles are of particular interest to the paleobiogeographer for a variety of reasons. First, they are reptiles, and hence they have an anatomy that is much more complex than that of some invertebrate groups, such as the extinct cephalopods known as ammonites. The more complex the anatomy, the more characters can be fed into the analysis of evolutionary relationships. Or, to put it in other words, the phylogenetic relationships of those extinct reptiles can be reconstructed in greater detail, and with a greater degree of confidence, than is possible for many extinct invertebrate groups of organisms. Second, and with the exception of the large ichthyosaurs, Triassic marine reptiles were not creatures that roamed the wide-open sea, as their pelagic descendants such as plesiosaurs and pliosaurs would do during Jurassic and Cretaceous times. During the Triassic, marine reptiles remained largely restricted to nearshore habitats, lagoonal settings like those of the Bahamas today, or epicontinental seas, i.e., shallow warm-water seas that formed across stretches of continents as a consequence of a transient sea-level rise. Such ecological constraints also mean that any particular species of Triassic marine reptiles had a geographically rather restricted distribution, unlike some of the larger ichthyosaurs, which thanks to their pelagic habits had already achieved a cosmopolitan distribution during the Triassic. The rather restricted geographic distribution of any one species of Triassic marine reptiles renders the major evolutionary lineages of Triassic marine reptiles at a global scale more highly diverse. This is because different species evolved in different geographic areas instead of few species spreading out to occupy vast geographic spaces. Putting all of these components together results in a great diversity of locally distributed species of Triassic marine reptiles—ideal conditions to relate the study of their evolutionary relationships to the evolution of the earth's continents (see Rieppel, 1999, for a more technical discussion). The big question with respect to the Triassic marine reptiles from southern China is whether there could be a possibility that some of them would share evolutionary relationships with species from the Eastern Pacific Faunal Province, which is western North America today. If so, how could that be the case, given the fact that these species were not adapted to disperse all the way across the Panthalassic Ocean? To travel all the way along the northern coast of the supercontinent Pangaea would not have worked either, as it would have taken these animals to high latitudes and hence to a cold climate zone that reptiles are not well adapted to tolerate. Evolutionary relationships between Triassic marine reptile species from the South China Block and the Western Tethyan Faunal Province would, in contrast, be far less surprising. At the time, there were small, microcontinental plates of southern origin moving north through the Tethys Ocean. These would eventually attach to the Asian plate, but during the Triassic, these microcontinental plates formed a series of shallow-water tropical stepping-stones stretching from east to west more or less along the equator, providing a connection between the Eastern and Western Tethyan Faunal provinces, which today are represented by southern China on the one side, and European and circum-Mediterranean localities on the other (see the Paleomap Project run by Christopher R. Scotese at <http://www.scotese.com/>, accessed June 21, 2016).

Among the first Triassic marine reptiles reported from southwestern China were sauropterygian remains—some woefully incomplete—found in the late 1950s by employees of the Chinese Geological Survey.
