They were described by the godfather of Chinese vertebrate paleontology, Chun-Chien Young (1897–1979), from the Institute of Vertebrate Paleontology and Paleoanthropology (IVPP) in Beijing, the latter administered by the Chinese Academy of Sciences. In my pursuit of a global systematic revision of sauropterygians as a prerequisite for later paleobiogeographic analysis, I first visited the IVPP in March 1997 to study these initial finds, kept in the IVPP'S collections. It was also a most welcome opportunity to visit Professor Mee-mann Chang at her home institution. I had previously met her in London and Zurich. Chang is the leading expert on fossil fishes working in China (Normile, 2001:237). As noted on the IVPP website, Chang's career was far from easy and straightforward (<http://english.ivpp.cas.cn/pe/cm/>, accessed June 21, 2016). Born in 1936, the daughter of a noted Nanjing pathologist, Chang turned to the study of rocks and the fossils they contain rather than choosing medicine as a career path. During the period known as the Great Leap Forward, launched in 1958 and aiming at industrialization and an agrarian collectivization under the rule of Mao Zedong's Communist Party, Chang heeded the call for geological research targeting China's natural resources. However, fossil fishes soon captured her interest and passion. So what better thing to do than to embark, in 1965, on graduate student research at the Swedish Museum of Natural History in Stockholm, at the time a leading institution in paleoichthyology. After the catastrophic failure of the Great Leap Forward, Mao reasserted himself in 1966 as the supreme ideological and political leader of China through launching the Cultural Revolution. Upon hearing the news, Chang dropped her research in Sweden and returned to Beijing. Unrest was rapidly rising in Chinese high schools and universities, from which emerged radicalized student groups, the Red Guards, who sought to purge elitist intellectualism at the universities in favor of "purist egalitarianism" (Spence, 1990:607). Confronted by Red Guards, Chang—like so many other young and promising students aiming for a distinguished academic career—was sent to work in the fields of remote rural areas. As the Cultural Revolution wound down, Chang seized the opportunity to resume her graduate student research at the Natural History Museum in Stockholm. Heavily influenced by the Swedish school, she delivered a painstakingly detailed anatomical analysis of a Devonian lobe-finned fish called _Youngolepis_ , named after Professor Chun-Chien Young. Her outstanding work was recognized by the Chinese Academy of Sciences, who named her an academician, an honor that kick-started her career at the IVPP. She was the first woman to rise to the rank of director of the IVPP, a post she held from 1983 through 1991. Today she holds a joint professorial position at the IVPP and Peking University, is a member of the Chinese Academy of Sciences, and a research associate at the AMNH, and she holds an honorary doctorate from the University of Chicago. She is also a foreign member of the Linnean Society of London, an honorary member of the Society of Vertebrate Paleontology, and a foreign member of the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences.

When Chang and I first met in London, she was engaged in discussions with Colin Patterson and other paleoichthyologists at the British Museum of Natural History, and through those acquaintances, she was exposed to the burgeoning cladistic revolution. It was my own interest and competence in this new method of phylogeny reconstruction that led her to endorse my work, following Colin's recommendation. Upon my arrival in Beijing in 1997, she immediately took me out to lunch. One of the dishes looked unfamiliar to me, so I inquired, "Are these glass noodles?" "No, these are jellyfish, of course, jellyfish!" she exclaimed. She was a wonderful host, inviting me to a party at her house with faculty members of the IVPP, who introduced me to the art of _gan bei_ (drink it all), using expensive imported French wine for this exercise in the Chinese way of toasting. "Drink ex, drink ex," my Chinese colleagues cheered on—what a waste of good-tasting wine! Chang also arranged for me to visit the Geological Museum of China in Beijing. It was the time when the first spectacularly preserved fossil birds known as _Confuciusornis_ had been found in Early Cretaceous lake sediments (some 125 million years old) of Liaoning province, the very locality that would later yield the famous feathered dinosaurs. The director took me to the main meeting room of the museum, where flat wooden boxes much like oversize cigar boxes were arranged on the long table. The boxes were lined with red velvet cloth, each containing a fossil bird. One after another, he picked them up and lifted the lid just long enough for me to glance at the fossil, leaving no time to spot any detail, let alone take any pictures.

It was Mee-mann Chang who opened the door for me to work in China in what would become a truly long-term collaborative engagement with Chinese colleagues. Unlike the newly discovered fossil birds, Triassic marine reptiles already described by C. C. Young were put at my disposal at the IVPP, allowing me to take notes and photographs, and to make drawings. Back in my lab, I started to analyze the phylogenetic relationships of these fragmentary Chinese sauropterygians—and lo and behold! I picked up an admittedly weak signal for trans-Panthalassan (i.e., trans-Pacific) relationships of one of the Chinese sauropterygians with a species that is known from the late Early Triassic of Wyoming, _Corosaurus_ from the reddish Alcova Limestone abundantly exposed near the city of Casper. This is also the easternmost occurrence of any sauropterygian in North America. However, given the incompleteness of both the Chinese fossil and _Corosaurus_ , few characters were available to link the two species, so the signal for possible evolutionary relationships remained weak indeed. The signal was there nonetheless, and it put research on Triassic marine reptiles from southern China on the map for me. But where were the fossils?

The Guanling Biota

Guizhou ranks among the poorest provinces in China. Its remote, mountainous terrain is difficult to navigate; the region is inhabited by a largely peasant ethnic minority population that grows corn and tobacco. In the summer, vast cole-crop (mustard plant) flower fields paint the landscape in a beautiful yellow, attracting throngs of tourists and motivating folkloristic festivals celebrated by the Miao people. During my first field season there in September 2000, I was horrified by the huge blue trucks that hauled coal out of the mountains along narrow, winding roads. A large barrel would be mounted on the top of the truck's cabin, dripping lukewarm water on the hot brakes. Small wonder we came across many accidents! A driver who had wrecked his truck by hitting the pillar of a bridge lay on the roadside wrapped in white sheets. He would soon be carried to his last resting place in his home village in a colorful ceremony with cymbals, pipes, and fireworks, accompanied by a live pig, destined to become the centerpiece for the ensuing banquet, along with a couple of chickens perhaps. People in Guizhou bury their loved ones on the hillsides of the karst mountains, choosing the site such that the headstone faces a beautiful vista. As the rush for fossils gathered steam, whole mountainsides were turned over and tunneled by farmers in search of sea lilies (crinoids), reptiles, good luck, and fortune. In those remote mountain villages, a house with a new roof signifies a farmer who successfully sold a large reptile skeleton. A family of six in Xinpu township, a major fossil excavation site near the town of Guanling, was estimated to earn around 10,000 yuan per year, but a well-preserved reptile fossil "can sell for tens of thousands of yuan," said Liting Wang, Guizhou's state geologist (<http://usa.chinadaily.com.cn/life/2012-03/28/content_14930069.htm>, accessed June 21, 2016). Yet with all their eagerness to uncover fossilized riches, in every instance, the fossil hunters leave the scattered tombs intact, with the headstones eerily sticking out like solitary monuments from the float of discarded shards of rock.

The gold rush for Triassic marine reptiles in southwestern China set in suddenly and unexpectedly in the late 1990s. Invertebrate fossils from the Triassic black shales in the area of Guanling county in Guizhou province had been reported in the scientific literature as early as the 1940s, but it wasn't until the late 1990s that the floodgates opened for a plethora of marine reptiles to come to light. The Guizhou government had started to invest heavily in economic development. Freeways were built connecting the capital of Guizhou province, Guiyang, with cities like Xingyi through new superhighways across breathtaking bridges. Housing projects sprang up to accommodate a growing trend toward urbanization. The amount of public debt rose in tandem with the demand for concrete. As limestone quarrying intensified, incredible riches of fossils came to light. Mee-mann Chang arranged for me to become part of a team of IVPP researchers who would focus on the study of these newly discovered Triassic marine reptiles. The graduate students, faculty, and staff whom I accompanied on the September 2000 field trip, funded by the National Geographic Society, all stayed in modest government housing in Guanling, the town located closest to the newly discovered fossil digging sites. The town was dirty and messy, and hot water was hard to come by. Guanling was indeed designated a national poverty county by the Ministry of Civil Affairs in 2008, although it now boasts a new five-star hotel (<http://usa.chinadaily.com.cn/life/2012-03/28/content_14930069.htm>, accessed June 21, 2016). Major nearby tourist attractions include the famous Huangguoshu waterfall, the Huangjiang big gorge (also known as the crack of the earth), and a newly established geopark (Wang et al., 2009). A couple of times during our many sojourns in town, we visited an old Communist Party training center sitting behind a tall, rusty fence, where the Guizhou government kept confiscated Triassic marine reptiles under lock and key in a dusty, poorly lit room—not the best conditions to get a good look at them. Where did these fossils come from? Who had collected them, and why had the government confiscated them?

The owner of the confiscated fossil reptiles that were kept on the Communist Party premises in Guanling had lost a small fortune through the government's actions. He was rumored to be a successful Chineseborn businessman now resident in the United States. He had bought the fossils from friends, with the declared purpose to build a private institute for paleontological research and development. It was suspected that development here would mean fossils on display in a museum exhibit accessible through the front door while fossils were traded through the backdoor. Before the government seized his collection, however, the amateur collector and fossil dealer had his fossils photographed, described, named, and published by Chinese paleontologists in a Chinese geological journal. Their owner perhaps hoped the importance—and with it the commercial value—of his fossils would increase as a consequence of their formal published description. He fought the confiscation of his collection in court, but the ruling was that the fossils belonged to the Guanling Buyei-Miao autonomous county, from which soil they had been dug up. The dismal descriptions of new genera and species of these Triassic marine reptiles were published in a Chinese journal of geology and geochemistry. The quality of the published photographs is extremely poor, allowing no identification of any detail beyond the general contours of the fossils. The genus and species names were properly introduced, however, so as to satisfy the international code of zoological nomenclature. Although the species diagnoses remained vacuous, the locality data of these commercially acquired fossils necessarily vague, and the photographs useless, all the elements were in place to render those genus and species names scientifically valid names. That way, this publication (Yin et al., 2000) became the first published report on the diversity of fossil reptiles from the Guanling area, of early Late Triassic (Carnian) age, some 235 million years old. Given their history, many of the specimens in the confiscated collection became holotypes—that is, paradigmatic exemplars of their species—which to the present day remain inaccessible to proper scientific investigation, description, and illustration. This, of course, represents a major stumbling block in the systematic treatment of all those fossil reptiles, which, together with associated invertebrates such as the beautifully preserved sea lilies (crinoids), constitute the famous Guanling biota.

Anybody wishing to publish the name of a new species is required, according to the international code of nomenclature, to not only provide a diagnosis that details how the species differs from all others in its genus, an indication of the locality where the new species was found, and an illustration of a representative specimen of the new species, but also to designate a holotype. For any properly described species, the holotype serves as the ultimate standard of comparison. In a loose sense, the holotype is for a species what the birth certificate is for a person. Anybody wanting to introduce a new species must make sure that the relevant specimens do indeed represent a new species different from all others in its genus that have previously been described. This must ultimately be ascertained through a detailed comparison with the holotypes of the congeneric species previously described. With the holotypes covered in dust in a dimly lit government building, this is impossible. Until the government of Guanling county decides to release the confiscated fossils to a public museum, the systematic treatment of the Guanling fauna continues to face problems. The good news, however, is that the government of Guanling county has stepped up its efforts to curb the at times excessive illegal private collecting and trafficking in fossils—to protect not only its natural heritage but also the landscape itself, and sometimes even the collectors. Extensive quarries created by fossil collectors threatened to ruin the view of a landscape composed of beautiful karst mountains. Collectors would dig uncontrolled tunnels into the fossiliferous layers of sedimentary rock, which can turn into death traps when they collapse. One family we visited had turned to the practice of removing the topsoil from the underlying rock after the corn had been harvested in the fall. Fossil digging continued throughout winter until spring, when the topsoil was spread out and corn was planted again. A Guanling Fossil Group geopark was established in 2003, upgraded the following year to the Guanling Fossil Group National GeoPark. The whole enterprise turned into a 30 million yuan project with the goal not just to protect the fossil sites but also to educate the local population and to attract tourism to this impoverished part of Guizhou province (<http://usa.chinadaily.com.cn/life/2012–03/28/content_14930069.htm>, accessed June 21, 2016). Today, a museum displays the rich diversity of fossil reptiles as well as the beautiful crinoids so characteristic of the Guanling biota. An IMAX theater brings the fossils to life in color movies, and signs along an extensive boardwalk trail explain the local geology. An expert visiting the geopark might be a little surprised or disappointed, however, by some of the specimens on exhibit, because some have visibly been doctored. The falsification of fossils is indeed epidemic in China, and increasingly difficult to recognize as the skill level of the farmers who sell these fossils improves.

There are two ways to collect Triassic marine reptiles in southwestern China. The scientifically best controlled, but also most tedious and likewise quite expensive, way is to hire local farmers for a dig, to compensate the village of these farmers for the loss of fertile terrain and crop, and to excavate the fossils with a proper permit and with expert scientific supervision. To fulfill all these requirements requires a lot of communication with local officials, mostly conducted over traditional Chinese banquets with their strictly regulated seating orders and hierarchically structured toasting sequences: _gan bei_ , often with _maotei_ , an expensive local liquor that ordinary people drink on Chinese New Year—if they can afford it. The benefits of excavating fossils following scientific standards, however, are many. Most importantly, the geographic and geologic (stratigraphic) provenience of the fossils is exactly known, and if prepared under scientific supervision, they will not have been tampered with. The downside of fossil excavation is that the employed farmers learn to recognize which layers in the sequence of rocks yield fossils—a knowledge that they may later use to their own advantage.

**6.2.** Fossil excavation site near Guanling, Guizhou province, southwestern China.

_Photograph courtesy of Li Chun, Institute of Vertebrate Paleontology and Paleoanthropology, Beijing_.

This brings us to the second method of collecting Triassic marine reptiles in southern China, which is to arrange ways to essentially buy them from farmers in some officially sanctioned way. One way to do so was dubbed by my colleagues as going fishing. The idea is to take a box lunch to some remote village, park the flashy suv in a central spot, then sit around while eating. The women will have spent hours in the fields by that time of the day, some of them already returning to the village with huge loads of tobacco leaves on their backs; the men, however, will slowly emerge from their dwellings, and after some meandering, they might invite the strangers to come to their houses to see the fossils they have for sale. It is in these poorly lit and dusty conditions that forged fossils become difficult to be identified as such. Forging a fossil here means to embellish specimens through the addition of parts taken from other, less completely preserved and hence less attractive skeletons. I remember having once been invited to describe what looked like a perfect skeleton of a nothosaur-like fossil. As I looked at the limbs, or also at the tail, something seemed to be amiss. Yet there were the typical irregular quartzite veins running through the skeleton, which attested to its genuineness. I asked for a needle and started to scratch. Sure enough, the quartzite veins had been painted on the doctored specimen and could easily be removed.

Composite specimens are nothing unusual in vertebrate paleontology, however. Many mounted dinosaurs on display in museums worldwide are replicas of composite specimens. Consider the case of the famous Howe Ranch in Big Horn County, Wyoming, where the AMNH's Barnum Brown (1873–1963) and his team collected around 4,000 dinosaur bones during a six-month campaign in 1934, an effort continued many years later by Swiss dinosaur hunter Hans-Jakob "Kirby" Siber (born 1942) (Bird, 1985; <http://www.sauriermuseum.ch/cms/upload/Press/Download/DL-english/The_Dinosaur_Museum.pdf>, accessed June 21, 2016). The dinosaurs collected in the Howe Quarry were land-dwelling animals. Their fossilized remains come from carcasses washed down the river all the way into a delta system. Because of the decay of the associated soft parts like muscles and tendons, the skeletons would fall apart in the process. During transport downstream, the individual bones of a skeleton would disarticulate. As these disarticulated elements were washed into the delta system, the smaller, lighter bones would travel farther than the larger, heavier ones. This is how the famous dinosaur graveyard developed in Howe Quarry, a jumble of disarticulated dinosaur bones sorted according to size, not according to the species they represent. The task of the paleontologist is thus to decipher this puzzle, step by step, to learn through extensive comparisons which bones represent the same species and hence go together, and to slowly put together the skeleton by assembling its parts. As one of my colleagues once remarked, there is no unidentifiable bone; there is only lack of anatomic knowledge (and this is probably too optimistic a statement). Any such skeleton is thus most likely composed of bones that came from several individual animals of similar size of the same species, and hence may represent a composite specimen.

But things with the Triassic marine reptiles in southern China are quite a bit different. Dead animals were not transported down river systems but rather sank to the seafloor, where the carcasses became embedded in fine-grained sediment. The seafloor sediment remained unperturbed through wave action because it was deposited at a depth below the storm wave base—that is to say, even if a heavy storm whipped up huge waves, these did not reach deep enough into the water column to whirl up the sediment covering the seafloor and the carcasses it contains. Lack of turbulence at such depth usually also results in a paucity, or even lack, of oxygen, which explains the scarcity or absence of scavengers. The carcasses of dead marine reptiles consequently remained undisturbed either by wave action or scavengers while the slow process of decay and subsequent fossilization proceeded. The result is a far lesser degree of disarticulation of the bones than is usual; the best and most beautiful specimens are thus represented by complete and articulated skeletons. But nature is never perfect; nor are the skills of the farmers who excavate these fossils. The temptation is thus great to embellish an incompletely preserved or damaged specimen to make it look more complete, as it then might fetch a higher price. Yielding to this temptation, farmers—who, of course, are not expert paleontologists—often mix up bones that come from skeletons of different species. The result is a chimera, which, if it remains unrecognized, adds to the embarrassment of the scientist who might describe it. Needless to say, there is much debate and discussion in the scientific literature about the genuineness of key Chinese fossils—not just from the Triassic of southern and southwestern provinces but from all over the country.

Today Guanling county is not the only area known to harbor Triassic marine reptiles in its soil. In Guizhou province alone, Xingyi and Panxian counties mark areas where Triassic marine reptiles are found—the Panxian biota of early Middle Triassic age, the Xingyi biota of late Middle Triassic age, with the two faunas ranging from 235 to 240 million years before the present. Another hot spot, similar to the Panxian biota, is found in Luoping county in Yunnan province, while another locality near Chaohu in Anhui province yields scientifically highly important new marine reptiles of late Lower Triassic age, some 248 million years old. These are just the most important sites that receive the greatest scientific, official, and public attention today. Farmers digging up fossils, fixing them up, and selling them have corrupted Chinese paleontology for decades. The illegal export of Chinese vertebrate fossils through Hong Kong, Macao, Taiwan, and Japan for sale abroad has further degraded paleontological resources in China (Liston, 2014:701). The director of the IVPP in Beijing, Zhou Zhonghe, speaks of "'illegal and unscientific collecting and commercial trading' that has flooded the market with fake fossils and caused an 'irretrievable loss' of crucial information" (Balter, 2013:1153). Li Chun from the IVPP, who is surely among the most successful fossil hunters in the Triassic of southwestern China today and who started his career as a graduate student participant in the September 2000 field trip mentioned above, estimates that "more than 80% of marine reptile specimens now on display in Chinese museums have been 'altered or artificially combined to various degrees'" (Stone, 2010a:1740). He is one of the most active campaigners against fraudulent fossils and illegal fossil trade, and has urged improved legislation: "I don't object to efforts to overhaul fossil protection," he says, "but any regulation should embody respect for research freedom" (Yu, 2009:925). He is likewise concerned about the potentially devastating environmental impact of fossil collecting, as seen, for example, at Wolonggang Mountain near Guanling (Wang et al., 2009). In a recent popular book, he wrote, "I have to say sorry to the mountains here, not because we take out their treasures, but because of the destruction of the landscape. A scar was left in the field." Eventually, after some bumps along the long and winding road of federal legislation, new laws were passed in China in 2010, which took effect in 2011, which declare all fossils to be state property and which place the authority of issuing excavation permits into the hands of the Ministry of Land Resources. Applications for excavation permits are to be reviewed by a national panel of paleontologists—the only caveat that remains being the fact that the Ministry of Land Resources will also be the sole arbiter to decide where high-priority fossils will be deposited, either in national or regional universities or museums. (A detailed account of current Chinese legislation on fossil excavation and export is provided by Liston and You, 2015.) This raises the potential problem that because of institutional policies and rivalries, accessibility of certain fossils might become limited for scientists (Stone, 2010b:1453). Again, the world is rarely perfect, and much remains to be done. "We will see" is Zhou Zhonghe's cautious verdict on the new legislation, as the motivation of fossil dealers to stay in business should not be underestimated (Stone, 2010b:1453). But progress is being made. The quarry in Xingyi county, Guizhou province, is now a protected area—an open-air museum, so to speak—where the local government set up a fossil preparation facility right next to the excavation site. This facility not only guarantees the genuineness of the fossils on display but also keeps one or several fossil preparators on the site for surveillance and protection. Geological parks are projected elsewhere, complete with plans for the construction of museums, with the hope that they will attract not only local schoolchildren but also tourism from further afield.

**6.3.** Nimaigu fossil quarry near Xingyi City, Guizhou province, southwestern China, now geopark with adjacent fossil preparation facility.

_Photograph by author_.

Companions of the Ancestral Turtle

The diversity of Triassic marine reptiles from southwestern China is unique. The only other place that comes close in terms of richness of Triassic marine reptiles is the Monte San Giorgio in southern Switzerland (canton of Ticino) and an adjacent locality just across the border in Italy, where a similarly favorable depositional environment—technically known as a Lagerstätte—prevailed during the Middle Triassic: a partially closed marine basin with fine sediment building up in a more or less oxygen-free environment at its bottom, below the storm wave base (Furrer, 2004). At both localities, southwestern China and Monte San Giorgio, the richness of the fossil record can be expressed both in numbers of fossils and numbers of different species. A great variety of species is represented by a large number of often exquisitely preserved fossils. The species of the two localities are often closely related to one another, which is not surprising because of faunal exchange between the Eastern and Western Tethyan faunal provinces at that time. However, the evolutionary relationships of Chinese Triassic marine reptiles extend not only to those known from Monte San Giorgio but also to those from the western Tethys in general.

Some of the lineages of Triassic marine reptiles, mainly sauropterygians, were introduced in chapter 1. Of these, placodonts—both unarmored and armored—occurred on either side of the Tethys Ocean. Skeletons representing the genus _Placodus_ from both Europe and southern China are hardly distinguishable from one another, indicating very close relationships. Of the several species of armored placodonts known from China, one, a cyamodontoid placodont called _Psephochelys_ , exhibits a curious mixture of characteristics otherwise known from two different species of European armored placodonts, _Psephoderma_ from the Upper Triassic of northern Italy and _Placochelys_ from the Upper Triassic of Hungary, the latter the putative turtle ancestor described by Otto Jaekel. Other sauropterygians include pachypleurosaurs, of which several species are known from China. The best known is _Keichousaurus_ , a small, lizardlike creature less than a foot long, known from literally thousands of specimens. They include everything from sexually dimorphic males and females (including gravid females) to a good number of embryos (Cheng, Wu, and Ji, 2004). One is tempted to consider a shallow-water deposit near Xingyi in Guizhou province as having been a communal breeding ground for _Keichousaurus_ , where females gathered to give birth to live offspring. Hundreds of _Keichousaurus_ specimens have been illegally exported and put up for sale along with fake fossils, to be purchased by museums and private collectors all over the world. Pachypleurosaurs are a fairly diverse lot in both the Eastern and Western Tethyan faunal provinces. Their evolutionary relationships indicate that they may have spread from east to west, entering the German Muschelkalk sea through a doorway located in Upper Silesia, then spreading south from the Germanic into what today is the Alpine Triassic through a gateway located in Burgundy. It remains unknown whether this is just pachypleurosaur history or whether this pattern of east-to-west dispersal is also characteristic of other lineages of Triassic marine reptiles. The reason is the current lack of well-supported and well-resolved patterns of phylogenetic relationships. This is not a consequence of a lack of fossils, though. Nothosaurs are again well represented among the Triassic marine reptiles from southern China, as are pistosaurs, the latter close to the ancestry of the plesiosaurs that roamed the Jurassic seas. But as is often the case when new fossils come to light, the new species thus recognized often show character combinations that spoil the old classification of sauropterygian diversity, which was based mainly on European forms. What to do when a fossil comes to light that has not been tampered with, yet sports a nothosaurlike skull on a pistosaur-like neck? A lot of work will be required to incorporate the richness of the sauropterygian fossil record from southern China into an amended global sauropterygian family tree.

Sauropterygians are, of course, not the only lineage of Triassic marine reptiles found in southwestern China. Ichthyosaurs are abundant as well. One quarry near the city of Chaohu in Anhui province, with deposits of late Lower Triassic age, some 248 million years old, yields _Chaohusaurus_ , an early and primitive ichthyopterygian that promises to shed new light on the origin of these dolphinlike reptiles. An even more important discovery from the quarry near Chaohu is a small, ichthyosaur-like creature with a short snout, edentulous jaws, and large, flipperlike forelimbs with a very poorly ossified wrist. This species, called _Cartorhynchus lenticarpus_ , seems to lie even closer to the origin of ichthyopterygians; it might have been able to move across land in a seal-like manner (Motani et al., 2014).

Another enigmatic and little-known clade of Triassic marine reptiles is the thalattosaurs. Although they are relatively rare in European deposits, they are more abundant in western North America, but they reached their greatest numbers and diversity in southwestern China. The thalattosaur genera that stand out are _Anshunsaurus_ , found at a locality in the Anshun prefecture near the famous Huangguoshu waterfall some 40 kilometers from Guanling, and _Xinpusaurus_ , a genus based on one of the specimens seized by the Guizhou government. Both genera comprise two species, and in each of these genera, one of the two species is distinctly larger than the other. This illustrates a familiar pattern of habitat partitioning, as the larger species will favor different prey than the smaller, congeneric species. Instead of competing with each other, these congeneric species thus evolved to partition the food resources between themselves.

Southwestern China was also a hotbed for protorosaur evolution and diversification. Protorosaurs are a diverse group of reptiles that persisted from the Late Paleozoic through the Triassic. They comprise terrestrial, arboreal, and aquatic species, both freshwater and marine; their fossils are found in Europe, Africa, North America, Asia (Russia, India, China), Australia, and even Antarctica. _Prolacerta_ is a species known from Antarctica and South Africa, which at one time had been thought to be ancestral to modern lizards, as its name betrays ( _Lacerta_ is Latin for lizard). Whereas protorosaurs have, in fact, nothing to do with the origin of scaly reptiles (i.e., squamates, which include lizards and snakes), their position within reptiles as a whole, as well as the interrelationships among them, continue to be a matter of debate. It is even doubtful that all the species that are currently included among protorosaurs do indeed form a natural group, i.e., an evolutionary lineage of unique origin. Some of the protorosaurs found in Triassic marine deposits are characterized by a grotesquely elongated neck, the function of which remains difficult to explain. A moderate neck elongation is seen in _Macrocnemus_ , a genus known from Monte San Giorgio and southwestern China that reached about 3 feet (just under 1 meter) in total length. The name of _Macrocnemus_ refers to its long tibia, implicitly to its long hind limbs, which are much longer than the forelimbs. _Macrocnemus_ may have been a predominantly terrestrial organism, which in rapid locomotion may have resorted to bipedal running, as is also seen in modern lizards with similar disparate limb proportions. In contrast to _Macrocnemus_ , the neck vertebrae are extremely narrow and spectacularly elongated in the closely related genus _Tanystropheus_ , an animal with a fully marine lifestyle that grew to up to 20 feet (6 meters) in length. The first remains of _Tanystropheus_ to become known were isolated neck vertebrae from the Middle Triassic of the Germanic Muschelkalk. Given their strange proportions, as well as the fact that they are hollow, as are the bones in pterosaurs, Franz Baron Nopcsa, the eccentric paleontologist from the University of Vienna, first interpreted these isolated vertebrae as phalanges in the much-elongated fourth finger of a pterosaur called _Tribelesodon_. The forth digit in the forelimb is the one that in pterosaurs supports the leading edge of the wing membrane (patagium). It was not until articulated skeletons of _Tanystropheus_ came to light through excavations at Monte San Giorgio that the true nature of these strange elements was recognized. Today, several specimens of _Tanystropheus_ are known from the southern Alps and southwestern China, again closely resembling each other and hence closely related. The neck of _Tanystropheus_ comprises a total of 13 vertebrae, as opposed to the 12 dorsal vertebrae that make up the trunk. However, the neck of _Tanystropheus_ as a whole is nearly three times as long as the trunk. In addition, the cervical ribs are much elongated and aligned along the cervical vertebral column, straddling several intervertebral joints and thus forming strong bundles of bony rods on either side of the neck. This must have contributed to a certain stiffness of the neck, rendering the functional explanation of neck elongation in _Tanystropheus_ even more difficult.

**6.4.** Life reconstruction of _Dinocephalosaurus_ , hunting among school of fish of genus _Saurichthys_. Both species occur in same deposits of Middle Triassic age in Panxian, Guizhou province, southwestern China.

_Artwork by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

A similarly extreme elongation of the neck is observed in _Dinocephalosaurus_ , a bizarre protorosaur exclusively known from the early Middle Triassic of Panxian county, Guizhou province. Only one specimen is currently known in which the entire skeleton, from tip of snout to tip of tail, is preserved. The specimen measures a total of 5.1 meters (just over 16 feet) in length, of which 16 centimeters are attributable to the skull, 214 centimeters to the neck, 100 centimeters to the trunk, and 160 centimeters to the tail. The first specimen found and described, i.e., the holotype, consists of a complete, three-dimensionally preserved skull and a few associated neck vertebrae. Because it was of impressive size, with jaws furnished with a robust dentition including prominent maxillary fangs, the species was called _Dinocephalosaurus orientalis_ by Li Chun, which means "saurian with a terrible head from Asia." Once complete skeletons became known, however, the skull lost some of its frightening appearance, as it is relatively small compared to the whole animal. Neck elongation in _Dinocephalosaurus_ must have evolved independently, i.e., in convergence to _Tanystropheus_ , because the neck in _Dinocephalosaurus_ comprises a total of 33 vertebrae, with the latter not hollow, more robustly built, and individually somewhat less elongated than those in _Tanystropheus_. This might have allowed for a greater flexibility of the neck, but again, and as in _Tanystropheus_ , the cervical ribs are much elongated, straddling up to six intervertebral joints, thus forming strong bundles of bony rods on either side of the neck, presumably contributing to its stiffness.

It is hard to speculate what the function of these extremely elongated necks—two and a half up to nearly three times as long as the trunk—might have been. That there was some adaptive advantage to be had from such neck elongation may be indicated by the fact that it evolved independently twice, in _Tanystropheus_ and in _Dinocephalosaurus_. One hypothesis is that the long neck facilitated feeding under water, i.e., feeding in a dense medium, which poses special problems. Think of grabbing a bar of soap floating in water. As you try to quickly close your hand around the slippery soap, the very closing of the hand creates a jet stream that carries the soap away. The same thing happens when an underwater predator closes its mouth to grab a food item. The closing of the mouth causes water to be expelled from the buccal cavity, creating a jet stream that carries the potential prey away. To avoid that effect, many modern animals that feed under water, like bony fishes, salamanders, and aquatic turtles, pursue a strategy of suction feeding. Aquatic salamanders and turtles increase the volume of the buccal cavity by a depression of the floor of the mouth and of the throat by means of the throat skeleton and its associated musculature. As the jaws open, water is rapidly sucked into the buccal cavity and into the expanded throat region, carrying the targeted prey with it. Once the jaws have clamped down on the prey, the excess water can be expelled. If, in _Tanystropheus_ and _Dinocephalosaurus_ , the cervical ribs splayed outward as the jaws opened, then a large volume would have been created for the esophagus to expand in the neck region. This would have provoked vigorous suction, pulling the targeted prey into the reach of the jaws (Li, Rieppel, and LaBarbera, 2004).

Even more enigmatic than the thalattosaurs or _Dinocephalosaurus_ are the saurosphargids. It is really only the newly discovered Chinese material that began to shed some light on fossils belonging to that group. The name _Saurosphargis_ first appeared in Frech (1903) as a nomen nudum (naked name), so called because the publication of this name was accompanied neither by a diagnosis nor by an illustration of the fossil it referred to; nor was a holotype explicitly designated. The fossil designated by that name was a fragment of a trunk skeleton kept at the University of Breslau, now Wroclaw, in Poland. It came from deposits of the lowermost Middle Triassic, possibly uppermost Lower Triassic near Gogolin (now Gorny Slask) in Upper Silesia. After Hitler's designation of Breslau as a fortress to be defended at all costs early in 1945, the city became the locale of a siege by the advancing Red Army that lasted for three months. In the course of the battle, most of the museum collection, the unique specimen of _Saurosphargis_ included, was destroyed. A good photograph of the specimen was published, however, by F. von Huene in 1936, along with a description, which made it possible to identify complete skeletons from southern China as belonging to the same genus—or at any rate to be closely related to _Saurosphargis_.

The Chinese specimens from the lower Middle Triassic of Panxian county in Guizhou province are complete skeletons including the skull, and they thus refute the earlier hypothesis that _Saurosphargis_ might be a primitive placodont, because it lacked the characteristic crushing tooth plates. However, the precise relationships of saurosphargids remain a matter of ongoing research. Most interesting is a highly derived member of the saurosphargids discovered by Li Chun from the IVPP, _Sinosaurosphargis_ from the lower Middle Triassic Luoping locality in Yunnan province, which is of the same age and in close geographic proximity to the Panxian fauna of Guizhou province (Li et al., 2011). _Sinosaurosphargis_ has a skull with a narrow, anteriorly rounded snout and a wide temporal region, which is deeply embayed from below, suggesting the loss of the lower temporal arch. In contrast to _Saurosphargis, Sinosaurosphargis_ has no upper temporal fenestra, which appears to have been secondarily closed, a hypothesis that has to be entertained for turtles as well. More interesting still is the trunk skeleton, which is strikingly turtlelike (Hirasawa, Nagashima, and Kuratani, 2013). The shape of the body is broad, flattened, and almost disklike. The ribs of the trunk extend from the vertebral column straight outward in an almost horizontal plane, rather than forming a true rib cage as in other, more generalized tetrapods. This being a fossil, one is left to wonder which developmental mechanisms rendered the growth of ribs turtlelike in _Sinosaurosphargis_. Moreover, the ribs are horizontally broadened, forming flat, bladelike structures that contact their neighbors all along their length. While reminiscent of the dorsal ribs in turtles, which eventually form the costal plates of the carapace, the dorsal ribs in _Sinosaurosphargis_ only loosely contact each other along their length, without forming any true sutures. The rigid disklike structure formed by the ribs of the trunk in _Sinosaurosphargis_ is, in addition, completely covered by a closed layer of osteoderms that meet each other in weakly interdigitating sutures. _Sinosaurosphargis_ thus comes across as an almost perfect representation of the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor—if there ever had been one. The rib orientation and consequent body shape are already turtlelike. The ribs are broadened so as to contact each other along their length. Some commentators ascertained "that the rib cage of _Sinosaurosphargis_ exclusively functioned as a shell," as does the carapace in turtles (Hirasawa, Nagashima, and Kuratani, 2013:5). Further, the whole dorsal side of the trunk is covered by osteoderms. All that would apparently be needed to transform _Sinosaurosphargis_ into a turtle would be the fusion of osteoderms into the larger plates characteristic of the carapace and then the subsequent fusion of some of these plates (neurals and costals) with the underlying endoskeleton (dorsal vertebral neural arches and ribs). Although the study of the embryonic development of the turtle carapace sketched in the previous chapter refutes the idea of a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor, _Sinosaurosphargis_ did also not come out as ancestral to turtles in an analysis of its evolutionary relationships. It was a different species from the marine Triassic deposits of southwestern China that was recognized as an ancestral turtle. This most spectacular find to date by Li Chun firmly removes _Sinosaurosphargis_ from any consideration as a possible turtle ancestor, thus laying the theory of a Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor to its final rest.

**6.5.** Holotype of _Sinosaurosphargis yunguiensis_ from Middle Triassic of Panxian, Guizhou, southwestern China. Species is characterized by dorsal dermal armor composed of broadened trunk ribs overlain by dense cover of osteoderms.

_Photograph courtesy of Li Chun, Institute of Vertebrate Paleontology and Paleoanthropology Beijing_.

An Ancestral Turtle on the Half Shell

Li Chun reminisced how one day in 2007, when pursuing fieldwork on the Half Shell in Guizhou near Guanling, his car broke down (<http://usa.chinadaily.com.cn/life/2012–03/28/content_14930069.htm>, accessed June 21, 2016). He took the vehicle to a garage, where the owner, after inquiring about Li's whereabouts, showed him a strange fish fossil that originated from the area. Li felt his blood pulsating as he recognized in the fish fossil the same species he had already seen and acquired fossils of in Beijing: a turtle in the making. _Odontochelys semitestacea_ comes from early Carnian, i.e., early Upper Triassic marine deposits near Guanling in Guizhou province, and is thus the oldest undisputed turtle currently known—some 220 million years old (Li et al., 2008; but see discussion in chapter 2 on the Permian _Eunotosaurus_ and the late Middle Triassic [Ladinian] _Pappochelys_ as putative stem-turtles). It is also the most primitive one. The distinction of being the oldest and most primitive turtle had previously gone to _Proganochelys_ from the middle Upper Triassic (Norian) of Germany. But _Proganochelys_ is, in fact, already a finished turtle, complete with carapace and plastron about 3.3 feet (1 meter) long. The upper and lower jaws of _Proganochelys_ had already lost the dentition and, as in modern turtles, were covered by a keratinous beak called rhamphotheca. The primitiveness accorded to _Proganochelys_ is based on details of skull structure, including the presence of denticles on the bony roof of the mouth, the dermal palate. Such are absent in all other turtles, both fossil and extant, as also is the palatobasal articulation, which joins the dermal palate to the floor of the braincase in _Proganochelys. Odontochelys_ , by comparison, tells a quite different story.

**6.6.** Holotype and paratype of _Odontochelys semitestacea_ from early Late Triassic Guanling biota, Guizhou province, southwestern China. Holotype ( _left_ ) shows broadened ribs; paratype ( _right_ ) shows fully developed plastron.

_Photograph courtesy of Li Chun, Institute of Vertebrate Paleontology and Paleoanthropology, Beijing_.

This ancestral turtle is a relatively small animal, 40 to 50 centimeters (15 to 19 inches) in length from the tip of the snout to the tip of the tail. The skull is not truncated anteriorly, as it is in _Proganochelys_ and modern turtles, but instead extends into a pointed snout. The upper and lower jaws are furnished with small, pointed, peglike teeth, and denticles are also strewn across the dermal palate. As in all diapsid reptiles, the tympanic membrane was suspended from the quadrate bone at the back end of the skull. But whereas in modern turtles the quadrate bone forms a funnel-shaped structure across which the tympanum is stretched, it is a simple, posteriorly concave structure in _Odontochelys_ , a primitive feature also shared by _Proganochelys_. The vertebral count for _Odontochelys_ is eight cervicals in the neck, nine dorsals in the trunk, two sacrals, and minimally 20 caudals, which for turtles translates into a long tail, another primitive feature. Whereas the pectoral girdle in _Odontochelys_ , and especially the shoulder blade (scapula), again retains a primitive morphology relative to modern turtles, the pelvic girdle shares distinct specializations that are also characteristic of _Proganochelys_ and modern turtles. The limb skeleton of _Odontochelys_ is robust, with four—instead of three, as in modern turtles—phalanges in the third and fourth digit of hands and feet, another primitive feature of _Odontochelys_. The fusion of two bones in the ankle (astragalus and calcaneum) indicates that the specimens were near adulthood, if not fully adult.

The most interesting aspect of _Odontochelys_ is the trunk skeleton, however. Osteoderms are altogether absent. The plastron, or ventral shield, is fully developed in _Odontochelys_ , even specialized in that it incorporates two instead of only one pair of mesoplastral plates. The hyoplastral, mesoplastral, and hypoplastral plates furthermore expand into distinct and pointed lateral projections, which results in curiously frilled lateral margins of the plastron. But whereas the plastron is fully ossified, the carapace failed to do so. A carapacial disk must have been developed in _Odontochelys_ , as its dorsal ribs resemble those of a modern-day turtle embryo, except for their mode of articulation on the dorsal vertebrae. In tetrapods generally, the dorsal ribs articulate on the vertebral centrum and its neural arch. Such is not the case in _Proganochelys_ and modern turtles, where the dorsal ribs instead articulate right on the boundary between two successive trunk vertebrae. This results in an alternate arrangement of the neural and costal plates, which develop from the dorsal neural arches and ribs, respectively. Such an alternate arrangement of neural and costal plates increases the robustness of the carapace. In contrast, _Odontochelys_ retains the primitive condition, with its dorsal ribs articulating on short and stubby transverse processes projecting from the vertebral centrum. However, as in modern-day turtle embryos, the dorsal ribs extend laterally in _Odontochelys_ , their growth redirected by the carapacial ridge, which must have been developed in the area between the fore- and hind limbs. The fact that all the ribs are projecting laterally, rather than being arranged in a flabellate (fan-shaped) manner, as in modern turtles, with the anteriormost one trending anterolaterally and the posteriormost one trending posterolaterally, indicates that the carapacial ridge in _Odontochelys_ may have been incompletely differentiated along the anterior and posterior margin of the carapacial disk (Nagashima et al., 2009). As a result, the shoulder blade (scapula) is located in front of, rather than below, the anteriormost dorsal rib. The dorsal ribs themselves are bladelike and broadened in a horizontal plane through the development of incremental bone along their anterior and posterior edges. However, as at a certain early developmental stage of a modern turtle, the ribs, although broadened, still remain separated from one another by a distinct intercostal space, which in _Odontochelys_ persists into adulthood. The dorsal vertebral neural arches are capped by squarish neural plates, but whether a nuchal plate (the first one to ossify in the carapace of modern turtles) was present or not in _Odontochelys_ unfortunately remains unknown. None of the other bony elements known to occur in the carapace of modern turtles was formed in _Odontochelys;_ a lateral bridge was likewise not developed. _Odontochelys_ is thus a truly transitional form, one that is on the way to becoming a turtle but has not quite arrived yet. Outwardly, _Odontochelys_ must have looked like a turtle, but the neck, head, and limbs could not be brought under the carapace, which was a soft structure constituted by a thickening of the skin and supported by broadened ribs.

**6.7.** Life reconstruction of _Odontochelys semitestacea_.

_Artwork by Marlene Donnelly, The Field Museum_.

Although _Odontochelys_ was found in marine sediments, the size and the anatomy of its limbs render it unlikely that it roamed the open sea, as modern sea turtles equipped with large flippers do. But its limb proportions do indicate that it was an aquatic turtle, comparable to those modern freshwater turtles that inhabit stagnant or small bodies of water (Joyce and Gauthier, 2003). It is conceivable, therefore, that _Odontochelys_ lived along the margins of the marine basin in a lagoonal setting, the corpses having drifted out into open waters after death to fossilize in the sediments in which we find them today. Indeed, the occurrence of fossilized driftwood and other plant remains in the same sediments indicates a relative proximity of coastal waters. Aquatic habits of _Odontochelys_ would also explain why the plastron, in contrast to the carapace, was fully developed and ossified. It would have protected the turtle from predators attacking a swimming organism from below, while at the same time it provided "bone ballast" to help counteract positive buoyancy caused by air-filled lungs (Taylor, 2000:16). Beyond such function, bone ballast can even allow for an adaptive increase in the size and hence lung volume. As one expert put it, "An increase in lung volume is potentially highly adaptive for animals that are slow swimmers, that feed on stationary or slowly moving food, and that live in shallow waters" (Taylor, 2000:16). This description of the kind of animal that would profit from enlarged lungs at the cost of having to carry bone ballast seems to capture _Odontochelys_ nicely. Neutral buoyancy achieved through bone ballast, combined with a larger supply of oxygen, would certainly suit a slow-moving predator foraging in shallow water. Add to that the insight that bone ballast such as the plastron in _Odontochelys_ , in addition to offering protection and countering buoyancy, would also fulfill a host of additional physiological functions, as does the shell in modern turtles (Jackson, 2011).

It has been noted already that secondarily marine reptiles often show a reduced degree of ossification in parts of their skeleton, perhaps an effect of the reduced gravitational pull in the aquatic environment. For example, elements in the wrist and ankle may be weakly ossified, or they may fail to ossify altogether. The question has thus come up whether _Odontochelys_ does indeed represent a primitive condition with respect to the ossification, or rather lack thereof, of the carapace. Could it not also be possible that _Odontochelys_ has secondarily reduced the carapace, as is also the case in the leatherback sea turtle ( _Dermochelys_ ), although an osteoderm covering is still retained in the latter species? (Reisz and Head, 2008). That such is unlikely, however, is indicated by many other primitive features seen in _Odontochelys_ , in particular the articulation of the dorsal ribs on short transverse processes of the dorsal vertebrae. A complete carapace was developed in _Proganochelys_ , where the dorsal ribs have already shifted their articulation to the boundary between two successive dorsal vertebrae. The same shift of the articulation of the dorsal ribs would be expected to be present in _Odontochelys_ if a fully developed and ossified carapace had ancestrally been present.

Outlook

_Odontochelys_ provides a striking example of a primitive turtle, and as such, it revived an age-old controversy. William Harvey (1578–1657) not only earned an everlasting place in the pantheon of the history of medicine for his discovery of blood circulation but was also interested in the embryonic development of the chicken. In the published report of his observations that appeared in 1651, Harvey compared the early chicken embryo, spread out on the surface of the yolk, to a maggot. The implicit idea here is that the higher animal, the chicken, recapitulates during its embryonic development a condition of form that is characteristic of a lower animal, the maggot. Naturalist-philosopher Charles Bonnet of eighteenth-century Geneva expressed the same idea when he found the playful behavior of a toddler to be more similar to the behavior of adult higher primates than to that of elder brothers and sisters. Darwin is known to have compared the behavior of his small daughters to that of the orangutans kept in Regent's Park Zoo in London. But it was left to Swiss-born comparative anatomist and paleontologist Louis Agassiz (1807–1873), the founder of Harvard's Museum of Comparative Zoology, to fully work out the famous proposal of a threefold parallelism. What Agassiz set out to prove was that the same sequence of transformational steps are expressed, in parallel, in the ascending classification of organisms, in embryonic development, and in the fossil record. One famous example he could draw on had been worked out by Étienne Geoffroy Saint-Hilaire at the Paris National Natural History Museum, who had studied the anatomy and embryology of fossil and living crocodiles. In primitive crocodiles, which appear early in the fossil record, the internal nares, or choanae, are located in an anterior position in the bony roof of the mouth; in advanced crocodiles, appearing later in the fossil record, the internal nares are displaced to a position at the far back end of the dermal palate. The position of the internal nares is thus an important character for the classification of crocodiles. It so happens that during the embryonic development of modern crocodiles, the gradual shift of the internal nares from an anterior to a posterior position can be observed, in correlation with the closing of the bony roof of the mouth (dermal palate) in an anteroposterior progression. Correspondingly, the earliest crocodiles that show up in the Triassic period show a relatively anterior position of the internal nares, in contrast to the Jurassic and Cretaceous crocodiles, in which the posterior shift of the internal nares is successively completed. A parallelism thus obtains between classification, embryonic development, and the sequence of appearance of fossils through geologic time.

Agassiz was an opponent of Darwin's theory of evolution, in contrast to Ernst Haeckel from the University of Jena, who through his work earned the reputation of being the German Darwin (Hoβfeld, 2007:454). It was Haeckel who, in a simplifying way, captured this parallelism in his famous biogenetic law. To disambiguate the term "evolution," which had previously been used to refer to embryonic development, Haeckel introduced the term "ontogeny" to refer to individual development. The term "phylogeny" he introduced to refer to Darwinian evolution, i.e., descent with modification. His slogan, the biogenetic law, then said that "ontogeny recapitulates phylogeny." Although a crass oversimplification, it captured the imagination of many of Haeckel's followers as much as it generated discussion and attracted criticism. There is no need to delve into the intricacies of the voluminous literature surrounding this issue. But even a cautious and hesitant acceptance of some of Agassiz's and Haeckel's ideas would point to the fact that information gleaned from embryonic development can prove important for the reconstruction of evolutionary relationships. With respect to the evolution of the carapace, for example, the oldest undisputed fossil turtle currently known, _Odontochelys_ , is not only the most primitive one in many respects but also shows striking parallels to embryonic stages of modern-day turtles. Although a passionate critic of Haeckel, Swiss comparative anatomist and phylogenetic systematist Adolf Naef (1883–1949) even went a step further. He again urged caution and acknowledged exceptions to the rule that the study of embryonic development can meaningfully inform the reconstruction of the tree of life. Yet given the ostensible success of comparative embryological investigations in deciphering evolutionary pathways, he went so far as to stipulate that the study of embryos could indeed result in predictions as to which kind of intermediary fossils might still be discovered in the future (Rieppel, Williams, and Ebach, 2013). In the case of turtles, Rathke's study (1848) on turtle development, as well as subsequent studies in turtle embryology and carapace development, should already have long ago rendered the Polka Dot Turtle Ancestor obsolete and focused expectations on some ancestral condition that is now beautifully exemplified by _Odontochelys_.

But whereas _Odontochelys_ did shed new light on the evolution of the turtle shell, nicely complementing ongoing developmental studies, it did not reveal the evolutionary origin of turtles. Although primitive in many respects relative to _Proganochelys_ and modern turtles, the mere presence of primitive features does not also reveal ancestry. But then, and in the light of the cladistic revolution, to ask for ancestors may not be the right thing to do anyway. The mystery of mysteries that still awaits its solution concerns the sister group relationships of turtles among reptiles, diapsids in particular. Such sister group relationships are generally revealed through shared evolutionary innovations. It is thus not the many primitive characteristics of _Odontochelys_ that promise to reveal the sister group relationships of turtles, because many other reptile groups may share the same primitive features—teeth on the upper and lower jaws, for example. What would be required for the resolution of the sister group relationships of turtles would be for _Odontochelys_ to share some evolutionary innovation with some other reptile group outside turtles. In that respect, _Odontochelys_ remains silent. Those evolutionary innovations that it does have are the same that characterize later, as well as modern, turtles. The ankle of _Odontochelys_ , for example, shows the same anatomical and functional specializations also seen in modern turtles—specializations that furthermore are also present in lepidosauromorphs, i.e., in the tuatara and scaly reptiles (lizards and snakes). _Odontochelys_ thus leaves us with the same alternatives of turtle relationships as had been recognized before, discussed in chapter 2. Comparative anatomy points strongly, but not exclusively, in the direction of a lepidosauromorph relationship of turtles. Molecular (DNA) data point overwhelmingly, but again not exclusively, in the direction of relationships of turtles with archosauromophs, a group that comprises crocodiles, dinosaurs, and birds, among others. After the recognition of the Permian _Eunotosaurus_ and the Ladinian _Pappochelys_ —both identified as diapsids—as putative stem-turtles, the number of paleontologists still linking turtles to Paleozoic parareptiles will probably shrink rapidly (Bever et al., 2015, and references therein; Schoch and Sues, 2015). In 2013, the 10th International Congress of Morphology was held in Barcelona, Spain, featuring a special symposium on turtle development and evolution. One science writer sketched the symposium as one where biologists told "dueling stories of how the turtles get their shells" (Lubick, 2013:329). Watching the battle over turtle origins and evolution rage among developmental biologists, molecular systematists, and paleontologists, her conclusions were, "So far, the groups have agreed to disagree" (Lubick, 2014:37).

Zuwachsknochen
OLIVIER RIEPPEL is Rowe Family Curator of Evolutionary Biology at The Field Museum in Chicago. His current research focuses on Triassic marine reptiles from southern China. He has also contributed extensively to the comparative anatomy and evolution of modern reptiles, most notably the evolutionary origin of turtles, and snakes. He publishes widely in the history and philosophy of comparative biology on topics as diverse as species concepts, mid-eighteenth-century French biology, and the history of phylogenetic systematics. Rieppel is on the editorial board of several peer-reviewed scientific journals and has himself published more than 350 scientific papers and eight books.
